Previously, an N-heterocyclic carbene (NHC) functionalized MCM-41 was synthesized and used as catalyst for the cycloaddition of CO2 with epoxides and aziridines (Scheme 39). The authors showed that the NHC–CO2 adduct was the catalytic active species. Different oxazolidinones were obtained with excellent yield and regioselectivity, under mild reactions conditions, 100 °C and 2 MPa CO2 pressure (Table 3, entries 8–11).311
 Scheme 39  Catalyzed cycloaddition of aziridines with CO2. 


2.3 Conclusion. 
Several investigations have focused on substituting toxic carbonylating agents by inexpensive, nontoxic and accessible CO2. Nevertheless, due to the CO2 inertness, the CO2 fixation to produce 2-oxazolidines is a difficult task and several limitations such as moderate yields and selectivity, the use of large amounts of homogeneous catalysts and harsh reaction conditions constitute impediments for the high-scale production. Therefore, the design and development of efficient, selective, recyclable and economic catalysts is still challenging. Today, we have knowledge and tools for the rational design of micro and mesoporous materials that can act as molecular sieves for reactant selection, while containing well-isolated single or multiple active sites accommodated on the zeolite walls, and so accessible to reactants through their diffusion. From there, materials could be developed with regular pores and cavities, even by “ab initio” synthesis312 that can control the reactant adsorption and transition-state stabilization and supplying the desired process selectivity.313 Besides, kinetic, theoretical and modelling studies, are still required to a rational design of the catalysts. Then, owing to their robustness, versatility in their possibilities of functionalization, and zeolite and OMS-based catalytic systems containing multi-active centers for activation of CO2 molecules and others components of the different CO2 fixation pathways may offer opportunities worth being explored for the production of carbamates.

3 Other formation of C–N bond
The use of CO2 as C1 building block is of great interest, and as aforementioned the main disadvantage is thermodynamic stability and kinetic inertness of CO2. The use of high-energy substrate such as epoxide and aziridine constitutes an effective strategy for the fixation of CO2, and among the active substrates, amines are good candidates for the formation of new C–N bond.252,314,315 Important examples are the synthesis of urea, carbamates or oxazolidinones and polyurethanes.
Quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione constitutes an important class of nitrogen-containing heterocycles with biological activities that find a large number of applications as pharmaceuticals.316–319 The synthesis of quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione derivatives starting from non-toxic, low cost and readily available substrates such as CO2 and 2-aminobenzonitrile is of great interest and constitutes an environmentally friendly process with 100% atom efficiency and an alternative route to the use of toxic phosgene, carbamates,320 anthranilic acids with urea,321,322 potassium cyanate and chlorosulfonyl isocyanate.319 A new, straightforward and overall green synthetic route of quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione in the presence of DBU has been reported. In the presence of a base catalyst, the formation of quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione involves the nucleophilic attack of the amino group on CO2. After cyclization, aperture and new cyclization, quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione is produced (Scheme 40).
 Scheme 40  Synthesis of quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione by carbonylation of 2-aminobenzonitrile and CO2. 
The use of CO2 and 2-aminobenzonitrile in the preparation of quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione is a recent synthesis pathway and has to be further explored. In fact, we have found two references using ordered micro or mesoporous materials. Recently, mesoporous basic zeolite for various transformations involving large organic molecules have been synthesized154 and functionalized with primary, secondary, and tertiary amines via a post-synthetic method via covalent bonding. Accordingly, nanocrystalline zeolite (Nano-ZSM-5) functionalized with basic ionic liquids was used for the quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione synthesis. Under optimized reaction conditions, 150 °C, 12 h, in protic solvent (DMF), selected CO2 pressure (3.5 MPa), 93.6% yield was achieved.166 The same group previously reported the use of covalent amine (3-(2-(2-aminoethylamino) ethylamino) propyltrimethoxysilane, AEPTMS) functionalized MCM-41, as an efficient and recyclable catalyst for the synthesis of a variety of quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione compounds from substituted 2-aminobenzonitriles and CO2 in water.323 Solvent played a fundamental role and highly polar solvents were adequate for achieving high yield, the best yields being achieved in DMF and water. Under optimized reaction conditions, at 130 °C, 18 h and 3.5 MPa CO2 pressure, 91% quinazoline-2,4(1H,3H)-dione yield was achieved. The successful scope of the catalyst, as well as the possibility of reusing the catalyst for five consecutive recycles was shown for various substrates.
4 Synthesis of carboxylic acid

4.1 Carboxylation reaction via CO2 insertion into the C–M bond for the synthesis of carboxylic acid. 
Carboxylic acids constitutes an important class of commodity chemicals with numerous applications as solvents, intermediates for pharmaceuticals, flavors and fragrances and monomers for polymer productions. Carboxylic acid is usually prepared by oxidation of hydrocarbons with the exception of acetic acid that are issued from carbonylation of methanol.
The production of carboxylic acid from CO2 insertion into the C–H bond is a green and atom economic reaction. Nevertheless, this reaction is thermodynamically unfavourable, being both endothermic and entropically disfavoured. Thus, this transformation is very challenging and the design and preparation of new catalysts to overcome this kinetic barrier is of great interest. Then, the preparation of carboxylic acids via carboxylation of alkanes by insertion of CO2 into a C-metal bond is still at an early stage and offers numerous research opportunities.


4.2 Synthesis of acetic acid. 
4.2.1 Introduction. 
Acetic acid is an important raw material in the chemical industry for the production of vinyl acetate and acetic anhydride. Acetic acid is produced by fermentation and chemical routes. Every year, 7 Mt of acetic acid are produced and 190 kt are issued from fermentation. Carbonylation of methanol, oxidation of aldehyde and oxidation of ethylene are the main chemical pathways, while the direct conversion of CO2 and CH4 to produce acetic acid is an attractive route to mitigate global warming and emission of greenhouse gases (Scheme 41). Using two gases with greenhouse effects, this route of acetic acid synthesis is considered green and represents an atom economical reaction. Nevertheless, the inertness of both molecules CH4 and CO2 makes this conversion very difficult and thermodynamically limited. Calculation of Gibbs energy showed an increase, ΔG = 71.17 kJ mol−1,324 and, the development of an efficient catalyst to overcome the kinetic barrier constitutes a great challenge.
 Scheme 41  Direct synthesis of acetic acid from CO2 and methane. 
Many heterogeneous catalytic systems have been developed such as Cu/Co supported on oxide,325 Pd and Pt on carbon or alumnina,326 Pd or Rh on silica.327 Nevertheless, up to now these catalysts have exhibited poor selectivity, low acetic acid yield and turnover frequency, and operate at high temperature, >425 °C.
4.2.2 Zeolite based catalysts for acetic acid synthesis. 
(a) Zeolite-based catalysts containing Cu and Fe for acetic acid synthesis. 
Recently, zeolites, especially metal ion-modulated zeolites, were found to be highly active in the dissociation of the C–H bond328 of methane and the C–C coupling between the methyl group (–CH3) and CO2.329 Accordingly, numerous studies have focused on the activation of CH4 molecules in metal exchanged zeolites.330,331 Naturally, CH4 is converted into methanol by the methane monooxygenase (MMO) enzymes using Cu and Fe as active metals. Two forms of monooxygenase exist, cytoplasmic (soluble) and membrane-bound (particulate) with different active sites, a bis(m-oxo)diiron332 and Cu cluster,333,334 respectively, the latter catalyzing the oxygen insertion into the C–H methane bond. Different studies attributed the catalytic activity to a Cu dimer334 or to a trinuclear Cu cluster.335
Accordingly, numerous catalytic systems based on iron and copper–oxygen species in inorganic matrices were studied for methane oxidation. One of the most promising catalysts is the copper and iron exchanged zeolites. Following these leads, different catalytic systems were designed and explored to determine the nature of active Cu-clusters for the conversion of CH4 into methanol. Recently,336 a study of cationic species in Cu-MOR materials showed that, in contrast to other metal-modulated zeolites337,338 where different cationic species are present, in Cu-MOR only homogeneous single sites are anchored. Trinuclear Cu-oxo clusters, [Cu3(μ-O)3]2+, anchored to two framework Al atoms located at the pore mouth of the 8-MR side pockets were well described. Moreover, these trinuclear Cu-oxo clusters were shown to be highly stable under dry conditions. The C–H bond activation of methane molecules to methanol production has been the objective of many studies. Despite the efforts and numerous works, the nature of the active sites and the mechanism of activation of both CH4 and CO2 molecules and their conversion into acetic acid on metal modulated zeolites are still the subject of numerous discussions and studies. A general mechanism of bifunctional activation with the formation of metal methyl species and CO2 protonation is favoured and depicted in the Scheme 42.329,339,340
 Scheme 42  Possible scheme for direct conversion of CH4 and CO2 over metal-modulated zeolite. 
Modelling studies have been most useful for a deeper understanding of the catalysis of metal-modulated zeolites for the direct conversion of CH4 and CO2. A recent theoretical study over Cu-modulated BEA, MFI, MOR and TON zeolites was performed by means of DFT calculations. The results showed that the catalytic activity of Cu-modulated zeolites is closely related to the structure of both active copper species and zeolite pore channels. The isomorphic substitution of the Si atom with the Al atom in the zeolite framework induces a negative charge that is compensated by mononuclear [Cu]2+, binuclear [CuOCu]2+, or trinuclear [Cu3(μ-O)3]2+ clusters.341 The study showed that under specific catalyst activation conditions, different Cu-cationic species can be preferentially stabilized in the corresponding optimized structures. The authors showed that [CuOCu]2+ is preferentially stabilized in the three-dimensional BEA and MFI zeolites while [Cu3(μ-O)3]2+ is preferentially stabilized in the one-dimensional MOR and TON zeolites. Experimental data reported in the bibliography also indicated that binuclear [CuOCu]2+ in the MFI zeolite342 exhibited high activity, while trinuclear [Cu3(μ-O)3]2+ was the dominant active sites in the MOR zeolite.343 Zeolites incorporating [CuOCu]2+ and [Cu3(μ-O)3]2+ species showed to be active for the direct conversion of CH4 and CO2, in comparison with the mononuclear copper ([Cu]2+) species. Specifically, the authors claimed that [CuOCu]2+ in the MFI and BEA zeolites are suitable to stabilize the transition state of the rate-determining C–C coupling by electrostatic forces and reduce the free energy and enthalpy barrier. On the other hand, the authors claimed the ability of [Cu3(μ-O)3]2+ present in the MOR zeolite to stabilize the transition state of the C–C coupling (Fig. 13). The differences in stabilization ability, resulted in a higher catalytic activity with a low apparent activation energy (57 kJ mol−1) in the MOR zeolite with [Cu3(μ-O)3]2+ clusters.
 Fig. 13  Direct conversion of carbon dioxide and methane to acetic acid over Cu-modulated zeolites. Reprinted with permission from ref. 337. Copyright 2019, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
In an experimental study in continuous fixed-bed reactor using Cu-exchanged M-ZSM-5 (M = Li+, Na+, K+ and Ca++), a synergetic effect between metallic Cu species and the basic cations was suggested to allow the simultaneous activation of CH4 and CO2 molecules to form acetic acid.329 The nature of the metallic Cu species was not discussed, and previous reduction of the catalyst was carried out before its use to prevent the formation of CuOx. The results showed that the CO2 activation was dependent on the alkali cations, favouring the CO2 insertion into C–H activated bond of CH4. Cationic properties such as size (Li < Na < K) and cation charge control both charge density and number of cations. Cu–Ca-ZSM5 exhibited the lowest activity. Adsorption studies revealed the higher CO2 adsorption capacity of Cu–K-ZSM5, attributed to the acid–base interaction between the ZSM-5 framework and CO2 molecules. Cu–Na-ZSM-5 catalyst gave the highest rate of acetic acid formation at the initial period but showed a higher deactivation after 1 h than Cu–K-ZSM-5. At 9 min of TOS, 18% of CH4 conversion and acetic acid yield can be achieved, at 500 °C in the presence of the Cu–Na-ZSM-5 catalyst. However, after 1 h, 0.6 and 0.15% of CH4 conversion and acetic acid yield, respectively, were obtained. The best acetic acid production (0.395 mmol gcat−1 h−1) was registered on the Cu–K-ZSM-5 catalyst, at 500 °C, for 10 h. The recyclability of the catalyst was shown and the regenerated catalysts exhibited similar activities to the fresh catalysts (close to 70% acetic acid). The deactivation was mainly due to Cu0 aggregation that could be re-dispersed via a calcination process.
Another theoretical study explored the effect of the metal cations within the zeolite for the direct conversion of CO2 and CH4 to acetic acid. The analysis and comparison of the catalytic properties of MFI zeolites exchanged with Be, Co, Cu, Mg, Mn and Zn cations to compensate the charge induced by the substitution of two Al atoms, was addressed.344 The location of the exchanged metal was within the six-member ring located at the surface of the straight channel. The calculation results showed CH4 dissociation is the rate-determining step, and CH4 dissociation and the free energy barriers of CH4 dissociation and C–C bond coupling were related to the electronegativity of the metal cation. Therefore, the lowest apparent activation energy of 11.5 kcal mol−1 was obtained over the Cu-MFI. Additionally, the authors showed that the co-adsorption of H2O allowed to reduce the acetic acid desorption energy which can be reduced from 42.0 to 20.5 kcal mol−1.
(b) Zeolite based catalysts containing Zn for acetic acid synthesis. 
Numerous investigations on the activation of C–H bond, for methanol or acetic acid production have reported the effect of the local structure of the active sites. Following this, the activity of Zn modified H-ZSM-5 containing isolated Zn2+ cations or small ZnO clusters was studied by means of DFT.340 The conversion of CH4 and CO2 revealed to be strongly dependent on the local structure of the active sites. Thus, Zn2+ cations located in the 5-membered rings of the sinusoidal and straight channels exhibited the best catalytic activity, higher than other Zn2+ cations or small ZnO clusters owing to suitable adsorption properties of CH4 and CO2 and favourable activation energies. Previously, a work based on solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy, reported the direct conversion of CH4 and CO2 into acetic acid on zinc-modified H-ZSM-5 (Zn/H-ZSM-5), at low temperature (250–500 °C), and described the reaction mechanism.345 The nature of Zn species was not discussed but the efficient activity and selectivity of the bifunctional zeolite was shown. As was proposed,346,347 the selective formation of methyl-metal species from methane was confirmed (zinc methyl species (–Zn–CH3)) with the simultaneously formation of protons. This mechanism can be well accepted in analogy to the activity of organozinc compounds.348 The adsorption of CO2 on the surface provides a reservoir for CO2 to be inserted into the Zn–C bond. Finally, the zeolite framework offers excellent ability to stabilize and transfer Brønsted protons and to protonate the surface acetate species (Fig. 14).
 Fig. 14  Mechanism to the activation of CH4 and the formation of zinc methyl species and Brønsted proton, while CO2 activation led to the formation of carbonate species (Steps 1 and 2). The insertion of CO2 into zinc methyl species provided surface acetate species that produced acetic acid upon protonation with the restoration of Zn/H-ZSM-5 catalyst (Steps 1 and 2). Reprinted with permission from ref. 345. Copyright 2013, American Chemical Society. 
Currently, it is well accepted that the catalytic activity is determined by the structure and nature of the active sites. The results of adsorption energies for zinc-modified H-ZSM-5 catalysts showed that adsorption strength of CH4 was dependent on Zn2+ cation location and decreased in the following order for the four-, five-, and six-membered rings in straight and sinusoidal channels, Z4 > Z5 > Z6 and M5 > M6, respectively, accordingly to the charge distribution of Zn/H-ZSM-5 based on the Mulliken charge analysis. Consequently, Zn2+ cations located in the 5-membered rings of the channels (straight and sinusoidal) exhibited better catalytic activity than other isolated Zn2+ sites and small ZnO clusters due to the favourable adsorption properties of reactants and activation energies.340
(c) Zeolite-based catalysts containing Au for acetic acid synthesis. 
The possible reaction mechanism for the direct formation of acetic acid from CH4 and CO2 over the Au-exchanged ZSM-5A has been explored by DFT study, involving Brønsted acid sites to the CO2 protonation and Au(I) active center to the formation methyl-gold species.339 The formation of methyl-gold species go through the CH4 molecule activation over the Au(I) active center in which the C–H dissociation proceed via the homolytic σ-bond activation, the calculated energy barrier for methane dissociation on Au-ZSM-5 being only 10.5 kcal mol−1. The methyl-gold species are very stable and the CO2 insertion difficult, 52.9 kcal mol−1 being the activation energy of the concerted mechanism (Fig. 15). The understanding of acetic acid formation over exchanged zeolites is a valuable contribution in the path to the design of more efficient catalysts.
 Fig. 15  Energy profile for the acetic acid formation via the concerted bi-functional mechanism. Bond distances are in Å and energies are in kcal mol−1. Reprinted with permission from ref. 339. Copyright 2012, Royal Society of chemistry. 
4.2.3 Conclusions. 
The reported works provide relevant and crucial insights about the mechanism of the direct conversion of CO2 and CH4 into acetic acid within the pores of microporous materials, the limiting step and barrier energies that catalytic systems must overcome. The structure and composition of the zeolite framework stabilize extraframework metal species that are able to activate methane and form methyl-metal species. Up to now, among the few reported experimental data, the best results were achieved in the presence of Cu–K-ZSM-5 catalyst with 0.395 mmol gcat−1 h−1 acetic acid production, at 500 °C, for 10 h.329 The synthesis of an efficient catalyst with active sites for the simultaneous activation of CH4 activation and CO2 insertion remains crucial and challenging. Thus, the catalyst based on zeolite or OMS should have metal active sites for CH4 activation, adequate CO2 adsorption capacity and CO2 insertion activity that will be achieved by the incorporation of Lewis acid sites, basic sites and oxygen vacancies. So far, important insights have been made using both experimental and theoretical approaches. However, systemic study and understanding of reaction mechanisms, determination of reaction intermediates and catalyst active species by computational studies and in situ characterization are imperative. Furthermore, a performing reaction system should be designed to overcome the limitations of thermodynamic equilibrium and improve the production of acetic acid. In addition, a reaction system using plasma could offer new opportunities.


4.3 Synthesis of propanoic acid. 
Propanoic acid finds wide application as an intermediate in the synthesis of commodity chemicals and polymers, and used as a preservative in food. Propanoic acid is mainly produced by hydrocarboxylation of ethylene in the presence of Pd(AcO)2349 and could be ideally prepared from the insertion of CO2 into ethane (Scheme 43). To overcome thermodynamic limitations the design and preparation of new catalysts is a great challenge. Very few references are reported in the bibliography concerning the preparation of propanoic acid from CO2.
 Scheme 43  Direct synthesis of propanoic acid from CO2 and ethane. 
In analogy with the synthesis of acetic acid, metal modulated zeolite can be good candidates to activate CO2 and C–H bond to allow the insertion of CO2 into ethane. In this sense, the activation of short chain alkanes and specially ethane over zeolites has been reported.350,351 Accordingly, a theoretical study using density functional theory (DFT) has recently investigated possible reaction pathways for the conversion of CO2 and C2H6 to propanoic acid over Au-exchanged MCM-22 zeolite catalyst.352 The insertion of CO2 was envisioned following two different routes. The first one involves only the metal center, the CO2 insertion into the Au-H bond followed by the C–C coupling and the formation of propanoic acid with an activation energy for the rate-determining step of 48.2 kcal mol−1. The second route involves the metal center and framework Brønsted acid sites with the formation of gold ethyl hydride species with the corresponding transfer of hydrogen to the oxygen framework providing the Brønsted-acid site, followed by the protonation of CO2 and the simultaneous C–C coupling. In this second case, the activation energy is 44.2 kcal mol−1 and it is the favoured route, at low temperatures (Scheme 44). The authors estimated that an increase of temperature could be enough to overcome the barrier energy to perform the direct insertion of CO2 into ethane in the presence of Au-exchanged MCM-22.
 Scheme 44  Proposed mechanism for the direct synthesis of propanoic acid from CO2 and ethane over Au/MCM-22 catalyst. 


4.4 Conclusions. 
Carboxylic acids would ideally be produced from the direct reaction of carbon dioxide with alkanes and some studies provide essential data to rationalize the activity and selectivity for the conversion of CO2 and alkanes to carboxylic acids. However, the use of CO2 as a renewable building block for the synthesis of carboxylic acids via green processes is still at an early stage and, despite great scientific interest and advantages, remains a big challenge. Different synthetic routes involving the use of activated substrates, stoichiometric organometallic reagents, reducing agents, salts, with the co-production of large amounts of residues present some achievements but they have significant drawbacks. Undoubtedly, the development of efficient and multifunctional heterogeneous catalysts is crucial. Computational chemistry and in situ spectroscopy should be important tools to provide in-depth knowledge determine the active species and reaction intermediates that will enable the design of new catalysts and processes to achieve environmental and economic goals. Zeolite and OMS will definitely play a crucial role due to their unique tunable properties and their chemical and thermal stability. So far, CO2 fixation in carboxylic acids remains unattainable from an industrial point of view.

5 Hydrogenation of CO2
Hydrogen is a promising fuel but it lacks the energy density required for practical use. Conversion of CO2 and hydrogen into liquid fuels such as methane or methanol, via Power-to-Liquid (PtL) processes, is more desirable from an energy density perspective. Moreover, considering the intermittence of renewable sources to produce H2,353 CO2 hydrogenation to methanol, methane or dimethylether can provide an important approach to store energy. However, a significant improvement in catalyst technology for CO2 conversion is required for carbon capture and utilization to become an economically viable alternative. Hydrogen can be produced from fossil sources such as natural gas and coal, and from water electrolysis using electricity from nuclear plants or from renewable sources, such as wind, solar, geothermal, and hydroelectric power. Therefore, to fulfill the concepts of sustainability and green chemistry and make CO2 hydrogenation a valuable strategy, hydrogen has to be green and produced from non-fossil sources. Currently, different approaches to green H2 production, such as water electrolysis using fuel cells, the biomass thermo-chemical process, anaerobic fermentation and water splitting through photo-electrolysis, are being considered.

5.1 Hydrogenation/reduction of CO2 to formic acid. 
For several CO2 conversions, the enthalpy changes revealed that transformations are exothermic despite the high stability of CO2. Nevertheless, in almost all cases, the Gibbs free energy changes are positive and thermodynamically unfavoured.354–357 Negative Gibbs free energy changes often correspond to the hydrogenation reaction where very stable compounds, such as water, are produced, or to CO2 transformation where C–O bonds are not broken.358 Although CO2 transformations are not thermodynamically favourable, in practice, by working under adequate conditions such as pressure, temperature and ratio of reactants, processes involving CO2 activation and reactions must be possible and of clear interest. Hydrogenation/reduction processes are attractive and valuable processes even if it is still challenging to convert CO2 into platform molecules with high activity and selectivity. If this is achieved and industrially applied, it will impact our daily life, economy, CO2 mitigation, environment and climatic changes.
5.1.1 Introduction. 
Formic acid is the simplest organic acid. It is eco-friendly, noncorrosive, easily biodegradable, and a valuable chemical used as a dye in the textile and leather industries, as an intermediate in the chemical and pharmaceutical industry,359 a green solvent and moreover a promising hydrogen storage component. Reversible processes of hydrogenation/dehydrogenation make possible the hydrogen storage through the formation of formic acid from CO2 hydrogenation, and the decomposition into CO2 and H2 is thermodynamically downhill (Scheme 45).360,361
 Scheme 45  Reversible hydrogen storage. 
The current industrial methods for formic acid production include hydrolysis of methyl formate or formamide, oxidation of biomass359 and the reaction of CO with water.362 All these methods lead to the production of hazardous wastes and high energetic costs. According to the report of Coval Energy, the global annual demand for formic acid was around 870 ktons in 2021 with a forecasted compound annual growth rate (CAGR) of 3.87% during the period 2022–2027.363
Direct hydrogenation of CO2 into formic acid is a green and 100% atom economy reaction allowing to mitigate CO2 emissions and to store H2 in a liquid form. CO2 hydrogenation is an important approach to use CO2 to produce C1 chemicals but it is still challenging due to CO2 inertness and stability. Moreover, this conversion is entropically disfavoured since it involves a phase change from gaseous reagents into a liquid product (Scheme 46).
 Scheme 46  Hydrogenation of CO2 into formic acid. 
Significant progress in the development of highly efficient and robust homogeneous catalytic systems, especially Ru362,364 and Ir-based metallic complex requiring the use of a co-catalyst such as phosphines, amines or carbonate salts, has been achieved.365–368 Nevertheless, in spite of their excellent catalytic performance in terms of activity and selectivity369 with TON and TOF of up to 110000 h−1,370 several drawbacks such as toxicity, difficult separation of formic acid or formic acid derivatives and the absence of recyclability of the homogeneous catalysts make it necessary to develop efficient, robust and recyclable catalytic systems.371,372
5.1.2 Zeolite-based catalysts for formic acid synthesis. 
Heterogeneous catalysts have been widely explored for formic acid production from CO2 in the presence of nanoparticulate noble and non-noble metals supported on carbon, metal oxides and silica,373 see for instance, RANEY® nickel in the presence of amines,374 synthesized nanoporous nickel,375 Pd/C,376 and Au/TiO2.377 However, only few works have studied the potential catalytic properties of zeolite based catalysts for this reaction.
Virtual screening is an important tool to avoid or support tedious studies based on experimental trial and error studies. Computational screening is a method that broadly combines computational algorithms for selecting promising candidates from a large library of materials. The combination of the modular building-block approach and the potential for computational screening has allowed the introduction of the Materials Genome Initiative (MGI) to “discover, develop, and deploy new materials twice as fast”.378 MGI attempts to combine theory, computation, synthesis, and characterization to boost the discovery and synthesis of new materials.379 In agreement, a computational screening of well-known zeolites as great catalysts combining molecular simulation and machine-learning techniques is of much use for advancing synthesis and reactivity380–383 and also for conversion of CO2 into useful products.384 In this study, the combination of adsorption simulations, dual-adsorption models, energy–entropy calculations, and statistical and machine learning modelling techniques allowed establishing structure–property relationships based on structural characteristics (pore size, surface area, and voids) and zeolite performance for the production of methane, methanol, formic acid and formaldehyde. Therefore, promising zeolite candidates were determined to have a cavity size of around 6 Å to maximize the strength of dual-adsorption of hydrogen and CO2. Then, the possible mechanism of CO2 hydrogenation to formic acid using Lewis acid zeolites has been investigated by using density functional theory (DFT).385 The CO2 hydrogenation and formate ion formation was considered to be the rate-determining step for Sn-ZSM-5 zeolites. The activation barrier was calculated for both perfect (38.2 kcal mol−1) and defect Lewis center (33.0 kcal mol−1). A Sn-ZSM-5 active site allows minimizing the activation energy in comparison to Sn-BEA or Sn-FAU. The order of activation energy for the metal center was determined to be Hf > Zr > Sn > Ge, which is dependent on the charge transfer from the catalytic active site to the reacted molecule. This result showed the importance of charge transfer from the metal to CO2 for the activation of the CO bonds and also the H–H bond breaking via oxygen abstraction at the Sn–OH sites of defect zeolites.
Recent studies showed the good catalytic properties of molecular-sized Ir4 clusters stabilized in FAU and MWW zeolites for hydrogenation and dehydrogenation reactions.51,386,387 Accordingly, a theoretical work reporting the CO2 mechanism to produce formic acid catalyzed by faujasite-supported Ir4 clusters was reported.388 Both hydrogenation of CO2 into CO, thermodynamically favoured, and hydrogenation of CO2 into formic acid, kinetically preferred under water free-conditions, can take place. The important effect of confined water cooperation for both hydrogenation processes was shown. The co-adsorbed water molecule facilitates the H+ transfer step by a water molecule between the zeolite Brønsted acid sites and Ir4 cluster. However, if the formation of the Ir–COOH carboxyl moiety is facilitated by the presence of water this latter also promotes the selective decarbonylation of COOH species and the production of CO. The authors so concluded that it is impossible to obtain formic acid in the presence of water using Ir4/FAU as the catalyst, and that only an effective water-removal and optimized process could ensure the selective formation of formic acid.
5.1.3 OMS-based catalysts for formic acid synthesis. 
The immobilization of homogeneous catalysts via covalent bonding is an alternative to overcome the problems of separation and to improve the environmental impact and costs, by combining the advantages of both homogeneous and heterogeneous catalysis. This strategy has received a lot of interest for the preparation of formic acid.372,389–391
Following the above direction, MCM-41 functionalized with γ-NH2, CN and SH-propyl trimethoxysilane incorporating RuCl3 were prepared and tested in the hydrogenation of CO2 into formic acid, at 80 °C, 5.4 MPa H2 and 9.3 MPa CO2 (SC) partial pressures, 1 h and in the presence of ethanol as the solvent.392 The importance of the nature and use of a solvent as well as of the ligands showed the influence on the activity of the MCM-41 immobilized ruthenium complex. The three types of MCM-41 immobilized ruthenium complexes exhibited a TON of up to 1022. Nevertheless, the stability of the catalyst was limited. The structured mesoporous material with SH groups showed the best stability but the lowest activity while the material with NH2 groups exhibited lower stability but better activity. The differences in activity and stability were attributed to the complexation ability of the different groups (NH2, CN and SH). More recently, the preparation and catalytic performance of SiO2 and MCM-41 supported Ruthenium materials for the selective hydrogenation of CO2 to formic acid, at 80 °C, 40 bar (PH2:PCO2 = 1:2) were reported.393 In terms of TON/TOF values, Ru/MCM-41 exhibited higher catalytic performance than Ru/SiO2 due to its textural properties and large surface area that allow high metal dispersion. Additionally, the catalytic performance of Ru/MCM-41 was improved with the use of sulfated and fluorinated ionic liquid [DAMI][CF3CF2CF2CF2SO3] (DAMI = 1,3-di(N,N -dimethylaminoethyl)-2-methylimidazolium). In this way, the production of formic acid with TON value up to 1780 with Ru/MCM-41 in [DAMI] [CF3CF2CF2CF2SO3] ionic liquid medium and the stability of catalytic properties in the presence of water were demonstrated. The recyclability of the catalyst was shown and Ru/MCM-41 in [DAMI] [CF3CF2CF2CF2SO3] was recycled eight times with practically no loss of activity. This catalytic reaction system offers a green attempt to the production of formic acid and can be used as a leading direction for further developments.
5.1.4 Conclusion. 
This short review of the state-of-the-art related to the use of ordered microporous and mesoporous based catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation into formic acid clearly revealed that the potential of these types of materials has been underconsidered despite the requirement of a novel catalytic system for the challenging formic acid production as a hydrogen storage material.
Indeed, the direct hydrogenation of CO2 to formic acid is an ideal reaction with 100% atom economy. Nevertheless, at the same time, it is a very challenging task since activating both H2 and CO2 molecules is a hard task, and CO2 hydrogenation to formic acid is thermodynamically unfavored. Moreover, to achieve a higher yield and turnover number, stoichiometric amounts of organic or inorganic bases (DBU, NaOH, KOH, K2CO3, and TEA) are widely used to shift the thermodynamic equilibrium to formate. Note that the present reaction procedure requires further neutralization steps with a stoichiometric amount of acid that produces a high amount of salts and waste, and tedious recovery of formic acid. To achieve environmental and economic goals, a greener and more efficient process is imperative, and heterogeneous catalysts are very valuable, attractive, and prime candidates. Thus, an efficient catalyst will have active centers to adsorb and activate hydrogen (metal species) based on noble metals (Ru, Rh, Pd, and Au) and other metals such as Co, Ni, Cu and Fe, high CO2 adsorption capacity and CO2 activation, which will be achieved by the incorporation of acidic and basic Lewis sites and oxygen vacancies. Undoubtedly, the support will play a key role in adsorbing CO2, and dispersing and stabilizing the different active species. Zeolite and OMS will be major players due to their unique and tunable properties (high surface area, Lewis and Brønsted acid/base properties, and chemical and thermal stability). In addition, a thorough understanding of the reaction mechanism, kinetics and determination of the active species and reaction intermediates will only be possible through computational chemistry and in situ spectroscopy studies, fundamental tools for the design of new catalysts and processes.


5.2 Hydrogenation of CO2 to methanol. 
5.2.1 Introduction. 
In the context of H2 and CO2 conversion into liquid fuels via Power-to-Liquid processes, methanol production from CO2 is of much interest. Methanol is a large scale product with an industrial production capacity of about approximately 140 million metric tons in 2018 that is expected to double to reach 280 million metric tons by 2030.394 Industrial production of methanol is from synthesis gas mixtures (H2/CO2/CO) at high pressures (50–100 bar) and temperatures (200–300 °C) over Cu/ZnO/Al2O3 catalysts.395 Methanol constitutes an important chemical feedstock and a platform molecule to produce a large variety of bulk commodities396 and fuels397 for engines and fuel cells. Numerous methanol commodity derivatives are produced for their use in our daily life such as paints, polymers, plastics, resins, adhesives, and antifreezes. Taking into account the potential of methanol, George A. Olah and co-workers395 published in 2006 a report of current fossil fuels as energy sources and future alternative sources. There, they discuss the important and future role of methanol and advocated the “methanol economy” whose concept purveys the idea that methanol can be used as an alternative way for storing, transporting and using energy.398

Fig. 16 illustrates the importance of methanol as carbon feedstock obtained from green hydrogenation of CO2 to reach carbon neutrality, and a crucial pathway to reach circularity. One of the most promising route to produce methanol is the direct CO2 hydrogenation. The development of active catalysts and specially zeolite-based catalysts for the conversion of methanol to olefins (MTO),399–403 aromatics, propylene (MTP), dimethyl ether404,405 and fuels406 will play a key role due to the flexibility in their catalytic properties such as the versatility in their composition and the nature of the active sites, robustness, stability and ready availability of zeolites and OMS, and should mark a new breakthrough in the field of molecular sieves.
 Fig. 16  Importance of methanol as carbon feedstock obtained from green hydrogenation of CO2 to reach carbon neutrality. 
5.2.2 Thermodynamics for CO2 hydrogenation to methanol. 
Methanol is typically synthesized from syngas (H2 + CO) obtained from fossil fuels.371,407 This process is highly exothermic and the major challenge is to control and remove heat excess to shift the equilibrium towards methanol production minimizing side reactions and catalyst sintering. The commercial catalyst generally contains Cu and a mixture of oxides such as ZnO/Al2O3 and operates under relatively “soft” conditions, 210–270 °C and 50–100 bar.408 The main side products are higher alcohols, methane, methyl formate and dimethyl ether. The formation of methanol by direct hydrogenation of CO2 constitutes an alternative and a challenging route. Direct hydrogenation of CO2 competes directly with reverse water-gas shift (RWGS) and the large production of water has a severe negative effect over the catalyst life and the performance of ternary catalyst Cu–ZnO–Al2O3. Moreover, the hydrophilic properties of alumina favour sintering and aggregation of metals. CO produced from RWGS can undergo hydrogenation into methanol (Scheme 47). From thermodynamics standpoint, the enthalpy variation shows that CO and CO2 hydrogenations are exothermic and involve a decrease of volume which indicates that the use of lower reaction temperatures and higher pressures is thermodynamically favourable, while the reverse water-gas-shift reaction is strongly endothermic (Scheme 47). However, a higher temperature favours CO2 activation, while an increase in temperature also promotes the undesirable formation of CO and H2O, limiting CO2 conversion.
 Scheme 47  Formation of methanol by direct hydrogenation of CO2 and CO produced from RWGS. 
5.2.3 Mechanism insights for CO2 hydrogenation to methanol. 
A thorough understanding of the reaction mechanism and how the catalyst participates should help to realise a molecular design of a catalyst able to work at low temperature (<200 °C). Two main kinetic expressions have been reported for the formation of methanol considering the commercial Cu/ZnO/Al2O3 catalyst.409,410 The first kinetic model considers the formation of methanol from both CO and CO2 whereas the second model only considers CO2 as the source of methanol. Despite the large number of mechanistic studies based on the theoretical and experimental data, the mechanism of methanol synthesis from CO2 remains controversial as evidence for the formation of both, formate,411–413 being the hydrogenation of HCOO to H3CO the rate-limiting step,414 and CO, as intermediates415–417 (Scheme 48). Indeed, some reports postulate that the first step of the direct hydrogenation of CO2 is the formate intermediate formation, while others propose that RWGS takes place followed by CO hydrogenation into methanol.416Scheme 49 illustrates the possible oxygenated products produced from CO2 hydrogenation.
 Scheme 48  Proposed reaction mechanism for CO2 hydrogenation to methanol according to different studies. 
 Scheme 49  Possible oxygenated products obtained from CO2 hydrogenation. 
5.2.4 Catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation to methanol. 
There are more than 200 papers published in the last decade dealing with the hydrogenation of CO2 into methanol. Nevertheless, few studies reported the direct hydrogenation of CO2 into MeOH using zeolite based catalysts. However, the textural properties of ordered mesoporous silica have been used to disperse the metal-oxide active phase and to take advantage of the confinement. Due to the enhanced catalytic performance of the ternary catalyst Cu/ZnO/Al2O3, and the similarity between syngas-to-methanol conversion, Cu-based and Cu/ZnO based materials have been extensively investigated. Up to now, Cu is regarded as the main active component for CO2 hydrogenation and ZnO is regarded to promote hydrogen spillover and the dispersion of Cu nanoparticles. Numerous papers described the catalytic performance of catalysts based on Cu and/or Pd.371,418 Actually, bifunctional metal-zeolite based materials are preferentially used for the synthesis of dimethylether, olefins or aromatics since it is possible to take advantage of the acid properties of these materials and shift the process selectivity avoiding competitive side reactions. Indeed, the presence of multinuclear metal centers and active sites in a single solid catalyst presents several advantages over mononuclear catalysts due to the cooperativity effect between the different centers such as synergetic activation of substrates and electronic interactions between the metal centers, which is potentially beneficial to the catalytic activity. Hence, investigations have focussed on the preparation of metal oxides with excellent activity and stability for the hydrogenation of CO2 and efficient catalysts should exhibit high specific surface area, high number of active sites, high dispersion, optimum acid/base site distribution, and small particle size in order to increase the activity and selectivity to methanol.
(a) OMS-based catalysts for CO2 conversion to methanol. 
(i) Cu/OMS-based catalysts for CO2conversion to methanol. Cu is a good monometallic catalyst for methanol synthesis419 but, if used alone, results in low catalytic rates and selectivity towards methanol. However, the Cu catalytic performance can be enhanced by forming a Cu/ZnO active phase. Indeed, pure Cu surface is shown to convert CO2 to CO, while combination of Cu and ZnO greatly boosts the methanol formation,420 since the use of metal oxides allows high dispersion and stabilization of Cu due to special metal/metal interaction, basicity, reducibility properties and oxygen lattice, that favour the coordination and activation of CO2, maximizing the catalyst activity for methanol synthesis.421,422 So far, Cu/ZnO catalysts for industrial applications or theoretical research are still in the forefront of the investigation due to their prominent catalytic properties.422,423 The studies to improve Cu-based catalysts properties for CO2 hydrogenation into MeOH focus on improving the Cu nanoparticle dispersion by varying the preparation method and/or metal precursors and by incorporating promoters.424,425
To optimize the catalytic features, the understanding and definition of the nature of active sites in Cu/ZnO is fundamental. The metal oxide interface is crucial to enhance and create catalytic ability and the synergy of Cu and ZnOx at the interface was shown to promote the methanol synthesis.426,427 Nevertheless, destructive effects of the hydrothermal reaction conditions induce a rupture at the Cu–ZnOx interface, sintering of Cu particles that modify the catalytic properties and decrease methanol selectivity.428,429
The interaction between Cu and oxide components enables the formation of Cu–MOx (M = Zn and/or Zr) active species at the Cu–MOx interface and the catalytic activity depends on the interaction between Cu and metal oxide components.430 Therefore, the confinement or encapsulation431 of Cu–MOx on the walls of porous materials was considered as a promising route to improve the metal dispersion and the sintering resistance, and thus enhance the stability and selectivity of the catalytic system to methanol production. Accordingly, due to their textural properties (high surface areas, thermal and chemical stability and pore-size) ordered mesoporous materials offer opportunities to well-disperse and stabilize Cu–MOx or other active metal-oxide compounds (Fig. 17).
 Fig. 17  High dispersion and stabilization of metal–MOx (active metal–oxide phase) on OMS. 
(ii) Binary OMS-based catalysts for CO2hydrogenation to methanol. As we mentioned above, the application of Cu alone-catalysts are limited due to the low stability and selectivity to MeOH. Therefore, modified catalysts with MOx (Ce, Mn, Ga, Zr, and Ti) have been the purpose of many investigations due to the interfacial effects between the different metal phases that contribute to boost the catalytic performance of the final catalysts.432–434 In particular, the basic properties of some oxides are expected to enhance the adsorption/activation of CO2. For example, in Cu/ZrO2 catalysts, La doping promoted formate hydrogenation into MeOH and inhibited formate dissociation into CO.435 Following these leads, Cu–LaOx catalysts supported on SBA-15 were prepared wherein a Cu–LaOx interface was generated through the interaction of highly dispersed Cu nanoparticles with LaOx in the SBA-15 wall.436 The presence of LaOx species was beneficial to prevent the sintering of Cu and to enhance CO2 activation by adsorption of CO2 molecules at the Cu–LaOx interface. The optimized Cu1La0.2/SBA-15 catalyst displayed the best catalytic performance in terms of methanol selectivity (81.2%) and stability, with 5.7% CO2 conversion (Table 4, entry 2). The preparation and characterization of Cu–Ga/SBA-15 catalysts was also reported.437 The presence of Ga promotes the dispersion and stabilization of the Cu phase but also provides acidic sites with enhanced acidic strength due to the combination with silica. The addition of Ga improved the catalytic performance of the un-doped Cu-catalysts. 3% CO2 conversion and 71% MeOH + DME were reached with the Cu–Ga/SBA-15 catalyst (Table 4, entry 3). Attempts to disperse Cu/ZnO on SBA-15 (CZS), Al-SBA-15 (CZAS), Al2O3 (CZA) and Al2O3–ZrO2 (CZAZ) using impregnation methods were also investigated.438 The Cu/ZnO dispersed on the SBA-15 support presented the largest Cu surface area and dispersion, and the smallest particle size and was easily reduced. H2-TPR profiles showed two reduction peaks at 290 °C corresponding to the reduction of Cu2+ into Cu0 and the reduction of Cu2+ into Cu+ and into Cu0 at 337 °C. However, for Cu–ZnO/Al-SBA-15 (CZAS), a single and broad reduction peak at 370 °C was registered, attributed to strong metal–support interactions, in agreement with larger average particle size and lower metallic phase dispersion (Fig. 18). Thus, Cu/ZnO-SBA-15 exhibited the highest catalytic activity in comparison to Cu/ZnO–Al-SBA-15, Al2O3 and Al2O3/ZrO2-supported catalysts. The highest 91.32% methanol selectivity was obtained with the Cu/ZnO-SBA-15 catalyst with up to 14% CO2 conversion (Table 4, entries 4–6). The same group also studied the effect of the Cu/Zn ratio in Cu–Zn-SBA-15 catalysts on the catalytic performance in a fixed-bed.439 A positive effect of the incorporation of Zn was shown on Cu surface areas and dispersion, while a high Zn content induced an increase in the particle size and agglomeration of Cu. CO2 conversion and methanol selectivity increased while the Zn content decreased from 70% to 30%. This trend was attributed to the synergetic effect between Cu and ZnO that promotes H2 spillover. Then, a maximum 51.4 gMeOH h−1 gcat−1 productivity was reached for a metal ratio Cu:Zn = 7:3 and a linear relationship between the Cu surface area and methanol productivity was shown, with 14.2% CO2 conversion and 92.1% MeOH selectivity (Table 4, entry 7).
