 Fig. 18  H2-TPR profiles of Cu/ZnO supported catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 438. Copyright 2019, Frontiers. 
Recently, the catalytic performance of Cu supported on Zr-doped SBA-15 was explored.440 The Zr doping allowed promoting the formation of acid sites, and because Cu is prone to occupy the acid sites, higher Cu dispersion was reached. Before Cu nanoparticles’ confinement inside the pores, the external surface of Zr-SBA-15 was methylated and functionalized with amine groups to promote Cu2+ coordination. High catalytic activity of Cu/Zr-SBA-15 was reached, achieving 15% CO2 conversion but with low selectivity to MeOH, close to 27% (Table 4, entry 8). In a following work, the Cu/Zr-SBA-15 catalytic performance was reported under optimized reaction conditions and exhibited 25% CO2 conversion after 14 days with considerably improved methanol selectivity close to 80% (Table 4, entry 9).441 More recently, the same group reported the preparation of Cu/Zr-SBA-15 following different synthetic routes. Zr-SBA-15 was synthesized using different silica sources (TEOS and SMS) while Cu was loaded following two methods, infiltration (Inf) or evaporation induced wetness impregnation (EIWI).442 Zr-SBA-15 presented different morphological properties influenced by the silica source and synthesis conditions, while the Cu loading method has an influence on the strength and density of acid sites. Differences in catalytic selectivity were attributed to the Zr content that contributes to the electron/oxygen mobility of Cu and controlled the amount of weak and medium acid sites. The DME selectivity increases when increasing the strength of acid sites with medium acid strength. Then, Cu/Zr-SBA-15_EIWI with high total concentration of acid sites exhibited high methanol selectivity (92%) at 6.5% CO2 conversion (Table 4, entry 10). DRIFTS studies revealed that DME formation came from two surface methoxy groups in the CO2 hydrogenation reaction, when its concentration is high enough to form DME, while methanol formation is favoured since it only requires one molecule (Fig. 19).
 Fig. 19  Methanol and DME formation from methoxy groups on the catalyst surface. Reprinted with permission from ref. 442. Copyright 2019 Elsevier Inc. All rights reserved. 
(iii) Tertiary OMS-based catalysts for CO2hydrogenation to methanol. Numerous investigations have focused on the confinement of the Cu–ZnO–ZrO2 active phase in OMS443 due to the activity of Cu–Zn–Zr catalysts444,445 for methanol synthesis. Accordingly, the ordered mesoporous channels of SBA-15 were exploited to control the particle size, dispersion, and morphology of Cu-oxide nanoparticles, and to promote the interaction between multi-active sites for the double activation of H2 and CO2. CO2 is adsorbed on the active support while H2 is adsorbed on the Cu phase to provide atomic hydrogen. The presence of zirconia was shown to enhance both the yield and selectivity to methanol. The maximum activity for the catalyst CZZS_20_1 (Cu–ZnO–ZrO2/SBA-15) with a space-time-yield (STY) of methanol of 376 mgCH3OH h−1 gcat−1, 37-times higher than that of the unsupported homologue was reached. The enhanced catalytic performance of the SBA-15 supported catalyst was attributed to the exposure of the active phase to the CO2 and H2. An experiment of 50 h of TOS support showed the high performance, stability and the industrial potential of the CZZS_20_1 catalyst (Table 4, entry 11).
The confinement of the Cu–ZnO–MnO (CZM) active phase was also achieved in morphologically distinct siliceous porous carriers such as SBA-15, MCF and KIT-6.446 SBA-15 is a 2D mesoporous silica with pore channels in a hexagonal arrangement, MCF (mesostructured cellular foam) is a 3D mesoporous silica with large uniform spherical cells and KIT-6 is an ordered 3-D mesoporous silica with a gyroid cubic structure. The effect of different textural properties was expected to greatly influence the catalytic behavior of the prepared catalyst. Indeed, a large surface area carrier was expected to promote the formation of small metal nanoparticles with high dispersion, enabling the exposure of a large metal surface area to the reactants. CZM-SBA-15 and KIT-6 resulted to be the most efficient catalysts with 99% methanol selectivity and particularly, CZM KIT-6 exhibited a higher CO2 conversion (8.2%) and STY (Table 4, entries 12 and 13). The Cu0 particle size after reduction followed the order CZM/KIT-6 < CZM/SBA-15 < CZM/MCF, inversely to the Cu0 surface area that followed the order CZM/KIT-6 > CZM/SBA-15 > CZM/MCF. Thus, together with a higher copper surface area, the particular morphological features of KIT-6 enabled higher CO2 effective diffusivity. Therefore, the CO2 conversion was correlated with the order of copper surface area, since dissociative adsorption of H2 occurs on the copper sites, and as could be expected, the production of atomic hydrogen increases when increasing the copper surface area. The H2-TPR reduction peak was deconvoluted into two peaks assigned to the low-temperature reduction of well-dispersed CuO, and to the high-temperature reduction of bulk CuO (Fig. 20).435 For CZM/KIT-6, a lower temperature for both reduction peaks was recorded, indicating the higher dispersion of CuO and lower metallic phase/support interaction for this sample.
 Fig. 20  H2-TPR profiles of (a) CZM/SBA-15, (b) CZM/MCF and (c) CZM/KIT-6. Reprinted with permission from ref. 446. Copyright 2018, Hydrogen Energy Publications LLC. Published by Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
A study of the Ce content in Cu–ZnO–CeOx dispersed in SBA-15 showed that methanol and CO synthesis can be controlled at the interface of Cu/ZnO/CeOx due to the synergistic effects between the three active components and the basic character of CeOx.227 Suitable morphological features of SBA-15 promoted the mass and heat transfer as well as a high dispersion and exposure of the active phases, enabling the production of methanol and CO. Cu is regarded as the active site for activating hydrogen while ZnO and CeOx are the promoters, which exhibit excellent adsorption and activation capacities for CO2. CeO2 is more efficient than ZnO in promoting the dispersion of Cu nanoparticles and methanol generation, while ZnO promoted the dispersion of CeOx and the production of CO. Both ZnO and CeOx were beneficial for hydrogen spillover but mainly took part in CO2 conversion and influence product selectivity. XPS and Cu LMM XAES spectra of the reduced sample showed no satellite peaks of Cu 2p3/2 and Cu 2p1/2 in the XPS spectrum, indicating that Cu2+ was completely reduced, while two peaks at 932.7 and 952.7 eV were assigned to Cu 2p3/2 and Cu 2p1/2 of Cu+ or Cu0, respectively. Moreover, a broad Auger kinetic energy peak registered on the Cu LMM XAES spectrum was fitted into two peaks centered at 916.9 and 913.8 eV, corresponding to Cu0 and Cu+ species, respectively. The shift towards lower values indicates the strong interaction between metallic species, Cu, ZnO and CeOx due to the Lewis acidity of ZnO and CeOx which promotes the presence of Cu+ beneficial for the hydrogenation process. The H2-TPR profiles of the calcined samples revealed only a symmetrical peak for Zn/Ce ≤ 1 indicating the presence of only one oxidation state of CuOx with uniform particle size. High dispersion of metal oxides generally involves strong metal/support interaction leading to a shift to higher temperature of the reduction peak. However, the incorporation of Ce also promotes high dispersion of CuOx and induces a reduction peak shift to lower temperature attributing to easier reduction of the catalyst via hydrogen spillover, at the interface of Cu/CeOx.447 The catalytic performance over 10Cu8Zn2Ce/SBA-15 was maintained for 100 h (Table 4, entry 14). This study revealed new aspect to design highly dispersed active species with controlled selectivity to methanol synthesis.
(iv) Pd and other metallic catalysts for CO2hydrogenation to methanol. Pd exhibits suitable catalytic properties for CO2 hydrogenation and the presence of specific metal oxides such as Ga2O3 or CaO was shown to enhance the formation of methanol.448–450 The catalytic features of Pd catalysts based on morphologically distinct siliceous porous supports (MCM-41, SBA-15, MCM-48, MSU-F and amorphous SiO2) for CH3OH synthesis integrating alkali/alkaline earth metal additives were investigated.451 The rate of CH3OH formation in the presence of supported Ca/Pd catalysts was a function of average pore diameter, and decreased in the following order: MCM-41 > SBA-15 > MCM-48 > MSU-F ∼ amorphous SiO2. The study revealed that the smaller mesopores of MCM-41 and SBA-15 enabled the formation of small Pd nanoparticles, increasing the catalytic performance in comparison to that of the amorphous supported catalyst. Moreover, the addition of the promoter improved the catalytic activity of the catalysts, with Ca promoting the rate of CH3OH formation rather than the formation of CO. Pd(6)/Ca(0.4)/SBA-15 was active and stable for 40 h on stream to CH3OH and CO production. Nevertheless, the CH3OH selectivity (31%) remained low and required to be improved in comparison to CO selectivity (Table 4, entry 15). A few years later, the same group reported the catalytic performance of bimetallic Cu–Pd catalysts.452 Characterization results confirmed the formation of Pd–Cu alloys for hydrogen adsorption capacity for facilitating methanol production. In the precedent paper, the rate of methanol formation varied with the pore diameter of the supports, while Pd(0.25)–Cu/SiO2 was the most active catalyst with an improvement in the methanol formation rate by 27%.
Two reaction pathways were considered to take place on Pd–Cu alloy surfaces: formate hydrogenation and RWGS + CO hydrogenation. Nevertheless, the methanol STY remained low and cannot be considered for industrial application (Table 4, entry 16).
In2O3-based catalysts have been reported to possess oxygen vacancies able to activate CO2 and to exhibit higher methanol selectivity. Then, very recently, Pd, as a promoter of H2 dissociation, was introduced into In2O3/SBA-15.453 Based on DFT calculations, the synergetic effect of Pd with In2O3 was explained on the basis of the high ability of Pd sites to dissociate H2 and thus to promote the formation of more active oxygen vacancies on the In2O3 surface suitable to activate CO2. Under the best reaction conditions, 84% methanol selectivity and 12.6% CO2 conversion were achieved, giving an STY of 352 mgCH3OH h−1 gcat−1, at 260 °C and 5 MPa. No noticeable catalyst deactivation was observed for 120 h on stream. Taking into account the methanol selectivity, the space time yield, and the catalyst stability, these results illustrate the relevance of Pd–In2O3/SBA-15 for industrial application to methanol production from direct CO2 hydrogenation (Table 4, entry 18). In earlier attempts, a ternary Au/ZnO/TiO2 catalyst with small Au particles was studied for methanol synthesis from CO2 hydrogenation.454 Accordingly, Au–CuO/SBA-15 catalysts were prepared and their catalytic behavior was studied for CO2 hydrogenation. A maximum of 13.5% selectivity to methanol was obtained at 24.2% CO2 conversion in the presence of the Au–CuO/SBA-15 catalyst with 2 wt% Au content.455 The interaction between Au and CuO helps in improving the thermal stability and the hydrogen adsorption/dissociation at the Au/CuO interface. Nevertheless, the stability and catalytic performance of Au–CuO/SBA-15 were limited and unsatisfactory (Table 4, entry 19).
(b) Zeolite-based catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation to methanol. 

Ab initio molecular orbital and density functional theory calculations have been used to investigate the zeolite-catalyzed hydrogenation of CO2 into methanol in a concerted pathway.457 The authors showed that appropriately designed zeolite could exhibit excellent catalytic activity and allow reducing the reaction barrier by more than 200 kJ mol−1. Thus, an ideal zeolite will be a basic one obtained by exchanging acid protons with alkali metal cations to activate CO2. Then the authors proposed for the catalytic hydrogenation of CO2, a zeolite incorporating Ge, N, and Na+ into the framework. Previously, theoretical calculations revealed that a controlled substitution of specific atoms into the zeolite framework could improve the catalytic properties to complete hydrogenation of CO2.458 Previous studies reported the activity of Ru–zeolite based catalysts to CO2 hydrogenation with low methanol selectivity. Ru–NaY zeolite and mesoporous FSM-16 catalysts were prepared by ion-exchange and chemical vapor deposition (CVD) methods. High activity for CO2 hydrogenation but low selectivity to methanol were registered due to high methane selectivity. Co incorporation promoted MeOH selectivity of up to 7% over Co–RuY for 16 h.459,460 In previous work, Rh-ion-exchanged zeolite catalysts (RhY) showed high activity to CO2 conversion with low selectivity (up to 2.7%) to methanol.461,462
To overcome the thermodynamic equilibrium limitation for CO2 hydrogenation and improve the MeOH yield and selectivity, the continuous MeOH and water removal via selective membrane separation has received attention. Indeed, higher conversion under milder reaction conditions (lower temperature) can be reached using separation membranes. Zeolite membranes present robustness, different topologies and morphologies and versatility in their composition, with the corresponding textural and catalytic properties.
So, the use of a zeolite membrane reactor conformed with zeolite A463,464 revealing the possibility to selectively separate water and methanol with the corresponding increase in conversion, as was predicted with a mathematical model465 when compared with the operation in a traditional reactor.466,467 Nevertheless, the zeolite membrane showed limited stability for direct CO2 hydrogenation into methanol under current process conditions.
5.2.5 Conclusion. 
The direct hydrogenation of CO2 to form methanol requires further development and improvement of catalyst activity, selectivity and stability for industrial application. The hydrophobic properties of the catalyst surface have to be considered, and further studies on the nature of the catalyst active species and reaction intermediates are required for a successful design of an efficient and selective catalyst. The reported catalysts are multifunctional and multidimensional, characterized by different active sites with different chemical and oxidation states, with different electronic and geometrical environments with specific activities for each stage of the CO2 to methanol transformation process. It is therefore crucial to determine and control the active species, reaction intermediates and kinetics for the rational design of more efficient catalysts. Thus a better understanding and knowledge of what happens at the molecular level should help to find consensus on the mechanisms involved in the hydrogenation reaction of CO2 to methanol of which adsorption phenomena are key. Therefore, the successful design of a new efficient catalyst requires the combination of computational chemistry studies with in situ spectroscopy to elucidate the fundamental steps for the adsorption and activation of CO2 and H2, for the hydrogenation reaction and the desorption of the product.
In addition, special attention should be paid to the morphology of the support to improve the physical isolation and shielding of metal species. In this regard, the use of OMS materials, hierarchical zeolites, two-dimensional zeolite nanosheets and core–shell materials can promote the amount, dispersion and stabilization of the active centers.
Considering thermodynamics, the development of catalysts able to work at low temperature and high pressure is a clear objective. The morphology, topology, thermal stability, hydrophobic/hydrophilic, acid/base and redox properties of zeolite and OMS materials are properties that can be tailored when designing new catalysts and they should play a key role.
Patent literature468–470 indicates some distinguishable pilot plant processes by Air Liquide Forschung und Entwicklung GmbH (ALFE) and Lurgi GmbH or Mitsui process471 using Cu/ZnO/ZrO2/Al2O3/SiO2 catalysts, while zeolite and OMS based catalysts are far from reaching industrial targets. Air Liquide Forschung und Entwicklung GmbH (ALFE) and Lurgi GmbH use a Cu/ZnO/Al2O3 catalyst developed by Süd-Chemie (Clariant),468,470 working in Pilot Plant, with a gas composition of 24/2/74 = CO2:N2:H2 and GHSV of 10500 h−1, at 8 MPa and 250 °C give 35–45% CO2 conversion and an MeOH STY of about 0.6 kg Lcat−1 h−1. The catalyst suffered from slight deactivation and was stable over 600 h. The water content of MeOH is 30–40 wt%, which is superior to the water content in MeOH produced from syngas (10–12 wt%). The Mitsui process uses a Cu/ZnO/ZrO2/Al2O3/SiO2 catalyst471 at 250 °C, 5 MPa, and GHSV = 10000 h−1, and produces high MeOH purity (0.26 kg h−1), with high water content (0.26 kg h−1), at CO2 conversion of up to 86%. No data were provided for methanol selectivity, or for the H2/CO2 ratio used, so methanol selectivity could not be determined. We present here the results reported for CO2 conversion and MeOH selectivity using structured mesoporous materials as supports, and the promising results are outlined (Fig. 21).
 Fig. 21  MeOH selectivity was plotted versus GHSH for different metal–OMS catalysts. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high MeOH selectivity versus high CO2 conversion. 


5.3 Catalytic methylation of aromatics with CO2/H2. 
Aromatics are important intermediates for petrochemical and chemical industries. Among them, xylenes found numerous applications in polymer (polyester), resin and plasticizer synthesis. Thus, C-methylation is a key transformation for the preparation of xylenes which is usually performed with MeOH and zeolites.472–474 Methylation of toluene with methanol over the zeolite catalyst (ZSM-5) affords water and xylenes with 23.5% p-xylene, 52.4% m-xylene, and 24% o-xylene, which corresponds to the thermodynamic equilibrium composition at 400 °C.475 The modifications of the catalytic properties of zeolites by controlling the acidity on the external surface of the zeolite, the pore sizes and products diffusion inside the pore system allowed maximizing p-xylene selectivity.474,476–479 Therefore, p-xylene selectivity of up to 100% over the Mg modified ZSM-5 catalyst at low space time has been reported at 440–450 °C with high steam content in feed.480,481 Taking the above into account, it is clear that the use of CO2/H2 for the catalytic methylation of aromatics constitutes a green alternative and could help to reduce atmospheric CO2 concentrations (Scheme 50). Since methanol synthesis from CO2 hydrogenation81,371,482,483 has been widely studied, the combination of catalytic properties or directly of catalysts suitable to perform methanol synthesis and C-methylation should enable the methylation of aromatics starting from CO2/H2. It seems that for this cascade process, it may be worthwhile to study micro- and mesoporous catalysts. Very recently, the catalytic methylation of aromatics using CO2/H2 using a physical mixture of Re/TiO2 and H-MOR, both catalysts suitable for the methanol production and subsequent C-methylation at low temperature, was reported484 with good catalytic performance at 1 MPa PCO2, 5 MPa; PH2, 250 °C and 20 h in a batch reactor. Under such conditions, 52% and 42% yields of methylated benzenes were obtained on the base of benzene-based yield and CO2-based yield, respectively. The catalysts screening showed that the best support for Re was TiO2 and that Re was the best metal for CO2 hydrogenation, while H-MOR was the best zeolite for C-methylation among ZSM-5, BEA, FAU and CHA structures, with an optimum Si/Al2O3 of 90. The process was also performed over the optimized catalytic system using MeOH, CO/H2 and HCOOH/H2. Best results were obtained with MeOH indicating that MeOH could be the intermediate, with MeOH formation occurring over Re/TiO2 and methylation over HMOR(90). These results are promising although the selectivity to xylenes and p-xylene is still low and no data for a continuous process have been reported. Most recently, methylation of toluene using CO2/H2 over ZnZrOx–ZSM-5 (ZZO-Z5) dual functional catalysts was also studied.485 The in situ DRIFT study showed, comparing methanol and CO2/H2 methylation routes, that reactive methylation species H3CO* were more easily obtained from CO2 hydrogenation over ZZO-Z5 catalysts. The results suggested that after CO2 hydrogenation following the formate route, the reactive methylation species formed over ZZO migrated into zeolite pores where toluene methylation took place. Under optimized reaction conditions in a fixed-bed reactor, the ZSM-5 (Si/Al = 85) modified in order to decrease the pore size, with 10 wt% of ZZO (ZZO-4SZ5), at 360 °C, 3.0 MPa, GHSV = 12000 mL g−1 hour−1 and WHSVToluene = 1 h, allowed reaching 92.4% xylene selectivity with 70.8% p-xylene selectivity and 13% toluene conversion (Fig. 22). These results clearly showed the potential and feasibility of C-methylation of toluene using CO2/H2.
 Scheme 50  Catalytic methylation of aromatics with CO2/H2. 
 Fig. 22  Toluene methylation using CO2/H2 over the ZZO-Z5 catalyst versus toluene methylation using methanol over optimized Z5. Reprinted with permission from ref. 485. Copyright 2020, The American Association for the Advancement of Science. 
We see how these works constitute the seed for the exploration of C-methylation of aromatics with CO2/H2 and xylene products, showing the great potential of this new reaction and of the multifunctional catalytic system integrating zeolites.


5.4 Direct conversion of CO2 to dimethylether (DME). 
5.4.1 Introduction. 
Worldwide DME production stands at approximately 5 million tons per year, being mainly obtained by methanol dehydration, and the majority of DME production is currently in China.486 Dimethyl ether (DME) is the simplest ether, is non-toxic, non-carcinogenic, non-teratogenic, non-mutagenic and environmentally benign487 with a boiling point of 25 °C. Under normal atmospheric conditions, DME is a colorless gas and can be easily stored as a liquid at room temperature and low pressure (∼0.5 MPa). DME is considered a potential clean transport fuel and an alternative to diesel,488 especially in compression ignition diesel engines due to high cetane number (55–60)489 and low auto-ignition temperature (235 °C).490 DME is a clean energy source and does not produce sulfur oxide or soot during combustion, and its environmental impact is low especially if it is produced from renewable resources.491 DME is used extensively in the chemical industry, progressively replacing petroleum derivatives for the synthesis of a large number of chemicals such as methyl acetate, dimethyl sulphate, olefins, isoparaffin-rich gasoline,492 methylating agent, solvent and aerosol propellant (Fig. 23). A life cycle analysis revealed the interest of methanol and DME as clean transport fuels and showed that methanol and DME can be economically competitive with respect to oil derived fuels.487,493–495
 Fig. 23  Illustration of DME synthesis and uses. 
DME synthesis can be performed in either a two-step or a single-step process, commonly referred to as direct or indirect synthesis, respectively. DME is usually produced directly from syngas (CO/H2)405,496–498 eventually with low amount of CO2 (<3%) or by dehydration of methanol,404,499–501 which in turn is produced from syngas.
The conventional indirect synthesis undergoes firstly methanol production in the presence of a metallic-based catalyst from syngas or CO2 hydrogenation, followed in a second step by methanol dehydration over a solid acid catalysts such as γ-alumina or zeolite.495,502–505 Since methanol synthesis from CO2 hydrogenation was above-reviewed, only the direct DME synthesis in a single-step (with economic advantage over a two-step process), requiring the use of efficient bifunctional catalysts, has been covered here.
The direct DME synthesis from CO2 hydrogenation is very attractive506 since it is environmentally friendly and can be an economical route. The catalytic hydrogenation of CO2 for direct synthesis of DME enables increasing considerably the CO2 conversion because methanol dehydration breaks the thermodynamic balance of methanol synthesis. However, direct DME synthesis produces a larger amount of water than methanol synthesis alone that damages the activity and stability of the catalysts. It is generally assumed that direct transformation of CO2 to DME involves methanol synthesis and methanol dehydration and requires bifunctional catalysts. These active sites can be integrated in a hybrid catalyst507–510 or can be present in two different catalysts physically mixed.511–513 In the last case, the combination of both types of active sites, metal-oxide and acid sites, present some limitations such as no uniform distribution of active sites, mass transfer constraints and limited reproducibility. Therefore the presence of metal oxide and acid sites in a single system500,514,515 could improve mass transfer, CO2 conversion and rates of hydrogenation/dehydration, due to the proximity of active sites.
5.4.2 Thermodynamics of direct DME synthesis. 
The DME synthesis involves three main reactions, the RWGS, the methanol synthesis and dehydration (Scheme 51). With the exception of RWGS, all the reactions are exothermic and so thermodynamically disfavoured with high temperature. The dehydration reaction of methanol occurs without variation in the number of moles and is slightly exothermic over solid acid catalysts.516,517 Accordingly, the process pressure does not affect the extent of the dehydration reaction while lower temperatures are thermodynamically beneficial. Besides, the dehydration reaction of methanol allows shifting the thermodynamic equilibrium for CO2 hydrogenation and working at lower pressure and higher temperatures (>240 °C) that have been shown to promote the CO2 activation rate and the formation of methanol or carbon monoxide.518
 Scheme 51  Equations of different reactions involved in direct DME synthesis from CO2 hydrogenation. 
As it occurs for methanol synthesis, where the active catalyst for methanol from syngas showed to be active for CO2 hydrogenation, the active catalysts for DME synthesis from syngas exhibit activity to DME synthesis from CO2. For methanol synthesis, the suitable properties of Cu0 for H2 dissociation, and the dispersion effect of carrier oxides519–522 such as ZnO, Al2O3, and ZrO2 providing an active metal–oxide interface to the CO2 adsorption and activation have been largely described and studied. Accordingly, the direct CO2 hydrogenation into DME is generally performed in the presence of Cu-based methanol synthesis catalyst (CuO–ZnO–Al2O3 or CuO–ZnO–ZrO2)511,523 and an acid catalyst, such as γ-Al2O3, silica–alumina or zeolites for methanol dehydration. Due to its acid properties γ-alumina could be a good candidate for the methanol dehydration reaction but as we discussed for methanol synthesis, alumina presents some limitations. The hydrophilicity of γ-alumina and ZnO has a negative effect over the metal particle stability and favours sintering and agglomeration. Moreover, water has strong competitive adsorption with methanol and so it inhibits the methanol dehydration step. Zeolites offer great potential due to suitable and controllable Brønsted and Lewis acid properties, water resistance and shape-selectivity. Among zeolites, HZSM-5 zeolite based catalysts have been widely used for methanol dehydration due to their suitable acid properties that have to be tuned to avoid the formation of hydrocarbons. Following these leads, numerous catalytic systems based on Cu and HZSM-5 have been studied for the direct hydrogenation of CO2. The metal particle size, usually Cu, plays a great role in methanol synthesis activity, and consequently in DME formation. Nevertheless, the stability of Cu particles, under reaction conditions, is limited due to sintering and agglomeration, as it was discussed for methanol synthesis. Hence, different strategies, such as the addition of a dispersive phase or promoters (ZnO, ZrO2, La2O3, CeO2, and MnO2) or the addition of a second hydrogenation metal with suitable properties to dissociate H2 such as Pd, are followed to improve the bifunctional catalytic system. Then, different preparation methods have been developed, including co-precipitation, sol–gel, citrate or impregnation routes, in order to increase the catalyst activity and stability. The main challenge is to reach high DME selectivity (>70%) at a high CO2 conversion level, minimizing CO production and avoiding catalyst deactivation.
5.4.3 Cu-based catalysts for direct DME synthesis. 
Cu-based zeolite catalysts have been the main zeolitic catalysts reported up to now for the transformation of CO2 into DME. Catalytic systems to direct transformation of CO2 to DME have to contain active sites for methanol synthesis and for methanol dehydration that could be integrated with a hybrid catalyst or which are present in two different catalysts physically mixed. For the first time a hybrid catalyst incorporating both functionalities for methanol synthesis and methanol dehydration was prepared by impregnation in 2001.524 The Cu–Mo supported HZSM-5 catalyst was found to be highly active for DME synthesis. The addition of a small amount of Mo enhanced the catalytic activity of the hybrid catalyst that was found to be stable for 10 h of time of stream. DME was produced with high selectivity of up to 77% and low 12.3% CO2 conversion, at 240 °C and 2 MPa (Table 5, entry 1).

562





(a) SAPO-based catalysts for direct DME synthesis. 
Recently the catalytic performance of the Cu–In–Zr–O (CIZO) mixed oxide nanomaterial and commercial SAPO-34 zeolite physically mixed has been reported.525 The best CIZO/SAPO ratio was 50/50 by weight, and a maximum of 4.3% CO2 conversion and 65.1% DME selectivity were achieved at 250 °C and 3 MPa (Table 5, entry 2; Fig. 24). These catalytic data are inferior in comparison to the catalytic performance of other multi-functional catalytic system reported previously.
 Fig. 24  (a) Conversion and product distribution over CIZO and 50CIZO–50SAPO mixture. (b) Influence of proximity of active sites on the performance of the CIZO–SAPO catalytic system: (A) powder mixing, (B) pellet mixing, and (C) dual bed. Reprinted with permission from ref. 525. Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. 
(b) Cu–ZnO–Al2O3-based zeolite catalysts for direct DME synthesis. 
A large variety of catalysts for direct CO2 hydrogenation into DME have been studied such as Cu–ZnO, Pd and bimetallic catalysts. Among them, Cu–ZnO based catalysts are undoubtedly the most commonly explored and used. Among the Cu based catalysts, the mostly studied and employed catalytic system for direct DME synthesis from CO2 are conventionally Cu–ZnO–Al2O3 and Cu–ZnO–ZrO2 based catalysts. Cu–ZnO–Al2O3 based catalysts are traditionally used for direct hydrogenation of CO2 into methanol and have been widely used and studied for direct hydrogenation of CO2 into DME. The catalytic system should consist of a physical mixture or hybrid catalysts with active sites for both methanol synthesis and dehydration that will involve a zeolite function.
(i) Physical mixture of Cu–ZnO–Al2O3and zeolite catalysts for direct DME synthesis. The catalytic performance of CuO–ZnO–Al2O3 prepared by a simple and fast urea-nitrate combustion method that was physically mixed with HZSM-5 by grinding showed to be dependent on the amount of urea used as fuel, affecting the grain size and copper surface areas and so the catalytic properties.523 The catalyst prepared with 40% of the stoichiometric amount of urea exhibited the best catalytic properties due to its higher Cu surface area and the smaller Cu particle size. At 270 °C and 3 MPa, maximum 30.6% CO2 conversion, 15.1% and 49.2% DME yield and selectivity were achieved, respectively (Table 5, entry 3). A catalytic system based on pretreated carbon nanotubes with graphitized tube-walls (multi-walled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs)), was used as the carrier for the preparation of CuO–ZnO–Al2O3/HZSM-5 catalysts by ultrasound-assisted co-precipitation.526 CuO–ZnO–Al2O3 nanoparticles were densely deposited outside and inside the wall of carbon nanotubes and bifunctional catalysts were obtained by mechanical mixing of MWCNTs and HZSM-5. At 262 °C and 3.0 MPa, 46.2% CO2 conversion, 20.9% and 45.2% DME yield and selectivity, respectively, were obtained (Table 5, entry 4). Recently, CuO–ZnO–Al2O3 catalysts with a high BET surface area and Cu surface area were prepared by a co-precipitation method and used after physically mixing with HZSM-5 for DME synthesis.527 The bifunctional mixture showed high activity and DME selectivity due to the microstructure of the catalysts that was controlled by the preparation method used. Maximum 21.9% DME yield, 30.5% CO2 conversion and good 72% DME selectivity were achieved at 260 °C and 4.2 MPa (Table 5, entry 5). A stability test of the catalyst mixture showed a notable decrease in activity within the first 20 h. Nevertheless, high catalytic performance was maintained for 100 h, with 21.4% CO2 conversion and 55.5% DME selectivity. The changes in the catalytic activity during the time on stream were attributed to Cu sintering and alterations in the surface area.
(ii) Hybrid catalyst based on Cu–ZnO–Al2O3and zeolite for direct DME synthesis. Cu–ZnO–Al2O3/FER catalysts have been prepared by co-precipitation528 giving a maximum production rate of DME for an optimal zeolite Si/Al molar ratio of 12 owing to the larger metallic surface area of Cu nanoparticles and relatively higher hydrophobic surface. The authors reported that a smaller Cu particle size decorated with ZnO moieties in the larger Cu–ZnO–Al2O3 matrices increased the hydrophobic surfaces of the catalytic system, preventing the poisoning of acid sites of the zeolite and Cu aggregation. CO2 conversion of 29.7% with 55.2% DME selectivity at 250 °C and 5 MPa was achieved (Table 5, entry 6).
(iii) Morphology and structuration of the catalysts for direct DME synthesis. Different strategies to design and prepare new structured zeolitic catalysts with high dispersion and stabilized metallic active sites have been investigated. For methanol synthesis, it was demonstrated that the yield and selectivity were highly dependent on Cu dispersion and surface area. However, Cu in Cu–ZnO based catalysts is thermally unstable and the particle size increases with temperature over 250 °C inducing the loss of surface area and sintering. Moreover, the production of water causes poisoning of acid active sites and Cu sintering. Rare earth metal oxides added in small amounts usually act as promoters to improve the dispersion of the active phase and the thermal stability of the catalysts. Following these leads, and to improve the catalytic properties of CuO–ZnO–Al2O3/HZSM-5, a new design of catalyst structure was developed. An encapsulated catalyst composed of CuO–ZnO–Al2O3 composite nanoparticles as the core and HZSM-5 as the shell was prepared, with the HZSM-5 shell being synthesized hydrothermally onto CuO–ZnO–Al2O3 nanoparticles.529 At 270 °C and 3.0 MPa, 48.3% CO2 conversion and 23.4 and 48.5% of DME yield and selectivity were reached (Table 5, entry 7). No deactivation of the catalyst was observed for the first 24 h. Then, the catalyst stability was attributed to the core–shell structure that allowed controlling the consecutive order of the reaction, methanol synthesis and methanol dehydration. In 2012, a hydrothermal route for the preparation of new hybrid materials composed of CuO–ZnO as the core layer and HZSM-5 as the shell layer was described.530 The comparison of hydrothermal, impregnation and mixing preparation methods showed that the one-step crystallization method provided a catalyst with higher catalytic performance. CO2 conversion of 21.3% with 50.8% DME selectivity and MeOH + DME yield up to 12.4%, at 245 °C and 3 MPa was achieved by this catalyst preparation method (Table 5, entry 8).
(iv) Promoter incorporation to the Cu–ZnO–Al2O3based-zeolite catalysts for direct DME synthesis. As was mentioned for methanol synthesis, the incorporation of metal oxides to Cu-based catalysts promotes the Cu dispersion, stabilization and the formation of new metallic active phases for the methanol synthesis by CO and CO2 hydrogenation. GaO, ZrO2, Cr2O3, MnO2, or CeO2 have been used as promoters and from these, ZrO2 was particularly prominent. Following these leads, a series of CuO–ZnO–Al2O3–La2O3/HZSM-5 (CZA/HZ) bifunctional catalysts with various La loadings were prepared by co-precipitation.531 The controlled addition of La2O3 improved the reducibility and dispersion of bifunctional catalysts, and a decrease in the CuO particle size was observed. H2-TPR profiles showed that La incorporation induced an increase of peak area of reduction of CuO indicating that CuO species dispersion was improved by incorporating La. Moreover, a shift of the reduction peak positions to higher temperatures was observed with La incorporation of up to 2%, as well as a shift to lower temperatures. These results indicate that La2O3 also makes difficult the reduction of CuO because of the strong interactions between metallic phases ZnO–CuO–La2O3. It was concluded that La doping allows enhancing the dispersion of the CuO copper oxide and reduction of CuO while the strong interactions between CuO–ZnO–La2O3 hinder the reduction of CuO, implying that a balance between the benefits and detriments of La2O3 addition has to be found (Fig. 25). On the other hand, the addition of La into the CuO–ZnO–Al2O3–La2O3/HZSM-5 catalyst modified the strength of the strong acid sites, and an optimum catalytic performance was obtained with 2 wt% La content. Indeed, NH3-TPD profiles showed that the amount of the strong acid increased to a maximum with La content, up to 2 wt%, and then decreased. This behavior was attributed to the acidic properties of the lanthanum ions, and to the polarization of the hydroxyl group of the silicon and aluminum framework, leading to stronger acidity.532 At higher La contents, the formation of La2O3 with basic properties involves the formation of new basic sites on the catalytic surface.533 At 250 °C and 2 MPa, high 43.8% CO2 conversion and 71.2% selectivity to DME were reached (Table 5, entry 9). In the same way, ZrO2 was added to the CuO–ZnO–Al2O3 active phase. A series of composite catalysts were prepared by a wet mixing method of CuO–ZnO–Al2O3–ZrO2 component to HZSM-5 zeolite with a molar ratio of 2:1.521 H2-TPR profiles showed that for Zr content <5 wt%, a main reduction peak with a shoulder at higher temperature was registered, implying the presence of different Cu species, with the main peak being assigned to high dispersed copper oxide species and the shoulder to CuO bulk. For Zr content >5%, only one reduction peak was recorded, lightly shifted at higher temperature, indicating lower reducibility of CuO due to strong interactions of CuO–ZrO2–ZnO–Al2O3. In comparison to other promoted catalysts, 24.1% CO2 conversion and lower 26.6% and 6.4% of DME selectivity and yield, respectively, were achieved at 260 °C and 3 MPa, (Table 5, entry 10).
 Fig. 25  H2-TPR profiles for different La contents of CZA-HZ catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 531. Copyright 2013, The Chinese Society of Rare Earths. Published by Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
(c) Cu–ZnO–ZrO2-based catalysts for the direct DME synthesis from CO2 hydrogenation. 
Hybrid catalysts composed of Cu–ZnO–ZrO2 and zeolites for methanol dehydration such as ferrierite or ZSM-5 for DME synthesis have been largely explored and showed to be usually more efficient catalysts than conventional Cu–ZnO–Al2O3-based catalysts. Indeed, the interactions of Cu metal particles with ZnO and ZrO2 lead to the stabilization of different Cu species, Cu1+ and Cu0.418
(i) Effect of proximity between acid and metallic active sites. The catalytic behavior of the adequate combination of Cu–ZnO–ZrO2 materials and commercial H-ZSM5 to the direct CO2 hydrogenation reaction to DME was studied in different reactor bed configurations (physical mixing (ZZ-M), dual-bed (ZZ-D) and mono-bed prepared by grinding and pelletization (ZZ-G)).518,534 At 3.0 MPa, the CO2 conversion varied from 1.6 to 16.1%, at 180 and 240 °C, respectively. Meanwhile the DME selectivity decreased drastically from 70.9 to 33.9% and methanol selectivity decreased from 21.7 to 11.8% due to a notable CO selectivity increase from 7.4 to 54.3%. The ZZ-M system exhibited the best performance with STY values increasing from 130 to 430 gtotalMeOH kgcat−1 h−1, at 180 and 240 °C, respectively, while the ZZ-G system reached the lowest productivity (Table 5, entry 11). The best results achieved over the mixed catalyst in comparison to the dual bed system configuration were attributed to the enhanced methanol mass transfer from the methanol catalyst sites to the zeolite acid sites owing to the proximity of the active centres. Grinding was negative because of deterioration of the interaction between the methanol catalyst and zeolite due to strong mechanical stress. The methanol catalyst (ZCZ) structure was damaged favouring the redistribution of Cu over zeolites. In this respect, different strategies have been developed to improve the proximity of the different functionalities. One approach is the co-precipitation of metal oxides in the presence of acid carriers in order to provide hybrid materials with both metal-oxides and acid sites in a single catalyst grain. Accordingly Frusteri and co-workers reported different investigations where the effects of carriers, precipitating agents and the ratio of metal oxides:carrier were studied. They compared the DME productivity achieved in the presence of a physical mixture of the CuZnZr methanol catalyst and a zeolite, and in the presence of a multifunctional system, in which the different active sites were integrated during the gel-oxalate precipitation of the Cu–Zn–Zr precursors in a solution containing dispersed MFI-zeolite.535 In principle, the synthesis of a hybrid system by co-precipitation should maintain the adequate properties of the active sites and enable a higher yield of DME due to the regular and homogeneous distribution, and proximity of the active sites. In the presence of the co-precipitated catalyst, the DME selectivity was higher while the CO2 conversion was practically the same, 15.9% versus 15.4%. The CO2 conversion was not improved by the proximity of the acid sites that were supposed to shift positively the equilibrium and achieve higher value than for CO2 hydrogenation into MeOH (18%). Under optimized reaction conditions, 240 °C and 5.0 MPa, maximum CO2 conversion of up to 23.6% with lower 24.5% CO selectivity, and therefore higher selectivity to MeOH and DME, 25.5 and 49.3%, were achieved, respectively (Table 5, entry 12). The authors adopted co-precipitation as a simple method to prepare Cu-based hybrid catalysts, and ensure the presence of the multifunctionality grain-to-grain.
(ii) Effect of catalyst preparation on the catalytic activity. It was shown before that co-precipitation resulted in a simple method to prepare Cu-based hybrid catalysts, and ensure the presence of the multifunctionality grain-to-grain. Therefore, a study of the impact of different parameters of co-precipitation to the preparation of Cu–ZnO–ZrO2/H-ZSM5 multifunctional catalysts on their catalytic features was performed.536 Likewise, different precipitating agents (sodium bicarbonate, ammonium carbonate, oxalic acid and urea) were used under ultrasound to match morphological homogeneity. The multifunctional catalyst prepared via ammonium carbonate precipitation exhibited the best catalytic performance, high DME selectivity, with a maximum DME productivity, 0.225 kgDME kgcat−1 h−1 (Table 5, entry 13). The characterization study revealed that a balanced distribution of different sites on the catalyst surface became determinant: the strength of basic sites, the ratio between acid and basic sites and the Cu particle sizes were crucial to maximize the catalytic performance and minimize CO selectivity. The CuO–ZnO–ZrO2/HZSM-5 system revealed to be a promising catalyst for direct conversion of CO2 into DME.534–536 CuO–ZnO–ZrO2/HZSM-5 bifunctional catalysts were generally prepared by physical or mechanical mixing methods. However to improve the yield of the consecutive methanol synthesis and dehydration, high and homogeneous dispersion of both functionalities is crucial to favour an efficient mass transfer rate from the metal-oxide sites for methanol synthesis to the acid sites for methanol dehydration.536 Therefore, co-precipitation methods were developed to prepare the CuO–ZnO–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalysts. Nevertheless, these methods present some drawbacks such as the use of metal salts associated with the proton zeolite exchange with both Na and Cu cations. Consequently, the acidic properties of HZSM-5 are reduced, affecting the methanol dehydration step. A few years ago, the preparation of CuO–ZnO–ZrO2/HZSM-5 by a solid-state route was reported.537 A study of calcination temperature showed that the catalytic performance of CuO–ZnO–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalysts for DME synthesis decreased gradually with an increase in temperature from 300 to 600 °C whereas the CO yield increased. The characterization study revealed that reduction of metallic copper surface area, adsorption capacity of CO2, surface area, and reducibility of CuO were strongly affected by the calcination temperature and the suitable temperature was 300 °C. Highest 22.2% CO2 conversion, and 67.6 and 15% DME selectivity and yield were achieved with a DME productivity of 0.507 gDME gcat−1 h−1 (Table 5, entry 14). Recently, the catalytic properties of Cu–ZnO–ZrO2-FER catalysts prepared by the co-precipitation method has enabled to obtain materials with smaller CuO and Cu particle sizes resulting in better dispersion of the active phases, higher surface area, and lower reduction temperature.538 Nevertheless, in comparison to other studies, these data remained low. Highest 17.5% CO2 conversion, and 28.4 and 5% DME selectivity and yield were achieved, at 250 °C and 2 MPa (Table 5, entry 15).
(iii) Effect of zeolite topology on the catalytic activity. The effect of zeolite framework topology is well-known to influence the distribution of the reaction products58 and to control the diffusion and adsorption/deposition of metallic species into the channels. The differences in the voids and microenvironments defined by the topologies of zeolites strongly influenced the stabilization and dispersion of metallic active sites.50,87,539,540 Accordingly, zeolites with different topologies were used as support of the CuZnZr metallic active phase. Recently, the superiority of ferrierite based catalysts with respect to other zeolite based catalysts typically used, such as ZSM-5, was shown. Different zeolite structures, FER and MOR,509 were used as carriers to study the topology impacts on acidity, surface area, microporosity and metal-oxide loading, and in turn on the catalytic performance of the catalysts. The CuZnZr/FER catalyst exhibited very good activity and selectivity to DME formation, at 5.0 MPa and 280 °C, with very interesting results on DME productivity up to 0.752 kgDME kgcat−1 h−1, and no coke formation was observed (GHSV = 8800 NL kgcat−1 h−1) (Table 5, entry 16). The presence of well dispersed metal-oxide clusters of CuZnZr mixed oxides, with size between 30 and 150 nm, on the external surface of the FER zeolite revealed to be a key factor to enhance the mass transfer of MeOH from CuZnZr sites to acid sites and favoured the formation of DME. A CuZnZr:FER weight ratio greater than 1 enabled higher catalytic performance, with CO2 conversion of up to 23.6% at 260 °C and a MeOH/DME yield of up to 15% (Table 5, entry 18).541 The comparison of the catalytic behavior of Cu–Zn–Zr on MOR, FER, MFI revealed that functionalities such as the metallic dispersion and acidic activity were strongly affected by the zeolite structure. The FER zeolite enabled a better dispersion of cluster oxides and the formation of Lewis basic sites for CO2 activation and provided a high number of suitable Brønsted acid sites for the MeOH dehydration step. The optimum and homogeneous dispersion of active sites was attributed to the ferrierite lamellar structure with interconnection of bi-dimensional channels. A maximum DME productivity of 0.6 kgDME kgcat−1 h−1 in the presence of a Cu–Zn–Zr/FER catalyst, at 260 °C and 5 MPa (Table 5, entry 18), was achieved owing to the enhanced methanol mass transfer owing to the proximity of the active sites. Nevertheless, a progressive deactivation due to water formation was observed. Currently, the same group reported the catalytic performance of the CuZnZr hybrid catalyst using different zeolites (Sil-1, MFI, Y, FER, BEA, and MOR) and a gel–oxalate co-precipitation method.542,543 Different textural, structural and morphological properties according to the carrier structure were observed. Mordenite revealed to be the most efficient carrier for the generation and dispersion of functionalities with high DME productivity of 0.55 kgDME−1 kgcat h−1 at 280 °C and 3.0 MPa (Table 5, entries 19–23). The deactivation was again attributed to the water formation and inhibition of active sites.
(iv) Effect of Si/Al ratio on the catalytic activity. Since the Si/Al ratio allows controlling the acid and adsorption properties of zeolites, the Si/Al ratio directly influenced their catalytic performance. Accordingly, different Cu–ZnO–ZrO2/FER (CZZ/FER) hybrid catalysts using ferrierite samples with different acidities and particle sizes were prepared.508 The catalytic results revealed that samples with larger acidity exhibited higher deactivation whereas the use of TEM allowed attributing the deactivation to metal sintering, which was proportional to the Si/Al ratio (Fig. 26; Table 5, entry 24). An exhaustive FT-IR spectroscopy study allowed concluding that both negatively influenced CO2 activation by Cu particle sintering and acidity loss due to H+ exchange with Cu2+ cations were responsible for the rapid deactivation of Cu–ZnO–ZrO2/FER hybrid catalysts.544
 Fig. 26  Influence of Brønsted acid sites (A) and metal particle sintering (B) on the deactivation kinetics of the hybrid catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 508. Copyright 2018, Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
(v) Effect of promoter incorporation on the catalytic activity. Many efforts have been taken to improve the catalytic properties of bifunctional catalysts such as modifying the synthesis method by sol–gel, co-precipitation and by designing new core–shell structures. Another strategy to improve the active metallic phase dispersion and stability is the incorporation of promoters (Ga, Mn, La, Ce, Y, Si and Ti) allowing the formation of new active species for CO2 activation and establishing strong metal/metal and metal/support interaction.519–522 In comparison to single oxides, mixed oxides present higher surface area, thermal stability and enhanced acidity, improving their catalytic performance.545–548 Accordingly, the CuO–TiO2–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalyst with a Ti/Zr ratio of 1 prepared by co-precipitation exhibited higher catalytic performance than CuO–TiO2/HZSM-5 and CuO–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalysts (Table 5, entry 25).512 Maximum 15.6% CO2 conversion and 47.5 and 7.41% DME selectivity and yield were obtained at 250 °C and 3 MPa. With suitable 0.5 wt% V loading, the catalytic performance of the CuO–ZnO–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalyst was improved and a substantial increase of CO2 conversion from 28.9 to 32.5%, of DME selectivity from 55.1 to 58.8% and DME yield from 15.9 to 19.1% was observed, at 270 °C and 3.0 MPa (Table 5, entry 26).549 In comparison to 14% DME yield over the Pd modified CuO–ZnO–Al2O3–ZrO2/HZSM-5550 catalyst, this result could be interesting, but the selectivity remained low, 58.8% versus 73.5% (Table 5, entry 27). Following these leads, CuO–ZnO–CeO2–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalysts were prepared.226 The incorporation of CeO2 improved the catalytic performance of the bifunctional catalysts, and the CO2 conversion and DME selectivity were increased from 12.9 to 15.6% and from 17.3% to 46.6%, respectively, over the CuO–ZnO–CeO2–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalyst with a Ce/Zr molar ratio of 1/1, at 250 °C and 3.0 MPa (Table 5, entry 28). La doped Cu–ZrO2 catalysts were also prepared and revealed an increase of the amount of basic sites with La loading as well as a decrease in the crystallite size of CuO improving the Cu dispersion.435,551 Accordingly, La2O3-modified CuO–ZnO–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalysts were prepared by an oxalate co-precipitation method for DME direct synthesis (Table 5, entry 29).552 A suitable amount of La2O3 improved the catalytic performance of CuO–ZnO–ZrO2/HZSM-5, and maximum 34.3% CO2 conversion and 57.3 and 19.6% DME selectivity and yield were obtained over the catalyst with 1% La2O3 at 270 °C and 3 MPa. The addition of La2O3 modified the population and strength of acid sites as well as the dispersion of Cu and reducibility of CuO with a larger Cu+/Cu ratio. The addition of different oxides promoters (ZrO2, CeO2, La2O3, Al2O3, and Ga2O3) could play an important role over the catalytic performance of Cu–ZnO/FER hybrid catalysts. Accordingly, the incorporation of different promoters in the Cu–ZnO/FER system significantly affected the textural properties of the customized catalysts (surface area, pore volume, copper surface area and dispersion, metal-oxide distribution and acid/base capacity).553 The reaction rate appeared to be strongly dependent on the number of medium-strong basic sites, with the number and strength of the basic sites crucial for the CO2 activation, whereas the rate of DME formation depended on the concentration of weak-medium acid sites (Fig. 27). Consequently, different catalytic behaviors were observed and Zr, Al or Ga doped Cu–ZnO/FER catalysts exhibited similar patterns for the DME production, while the incorporation of Ce and La damaged the catalytic properties. CO2 hydrogenation into DME was performed at 260 °C and 3 MPa in the presence of a ZrO2 doped Cu–ZnO/FER hybrid catalyst with MeOH, DME and CO selectivity of 14.4, 36.5 and 49.1% with 21.3% CO2 conversion (Table 5, entry 30). Although the Cu–ZnO–ZrO2/FER hybrid catalyst exhibited high catalytic performance, rapid catalyst deactivation was observed.
 Fig. 27  Influence of acidity on the rate of DME (220 °C, 3 MPa; GHSV = 8.800 NL kgcat−1 h−1; CO2/H2/N2:3/9/1). Reprinted with permission from ref. 553. Copyright 2017, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
(d) Mechanistic studies for the direct DME synthesis. 
Mechanistic insights for the direct DME synthesis from CO2 hydrogenation with a bifunctional catalyst system formed with a physical mixture of a Cu–In–Zr–O (CIZO) mixed oxide nanomaterial and a commercial SAPO-34 zeolite catalyst were achieved by an in situ DRIFT study. It was found that changing the distance between CIZO and SAPO could modify the reaction pathways. Likewise, a short distance between CIZO and SAPO induced a shortcut methoxy-DME pathway instead of a typical methoxy–methanol–DME route, resulting in more efficient DME formation (Scheme 52).525
 Scheme 52  DME synthesis by CO2 hydrogenation can involve two pathways: methoxy–methanol–DME and methoxy–DME. 
Very recently, an intrinsic kinetic model for direct CO2 hydrogenation to DME over the Cu–Fe–Zr/HZSM-5 catalyst has been established.554 The addition of a small amount of ZrO2 in a Cu-based catalyst is known to improve Cu dispersion and stability. Therefore, the incorporation of ZrO2 (1.0 wt%) in the Cu–Fe/HZSM-5 catalyst helped in strengthening the adsorption interactions of the Cu–Fe catalyst and increasing the number of active sites on the catalyst surface. Different DFT studies have revealed that Cu-based catalysts doped with transition metal oxides adsorb H2 by dissociative adsorption onto Cu, while CO2 is adsorbed and activated over the transition metal oxides. To continue, CO2 desorbs from the transition metal oxide surface and adsorbs on metallic Cu and react with dissociated H on Cu.555,556 From a thermodynamic point of view, the comparison of the formic acid pathway and the CO pathway showed that the energy barrier for CO generation is lower than for formic acid production (50.46 kJ mol−1 < 166.31 kJ mol−1). Moreover, CO formation is an exothermic process while formic acid formation is endothermic (Fig. 28). These data allowed concluding that CO production is more favoured than formate generation. Nevertheless, HCO cannot exist because it is unstable and prone to decomposition back into CO and lead to the production and accumulation of CO which becomes the main CO2 hydrogenation product, implying a very limited contribution of RWGS to CH3OH formation. Recently, a reaction mechanism study over of Cu–ZnO–ZrO2-FER by DRIFTS revealed that methanol and DME were formed by CO2 direct hydrogenation rather than through a CO route.538 Carbonate, formate, and methoxy surface species were the main detected products of CO2 hydrogenation and no bands for adsorbed formyl or formaldehyde species were observed. A mechanism of methanol formation via the bidentate-formate species that underwent hydrogenation and DME formation via methanol dehydration and reaction of two surface methoxy groups was proposed (Scheme 53).
 Fig. 28  Potential energy diagrams for the methanol synthesis reaction via the formate (A) and CO (B) hydrogenation pathways. Reprinted with permission from ref. 554. Copyright 2015, American Institute of Chemical Engineers. 
 Scheme 53  Proposed mechanism for DME synthesis via methanol formation and dehydration. 
5.4.4 Pd and bimetallic catalytic systems for direct DME synthesis. 
Palladium catalysts have been commonly and widely used as hydrogenation catalysts and used for the direct CO2 hydrogenation into methanol due to numerous metal active sites for H2 dissociative adsorption and interfacial sites for CO2 adsorption and hydrogenation. In 2004, the preparation of bimetallic Pd–Cu catalysts allowed improving the DME yields and retarded the CO formation.550 The study of Pd-modified CuO–ZnO–Al2O3–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalysts’ performance revealed that the enhancement of the catalytic activity was due to hydrogen spillover from metallic Pd to the closer metallic Cu (active component for methanol hydrogenation). Pd doping also improved Cu stabilization against oxidation by CO2. CO2 conversion and DME selectivity of 18.7 and 73.6% were achieved at 200 °C and 3 MPa (Table 5, entry 27).
Following these leads, the catalytic activity of PdZn alloy nanoparticles on TiO2 for the hydrogenation of CO2 to methanol was also explored.557,558 The catalytic performances of PdZn alloy nanoparticles onto the solid acid ZSM-5 via chemical vapor impregnation, and of the mixture of 5% PdZn/TiO2 + ZSM-5 for the DME production were compared.559 The catalytic performance of the physical mixture PdZn/TiO2-ZSM-5 showed to be superior and this result was attributed to blocking of Brønsted acid sites when PdZn was directly deposited on the zeolite surface. Therefore, 5% PdZn/TiO2 + ZSM-5 allowed reaching CO2 conversion of 11% and a DME selectivity of 32% at 270 °C and 2 MPa with a DME production of 0.030 kgDME kgcat−1 h−1 (Table 5, entry 31). Previously, hybrid catalysts consisting of deposited Cu–ZrO2 on Pd-decorated multi-walled carbon nanotube (CNT) material and mixed with HZSM-5 zeolites following different routes were also used for the synthesis of DME.510 The study revealed that the mixing method affected the final catalytic performance of the hybrid catalyst and the stepwise precipitation/slurry mixing showed to provide the optimum catalysts. The addition of Pd-decorated CNTs into the Cu–ZrO2/HZSM-5 catalyst modified slightly the apparent activation energy for CO2 hydrogenation, but enabled to increase the metal Cu exposed area, improving H2 and CO2 adsorption performance of the catalyst. Over the bi-functional Cu–ZrO2–PdCNT/HZSM-5 hybrid catalyst at 250 °C and 5.0 MPa, maximum 12.5% CO2 conversion, 51.8% DME selectivity and STYDME 0.579 gDME h−1 ghydr.catal−1 were achieved (Table 5, entry 32). Under these reaction conditions, the catalyst showed stabilized catalytic performance after 20 h of TOS, remaining stable for 200 h with CO2 conversion and DME selectivity around 7.5 and 75%, respectively.
Since CO2 obtained from combustion of sulfur-containing substances enclosed numerous sulfur compounds, catalysts for DME synthesis strongly adsorb sulfur compounds and suffer from strong poisoning. The Pd-catalysts can maintain good catalytic performance in the presence of sulfur species due to the formation of a Pd-electron-deficient state by transferring an outer electron, resulting in poor interactions between Pd and the electron-acceptor sulfur. Different factors can favour the Pd-electron-deficient state such as the acidity of the carrier surface,560 very small particle size of Pd and the presence of electron-withdrawing additives.561 Then, Pd/HZSM-5 bifunctional catalysts incorporating CeO2 and CaO were prepared by impregnation.562 The study showed that CaO incorporation enabled reducing the strong acidity and weakened the CO2 adsorption while the presence of CeO2 induced an increase in the moderate acidity and favoured weak adsorption of CO2. Therefore, CeO2–CaO–Pd/HZSM-5 exhibited very good catalytic activity, and stability versus sulfur poisoning and coke deposition. The best catalytic performance was obtained over 3 wt% CeO2–1 wt% CaO–Pd/HZSM-5 catalyst, with CO2 conversion and DME selectivity of 21% and 67% after 20 h of reaction (Table 5, entry 33).
5.4.5 Conclusion. 
Direct hydrogenation of CO2 in DME is an attractive route considering the environmental and economic issues involved. Efforts are being made to improve the hydrophobic and acid properties of the catalytic surface, and the Cu nanoparticle size and dispersion in order to optimize both methanol dehydration, and CO2 and H2 adsorption and activation, by controlling the zeolite structure and/or the Si/Al ratio, and/or by incorporating a transition metal. The results showed the importance to balance adequately the acidity of the surface to promote methanol dehydration over strong acid sites. Similarly, the importance of the distance between the two types of active sites, the metal sites for methanol synthesis and the acid sites for methanol dehydration, has been studied. The proximity between the two sites was shown to be crucial to minimize mass transfer restrictions and to improve the DME selectivity, being more convenient to integrate the two types of active sites in a single moiety. Nevertheless, the developed catalysts exhibit limited catalytic performance since low DME selectivity is usually achieved because of high CO production. Moreover, the catalysts suffer from drastic deactivation due to the large amount of water produced during the reaction. Fig. 29 and 30 illustrate the catalytic performance of the different zeolite-based catalysts reported and highlight the catalytic performance of La–Cu–ZnO–Al2O3/HZSM-5 and Cu–ZnO–Al2O3/HZSM-5 due to the good DME selectivity and productivity. However, the direct transformation of CO2 into DME needs further development to reach industrial application. Exhaustive studies taking into account the catalyst stability and regeneration step are required.
 Fig. 29  CO2 conversion was plotted versus DME selectivity for all reported zeolite-based catalysts. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high selectivity versus high conversion level. 
 Fig. 30  CO2 conversion was plotted versus DME selectivity for all reported zeolite-based catalysts. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high yield versus high GHSV. 


5.5 CO2 methanation. 
5.5.1 Introduction. 
Nowadays natural gas constitutes 24% of global primary energy consumption of fuel and it is the third fuel used for power generation, and the main one for heat generation in industry and households.563 Currently, methane can be obtained from natural wells with fossil origin, from purifying biogas obtained by fermentation of organic matter and from the synthetic gas route. In 2022, natural gas reserves account for ∼50 years of current production. In 1872, CO2 hydrogenation into methane was first discovered by applying an electric discharge to a CO/CO2/H2 mixture.564 At the beginning of the twentieth century, Sabatier developed a process to produce methane and water starting from CO2 and H2 in the presence of a heterogeneous nickel catalyst.565 Since then, CO2 hydrogenation or CO2 methanation, also called the “Sabatier reaction”, has been studied for more than 100 years. Nowadays, because of the increasing demand for natural gas and the reduction of greenhouse gases, the production of synthetic natural gas (SNG) constitutes an important future energy carrier due to its environmentally friendly nature and high calorific value (Fig. 31). Moreover, SNG can be stored and distributed easily thanks to its worldwide existing infrastructure consisting of storage facilities, filling stations, and pipeline networks.566–569 More importantly, CO2 methanation is one of the most promising strategies within the concept of power-to-gas. Indeed, the main problem found with renewable energy is to adapt the production and demand, as there are situations where the amount of energy produced is greater than the demand when there is for examples excess wind, sun or rain. Then the storage becomes expensive and finally ends up wasting it. Accordingly, the Power-to-Gas (P2G) process is an alternative to adjust this demand since it allows the transfer of excess electricity from renewables from the electricity grid to the natural gas grid. P2G involves the conversion of electricity into thermal energy in the form of methane, and is still an incipient technology. An overview of the production costs of synthetic methane in a P2G process has been recently reported.570 The production costs depend on three determining economic factors such as the price and market of electricity, the price and market of SNG and the market price of CO2 and the availability. It was so envisioned that with the future expected development of capital and operational expenditure, electricity prices and gas costs, a viable and economic production of synthetic natural gas for the years 2030, especially for 2050, is feasible. The CO2 methanation process requires H2 and for sustainability and environment considerations, H2 has to be green. Therefore, H2 is obtained from water splitting that requires electric energy produced from green and sustainable sources. Current electrolysis technologies at an industrial scale are alkaline electrolysis571 and proton exchange membrane electrolysis.572
 Fig. 31  Scheme of methane production and uses. 
5.5.2 Thermodynamics of CO2 methanation. 
Methanation for the production of SNG is a very complicated process and the thermodynamics of the process has to be considered for the evaluation of any proposed technology. Then, numerous studies of thermodynamic equilibrium calculations of complex chemical systems based on Gibbs free energy minimization have been reported, providing information such as the type of thermodynamically stable reaction products together with their selectivity and yield, heat transfer requirements (endothermic or exothermic process) and the impact of reaction parameters (temperature, pressure, or reactant ratios).573–575 CO2 methanation is known to be a highly exothermic reaction. However, it is difficult to achieve because of the high kinetic barriers of the eight-electron reduction process. The hydrogenation process starting from CO2 and H2 involves six possible products, CO2, CO, H2, CH4, H2O and C deposited through three independent reactions (Scheme 54). Moreover, CO2 methanation is a reversible reaction and the reverse process is the steam reforming of methane (eqn (4)).
 Scheme 54  Different CO2 hydrogenation processes. 
Based on these reactions, different studies513,576,577 concluded that a reaction temperature over 600 °C favours CO formation from the reverse water gas shift reaction (RWGS) (eqn (3)) while CH4 formation is disfavoured and the C-deposits can be avoided to a great extent. Moreover, a H2/CO2 ratio increase revealed to have a positive effect on the production of CH4 and it has to be pointed out that carbon formation is not expected at a H2/CO2 ratio ≥ 4.573 At lower ratios significant carbon formation was observed even at 3 MPa. Then, a control of the H2/CO2 ratio and temperature is mandatory for the methanation process. From thermodynamic consideration, CO2 methanation should be performed at low temperatures (<300–350 °C), elevated pressure, and with a stoichiometric ratio of H2/CO2 = 4.578
Although catalytic CO2 methanation is thermodynamically favourable at low temperatures, because of kinetic limitation and high stability of CO2 molecules, high temperatures (200–750 °C) are normally required to enable CO2 methanation. To maximize the CH4 yield, the reaction system has to be maintained away from the region of thermodynamic equilibrium and reactions of eqn (2) and (3) have to be avoided. In addition, due to the high exothermicity, the development of local hotspots along the catalytic packed bed is responsible for catalyst sintering and deactivation.579 Therefore, the design and development of efficient and selective catalysts to methane formation coupled with an adequate reactor system is key and very challenging.
5.5.3 Catalysts for CO2 methanation. 
Since the discovery of Ni activity for CO2 methanation, numerous group VIIIB metals (Fe, Ru, Co, Rh, Ir, Ni, and Pt)574,580–586 have been carefully investigated and Ni, Ru and Rh based catalysts revealed to be the most active for this process. Various strategies have been attempted to enhance the stability and activity of the catalyst. The oxygen mobility and basic properties of the support constitute key parameters. The effects of the support, metal loading and the incorporation of additives such as metals, metal oxides or alkali metals have been shown to enhance the stability and catalytic performance and modify the state of the active phase.577,587 The addition of a second metal can change the electronic and geometric structures of single-crystal metals,588 inducing different physical and chemical properties to the metal-active phase.
The specific activity and selectivity of different metals over alumina have been studied by different groups.589–591 In 1976,589 the order of activity of supported metals was as following: Ru ≫ Fe > Ni > Co > Rh > Pd > Pt > Ir, and selectivity order Pd > Pt > Ir > Ni > Rh > Co > Fe > Ru. Previously, a simplified classification was reported considering the most important metal methanation catalysts, Ru, Ni, Co, Fe and Mo. Therefore, activity was reported in the order Ru > Fe > Ni > Co > Mo and selectivity in the order Ni > Co > Fe > Ru. Within the CO2 methanation catalysts, the Ni based remain the most extensively studied catalysts587,592–598 due to their good activity for CO2 hydrogenation, high selectivity to methane, low cost and availability. However, Ni catalysts deactivate easily, even at low temperature, due to the sintering of Ni particles, formation of mobile nickel sub-carbonyls and carbon deposition599 that results in a fast deactivation of the catalyst, and constituting one of the most important problems in industry. After Ni, Rh has been the most investigated metal for CO2 methanation. The oxidation state of Rh574 and particle size, as well as the presence of promoters greatly influence its catalytic performance.600 Likewise, a study of the Rh content over TiO2 modified metal particle sizes revealed that at low temperatures (130–150 °C), the rate of CH4 production per surface Rh atoms increased when the metal particle size increases up to 7 nm.582 Ruthenium has been largely used and studied for CO2 methanation and can be even more active591,601–605 than Ni but it is considerably more expensive.606–608
(a) Mechanism of CO2 methanation. 
Several studies have proposed different mechanisms for CO2 methanation over different catalysts,577,609–612 and two main routes with CO or bicarbonate/formate as main intermediates have been considered (Fig. 32). The nature and composition of the catalyst active sites, more specifically the presence of acid/basic sites (density and strength), control and determine the formation and nature of the reaction intermediates. With respect to the CO2 dissociation mechanism, it is envisioned that CO2 adsorption occurs preferably at the metal–support interface while CO2 dissociation takes place on the active metal surface.613 Then, CO hydrogenation takes place in the same way as CO methanation. The other mechanisms involve the formation of oxygenated species, carbonates and further, formates, preferably in the presence of basic sites that enable the adsorption and activation of CO2.
 Fig. 32  Different mechanisms have been investigated based on CO intermediates, or formate (bicarbonate) intermediates. 
(b) Zeolite-based catalysts for CO2 methanation. 
Numerous studies and catalytic systems have been developed to perform CO2 methanation and the design and development of an efficient and selective catalyst, able to perform methanation at relatively low temperature to reduce the costs of the process, remain as the main challenges. The development of a successful zeolite based catalyst for CO2 methanation could be a new breakthrough in the zeolite field owing to zeolite robustness to work continuously for days under severe conditions (temperature, pressure and moisture).614,615 Indeed zeolites are well-known to present great stability under severe reaction conditions as evidenced by their use in the FCC industrial process.
Despite the existence of different hydrogenation metals reported for CO2 methanation, Ni remains the best choice when considering activity, selectivity and price. Interestingly, Ni constitutes practically the sole reported metal supported on zeolites for this application. In the literature, we could find one example of Fe catalyst where Fe/13X was studied as the candidate to substitute Ni catalysts at elevated pressure. A very high dispersion of Fe in octahedral sites within the zeolite is necessary for selectively performing the CO2 methanation process and to suppress the Fischer–Tropsch activity towards C–C coupling at elevated pressure. A low Fe loading (1 wt%) and high Fe dispersion were needed to reach high reaction rates with 76% CH4 selectivity and 82% CO2 conversion, at 300 °C and 10 bar (Table 6, entry 1).616
