Nickel-based zeolite catalysts exhibit maximum CH4 selectivity but suffer from drastic deactivation due to water adsorption on active sites. Therefore, to comply with “Le Chatelier's principle” in situ removal of water from the reaction sites is required and the use of molecular sieves due to their adsorption properties should be beneficial. Accordingly, a recent study showed the importance of regeneration of the active sites of the catalyst (Table 6, entry 2).617 The catalytic performances of 5Ni/13X and 5Ni/5A samples were compared for alternated cyclic CO2 methanation and drying at 300 °C. Better catalytic performance of 5Ni/13X was demonstrated, which is attributed to its higher water adsorption capacity with the optimal catalyst regeneration by a simple drying step under an oxidizing (air) atmosphere which allowed the redispersion of the Ni nanoparticles618 and improved the catalyst stability.
Therefore, the development of a robust and successful zeolite-based catalyst is related to three key parameters which are hydrophobicity, nickel nanoparticle size and dispersion, and CO2 adsorption and activation.
In the literature, numerous works have reported the effect of compensating cation exchange on the basic properties of zeolite-based catalysts affecting the adsorption and activation of CO2, and also on the stabilization and dispersion of Ni nanoparticles that can be additionally modified by adding promoters, the effect of Si/Al ratio on the acid/base properties and the hydrophobicity, and the effect of the structure on the Ni stability and dispersion and also on the hydrophobicity inherent to each zeolite structure.
(i) Effect of Si/Al ratio of zeolite-based catalysts for CO2methanation. The Al framework content in zeolites allowed controlling the Brønsted acidity and hydrophobicity and hydrophilicity of the surface.58,619–621 Zeolites with enhanced hydrophobicity are obtained by increasing the framework Si/Al ratio and the number of silanols. For CO2 methanation, the hydrophobicity of the surface was shown to be of paramount importance due to the inhibitory effect for water adsorption.622,623 Recently, the catalytic performance of Ni-ITQ-2 and ZSM-5 zeolite catalysts with different aluminum contents624 revealed that the Al content is a more determinant factor than the structure of the zeolite. This is because of the lower water adsorption on the high Si/Al ratio zeolites that lowers the deactivation of the Ni components by water that reduces the CO2 surface coverage. Moreover, the high Si/Al ratio also improved the dispersion and stability of Ni nanoparticles as it was also described in a recent work.625 Comparison of the catalytic behavior of both zeolite structures showed higher activity for the pure silica ITQ-2 zeolite indicating the crucial effect of the aluminum content and its impact on the hydrophobicity and hydrophilicity of the catalytic surface and nickel particle size and dispersion. 68% CO2 conversion and 99% CH4 selectivity were achieved at 350 °C (Table 6, entries 3 and 4). The results obtained with 5Ni/ITQ-2(Si/Al ∼ ∞) are comparable to those reported in the presence of Ni-zeolite with promoters such as La2O3626 or Ce.627,628 Previously, the beneficial effect of increasing the Si/Al ratio in Y zeolite on the catalytic behavior of 15%Ni–Y zeolite samples for CO2 methanation was shown (Table 6, entry 5).621 In the case of Y zeolite, a lower Al content obtained by dealumination was associated with the lower affinity of the zeolites to water and the presence of mesopores enabling higher dispersion of the Ni particles regardless of the compensating cation present in the zeolite and their lower basicity. Therefore, the hydrophobicity of the zeolites resulted in a more determinant factor than the basicity, which was previously reported as a parameter of importance for CO2 methanation.629 Indeed, the influence of the distribution of acidic/basic sites and Ni dispersion on the catalytic behaviors for CO2 methanation catalysts has been described as a key factor for the CO2 adsorption and activation. Following this direction, a study of the physicochemical properties of the Ni/Al2O3, Ni/SiO2 and Ni/Beta zeolite (Ni/Hβ40) revealed that even if the mesoporous Al2O3 enabled a higher dispersion of nickel species, this sample exhibited a lower catalytic activity than the Beta zeolite based catalyst. The distribution of basic sites was determined with CO2-TPD profiles showing different desorption peaks assigned to weak, medium and strong basic sites in the temperature range of 100–200 °C, 200–400 °C and 500–600 °C, respectively. Therefore, the Ni/Al2O3 sample mainly exhibited weak basic sites, the Ni/SiO2 sample exhibited mainly medium basic sites and the Ni/Hβ40 sample mainly presented medium-strong basic sites, leading to the basicity order Ni/Hβ40 > Ni/SiO2 > Ni/Al2O3, with the presence of basic sites being crucial for CO2 adsorption and activation (Fig. 33). The DRIFT study showed different reaction networks over the different samples that exhibit the same Ni content but different physicochemical properties such as a higher density of Lewis acid sites in the Ni/Al2O3 catalyst and higher density of medium-strong strength basic sites in the Ni/Hβ40 catalyst. Therefore, the Ni/Hβ40 catalyst characterized by optimum density of the medium to strong basic sites exhibited superior activity to Ni/SiO2 while Ni/Al2O3 did not show adequate catalytic properties for CO2 methanation. Practically 100% CH4 selectivity and 80% CO2 conversion were achieved at 300 °C in the presence of Ni/Hβ40 (Table 6, entry 6).612 These results showed the importance of suitable hydrophobic and basic properties for CO2 methanation for both water elimination of the catalytic surface and CO2 adsorption and activation, respectively, which could be achieved in zeolites by adjusting the Si/Al ratio and cation exchange.
 Fig. 33  CO2-TPD profiles of the reduced Ni/Al2O3, Ni/SiO2 and Ni/Hβ40 catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 612. Copyright 2019, Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
(ii) Effect of the cations in exchanged zeolites for CO2methanation. The substitution of SiO4 with AlO4− tetrahedra in zeolite induces a negative charge that has to be compensated by exchangeable cations (H+, Na+, K+, Cs2+, and Ca2+). The amount, characteristic and properties of each cation determine the properties of the zeolites, such as acid/base properties612 and consequently affect the CO2 adsorption capacity.42,630–632 In addition, the nature of the compensating cation can influence the Ni metallic particles’ size and stability as well as the reducibility properties owing to electron donation from the alkali metal to nickel which is greater when the larger the ionic radius is.633 In this direction, a study of the effect of the environment of Ni particles demonstrated the influence of the size of the alkali metal cations since larger cations are able to inhibit the migration path of the nickel species and so to limit the metal sintering most efficiently than compensation cations with lower size.634 The effect of the nature of the compensating cation is greater when the lower the Ni content is, i.e. when Ni sintering is lower. Following this direction, a few years ago, a study of CO2 methanation using the USY zeolite exchanged with monovalent (Cs+, K+, Na+, and Li+) and divalent (Ba2+, Ca2+, Mg2+) cations and impregnated with 15% and 5% of Ni was carried out.621 The H2-TPR study demonstrated that the reducibility of Ni species was higher when bigger cations are present and the effect is greater for lower Ni content. This is so even if a similar amount of reduced Ni species was obtained (for the same Ni content) (Fig. 34), while similar Ni particles sizes were observed for monovalent cations and much bigger for the K+ sample. Therefore, the improvement of the catalytic performance with exchanged samples with monovalent alkaline cations was associated with the enhanced CO2 adsorption capacity and framework basicity of the exchanged zeolites. With respect to divalent alkaline earth cations, Mg2+ improved both CO2 activation and Ni dispersion. When a similar study was performed with the USY zeolite exchanged with alkali metals and impregnated with 5% of Ni, the authors reported enhancements of up to 200% in CO2 conversion and 300% in CH4 selectivity in the presence of the 5 wt% Ni/Cs-USY catalyst, at 450 °C (Table 6, entry 7).629
 Fig. 34  H2-TPR profiles obtained for mono- and divalent samples containing 5%Ni. Reprinted with permission from ref. 629. Copyright 2017, Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
Soon after, a study comparing the catalytic performance of 10 wt% Ni-supported Y and BETA zeolites reported the same benefits of Na+ cation exchange that improved the CO2 conversion due to the reducibility enhancement of NiO (electron donation from Na+ to Ni) together with the improved CO2 adsorption owing to the generation of weak basic sites in Na-based catalysts. Various and distinct Ni species were formed in both Ni/Na-BETA and Ni/Na-Y due to differences in their structure involving different nickel locations with different reducibility. Besides, similar CO2 methanation activity was obtained for both Ni/Na-Y and Ni/Na-Beta catalysts attributed to a similar available surface of active phase during CO2 methanation. In the presence of Ni/Na-BETA catalyst 73% CO2 conversion and 71% CH4 yield were reached at 350 °C (Table 6, entry 8).27
(iii) Effect of topology and morphology structure of zeolite-based catalysts for CO2methanation. The zeolite topology not only influences the reaction pathway and product distribution, but it is also crucial to the diffusion and adsorption/deposition of metallic precursors into the channels leading to different nickel locations (NiO on the external surface, Ni2+ pseudo-tetrahedrally, tetrahedrally and octahedrelly coordinated). Indeed the different voids and microenvironments defined by the zeolite topologies are responsible for stabilization and dispersion of metallic active sites.48,51,87,539,635 A recent work reported the effect of the zeolite framework type on the catalytic performances of Ni-based catalysts (15 wt%) using commercial USY, BEA, ZSM-5 and MOR zeolites with the Si/Al ratio close to 40 and ion-exchanged with Na+ or Cs+.636 The zeolites exhibited different particle sizes, and the BEA sample had the smallest particle size, and the intercrystalline space of the BEA sample was responsible for the formation of mesoporosity. TEM analysis revealed that USY, ZSM-5 and MOR samples exhibit similar Ni0 particles sizes, while the BEA exhibited higher dispersion and smallest Ni0 particles. Even if the zeolites presented a similar Si/Al ratio, their H+ exchange capacities were different, leading to distinct alkali and alkali earth compensating cation contents and so diverse CO2 adsorption capacities as follows: USY < BEA < ZSM-5 < MOR. The H2-TPR study demonstrated similar reducibility for USY and BEA samples with a greater amount of NiO reduced at T > 500 °C, probably due to NiO located in mesopores, and similar reducibility for MOR and ZSM-5 samples with NiO particles mainly located on the external surface. Since the hydrophobicity was established as paramount importance to shift the equilibrium and to avoid the deactivation of active sites, the hydrophobicity index was calculated for the different zeolites and followed the order: USY > MOR > BEA ≈ ZSM-5. Besides these characterization data, the catalytic performances followed the order: 15%Ni/Na-USY > 15%Ni/Na-BEA > 15%Ni/Na-ZSM-5 ≈ 15%Ni/Na-MOR. Thus, taking into account that Ni0 particles size, CO2 adsorption capacity and hydrophobicity are considered key parameters for the design of an efficient zeolite-based catalyst and the catalytic activity order, the authors concluded that the mandatory parameter is the hydrophobicity. When zeolite samples were Cs+ exchanged, an increase in the hydrophobicity index for BEA, ZSM-5 and MOR samples was observed as reported in the literature,637 while it remained the same for the USY sample. Regarding H2-TPR profiles, there were not considerable changes when comparing Cs+ and Na+ exchanged samples. However, the Cs+ exchanged sample favoured Ni0 dispersion owing to its ability to inhibit the migration path of the nickel species and to suppress the metal agglomeration as commented above. Thus, the dispersion of Ni0 particles followed the order BEA > MOR > USY > ZSM-5, while the catalytic activity of the exchanged sample followed the order 15Ni/Cs-USY > 15%Ni/Cs-BEA ≈ 15%Ni/Cs-MOR > 15% > Ni/Cs-ZSM-5. Since the characterization data did not allow supporting the activity order, the authors attributed the best catalytic performance of the USY sample to more favourable metal/support interaction inherent to the zeolite structure. Additionally, a characterization study of textural properties and the catalytic performance of a BEA sample with Si/Al = 243 was performed. The results showed an improvement of the catalytic activity attributed to its higher hydrophobicity despite presenting larger Ni0 particles and almost reaching the results obtained in the presence of the USY catalyst.636 Thus, in the presence of Ni/NaUSY and Ni/NaBEA catalysts, 70–71% CO2 conversion and 95–96% CH4 selectivity, at 375–400 °C, were achieved (Fig. 35 and Table 6, entries 9 and 10). These results revealed the importance of the metal/support interaction inherent to zeolite morphology over their catalytic performance.
 Fig. 35  Catalytic performances of the Na-BEA samples with Si/Al = 40 (1) and 243 (2) and of the Ni/Na-USY catalyst. Reprinted with permission from ref. 636. Copyright 2018, Elsevier Inc. All rights reserved. 
A previous study compared the use of different micro- and mesoporous supports (ZSM-5, SBA-15, MCM-41, Al2O3 and SiO2) to prepare Ni-based catalysts. The CO2 adsorption study evidenced that the incorporation of Ni onto different supports induced the formation of new basic sites. Ni/ZSM-5 presented an optimum amount of weak and medium basic sites able to absorb and activate CO2 molecules while stronger basic sites present in Ni/SBA-15 and Ni/SiO2 are not suitable to activate CO2 molecules and enable methanation.638 Among the prepared catalysts, the Ni/ZSM-5 catalyst displayed higher catalytic activity and no deactivation was observed up to 100 h, attributed to the basic properties and higher nickel dispersion (Table 6, entries 11–13).597
(iv) Preparation strategies of zeolite-based catalysts for CO2methanation. Different strategies for the preparation and reduction of the CO2 methanation catalysts have been studied in order to optimize the dispersion and stabilization of Ni particles. Ni-based USY zeolites have been largely studied and used as CO2 methanation catalysts. USY zeolite, due to ultra-stabilization treatments, is characterized by good stability under CO2 methanation conditions, i.e. temperature and presence of water, and offers opportunities as support. Then, the effect of the preparation method and the Ni content on the catalytic performance of Ni-based USY catalysts has been studied. Generally, higher metal loadings led to better catalytic performance, due to a higher availability and amount of Ni active sites.639 The comparison of catalytic behaviors revealed that Ni and Ce supported NaUSY zeolites prepared by impregnation exhibited higher catalytic performance than samples prepared by ion-exchanged Ni species due to easier reducibility of the NiO species.639 Besides the effects of the drying method after impregnation, the calcination and the reduction temperature on the catalytic performance of Ni-based USY catalysts showed that CO2 conversion and CH4 selectivity could be maximized by optimizing the preparation and pre-reduction conditions.640 Likewise, the calcination temperature influenced the reducibility and location of the Ni species and particle size after reduction. Therefore, at higher calcination temperatures, NiO located on the external surface was the main Ni species, while at 300 °C Ni2+ species octahedrally coordinated were the main Ni species. Moreover, an increase in the calcination temperature induced a decrease in the amount of Ni reducible species, an increase in reduction temperatures of Ni species, and the formation of larger Ni particles (Fig. 36).
 Fig. 36  H2-TPR profiles obtained for the 5%NiUSY samples reduced at different temperatures. Reprinted with permission from ref. 640. Copyright 2015, Springer Science Business Media New York. 
The pre-reduction temperature also affected the catalytic activity and was dependent on the Ni content, since at lower Ni content (5 wt%), the higher reduction temperature required (>550 °C) induced sintering, while for higher Ni content (14 wt%) no effect of the reduction temperature was observed. Therefore, in the presence of 14 wt% Ni/USY calcined at 500 °C and reduced at 600 °C, maximum 95% CO2 conversion and 70% CH4 selectivity were achieved at 400 °C (Table 6, entry 14).
The influence of the Ni precursors for the preparation Ni-Zeolite 13X and 5A catalysts was studied and the results showed that the use of nickel citrate and acetate resulted in a smaller NiO particle size compared to nitrate. STEM-EDX results indicate that all the Ni-precursor complexes diffuse more efficiently in the 13X than in the 5A zeolite structure because of the steric hindrance induced by the smaller pore size of the zeolite 5A. The best catalytic performance was achieved using 5%Ni–13X catalysts synthesized with nickel citrate. 79% CO2 conversion at 320 °C and 100% CH4 selectivity for 200 h were achieved (Table 6, entries 15 and 16).641 A X zeolite synthesized from waste fly ashes coming from the energy sector was also used as support to prepare Ni-based methanation catalysts (Ni/X zeoliteashes), and exhibited relatively lower catalytic performance in comparison to other published Ni-catalysts prepared with commercial zeolites (USY and Beta), because of the formation of unreduced Ni species due to remaining fly ash components such as MgO and Al2O3. At 450 °C, 49% CO2 conversion and 96% CH4 selectivity were achieved in the presence of 10 wt% Ni/X zeolite (Table 6, entry 17).642
A hydrothermal synthesis method was also followed for the preparation of an active and stable 10 wt% Ni@HZSM-5 catalyst starting from the Ni/SiO2 catalyst as the silicone source.643 In addition to the superior catalytic performance to that of the 10 wt% Ni/ZSM-5 sample prepared by impregnation, the 10 wt% Ni@HZSM-5 catalyst retained a similar nickel content and structure after use due to its special embedded structure. Good and stable 66.2% CO2 conversion and 99.8% CH4 selectivity at 400 °C, 0.1 MPa, for 40 h, were achieved (Table 6, entries 18 and 19). A desilication method was also reported to prepare a Ni-desilicated silicalite zeolite (5 wt%/d-S1) with intra-particle voids and mesopores enabling higher Ni dispersion inside the channels and smaller Ni particles by the impregnation method. The 5 wt% Ni/d-S1 material exhibited higher catalytic performance than the pristine sample and a relevant increase of CH4 selectivity and stability. At 450 °C, 61–64% CO2 conversion and 95–97% CH4 selectivity were maintained for 50 h (Table 6, entry 20). In the presence of a 5 wt% Ni/S1 catalyst, 42% CO2 conversion and 40% CH4 selectivity were initially reached, though a drastic decrease in CH4 selectivity was observed after 10 h of TOS.644
To overcome the mass transfer and diffusion limitations of zeolites for bulky molecules, hierarchical zeolites containing a secondary pore system or nanocrystalline zeolites have been prepared. More recently, the synthesis of a mesoporous material with uniform mesopore diameters and crystalline MFI zeolite walls has been described as a new alternative. Therefore, an amine functionalized mesoporous MFI nanosponge zeolite (AF-NSZ) was used as the support for the regioselective precipitation of the Ni species in the mesoporous channels resulting in highly dispersed and stabilized Ni nanoparticles.626 Good catalytic performance was achieved with a 15 wt% Ni/AF-NSZ mesoporous MFI nanosponge, with 78% CO2 conversion and 99% CH4 selectivity at 400 °C, 0.1 MPa, for 50 h (Table 6, entry 21). Despite the efforts spent to improve the dispersion and stabilization of Ni nanoparticles by modifying Ni deposition and by using zeolite-based materials with mesoporosity, the catalytic performance exhibited by these advanced catalysts does not outperform the results achieved in the presence of more conventional Ni/USY zeolite catalysts (Table 6, entry 5).
(v) Addition of promoter to the zeolite-based catalysts for CO2methanation. For CO2 methanation, CO2 activation is considered the rate-determining step645–648 and the development of the low temperature CO2 methanation process requires suitable and efficient CO2 adsorption and activation. The presence of additives such as metals, metal oxides, alkali metals and rare earth oxides has been shown not only to enhance CO2 activation at lower temperatures but also to improve the stability and catalytic performance, and modify the state of the active phase.577,587,649–651 Among CO2 methanation catalysts, cerium oxide and mixed oxide based catalysts have exhibited the best catalytic performance575,587,652–654 due to the suitable physicochemical properties of CeO2 for CO2 adsorption and activation, and oxygen mobility.655 The remarkable ability of CeO2 is due to the presence of oxygen vacancies that allow the storage and release of oxygen,225,656 also promoting the high dispersion and activity of supported noble-metal due to strong metal–support bonding.657 Comparison of the catalytic behavior revealed that Ni and Ce supported NaUSY zeolites prepared by impregnation exhibited higher catalytic performance than samples prepared by ion-exchanged Ni species due to the easier reducibility of the NiO species in the samples prepared by impregnation.639 Meantime, the Ce incorporation into the Ni-USY zeolite enhanced the catalyst activity and selectivity. At 400 °C, in the presence of 14%NiUSY and 5%Ni15%CeUSY catalysts, 65.5 and 68.3% CH4 selectivity, and 94.2 and 95.1% CO2 conversion, respectively, were achieved (Table 6, entries 24 and 25). The study of the effect of the order of Ce and Ni incorporation in USY zeolites indicated that stronger interactions between Ni and Ce species occurred when Ni was incorporated after or simultaneously, with an enhanced reducibility of the Ni species and a decrease in the Ni0 particle size. In contrast, the CO2 adsorption capacity decreased when Ce was incorporated before Ni. Therefore, co-impregnation was determined as the best preparation method (Table 6, entry 26).628 The incorporation of La2O3 and MgO in nickel mesoporous MFI nanosponge zeolite allowed modifying the surface basicity and promoting CO2 adsorption and the methanation process. The amine functionalization of the mesoporous MFI nanosponge zeolite (AF-NSZ) enabled the regioselective precipitation of the Ni precursor in the mesoporous channels resulting in highly dispersed and stabilized Ni nanoparticles (Table 6, entries 27–29).626 Therefore, La2O3 and MgO promoted Ni/AF-NSZ samples (15Ni–5La/AF-NSZ and 15Ni–5Mg/AF-NSZ) exhibited higher catalytic activity than the pristine 15Ni/AF-NSZ catalyst, at lower temperature (280 °C), due to higher CO2 adsorption and activation. Following the same leads, the addition of La2O3 to 10Ni/Na-BETA helped in increasing and optimizing the amount of CO2 adsorption sites and hydrogenation active sites resulting in an increase of the methanation process. In the presence of 10Ni–10La2O3/Na-BETA at 400 °C, maximum 82% CO2 conversion and 97% CH4 selectivity were achieved. At 350 °C, 65% CO2 conversion at 350 °C and 99% CH4 selectivity were maintained for 24 h (Table 6, entries 30–32).27
Owing to the high activity of Ru for CO2 methanation, especially at lower temperatures, the addition of Ru to Ni catalysts may be beneficial to enhance CO2 methanation.611 Accordingly, the effect of addition of Ru on the catalytic performance of Ni/13X and Ni/5A catalysts was explored. The catalytic performance of mono and bimetallic Ru and Ni 13X and 5A and the physicochemical characterization results showed that both zeolite structures remained unchanged after Ni, Ru addition. Nevertheless, only good metal dispersion was obtained for Ni into the pores of 13X owing to the improved diffusion of the Ni precursor while metal dispersion was worse for the bimetallic catalysts. The best results were obtained for the catalysts exhibiting a lower amount of weak acid sites. Thus, in the presence of monometallic 2.5%Ru13X and 5%Ni13X catalysts, good catalytic activity and stability were maintained at 360 °C, for 200 h with 97% CH4 selectivity (Table 6, entries 33 and 34).658 It was then concluded that the incorporation of Ru did not allow improving the catalytic performance of Ni catalysts.
(vi) Mechanistic studies of CO2methanation over zeolite-based catalysts. The CO2 methanation mechanism has been the target of numerous studies and two main pathways can be considered with CO and formate being the main intermediates (Fig. 39).577,609–612,647 The physicochemical properties of the catalysts are decisive since the nature and strength of acid and basic sites, and the presence of oxygen vacancies play a determinant role in the CO2 adsorption and activation, and influence the reaction pathway. The CO2 methanation process involves CO2 adsorption and activation over active sites (Fig. 37 and 38), preferably, basic sites. Over mixed oxides, the presence of oxygen vacancies on the surface constitutes decisive reactive sites for CO2 activation and formate formation as it was reported on Ru/CeO2 (Fig. 37).659 As mentioned above, CO2 hydrogenation involves adsorbed oxygenated species such as monodendate carbonates and formates while H2 activation and dissociation takes place over Ni0 nanoparticles.613 The oxygenated species are so converted into formyl and methoxy species finally hydrogenated into CH4. In Ni/USY660,661 and Ru/Al2O3 samples,647 due to the lack of basic sites and/or oxygen vacancies, CO2 methanation goes through monodendate formate dissociation over Ni particles and the formation of adsorbed CO which is later converted into CH4.660,662 Therefore, under methanation conditions, dissociated hydrogen reacts with physisorbed carbonates and/or CO2, giving rise to monodentate formates, then to carbonyls adsorbed on Ni0 particles and, finally, to methane (Fig. 38).
 Fig. 37  Mechanism of CO2 methanation on Ru/CeO2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 659. Copyright 2016, American Chemical Society. 
 Fig. 38  Proposed mechanism for CO2 methanation on NiUSY zeolites. Reprinted with permission from ref. 660. Copyright 2015, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
 Fig. 39  Different mechanisms involved in CO2 methanation over Ni/USY and Ce–Ni/USY catalysts. 
Studies in the absence of H2 revealed that CO2 was not adsorbed over acidic zeolite while in the presence of H2, formates and carbonyl species were identified.660 The results led us to conclude that the CO2 hydrogenation mechanism did not undergo carbonate formation as an intermediate step, but rather passed through the formate dissociation step leading, at low temperatures, to the formation of adsorbed CO and in a minor extension to methane. Therefore, in the absence of basic sites, CO seems to be the “true” intermediate in the CO2 methanation reaction. Moreover, CO dissociation/hydrogenation was confirmed to be the rate-determining step.660 In the same way, a proposed four-step reaction mechanism involving the carbonyl pathway and the cleavage of CO bonds from CO2 as the rate-determining step was reported over Ni(19 wt%)/MCM-41.663 The addition of promoters, and especially CeO2, allowed improving the activity and selectivity of Ni-based USY catalysts. A mechanistic study by in situ IR spectroscopy over the Ce–Ni/USY catalyst revealed that hydrogen dissociated on Ni0 particles reacted with hydrogen and bidentate carbonates, leading to the formation of monodentate formates, and then to CH4.664 The different mechanisms involved in CO2 methanation over the different Ni/USY catalysts are depicted in Fig. 39.
Recently, the catalytic activity of bimetallic Ni–Mo/SiO2 catalysts for CO2 methanation was investigated. Characterization data revealed that the incorporation of Mo improves the dispersion of Ni through strong metal/metal interaction and was optimum for Ni/Mo = 1, preventing CO adsorption at room temperature. Then, the catalytic study through the product composition indicated that CO2 methanation undergoes RWGS and CO methanation. The Mo content plays a key role over the catalytic pathway since the high Mo content favours CO desorption and limits CO hydrogenation because of the presence of smaller Ni ensembles and reduced accessibility to Ni0 active sites as a result of MoOx coverage. Therefore, for the Ni/Mo ratio close to 1, RWGS is the main process while a low Mo content (Ni/Mo = 0.1) favours CO2 and CO activation promoting CO methanation. Therefore, at 400 °C, 25–30% CO2 conversion controlled by thermodynamic equilibrium of the RWGS reaction with 100% CH4 selectivity was achieved.665
(vii) Plasma assisted CO2methanation. Plasma is a superheated matter wherein electrons are ripped away from the atoms to form an ionized gas. Heating a gas will form plasma, generally named the fourth state of the matter together with the solid, liquid and gas, occurring naturally in nebulas, stars, auroras, sun and through artificial lights (neons). The properties of plasma have been explored and used by the scientific community for application in different fields such as alternative to the thermocatalytic process and especially for reactions that require high energy consumption due to thermodynamic limitations. Indeed, non-thermal plasma (NTP) and thermal assisted plasma systems offer attractive opportunities and have been tested for various processes. In NTP, electrons are highly energetic with a very high electron temperature of 10000–100000 °C (1–10 eV),666 while the gas temperature is close to room temperature. Therefore, the impact of high energy electrons is enough to breakdown bonds and produce free radicals, excited atoms, ions and molecules.667 The non-equilibrium character of the plasma system aids in overcoming the barrier energy and thermodynamic limitations at low temperatures in chemical processes such as CO2 conversions.668–670 There exist different types of NTP such as dielectric barrier discharge (DBD), corona discharge, glow discharge or gliding arcs. Among them, DBD constitutes a versatile method with some advantages such as uniform distribution of micro-discharges, high-energy electrons and operability under ambient conditions. When an alternative current (AC voltage) is applied to one or both electrodes formed by two metal plates and covered by an insulator, a discharge is produced (dielectric barrier discharge plasma (DBD)). When an excessive and rapid discharge is suppressed by the insulator, this discharge does not produce an arc but bright purple plasma (Fig. 40). Dielectric barrier discharge plasma is one of the most popular methods to generate non thermal plasma (NTP) comprising a host of high-energy electrons, free radicals, chemically active ions and excited species, promoting chemical reactions. The big potential of NTPs to promote reactions under mild conditions is of great interest,666 and few examples of CO2 methanation under NTP at temperature <200 °C have been reported.
 Fig. 40  Scheme of planar DBD electrode devices. 
The activity of Ni-based zeolite catalysts has been also studied under non-thermal plasma-assisted conditions, and a mechanism of plasma-assisted CO2 hydrogenation was studied by operando time-resolved FTIR spectroscopy over Ni-HUSY (Fig. 41).662 CO was detected as the main product of the reaction, produced from CO2 dissociation in the gas phase, and CH4 was observed as a secondary product. The same authors performed CO2 hydrogenation using a packed-bed catalytic reactor under two different configurations, in-plasma catalysis (IPC) and post-plasma catalysis and showed that, CH4 production took place only when CO2 hydrogenation was performed in IPC mode, only when the catalyst could directly interact with plasma discharges.671 Thereafter, the effect of Si/Al ratio and Ce addition on the performances of Ni supported Cs-USY samples in DBD plasma-assisted catalysis showed that the hydrophobicity of the surface improved the CO2 conversion while the addition of Ce favoured the formation of additional sites for CO2 adsorption and activation. Therefore, in the presence of NiCe/Cs-USY, with a power supply of 25 W, 75% CH4 yield was achieved (Table 6, entries 35 and 36).627 NTP assisted catalytic CO2 methanation over Ni catalysts (15 wt%) supported on BETA zeolite doped with La as the promoter exhibited great catalytic performance for CO2 methanation.579 The Na+ exchange in the H-BETA zeolite enabled improving the CO2 conversion by a two-fold increase (65%) and fourfold TOF values at 6.0 kV, while the addition of La allowed reaching a maximum of 85% CO2 conversion and 97% CH4 with excellent stability for 15 h (Table 6, entries 33 and 34).
 Fig. 41  
Operando time-resolved IR reactor. Reprinted with permission from ref. 662. Copyright 2017, Springer Science Business Media, LLC. 
(c) OMS-based catalysts for CO2 methanation. 
Metal supported on mesoporous molecular sieves such as MCM-41 have been receiving more and more attention as a new type of nanostructured (OMS) material.598,672,673 Different parameters have been studied to improve the catalytic properties, robustness and stability of Ni zeolite-based catalysts for CO2 methanation such as the variation of the Si/Al ratio to optimize the adsorption properties (hydrophobicity), the zeolite structure to control the diffusion and adsorption/deposition of metal precursors, the Nickel dispersion and location, the addition of noble metals and/or transition metals to enhance the reducibility and dispersion of Ni by changing the electronic and geometric structure of single crystal-metals, or cation exchange with alkali and alkaline earth metals to create basic sites and enhance the adsorption and activation of CO2. Thus, OMS based catalysts owing to the high surface area and controllable pore size should allow the diffusion of metal precursors inside the pores and the establishment of favourable metal/support interaction for controlling the nanoparticle size, dispersion and stability to prevent Ni particle sintering. However, despite the opportunities that OMS-based catalysts may offer, few studies using OMS based catalysts for CO2 methanation have been published.
A series of Ni/MCM-41 catalysts were prepared using different Ni precipitation agents and used as catalysts for CO2 methanation. AP-XPS and in situ DRIFTS studies revealed the presence of carbonyl intermediates and, the co-existence of NiO and Ni0 species with a cooperative effect for the adsorption/activation and dissociation of CO2 and H2 molecules, respectively (Fig. 42). A proposed four-step reaction mechanism involving the carbonyl pathway and the cleavage of CO bonds from CO2 as the rate-determining step was reported. In the presence of Ni(19 wt%)/MCM-41, 73.2% CO2 conversion and 91.6% CH4 selectivity, at 400 °C, were obtained and remained stable for 150 h (Table 6, entry 37).663
 Fig. 42  Proposed reaction pathway for the CO2 methanation reaction over Ni/MCM-41 catalyst. Reprinted with permission from ref. 663. Copyright 2019, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
CeO2 has been shown to effectively promote the adsorption and activation of CO2 molecules on the catalysts674,675 Moreover, CeO2 has a great oxygen exchange capacity that allows reversible release and restore of oxygen atoms. Thus, within a certain loading range, it was shown that the addition of CeO2 improved the catalytic performance of Ni/MCM-41. Indeed, the incorporation of CeO2 allowed the stabilization of Ni species, with higher dispersion and reduction of Ni species, whereas excess of CeO2 induced Ni coverage and a decrease in the catalytic performance (Fig. 43). Thus, Ni–20CeO2/MCM-41 showed the best catalytic performance, with 85.6% CO2 conversion and 99.8% CH4 selectivity at 380 °C, which remained constant for 30 h (Table 6, entries 38 and 39).676
 Fig. 43  Benefits of the addition of CeO2 over the dispersion and stabilization of Ni particles and over the catalytic performance. Reprinted with permission from ref. 676. Copyright 2017, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
The catalytic performances of Ni-mesoporous SBA-15 and MCM-41 with and without CeO2 as the promoter were also compared. The H2-TPR study showed two main peaks at T < 500 °C and T > 500 °C mainly attributed to NiO species weakly interacting with the support, mainly located on the external surface, and strongly interacting with the support, respectively. The higher reduction temperature was also attributed to the smaller Ni0 particle size and support-dependent, since the larger pores allow for enhanced diffusion of the metal precursor and accommodate metal species with better metal dispersions.677 On the other hand, the incorporation order, Ce after Ni, affected the reducibility of NiO because of reduced accessibility. Despite bigger Ni particles and mainly located on the external surface, ascribed to smaller pores compared to Ni/SBA-15, Ni/MCM-41 exhibited highest conversion and TOF values, attributed to a lower amount of non-reactive carbonyl species poisoning Ni0 and to the support-dependent electronic properties of Ni (stronger metal/support interactions). However, at high temperature (400 °C) the both Ni supported catalysts exhibited similar catalytic performance (Table 6, entries 40–43).678
5.5.4 Conclusion. 
Among the CO2 hydrogenation reactions, CO2 methanation is the most favoured reaction from a thermodynamic point of view.679 However, the development of new catalysts able to perform the CO2 methanation reaction at lower temperature is still required. The activation of CO2 is of paramount importance to achieve high methanation activity at lower reaction temperature, and the oxygen mobility and basic properties (strength, number) of the support are key parameters to enhance the adsorption and activation of CO2. Therefore, the improvement of metal dispersion and reducibility by the addition of promoters, the control of basic properties by cation exchange, the preparation method and the reduction conditions allow optimizing the catalytic performance of CO2 methanation catalysts. Moreover, the hydrophobicity of the catalyst surface is of paramount importance to limit the water adsorption and avoid the water inhibitory effect on CO2 adsorption (Fig. 44). Then, the zeolite framework composition was found to affect strongly the zeolite-based catalyst activity due to important changes in the catalyst–CO2 and catalyst–H2O interactions. Among the different based-zeolite and OMS catalysts reported, USY based catalysts could present great potential due to the improved stability provided by post synthesis treatments and strong metal/support interaction. For future strategies, it should be particularly important to focus on the morphology of the carrier to improve dispersion, physical isolation and protection of metal species, and mass transfer and also to avoid pore blockage due to coke formation. Therefore, new strategies will consider the use of OMS materials, hierarchical zeolites, two-dimensional zeolite nanosheets and core–shell materials to promote the amount, dispersion and stabilization of active centers and mass transfer. Moreover, for a better understanding of the active species, the mechanism of the activation and cleavage of C–O (CO2), and of the H2 activation, and the determination of reaction intermediates, computational modeling and in situ spectroscopy studies should be fundamental tools for the rational design of efficient and stable catalysts for methanation. From a commercial point of view and industrial applicability, several catalytic processes and reactor technology are available and should be translated to CO2 methanation since at laboratory scale, high conversion and CH4 selectivity close to 100% are achieved. However, the viability for large scale CO2 fixation also depends on ready availability and price of CO2 and H2 produced from waste and/or renewable sources in order to a develop sustainable process, based on heat produced from renewable (sun, wind) or by nuclear energy to move towards zero carbon footprint.
 Fig. 44  Scheme of key parameters to control the catalytic properties of CO2 methanation catalysts. 


5.6 Hydrogenation of CO2 into higher alcohols (C2+OH). 
5.6.1 Introduction. 
Higher alcohols (C2–4) present higher energy density, lower vapor pressure, and lower affinity for water than methanol and so constitute an alternative for fuel and fuel additives.680–682 Additionally, C2+OH finds applications as solvents and as platform molecules for the synthesis of intermediates for the production of chemicals and fine chemicals (Fig. 45).683,684 Currently, ethanol and isobutanol are mainly produced by fermentation that is expensive due to difficult distillation and low throughput.685–688 Other alcohols are traditionally obtained by hydration of the corresponding petroleum-derived olefins.689,690 Thus, new sustainable routes of synthesis of C2+OH using a renewable source and green energy are of great interest and the CO2 hydrogenation, although is very challengeable, offers great opportunities, as well as for mitigating CO2 emissions. Since the beginning of the 19th century, the synthesis of higher alcohols (HAS) from syngas has been studied in the presence of Rh- and Mo-based catalysts, and modified Fischer–Tropsch synthesis or methanol synthesis catalysts. From mechanistic standpoints, the HAS can be considered as a combination of Fischer–Tropsch synthesis (FTS) and methanol synthesis (MS) pathways. FTS has been defined as a surface polymerization, following a carbide-based reaction pathway where the CO dissociative adsorption produces C species subsequently hydrogenated into alkylmonomers which undergo coupling forming hydrocarbons (HCs) (Fig. 46a).689,691,692 However, this reaction pathway makes this surface polymerization process different as the adding group may be a fragment, rather than a complete molecule, and the growing chain may also have to undergo further reactions before the addition of the next fragment is possible. During MS, CO is adsorbed and hydrogenated to methanol (Fig. 46b). HAS requires that both FTS and MS processes occur closely to produce new alcohols: the alkyl species  formed by FTS should couple with adsorbed CO* to give rise to CHyCO* (CxHzCO*), which, after subsequent hydrogenation, yields alcohol (Fig. 46c).
 Fig. 45  Hydrogenation of CO2 to C2+OH and uses. 
 Fig. 46  Simplified surface reactions occurring upon (a) FTS, (b) MS and (c) HAS results as a combination of the both FTS and MS, involving a CO or CHO insertion step. Reprinted with permission from ref. 689. Copyright 2017, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Studies on HAS starting from CO2 began in the 1980s, with the studies focusing on the nature of active species and elucidation of the reaction mechanism for the development of efficient catalysts. Rh-, Cu-, Mo-, and Co-based catalysts have been mainly developed for CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH even if some works reported the activity of Pt, Ru and Pd-based catalysts.681 Modified catalysts used for FTS and MS have also been used but exhibited still low activity and selectivity under harsh reaction conditions.
Hydrogenation of CO2 to C2+OH is an exothermic reaction similar to methanol synthesis and it is a more complex process because it involves C–C coupling, carbon chain growth and termination (Scheme 55). Different mechanistic pathways have been reported in the literature, involving first the activation and CO2 hydrogenation to C1 intermediates such as CHxO, CO, and CHx.693,694 The posterior coupling C1–CHx leads to C2+ intermediates which are further hydrogenated to produce C2 hydrocarbons and oxygenates in which the nature of the catalytic active species plays a determinant role. For example, the CO2 hydrogenation to ethanol over Cu nanoparticle active centers shaped and confined into the Na-Beta zeolite framework681 described the CO2 hydrogenation to via CH3OH* at the Cu surface with subsequent coupling  to provide CH3COO* easily hydrogenated on the catalyst surface to ethanol. On the other hand, the synthesis of C2+OH has also been reported as a combination of RWGS with the CO2 dissociation into CO* and O*, and the CO* coupling with  and/or hydrogenation (Fig. 47).682,695 The reaction mechanism requires a precise match (Fig. 47) between dissociative CO bond activation, to generate CO and , and non-dissociative C–O bonds to promote chain growth and the alcohol production (Scheme 55) and not alkanes. These results showed that the reported mechanism was highly dependent on the catalysts.
 Scheme 55  CO2 hydrogenation to higher alcohols involving different elementary reaction steps. 
 Fig. 47  CO2 hydrogenation into alcohols via CO2 dissociation into CO* followed by CO* insertion into  or *CxHy over Co/CeO2 catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 696. Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. 
An efficient catalyst should have multiple active sites for controlling every reaction step over the catalyst surface for CO dissociative adsorption, CO non-dissociative adsorption, C–C bond formation and chain growth for CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH. Further computational and mechanistic studies are still needed to clarify operando the nature of the intermediates and catalytic active species to design new efficient and selective catalysts.
5.6.2 Zeolite based catalysts for hydrogenation of CO2 to C2+OH. 
(a) Rh-based catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH. 
Rh-based catalysts with promoters have been widely studied for the C2+OH synthesis from syngas, since in the presence of Rh catalysts without modification the main products are CH4 or CO. Moreover, Rh is one of the main metals used for the development of CO2 hydrogenation catalysts to higher alcohols. The ability of Rh for catalyzing both CO dissociation and CO insertion lies in the presence of adjacent Rh0–Rhn+ species related to the metal surface modification by the support and/or the presence of promoters (Li, Fe, and Co), which allows the formation of C2 oxygenates (ethanol, acetaldehyde, and acetic acid).697–702 The addition of promoters due to the electronic effect induces electronic state modification of Rh, changes the bonding mode and leads to bridge-bonded CO species favouring its breaking. Rh-supported on metal oxides modified with alkaline and alkaline earth metals or transition metals led to maximum 4.91% of C2+OH yield in the presence of the RhFeLi/TiO2 catalyst, at 250 °C, 3 MPa, GHSV = 6000 mL g−1 h−1 and CO2/H2 ratio of 1/3.693 This data indicates that hydrogenation of CO2 to C2+OH is still very challenging.
(i) Alkali cation effect in zeolite based catalysts for CO2hydrogenation to C2+OH. Alkaline metals are electron-donors due to their relatively low electronegativity, and their ability to easily release electrons to more electronegative atoms. The presence of alkaline metals leads to higher electron density on Rh, promoting an electron back-donation CO intermediates-Rh, contributing to stronger Rh–CO bonds, leading to higher activation and/or stabilization of CO. Moreover, the addition of alkali metals can block the active sites and modify the hydrogenation activity, decreasing the alkane yield. Additionally, the presence of alkali metals can allow the formation of new active species promoting the C2+OH synthesis. The adsorption mode of CO can be also modified, and the adsorption of linear versus bridged (tilted) CO has been described, favouring C–O breaking, and lowering H2 adsorption due to higher coverage surface by CO. Accordingly, the presence of Li in supported Rh catalysts was reported for CO hydrogenation to promote acetic acid703 formation, and for CO2 hydrogenation to promote ethanol formation.704 In fact, the effect of Li addition to RhY zeolite catalysts (RhY), prepared by an ion-exchange method, for CO2 hydrogenation has been explored.461 The study of the Li/Rh ratio effect revealed that as the ratio increased, the methane selectivity decreased, while the CO selectivity, in contrast, increased with the simultaneous promoted production of methanol and ethanol. Then, for a Li/Rh ratio of 10, maximum CO selectivity of 87%, promoted ethanol selectivity of ∼1.5%, and a minimum methane selectivity of 8.4% were achieved, while the total CO2 conversion decreased from ∼92% to ∼52%, at 250 °C, 3 MPa, with a CO2/H2 ratio of 1/3, and GHSV = 6000 mL g−1 h−1. When 1.8% of CO was added to the CO2 + H2, the ethanol selectivity increased up to 13%. The authors concluded that two Rh active sites existed, no-modified metal centers with reactive adsorbed CO species and Li-modified Rh active sites (Rhb), that promoted CO2 adsorption and conversion into stable adsorbed CO species. Thus, the added gas-phase CO was expected to act as a source of , preferentially adsorbed and hydrogenated on the unmodified Rh centers (Rha). Thus, stable adsorbed CO* and  formed acyl groups which were subsequently hydrogenated to ethanol (Fig. 48).
 Fig. 48  Proposed mechanism for CO2 hydrogenation to ethanol via CO2 hydrogenation into CO* and  followed by  coupling over differentiated Rh active centers confined in Li–Rh/Y zeolite.461 
(ii) Metal confinement effect in zeolite-based catalysts for CO2hydrogenation to C2+OH. In order to improve the metal sintering resistance, in the last few decades, new synthesis methods have been developed to prepare metal species embedded in zeolite crystals to obtain new materials with unique catalytic properties denoted as metal@zeolite (Fig. 49).540,705–707 The interaction between the metal species and the zeolite framework can modify the geometric and electronic structures of the subnanometric metal species and confer new catalytic features. Additionally, these advanced materials owing to the confinement effect can undergo shape-selective catalysis for reactant and product diffusion, reaction and stabilization, in contrast to traditionally supported metal nanoparticles.
 Fig. 49  Representation of metal@zeolite, (a) isolated metal sites and (b) metal nanoclusters encapsulated in the micropores. (c) Metal nanoparticles fixed in the zeolite crystals. Reprinted with permission from ref. 705. Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. 
Accordingly, the catalytic performance for CO2 hydrogenation of Rh nanoparticles embedded in Na-zeolite S-1 (Na–Rh@S-1) by a ligand-protected crystallization method, with ∼0.75 wt% Rh content and variable Na content in the range of 0.13–0.26 wt% has been reported (Fig. 50).695 The study of Na content revealed that an optimum content of close to 0.19 wt% existed, and that Na favours the coexistence of Rh0 and Rh+ pair species. Moreover, a DRIFT study supported the hypothesis that Rh0 and Rh+ pair is crucial for the formation of ethanol, since it participates in the insertion or coupling of adsorbed CO and CHx species (Fig. 51). The non-dissociative C–O character of Rh+ allowed converting CO2 into CO* and HCOO* subsequently hydrogenated to methanol, while Rh0 sites allowed dissociating CO* and its further hydrogenation into . Finally, the  coupling promoted by the Rh0 and Rh+ pair leads to ethanol. Therefore, in the presence of the 0.19Na–Rh@S-1 catalyst, 10% CO2 conversion and 24% C2H5OH selectivity were achieved, with a space-time yield (STYC2H5OH) around 72 mmol gRh−1 h−1, at 250 °C, with a H2/CO2 ratio of 3, 5 MPa and GHSV = 6000 mL g−1 h−1. Over the Rh@S-1 catalyst, 2.9% CO2 conversion was achieved while the main product was CH4 with a small amount of CH3OH. The authors concluded that both the Na+ modification of Rh sites (Rh0 and Rh+ pair species) and the confinement effect allowed shifting the nature of reaction products from methane to C2+OH.
 Fig. 50  Hydrothermal synthesis of Na–Rh@S-1 by a ligand-protected crystallization method. Reprinted with permission from ref. 695. Copyright 2021, American Chemical Society. 
 Fig. 51  CO2 hydrogenation into alcohols via CO* and  coupling over Rh0 and Rh+ pair species. Reprinted with permission from ref. 695. Copyright 2021, American Chemical Society. 
(b) Cu-based catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH. 
Cu-based catalytic performances have been largely explored and demonstrated for methanol synthesis and RWGS, and Cu is one of the main metals studied for the development of CO2 hydrogenation catalysts to produce higher alcohols. In the presence of the CuZnFeK catalyst, highest 13.5% higher alcohol yield has been achieved, at 300 °C, with a H2/CO2 ratio of 3, 6 MPa and GHSV = 5000 mL g−1 h−1.708 Accordingly, the high-performance of Cu@Na-Beta for CO2 hydrogenation to ethanol with 100% selectivity on the organic product basis in a fixed bed has been recently reported. A two-step preparation method using dry gel conversion and a crystallization procedure allowed preparing Cu@Na-Beta consisting of CuO nanoparticles (2–5 nm) embedded in crystalline particles Na-Beta zeolites, with an EtOH space-time yield of 398 mg−1 gcat−1 h−1, at 300 °C, 1.3 MPa, with GHSV = 12 L gcat−1 h−1 and a CO2/H2 ratio of 1/3.709 Characterization and theoretical studies allowed showing that specific interaction between Beta zeolite structures with enclosed copper was responsible for the superior catalytic performance of Cu@Na-Beta for CO2 hydrogenation to ethanol.
A comparison study of characterization data and catalytic activity of Cu-impregnated Na-Beta zeolite (Cu/Na-Beta), Cu-impregnated silica (Cu/SiO2), and Cu-embedded Na-ZSM-5 following the same procedure (Cu@Na-ZSM-5) was performed to clarify the catalytic mechanism and explain the superior catalytic performance of Cu@Na-Beta. The XAFS study revealed a similar Cu crystal structure for all the samples that could not explain the significant differences in the catalytic activity for CO2 hydrogenation, while the XPS study showed the presence of a small amount of electropositive Cu species only for the Cu@Na-Beta sample, probably due to specific metal/support interaction. For mechanistic insights, GC-MS monitoring for transient experiments with H2/12CO2 switching by H2/13CO2 was performed over Cu@Na-Beta and revealed a synchronous change in the 12CH313CH2OH signal, indicating the ready coupling of 13CO2 with adsorbed methyl species . Additionally, density functional and slab model calculations allowed determining that the (221) facet of copper was the most effective for CO2 hydrogenation into ethanol and selected to mimic the catalyst with three models (perfect, Cu vacancies, and O-doped surfaces on the edges). The barrier/reaction heat calculated for a kinetically favourable route of methyl formation  indicated that  formation by C–O breaking from CH3OH was easier than by CH3O* decomposition into . Moreover, the calculation of energy reaction for the coupling  into CH3COO* revealed that this step was slightly endothermic while methyl formation from methanol, and CH3COO* reduction to CH3CH2OH were exothermic reactions. These findings are consistent with the non-detection of methanol, acetic acid and formic acid as reaction products. Therefore, the authors concluded that CO2 was first hydrogenated to  at the Cu-surface that subsequently reacted with adsorbed  to form CH3COO* that cannot desorb due to the high-energy barrier but further easily hydrogenated into ethanol. Two roles were attributed to the zeolitic Na-Beta framework that allowed, on the one hand, the confinement of Cu nanoparticles with unique shapes and surface active sites for CO2 hydrogenation, and on the other hand, to impose shape constraints to the reactants in the near-surface layer of the nanoparticles to avoid the formation of by-products.
5.6.3 OMS-based catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH. 
As mentioned above, the development of Rh-based catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation is promising due to the ability of the Rh active sites to dissociate CO together with the fact that CH4 or CO is the main product over the pure Rh catalyst. These abilities open up the possibility of combining both dissociative/non-dissociative capabilities by incorporating promoters, thus balancing the number of dissociative and non-dissociative metal centers to promote  coupling. Accordingly, different Rh-MOx/MCM-41 catalysts where MOx was WOx, FeOx, MnOx, CeOx and NbOx were prepared by the co-impregnation method and used for CO2 hydrogenation, in a fixed bed at 250 °C, 3.0 MPa, GHSV = 6000 h−1 and a H2/CO2/N2 ratio of 3:1:1.710 Since the VOx promoted catalyst exhibited the highest ethanol selectivity and yield, the characterization of VOx and the effect of VOx loading were studied. When VOx loading increased, the CO2 conversion and ethanol selectivity increased and reached a maximum of up to 12 and 24%, respectively, for 0.3 wt% VOx. However, a further increase in VOx loading induced a decrease in CO2 conversion, attributed to the passive effect caused by the excessive encapsulation of Rh species by VOx species. The authors concluded that the incorporation of VOx owing to the electronic effect induced the formation of Rh+ (VOx-Rh interfacial active sites) that modified the dissociation capacity of the catalyst due to the presence of tilted CO species leading to the easy cleavage of C–O and its subsequent hydrogenation to form . The higher ethanol yield was the result of the promoted CO2 activation and CO dissociation for an adequate VOx loading. Thus, at 0.3 wt% VOx, the amount of dissociative adsorbed CO is similar to that of non-dissociative adsorbed CO, leading to the highest selectivity and ethanol yield.
5.6.4 Conclusion. 
CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH is an attractive process to obtain new building block molecules (olefins from alcohol dehydration) and energy carriers since C2+OH has higher energy densities, and lower toxicity and corrosiveness than methanol. But, if CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH has been widely studied in the last decades, very few studies focusing on the development of hydrogenation catalysts based on zeolites or OMS have been reported besides the benefits of confinement effect, nanoparticle stabilization by electronic features and shape-selectivity (reactant, product, and transition state). Accordingly, if the best yield for heterogeneous catalysts has been achieved over the RhFeLi/TiO2 catalyst with 4.91% C2+OH yield, maximum 100% ethanol selectivity on the organic product basis has been reached over the Cu@Na-Beta catalyst.
However, high yield and selectivity of C2+OH remain a major challenge and could be achieved by ab initio design of the catalysts, introducing the necessary active sites, which can be rationalized and simulated by computational chemistry. Therefore, additional thorough mechanistic studies, based on operando analysis and characterization of the catalysts, reactants, products and intermediates are required to understand the reaction pathways and the nature of active sites to generate fundamental knowledge. Thus, the selectivity could be improved towards selected alcohol (C2–4), controlling the nature and number of active sites with dissociative, non-dissociative and propagation chain abilities. Actually, up to now, different mechanisms based on products, by-products and intermediate formations detected by DRIFT studies have been proposed and are highly dependent on the catalyst. Moreover, the cost and stability of the catalyst has to be optimized, and the metal choice is of paramount importance. In the case of the few reported works for OMS and zeolite based catalysts, both Cu and Rh exhibited high abilities for CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH, nevertheless, considering catalyst costs, Cu seems more indicated for industrial application. It is clear that zeolites and OMS based catalysts are good candidates for the development of selective and stable catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH, and which have so far been under-considered.


5.7 Hydrogenation of CO2 to C2+. 
5.7.1 Introduction. 
The production of hydrocarbons, aromatics, and olefins via catalytic hydrogenation of CO2 is an attractive route to produce fuels (C2+) and pave the way to CO2 circularity. In comparison to C1 products, C2+ products possess higher energy densities. Light olefins such as ethylene, propylene, and butylene are the top-produced petrochemicals worldwide and constitute important raw materials for the chemical industry and the production of plastics, fibers, and chemicals. They are generally produced through the hydrocracking of hydrocarbons of fossil sources under high temperature. Aromatics also constitute important platform chemicals for the synthesis of petrochemicals, polymers, and medicines. They are usually produced by oil thermal cracking and naphtha reforming. The alternative strategy to produce gasoline, olefins and aromatics from CO2 is an important way to mitigate greenhouse gas emissions, develop new sustainable routes for the production of value-added chemicals and reduce dependence on fossil sources (Fig. 52).
 Fig. 52  Hydrogenation of CO2 to C2+ and uses. 
The production of C2+ from CO2 hydrogenation involves a cascade process consisting of a first step of production of CO intermediates by RWGS or MeOH (DME), followed by Fisher Tropsch synthesis (FTS) or methanol to hydrocarbon route (MTH), respectively, comprising oligomerization, cracking and aromatization reactions (Scheme 56).
 Scheme 56  CO2 hydrogenation to liquid fuels, olefins and aromatics by the modified FTS or methanol mediated route. 
The main drawback for C2+ hydrocarbon production is the kinetic barriers for C–C coupling. Therefore, the great advantage of combining RWGS with FTS or methanol synthesis and conversion to hydrocarbons is the thermodynamics of the overall process.88,711
RWGS is an endothermic process, whereas the FTS reaction is exothermic, which makes the RWGS-FTS tandem thermodynamically favorable (Scheme 56). Thus, the equilibrium of the RWGS reaction can be shifted due to the consumption of CO by the FTS for hydrocarbon production. Similarly, for the methanol-mediated route, the hydrogenation of CO2 to methanol is exothermic and thermodynamically limited at high temperature, and kinetically limited at low temperature. However, the combination of methanol synthesis with conversion to hydrocarbons allows shifting the equilibrium due to methanol consumption.
Different catalysts (metals, metal oxides, metal carbides, MOFs, and zeolites) that have been applied for the synthesis of hydrocarbons from synthesis gas or methanol to olefins or aromatics (MTO/MTA) have also been tested for the hydrogenation of CO2 to hydrocarbons via the tandem process. Different strategies have been followed to optimize the catalytic properties and reaction conditions to enhance CO2 activation, decrease the energy barrier for C–C coupling and minimize the CO and methane yields. Therefore, the effects of the addition of alkali promoters, addition of iron to enhance CO2 adsorption and increase the surface CO/H2 ratio, addition of a second metal to improve strong interactions between the support and active phase and modify the electronic environment of active species, the modification of acid–base properties, for example to weaken the secondary hydrogenation ability of olefins or to avoid undesirable alkylation and isomerization have been explored.
Usually, hydrogenation of CO2 over Fe and Co based catalysts produces CO as the intermediates while over Cu/Zn catalysts methanol is the main intermediate. Only Fe, Co, Ni, and Ru of group VIII exhibit sufficiently high activities for the hydrogenation of CO to warrant their use as effective FT catalysts and among them, only Co- and Fe-based catalysts may be considered as practical FT catalysts, while Fe-based catalysts remain the preferred for commercial FT synthesis.712
The synthesis of C2+ products through the CO2 hydrogenation process is a combination of MeOH or CO production in a first step, in the presence of a multi-center metal-based catalyst, and of a second step involving oligomerization, cracking and aromatization processes in which the zeolite-based catalysts have played a key role. In the literature, recent reviews described the catalytic performance of metal-based solid catalysts, and zeolites owing to their unique and tunable properties have been widely applied for both their carrier and chemical properties.82,83,85–87 The combination of zeolites and metal sites in the presence of metal nanoparticles or metallic oxides offers great opportunities for the conversion of CO2 into liquid fuels, olefins and aromatics. The presence of targeted metal active species allows the activation and conversion of CO2 to intermediate CO or methanol (DME), while zeolites, due to their tunable acidity and pore structure, allow controlling and directing the nature and distribution of the products, as illustrated in Fig. 53. Accordingly the combination of NaFe2O3, ZSM-5 and SAPO-11 with defined pores and acidic sites is responsible for enhancing the isomerization activity and improving the composition of isoparaffins for direct CO2 hydrogenation (Fig. 54).713
 Fig. 53  Effect of zeolite pore size and structure over the nature and distribution of the direct CO2 hydrogenation products. Reprinted with permission from ref. 83. Copyright 2022, Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
 Fig. 54  Effect of combination of NaFe2O3, ZSM-5 and SAPO-11 for enhancing the isomerization and aromatization activity and improving the composition of isoparaffins and aromatics for direct CO2 hydrogenation. Reprinted with permission from ref. 713. Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. 
Since excellent and comprehensive reviews80,82–84 reported the recent advances in the direct CO2 hydrogenation to liquid fuels, olefins and aromatics this section describes the major achievements and proposes some future research approaches.
5.7.2 Direct hydrogenation of CO2 to fuels over bifunctional catalytic systems incorporating zeolites.. 
(a) Direct hydrogenation of CO2via modified FTS. 
The direct hydrogenation of CO2via modified FTS produces a mixture of hydrocarbons with broad distributions that can be adjusted by optimizing the morphology and structure of FT catalysts by controlling the metal active species (nanoparticle size, crystalline phase and exposed facets) and the properties of the zeolite (acidity, pore size and structure) and the addition of promoters. Accordingly, zeolites have shown to exhibit high activity for hydrocracking and isomerization of hydrocarbons and have been widely used.714,715
The direct hydrogenation of CO2via modified FTS involves the hydrogenation of CO2 to CO via the RWGS reaction, followed by hydrogenation of CO to hydrocarbons by FT synthesis. The most active catalysts reported for RWGS are Fe-based being Fe3O4 the active phase, while iron carbide (Fe5C2) have been reported as the most active catalysts for FT synthesis.716–718
Therefore, the main challenge is controlling the chain growth via C–C coupling on metal surfaces. The FTS products have been ascribed to the polymerization reaction and thus product distribution is determined by the Anderson–Schulz–Flory (ASF) distribution. According to the ASF model, the molar fraction (Mn) of a hydrocarbon product with a carbon number of n depends on the chain growth probability (α), which is given by the following equation: Mn = (1 − α)αn−1 where α is determined by the rates of chain growth (rp) and chain termination (rt), α = rp/(rp + rt).82,712 The modification of the catalyst composition and structure plays a key role in the chain growth value and in turn in the hydrocarbon product distribution (Fig. 55). However, the selectivity for specific hydrocarbons C2+ range is limited, and maximum 58%, 48%, 41% and 40% selectivities for C2–C4, C5–C11, C12–C20 and C21+ were predicted, respectively.
 Fig. 55  Hydrocarbon products distribution versus chain growth probability (α). Reprinted with permission from ref. 82. Copyright 2019, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
A recent study revealed the high catalytic performance of the multifunctional Na–Fe3O4/H-ZSM-5 catalyst for the CO2 reduction to C5–C11 (gasoline) with total hydrocarbon selectivity of up to 78%, low 4% methane selectivity at 22% CO2 conversion, with H2/CO2 = 3, at 320 °C, 3 MPa and WHSV = 4 L h−1 gcat−1 (Table 7, entry 1).719 The cooperation of the three types of active sites properly spaced, Fe3O4 (to catalyze RWGS), Fe5C2 (to catalyze FTS) and acid sites (to catalyze oligomerization, isomerization and aromatization), allowed catalytic conversion of CO2 to gasoline through cascade reactions (Fig. 56). Moreover, the multifunctional catalyst exhibited stable activity for 1000 h on TOS. These results showed the potential of the catalytic system based on zeolite for industrial application for the CO2 conversion to liquid fuels.

 Fig. 56  CO2 hydrogenation reaction over the Na–Fe3O4/zeolite multifunctional catalyst. Reprinted with permission from ref. 719. Copyright 2017, Jian Wei et al. 
Another study showed the effect of the pore size and structure over the isoparaffin/aromatic ratio in the gasoline-range hydrocarbons (Fig. 57). The catalytic performance of the catalytic system based on the MFI structure, with different Si/Al ratios, in the presence of Na–Fe3O4/HZSM-5(27), Na–Fe3O4/HZSM-5(160) and Na–Fe3O4/HZSM-5(300) produced a higher amount of aromatics (up to 61% of aromatics), while when the process was carried out over Na–Fe3O4/HMCM-22 (MWW structure), isoparaffins were the main hydrocarbon products (46% of isoparaffins). This last result was attributed to 10 MR pore openings with a lamellar structure consisting of two independent pore systems of the MWW structure with demonstrated catalytic properties for isomerization, alkylation and disproportionation.720 Additionally, a study of the adequate distance of the different active sites showed the effects over the selectivity of hydrocarbon products. When Na–Fe3O4 and HZSM-5 powders were mixed, the closest proximity of iron-based sites and acid sites led to the acid site poisoning by Na. However, when Na–Fe3O4 and HZSM-5 were combined by granule mixing, the adequate distance between iron-based and zeolite acid sites allowed the formation of olefin intermediates on iron-based sites and its diffusion through the pores of the zeolite towards the acid sites, where oligomerization, isomerization and aromatization reactions occurred. Therefore, maximum 73% C5–C11 selectivity at 34% CO2 conversion was achieved. Nevertheless, when the hydrogenation process was carried out in the dual-bed configuration with HZSM-5 packed below Na–Fe3O4 and separated by a thin layer of inert quartz sand, lower 67% C5–C11 selectivity (67%) at 34% CO2 conversion were reached and attributed to the larger distance of active sites.
 Fig. 57  CO2 hydrogenation reaction over Na–Fe3O4/zeolite multifunctional catalysts, with H2/CO2 = 3, at 320 °C, 3 MPa and WHSV = 4 L h−1 gcat−1. Reprinted with permission from ref. 719. Copyright 2017, Jian Wei et al. 
A recent work demonstrated the critical role of the structure and pore size of the zeolite over isoparaffin selectivity.713 Indeed, the combination of SAPO-11 and ZSM-5 with Na/Fe3O4 catalysts allowed enhancing the long-chain-hydrocarbon selectivity by performing oligomerization on the shorter chains, while the isomerization ability favored long-chain hydrocracking along with aromatization (Fig. 58). A further study of configuration of the different catalysts showed that the triple-bed system with first the Na/Fe3O4 catalyst followed sequentially by SAPO-11 and ZSM-5 allowed maximizing isoparaffin selectivity. Likewise, 71.7% gasoline selectivity was achieved with 38.2% isoparaffin selectivity, at a CO2 conversion of 31.2% (Fig. 58; Table 7, entry 2).
 Fig. 58  Effect of the combination of ZSM-5 and SAPO-11 on the isomerization and hydrocracking abilities over the formation of paraffins and aromatics. Reprinted with permission from ref. 713. Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. 
(b) Direct hydrogenation of CO2via the methanol mediated route. 
Direct hydrogenation of CO2via the methanol mediated route was performed over a bifunctional catalyst constituted by reducible In2O3 and H-ZSM-5, reaching high 78.6% gasoline-range (C5+) and low 1% methane selectivity, at 13.1% CO2 conversion.721 Since direct hydrogenation of CO2via the methanol mediated route usually occurs over Cu/Zn catalysts,434,527 the catalytic performance of In2O3/H-ZSM-5 was compared to that of Cu–Zn–Al/H-ZSM-5 and Cu–Zn–Al–Zr/H-ZSM-5 bifunctional catalysts. When typical Cu–Zn–Al/H-ZSM-5 and Cu–Zn–Al–Zr/H-ZSM-5 catalysts were used, the main product was CO with higher CH4 selectivity in the hydrocarbon distribution, similarly to other reported studies combining Cu–Zn, Cu–Zn–Zr and Cu–Zn–Cr oxides with zeolite (HY, H-Beta, SAPO-5, and SAPO-44).722,723 The high activity of In2O3 for the direct hydrogenation of CO2 to methanol was previously reported,724 and the combination of In2O3 with H-ZSM-5 allowed reaching a high C5+ hydrocarbons yield and selectivity (Fig. 59), at 340 °C, with H2/CO2 = 3 and 3.0 MPa, with good catalytic stability along 150 h of TOS. These results were attributed to the higher stability of intermediates involved in the CH3OH synthesis on the defective In2O3 surface with surface oxygen vacancies than on the Cu surface which strongly suppressed the formation of CO.723 On the other hand, the catalytic performance of the In2O3/H-ZSM-5 bifunctional catalyst was higher than one of other reducible metal oxides combined with H-ZSM-5 such as Ga2O3, Fe2O3, ZnO–Cr2O3 and ZnO–ZrO2 combined with a higher activity and C5+ selectivity (Table 7, entries 3–7).
 Fig. 59  CO2 hydrogenation over bifunctional Cu- and In2O3-based HZSM-5 catalysts with different mass ratios, at 340 °C, 3.0 MPa, 9 L h−1 gcat−1, H2/CO2/N2 = 73/24/3, and MeOH conversion over HZSM-5 (■ C5+, ■ C2–4, ■ CH4). Reprinted with permission from ref. 721. 2017, Nature Publishing Group. 
A hydrocarbon-pool mechanism was proposed for methanol conversion into hydrocarbons inside HZSM-5. First, CO2 is hydrogenated into methanol at the oxygen vacancies on the In2O3 surface. Methanol further diffused inside the pore of the zeolite at the acidic site where it is transformed to hydrocarbons after oligomerization, isomerization and aromatization reactions (Fig. 60).
 Fig. 60  Proposed mechanism for the formation of CH3OH at the oxygen vacancies on the In2O3 surface and further transformation to hydrocarbons at the acidic sites inside the pores of HZSM-5. Reprinted with permission from ref. 721. 2017, Nature Publishing Group. 
A study of integration manner of the active components revealed, as was observed in other works, that the hydrocarbon selectivity and distribution depended on the distance of the different active sites. When the hydrogenation process was performed in a dual-bed configuration, with HZSM-5 packed above the In2O3, a high methane selectivity of 66.3% was achieved. However, when In2O3 was packed above and HZSM-5, methanol was completely converted into hydrocarbons with 70.4% C5+ selectivity and 4.5% methane selectivity. Finally, a closer disposition of the active sites allowed suppressing undesired RWGS and maximized C5+ (78.6%).
Finally, experiments using the pellet catalyst were performed to show that tail-gas recycling, commonly used in industry for a more efficient utilization of the feed, allowed further improving the catalytic performance, with an increase of both CO2 conversion from 8.7 to 18.2% and C5+ selectivity from 78.6 to 84.1%, while CO selectivity decreased from 44.5 to 30%. These results showed the great potential of bifunctional catalysts based on zeolite for industrial applications.
5.7.3 Direct hydrogenation of CO2 to olefins over the bifunctional catalytic system incorporating zeolites via the methanol mediated route. 
The catalytic performance of ZnO-ZrO2/SAPO-34 bifunctional catalysts with high selectivity for the syngas conversion to lower olefins have been explored for the hydrogenation of CO2 to lower olefins (Table 8, entry 1).723 80% C2–C4 olefin selectivity was achieved with a low 3% methane selectivity at 12.6% CO2 conversion, at 380 °C, 2 MPa and H2/CO2 = 3. The comparison of the catalytic performances for CO2 hydrogenation of ZnO–ZrO2 and ZnO–ZrO2/SAPO-34 allowed concluding that hydrogenation over a tandem catalyst was not a simple sum of the two individual reactions but rather, a coupling reaction.725 Thus, if thermodynamics of CO2 hydrogenation is unfavorable, the conversion of methanol to lower olefins is thermodynamically favorable as well as the global process due to the coupling of both reactions. CO2 and H2 molecules were activated on ZnZrO, while the C–C coupling occurred on SAPO. Tandem catalysis improved thermodynamics and kinetics coupling through the transferring and migrating of the CHxO intermediate species, not methanol, which promoted the efficient conversion of CO2 to lower olefins (Fig. 61).
