 Fig. 61  Proposed reaction mechanism of CO2 hydrogenation on the tandem ZnZrO2/SAPO-34 catalyst. Reprinted with permission from ref. 725. Copyright 2017, American Chemical Society. 
Recently, the effect of the proximity of the different active sites of the tandem catalyst on the catalytic performance was demonstrated when the CO2 hydrogenation was carried out with both catalysts integrated in different configurations: in a dual-fixed bed or physical mixtures of metal oxides and SAPO-34 powders or granules and with or without quartz sand as the separator. Optimum catalytic results were obtained for physical mixtures of metal oxides and SAPO-34 powders offering a suitable distance between the different active sites to perform tandem catalysis.
Similarly, the catalytic performance of the bifunctional catalyst In2O3–ZrO2/SAPO-34 was explored due to the high catalytic activity of In2O3724 for the hydrogenation of CO2 to methanol.726 A lower olefin selectivity of 80% with only 4% methane selectivity was achieved with hydrocarbon product distribution different from classical ASF distribution (Table 8, entry 2). The bifunctional catalyst exhibited good stability of over 150 h of TOS, opening up opportunities for industrial applications.
A bifunctional ZnGa2O4/SAPO-34 catalyst, ZnGa2O4 with a spinel structure, was also explored for the hydrogenation of CO2 to lower olefins.727 A lower olefin selectivity of 86%, at 13% CO2 conversion with 1% methane selectivity was achieved (Table 8, entry 3). A study of correlation of the rates of formation of CH3OH/DME (Zn–Ga–O) and lower olefins (C=2–C=4) (Zn–Ga–O/SAPO-34) with the density of oxygen vacancies demonstrated their fundamental role in the activation and selective conversion of CO2 to methanol/DME on the Zn–Ga–O surfaces and their subsequent transformation at the acid sites of SAPO to lower olefins (Fig. 62). On the other hand, the in situ IR study allowed to propose a mechanism for the activation of H2 on the –Ga–O– and –Zn–O-pairs and formation of H species (H*) for the conversion of CO2 to CH3O* species further converted to methanol and DME which after diffusion through the SAPO-34 pores towards the acid sites selectively form lower olefins.
 Fig. 62  (a) Correlation of the rates of formation of CH3OH/DME (Zn–Ga–O) and lower olefins (C=2–C=4) (Zn–Ga–O/SAPO-34) with the density of oxygen vacancies. (b) Proposed reaction mechanism of CO2 hydrogenation on the tandem ZnGa2O4/SAPO-34 catalyst. Reprinted with permission from ref. 727. Copyright 2018, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
5.7.4 Direct hydrogenation of CO2 to aromatics over the bifunctional catalytic system incorporating zeolites. 
(a) Direct hydrogenation of CO2 to aromatics via the methanol mediated route. 
The selective hydrogenation of CO2 to gasoline and olefins has been successfully explored and achieved. Nevertheless, the selective hydrogenation of CO2 to aromatics remains challenging due to the high unsaturation degree and complex structures of aromatics.
Recently, an attempt of conversion of CO2 to aromatics in the presence of a bifunctional catalyst comprising nano-scaled spinel structural ZnAlOx and H-ZSM-5 was reported.728 A characterization study was performed to determine the active sites and the combination mode of both catalysts. The TPR results showed that both ZnAlOx and ZnO can be hardly reduced by H2 below 430 °C and 7% less zinc oxides could be reduced above 730 °C. Therefore, it was concluded that Zn2+ is responsible for the activation of CO2 hydrogenation. The IR study of absorption of DTBPy (2,6-di-tert-butyl-pyridine absorption) to investigate the external surface Brønsted acid sites that are able to catalyze the hydrogenation of CO2 to paraffins and not to aromatics revealed that the characteristic bands at 3365, 1614 and 1531 cm−1 disappeared after mixing, grinding and pressing under high pressure with ZnAlOx. These results indicate that the external Brønsted acids of H-ZSM-5 are blocked by ZnAlOx after mixing, which is beneficial for the aromatization process, and which was demonstrated by the catalytic performance recorded with different combinations of both ZnAlOx and H-ZMSM-5 catalysts (Fig. 63). The aromatics or C2–4 olefin selectivity follows the order ZnAlOx&H-ZSM-5 > ZnAlOx + H-ZSM-5 > ZnAlOx/H-ZSM-5, in contrast to C2–4 paraffin selectivity that follows the opposite order. Therefore, when CO2 hydrogenation was performed over ZnAlOx&H-ZSM-5 a high aromatics selectivity of 73.9% with low 0.4% methane selectivity was achieved. Among aromatics, a selectivity of 58.1% is achieved for p-xylene, while CO selectivity was 57.4% (Table 9, entry 1). The proposed mechanism involves the formation of methanol and dimethyl ether by hydrogenation of formate species formed on the ZnAlOx surface, which diffuse to H-ZSM-5 at acid sites where they are successively converted to olefins and aromatics.
 Fig. 63  Comparisons of the CO2 conversion and product selectivity over various combination of ZnAlOx and H-ZMS-5 catalysts, at 320 °C, 3 MPa, WHSV = 6 L h−1 gcat−1, H2/CO2/Ar = 3/1/0.2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 728. Copyright 2018, Youming Ni et al.

Although good catalytic performance has been reported for the hydrogenation of CO2 to hydrocarbons, especially, the methanol-mediated route allows achieving high aromatic selectivity within the hydrocarbon products in the presence of bifunctional catalysts composed of metal oxides and acid zeolites, the CO selectivity is still too high.
ZnO–ZrO2/H-ZSM-5 bifunctional catalysts that have exhibited high catalytic performance to convert selectively syngas to aromatics80,729 have also been explored for the direct hydrogenation of CO2 to aromatics. Accordingly, recently, the catalytic performance of a composite consisting of ZnZrOx and chain-like nanocrystal HZSM-5 was explored.730 A series of novel HZSM-5 nanocrystal clusters with chain-like morphology and chain length ranging from 0.16 to 1.41 μm were prepared through the incorporation of different amounts of n-octyltrimethoxysilane. The samples were denoted according to their chainlike morphology length Z5-0.16, Z5-0.21, Z5-0.73, and Z5-1.41. When the CO2 hydrogenation was carried out over the ZnZrOx the main products were methanol (53.8%) and CO (41.5%). The use of physical mixtures of ZnZrOx/HZSM-5 zeolites allowed the formation of aromatics with selectivity up to 75% (Fig. 64). Moreover, the passivation of external surface acid sites of HZSM-5 (with TPAOH) together with the b-axis length allowed enhancing the p-xylene selectivity up to 28.9%, while shortening the length of b-axis favored tetramethylbenzene (TeMB) production with 74.1% selectivity (Table 9, entry 2). The characterization study showed that CO2 activation at the surface oxygen vacancies of ZnZrOx enable the hydrogenation to CHxO species (formate, formaldehyde, methoxy, and methanol), which are further transformed into aromatics at the acid sites of HZSM-5. A high aromatic selectivity of 76% at 17.5% CO2 conversion, with a lower CO selectivity of 23.8% over the ZnZrOx/HZSM-5 were achieved owing to the directional component distribution in the bifunctional catalyst (Fig. 65). These results showed that the control of the morphology and the location of the active sites allowed directing the selectivity of the reaction towards the desired products.
 Fig. 64  Comparisons of the CO2 conversion and product selectivity over various combination of ZnZrOx and H-ZMS-5 catalysts, at 315 °C, 3 MPa, H2/CO2/N2 = 72/24/4, F = 17 mL min−1. Reprinted with permission from ref. 730. Copyright 2021 Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
 Fig. 65  Selective hydrogenation of CO2 to aromatics over ZnZrOx and chain-like nanocrystal H-ZMS-5 catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 730. Copyright 2021, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
Previously, direct CO2 conversion was carried out over the ZnZrO/ZSM-5 bifunctional catalyst. Tandem catalysis is thermodynamically favorable and enables the selective formation of aromatics through the coupling of CO2 hydrogenation over ZnZrO to CHxO species and aromatics formation over H-ZSM-5. A high aromatic selectivity of 70–73% at 9–14% CO2 conversion was reached and the CO selectivity was lowered to 42–44% (Table 9, entries 3 and 4).731,732
The main drawback of the direct conversion of CO2 to aromatics is the high CO selectivity (>40%) produced by the RWGS reaction. Therefore, an attempt to increase the oxygen vacancy content has been recently considered in order to promote CO2 activation and hydrogenation into stable intermediate species and lower RWGS.733 Indium oxide has been shown to contribute to the formation of vacancy-rich Zn5In2O8 oxide734 that in turn could contribute to the formation of stable methanol intermediate species. Thus, a series of highly efficient bifunctional catalysts consisting of physical mixtures of InZnZrOx solid solution and nano-ZSM-5 zeolite were used as bifunctional catalysts to convert CO2 into aromatics. A high aromatic selectivity of 90.6% at 13.8% CO2 conversion was achieved with a low 19.8% CO selectivity. These results were attributed to the formation of a high amount of oxygen vacancies on InZnZrOx surfaces, which directly improve the CO2 activation and the formation of CHxO intermediates, to the synergistic effect of hydrogenation and acid properties of InZnZrOx and H-ZSM-5, respectively, together with the improved mass transfer properties of nanostructured ZSM-5 zeolite for further conversion to aromatics (Table 9, entry 5).
The conversion of syngas to p-xylene has also been successfully performed over a hybrid catalyst, consisting of ZnCr2O4 spinel and core–shell structured zeolite.735 Accordingly, the direct conversion of CO2 to aromatics has been checked over a bifunctional catalyst composed of Cr2O3 and H-ZSM-5 zeolite.736 A high aromatic selectivity of 76% with 11.4% CO selectivity was achieved at 34.5% CO2 conversion over the bifunctional Cr2O3/H-ZSM-5 catalyst that exhibited a high TOS of over 100 h (Table 9, entry 6).736 A study of the effects of the proximity of the different types of active sites revealed that conversion of CO2 to aromatics was optimum for the closest proximity between Cr2O3 and ZSM-5 (Fig. 66, mode a). A prolonged distance between the oxide and zeolite (modes b and c) was prejudicial for the formation of aromatics. A further modification of the zeolite catalyst to passivate the external acid site allowed enhancing the selectivity towards BTX (benzene, toluene, and xylene) avoiding undesirable alkylation and isomerization. For this, a core–shell structured zeolite capsule catalyst was fabricated using H-ZSM-5 as the core and neutral silicalite-1 (S-1) as the shell. Thus, a higher BTX selectivity of 43.6% and PX selectivity of 25.3% were achieved over the Cr2O3/H-ZSM-5@S-1 catalyst (Fig. 67).
 Fig. 66  Selective hydrogenation of CO2 to aromatics over Cr2O3/ZSM-5 catalysts. (a) Physical mixing; (b) granule mixing; (c) dual bed. Reaction conditions: 350 °C, 3 MPa, H2/CO2 = 3, GHSV = 1.2 L h−1 gcat−1. Reprinted with permission from ref. 736. Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. 
 Fig. 67  Selective hydrogenation of CO2 to aromatics over Cr2O3/ZSM-5 and Cr2O3/H-ZSM-5@S-1 (a) fractions of products in aromatics, (b) scheme of highly selective production of BTX over Cr2O3/H-ZSM-5@S-1 catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 736. Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. 
A similar strategy was followed to prepare a bifunctional catalyst based on core–shell structured Zn-doped HZSM-5 (Zn-ZSM-5@SiO2) and a Cr2O3 oxide.737 A control of the acidity of Zn-ZSM-5@SiO2 allowed improving the para-xylene selectivity up to 38.7% at 22.1% CO2 conversion (Table 9, entries 7 and 8).
More recently, an efficient solvent-free method was reported for the preparation of a capsule-like zeolite catalyst (HZSM-5@silicalite-1-S) for the selective hydrogenation of CO2 to para-xylene. A study of the effects of the configuration modes of Na–FeMn and HZSM-5@S1-S core–shell zeolite over the catalytic performances was performed.738 The best catalytic performance was achieved for the granule-mixing 2.83Na–FeMn (90/10) and HZSM-5@S1-S zeolite without quartz stand, with 17% aromatic selectivity and 75.4% para-xylene (among xylenes). This was attributed to the efficient mass transfer of intermediates to the micropores of HZSM-5@S1. After optimization of the Na–FeMn and HZSM-5@S1-S ratio (1/2), maximum 81.1% para-xylene (among xylenes) was achieved, which was higher than ones achieved over above described core–shell zeolite (HZSM-5@SiO2 and HZSM-5@S-1-S) (Fig. 68 and Table 9, entry 9).
 Fig. 68  Selective hydrogenation of CO2 to aromatics over Na–FeMn and HZSM-5@S1-S with different iron/zeolite mass ratios (granule-mixing). Reprinted with permission from ref. 738. Copyright 2021, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
(b) Direct hydrogenation of CO2 to aromatics via modified FTS. 
Iron-based catalysts have been widely studied for the catalytic conversion of CO2via modified FTS due to its properties to catalyze both the RWGS and FTS reactions.739,740 Under working reaction conditions, iron based catalysts exhibit Fe3O4 active sites for RWGS and Fe5C2 active sites for FTS.741 Moreover, the addition of alkali metals such as K and Na allowed improving the effectiveness of iron catalysts owing to improved surface carburization and weakened secondary hydrogenation ability of olefins.742 On the other hand, the addition of Zn allowed promoting FTS, catalyst stability and light olefin selectivity owing to the formation of the ZnFe2O4 spinel phase and the ZnO phase, which induced an increase of the surface area, improved the Fe–Zn interaction, and modified the redox and adsorption properties.743 Combining all of the above, a series of Fe–Zn catalysts promoted by K were prepared and combined with ZSM-5 zeolites for the direct conversion of CO2 to olefins and aromatics.744 Moreover, to improve aromatic selectivity, the morphology of H-ZSM-5 zeolites was modified by treatment with NaOH solution, which could form a hollow structure in the zeolite channel and thus reduce the residence time of aromatic intermediates in the channel, which not only improved the aromatic yield, but also prevented the over-hydrogenation of hydrocarbons. Thus, 45.2% aromatic selectivity was achieved at 42.6% CO2 conversion over K–3Fe/Zn–H-ZSM-5 (Table 9, entry 10). These results showed that an efficient bifunctional catalyst can be rationally designed and constructed by modifying the component composition and zeolite morphology to directly and selectively convert CO2 to aromatics.
Similarly, the catalytic performance of the tandem catalyst system comprising Na/Fe and HZSM-5 catalysts was explored for the direct and selective production of aromatics from CO2.745 An aromatic selectivity of 94% from hydrocarbon products was reached over under industrially relevant conditions, with para-xylene selectivity up to 70% for Na/Fe and SiO2-coated HZSM-5 zeolite bifunctional catalysts (Table 9, entries 11 and 12). The results of the experiments and DFT calculations revealed that the Na-promoted Fe catalyst and HZSM-5 with high Brønsted acidity are key factors for enhancing the CO2 conversion and aromatic synthesis, while the SiO2-coated HZSM-5 zeolite allowed suppressing the isomerization of the initially formed p-xylene and p-ethyltoluene in the zeolite channels when they diffuse out of the channels onto the external SiO2-coated surface (Fig. 69).
 Fig. 69  Reaction routes for CO2 hydrogenation to aromatics over the composite Na/Fe-HZSM-5 and Na/Fe–SiO2-coated HZSM-5 catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 745. Copyright 2019, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
A prolonged 100 h test on the composite catalyst system showed that the Na/Fe catalyst exhibited good activity stability for both RWGS and FTS reactions, with CO2 conversion and CO to CO selectivity almost stable and around 30% and 27%, respectively. Unfortunately, the content of aromatics within the liquid hydrocarbons decreased from 91.6% to 70.3%, as well as the selectivity to p-xylene in xylenes, which decreased from 69% to 57%. The decrease in aromatics was attributed to the coke formation over coated-HZSM-5 zeolite with time on stream that implies lower activity for aromatization due to the lower amount of acid active sites, which led to the gradual increase of non-aromatic hydrocarbon products.
5.7.5 Conclusion. 
CO2 hydrogenation to gasoline, olefins and aromatics offers new opportunities to convert CO2 into value-added products and pave the path for CO2 circularity. In the last decade, significant advances in the development of tandem catalysts based on metal oxides and zeolites have been made for the selective hydrogenation of CO2 to lower olefins (C2–C4), gasoline (C5–C11) and aromatics (C6–C8) of wide interest for the chemical industry.
The high activity of heterogeneous catalysts is usually attributed to the synergy between the active sites and the support and/or the promoter. Nevertheless, the nature of the active sites responsible for the activation of CO2 and H2 and the intermediate species and reaction mechanisms are still elusive. Undoubtedly, the systematic study and understanding of the reaction mechanism, kinetics, thermodynamics, determination of reaction intermediates and active species due to the advantages of computational chemistry, and in situ and operando spectroscopy should allow a great understanding, to help to rationalize experimental results and thus the design of new efficient tandem metal oxide/zeolite catalysts and processes.
Besides the hydrogenation of CO and CO2 on metal oxide surfaces following the same mechanism involving formate and methoxide species, the cofeeding of CO2 and CO offers also an interesting strategy to lower CO formation by RWGS and maximize the yield and selectivity to hydrocarbon products.
Moreover, the hydrogenation of CO2 to hydrocarbons requires further development and improvement of catalyst activity, selectivity and stability for industrial application. A long-duration test should be performed to determine the catalyst stability as well as regeneration. Since CO2 conversion remains low, study of recycling is necessary. In the case of gasoline and aromatics, the separation should be easier, which for lower olefins would be a challenging issue.
In the same way, improvement of process selectivity is still required and challenging and expensive separation step should be avoided. Thus, new studies for the specific modifications of zeolite morphology, such as pore-size tuning and the poisoning of external acid sites, should allow controlling the hydrocarbon product selectivity.
As mentioned above, CO2 large-scale transformation will depend on CO2 availability and so on the capture technique, and H2 production that will be green to ensure a CO2-neutral process and a sustainable effective approach to the production of hydrocarbons. Undoubtedly, the future tandem catalyst for direct hydrogenation of CO2 to C2+ will be based on zeolite-type materials.

6 Syngas
On the path to greenhouse gas mitigation, the CO2 transformation into syngas constitutes a winning approach towards the sustainable production of chemicals and fuels. Further syngas conversion into MeOH or hydrocarbons allows reducing the CO2 emission and is a direct route for the production of fuels and chemicals through technologies already explored, developed and implemented (Fig. 70).
 Fig. 70  Scheme of renewable syngas production from greenhouse gas capture and conversion into fuels and chemicals. 

6.1 Catalytic reduction of CO2 with hydrogen (RWGS). 
6.1.1 Introduction. 
The conversion of CO2 to CO via a reverse water gas shift (RWGS) reaction has been widely explored due the availability of technologies such as the Fischer–Tropsch process and methanol synthesis to further convert CO into chemicals and fuels (Scheme 57 and Fig. 71).98,746,747 Indeed, a sustainable and renewable production of syngas will guarantee the sustainability of the Fischer–Tropsch process. On the other hand, the RWGS is an important reaction that can occur during several CO2 conversion processes such as methanation, as a possible first step for CO2 activation.748 Then, the main issues of the RWGS reaction are competitive CO2 methanation, and methanol formation limiting CO yield.749 The RWGS is an endothermic process and due to the stability and inertness of the CO2 molecule a high reaction temperature (500 °C) is required to produce CO and H2O.646 From a thermodynamics standpoint, low pressures allow limiting methanol synthesis358 and high temperatures favour the RWGS reaction and practically inhibit high exothermic methanation.573,750 Nevertheless, for economic and environmental considerations, lower temperatures are desired and avoiding CO2 methanation becomes a great challenge since methane is then a common side product.749 To date, the RWGS has not been industrialized due to high energy consumption and industrial costs.751 Precisely, the thermal design and optimization of the reactor is of paramount importance and heat energy will have to be produced from renewable or nuclear sources to minimize carbon footprint. Actually, only one industrial application in the tandem reaction of methanol synthesis, in the CAMERE process for the RWGS process, has been implemented.752–754
 Scheme 57  RWGS reaction. 
 Fig. 71  Representation of the different mechanism pathways over Au/TiO2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 755. Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. 
6.1.2 Mechanism. 

In situ characterization techniques are the key to determine the formation and nature of intermediates and adsorption strength on the catalyst surface. Then, techniques such as the transient quantitative temporal analysis of products (TAP), ATR-FT-IR or DRIFT spectroscopy and micro-calorimetry, as well as the use of isotopic kinetic studies to follow the evolution of intermediates have been used. The combination of in situ characterization techniques together with kinetic computational DFT calculations and modelling is essential for the elucidation of reaction mechanisms, the nature of the active sites involved and the rational design and development of efficient and selective catalysts.313 For CO2 adsorption and activation, the catalytic surface properties, and more specifically, the redox and acid/base properties of the support play a fundamental role in the formation of intermediates. Supports with oxygen vacancies such as TiO2 and CeO2 in contrast to Al2O3 or SiO2 allow the strong adsorption and activation of CO2 molecules and when an oxygen vacancy is in proximity to a metal site with adsorbed and dissociated H, it enhances the activity for the RWGS process.750,755,756
The RWGS mechanism has been studied on several metal supported catalysts, and different possible pathways for CO2 conversion, as presented below, have been proposed mainly based on the CO2 adsorption and reaction with dissociated H to form different intermediate species (formate, hydroxycarbonyl, and bicarbonates) or to be reduced directly into CO (Scheme 58 and Fig. 71):755
 Scheme 58  Possible proposed routes for the formation of CO over different types of supports and active sites.755,757 
– The carbonate route via CO2 adsorption over the surface to form carbonates that react with oxygen vacancies of reducible supports (TiO2 and CeO2756),
– The formate route via the adsorption on OH groups present on the surface (Al2O3) and
– The hydroxycarbonyl route757 after hydrogenation of CO2 at lower temperature.
Another mechanism was proposed for catalysts containing reducible oxides and originally for Cu that can be readily reduced and oxidized under reaction conditions. In this case, a metal (Cu) is oxidized by CO2 and reduced again by H2. This redox mechanism or direct CO2 dissociation over Cu-based catalysts involves the Cu0 oxidation into Cu+ with CO2 reduced into CO, while H2 reduces Cu+ to form H2O (Scheme 59).758,759
 Scheme 59  Redox mechanism or direct CO2 dissociation over Cu-based catalysts. 
However, the formate formation and decomposition to CO production is a widely accepted pathway mechanism in which first CO2 is hydrogenated into formate and afterwards the cleavage of the C–O bond takes place, while the carbonyl route by the dissociation of CO2 into CO over the metal was ruled out for metal based catalysts on reducible supports such as TiO2 and CeO2.
6.1.3 Catalysts for the RWGS process. 
The design and development of highly active and selective solid catalysts for the RWGS reaction should include the presence of well-defined multiple active sites combined with molecular selection through controlled adsorption, interactions and geometrical parameters that will lead to the formation of intermediates and transition-state stabilization. Accordingly, an efficient catalyst for the RWGS reaction requires
– Active sites for the adsorption of CO2 and the cleavage of the C–O bond but limited CO adsorption to avoid CO dissociation and subsequent hydrogenation into methane or CO hydrogenation into methanol. Therefore, to obtain an active and selective catalyst for CO2 reduction into CO, the optimal catalytic properties should be adjusted to adsorb and activate CO2, but not CO, by controlling the physicochemical properties of the support such as the presence of oxygen vacancies and/or of a promoter.
– Active sites (transition metal) for H2 dissociation and hydrogenation of released O (CO2) to produce H2O. The maximum metal activity should be achieved with suitable metal dispersion by selecting the optimal support and/or the addition of a promoter.
In the literature, the most frequently used metals for the development of catalysts for the RWGS process are Cu, Pt, and Rh supported on different types of supports (SiO2, Al2O3, TiO2, and CeO2) whose reducibility and acid–base properties play a key role over the activation of CO2 molecules and the reaction mechanism.760 Among them, the most commonly used catalyst for the RWGS process is Cu–ZnO/Al2O3 at low temperatures (170–250 °C) where high Cu dispersion was achieved.759,761,762 The advantage to prepare Cu-based catalysts is that Cu exhibits high catalytic activity at low temperatures for H2 dissociation with low methane production.758,763–766 Likewise, no CH4 production was observed over Au- and Cu-based catalysts.447,755 For CO2 adsorption and activation, the physicochemical properties of the support play a fundamental role and influence the formation of intermediates as mentioned above. Then, irreducible supports such as Al2O3 and SiO2 mainly enable high dispersion of the metal while a support with an oxygen vacancy such as TiO2 and CeO2 allows the strong adsorption and activation of CO2, while the presence of an oxygen vacancy closely to a metal site with adsorbed and dissociated H allows enhancing the activity for the RWGS process, as it was indicated by the differences in the activation energy for Au/TiO2 and Au/Al2O3 that imply different rate-determining steps (Fig. 72).755 Numerous catalytic systems have been reported in the literature for the RWGS reaction,755,760,767 and despite their huge potential to envelop or support metal nanoparticles, oxide mixtures, their high thermal stability, robustness and versatility in the chemical composition, metal-based zeolites and OMSs have been under-exploited to perform the RWGS reaction, and few references have been collected.
 Fig. 72  Arrhenius plot for the Au/Al2O3 and Au/TiO2 catalysts in the RWGS reaction illustrates the differences in the activation energy. Reprinted with permission from ref. 755. Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. 
(a) OMS-based catalysts for the RWGS. 
(i) Monometallic OMS-based catalysts for the RWGS. Nickel has numerous applications for CO2 conversion due to its high activity, abundance and low price with respect to other metals. As demonstrated for the synthesis of DME, the use of ordered mesoporous materials has allowed achieving a high dispersion of active metal centers following the optimized synthesis pathway.768–770 Accordingly, a novel hydrophobic encapsulation strategy was described for the preparation of monodispersed NiO particles in SBA-15 by a direct hydrothermal synthesis method.770,771 The results showed that the NiO content affected CO selectivity at lower temperature though high NiO loading was required to achieve high CO2 conversion and 100% CO selectivity as it was previously reported (Table 10, entries 1–3).770 The high surface area and the mesoporous channels of MCM-41 were also advantageously used for the preparation of new RWGS catalysts. A direct hydrothermal synthesis route allowed preparing well-ordered mesoporous silica incorporating transition metals, such as Ni, Cu, Zn, and Co, with a high Si/metal ratio of 5. Ni–MCM-41, Cu-MCM-41 and Co-MCM-41 catalysts showed good catalytic activity for CO2 conversion into CO while Zn-MCM-41 exhibited very low catalytic performance. The activation energy values obtained for the different samples showed that the Ni–MCM-41 catalytic activity for CO2 hydrogenation was up to seven times higher than those of Co-MCM-41 and Cu-MCM-41 materials with high selectivity to CO2 methanation whereas no methanation was observed for Cu-MCM-41. The Cu-MCM-41 catalyst exhibited the best catalytic properties for the RWGS with maximum CO selectivity at 550 °C.772 The Co-MCM-41 and Ni–MCM-41 materials exhibited high catalytic activity although these exhibited lower CO selectivities, 95 and 70%, respectively (Table 10, entries 4–7). Fe-KIT-6 prepared by direct hydrothermal synthesis with different Fe loadings showed well-ordered cubic structures and its structural integrity was preserved for Si/Fe ratios up to 10.773 The characterization of the Fe-KIT-6 sample (Si/Fe = 30) by EPR and XPS spectroscopies showed that Fe was present as a tetrahedrally coordinated iron atom in the KIT-6 framework, although the presence of extra-framework species/small iron oxide clusters could not be discarded. The Fe-KIT-6 sample with a higher Fe content exhibited the best catalytic performance for the RWGS reaction being the rate of hydrogenation a direct function of the amount of iron active sites and the reaction temperature, so a high iron content favoured methanation. Therefore, 97% CO selectivity at low 16% CO2 conversion was reached at 500 °C over Fe-KIT-6 (Si/Fe = 10) (Table 10, entry 8).

(ii) Bimetallic OMS-based catalysts for the RWGS. As aforementioned for methane, methanol or dimethylether synthesis, the addition of promoters allows modifying the CO2 adsorption capacity and activation, the reducibility, dispersion and stability of the metal, and the formation of new active species at the interface metal/support.519,774–776 The promoter can be a reducible transition metal oxide with oxygen vacancies such as TiO2 and CeO2 that play a key role for the CO2 activation, or second metals such Fe, Co, Ni Cu, and Zn.750,763
Accordingly, the catalytic performance of bimetallic CuO–NiO/SAB-15, NiO–CeO2/SBA-15 and CuO–CeO2/SAB-15 materials synthesized by the direct hydrothermal method in a strongly acidic medium was investigated. TEM–EDX analysis and characterization data revealed that, for bimetallic materials, in the case of NiO and CeO2, both oxides were simply mixed and well dispersed on SBA-15; in the case of CeO2 and CuO, CeO2 was covered by CuO while in the case of CuO and NiO, both oxides were separately well-dispersed in SBA-15. Compared with monometallic oxide/SBA-15, the obtained bimetallic oxides/SBA-15 catalysts exhibited excellent efficiency in terms of reducing CO2 to CO via the RWGS reaction and the CO2 conversion increased with temperature for all monometallic and bimetallic catalysts. Moreover, CO2 conversion over bimetallic samples was higher than that over monometallic ones. These results were attributed to the strong electronic interaction between metal active sites and the promoter, and/or the promoter and the reactant (CO2), which favour the RWGS reaction at lower temperatures. Thus, CeO2 doping allowed improving the catalytic properties of CuO and NiO owing to the presence of oxygen vacancies enhancing CO2 conversion. All the prepared monometallic and bimetallic catalysts exhibited 100% CO selectivity regardless of the temperature, except NiO–CeO2/SBA-15 which showed higher catalytic activity than all catalysts, at lower temperatures, but lower selectivity, in the temperature range of 400–700 °C due to methane production. However, at T °C > 700, the best catalytic performance was reached over NiO–CeO2/SBA-15, and 100% CO selectivity and maximum 55% CO2 conversion were achieved at 900 °C (Table 10, entries 9–14).777
Solid materials able to store and release oxygen reversibly at moderate temperatures are named oxygen storage materials (OSMs) or oxygen carriers. OSMs usually composed by transition metal oxides have received attention in the last few decades for the combustion of fuels with CO2 capture (as an oxidant)778 and for the RWGS779 through chemical looping, involving cyclic reduction and oxidation with oxygen storage materials (OSMs). Thus, numerous studies have investigated RWGS-chemical looping (REWGS-CL) involving cyclic reduction and oxidation of oxygen storage materials (OSMs) in order to maximize the efficiency of renewable H2, captured CO2 and the further CO conversion into fuels and methanol (Fig. 73).
 Fig. 73  Overall process of CO2 capture and conversion on H2-reduced oxides in the RWGS for the further CO conversion into fuels or methanol. Reprinted with permission from ref. 779. Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society. 
The RWGS-CL reaction is divided into two consecutive reduction and oxidation steps and the oxide material has the ability to form oxygen vacancies, adsorb and activate CO2 to exchange oxygen and produce CO and to be further reduced under H2 flow with water production (Scheme 60).779,780
 Scheme 60  Cyclic reduction and oxidation of oxygen storage materials (OSMs). 
The challenge is the design of an oxide material with high capacity to generate oxygen vacancies and stable over numerous cycles being the main drawback the metal oxide sintering at high temperatures. Perovskite-type oxides (ABO3) offer numerous synthesis and composition opportunities for matching and optimizing redox abilities.779,781–783 Due to their potential, perovskite oxides have been studied as potential catalysts for RWGS-chemical looping and specifically the La0.75Sr0.25FeO3 (LSF) perovskite.784–786 However, there is a limitation derived from the low surface area of the perovskites and different strategies have been followed to disperse perovskites on the support or to form composites. Accordingly, the perovskite oxide (LSF) was supported on SBA-15 by the addition of SBA-15 to the sol–gel perovskite synthesis media. The final material was tested for RWGS-chemical looping, and the CO yield was improved up to 10 times in the presence of the LSF/SBA-15 material in comparison to pure LSF. Nevertheless, the CO yield was low and between 2.5 and 3.6 mmol gLSF−1 at 700 °C (Table 10, entries 15–20). This attempt of supported perovskite oxides can be of interest for further studies.787
(b) Zeolite-based catalysts for the RWGS. 
(i) Monometallic zeolite-based catalysts for the RWGS. It has been demonstrated that the local environment of an active site influences strongly the activity and selectivity of a given reaction by adsorption and for the stabilization of a reaction transition state on solid catalysts.788 Moreover, Rh is well-known to exhibit high activity for CO2 hydrogenation. Therefore, a recent study explored the effect of the surroundings of Rh nanoparticles over the CO2 hydrogenation selectivity towards methane or CO production.789 In the case of zeolites, the neighbouring of the Rh metal nanoparticles could be varied by changing the framework composition and/or the zeolite structure. Then, a new and efficient strategy for the preparation of supported metal nanoparticles with high resistance to sintering by fixing the nanoparticles within zeolite crystals by the seed-directed growth technique was carried out.790 Therefore, the Rh nanoparticles were enveloped within the crystals with a defined and specific environment controlled by the zeolite composition (Si/Al ratio) that determines the hydrogenation pathway towards methanation or the RWGS. The catalytic data showed that in the presence of Rh@S-1 (pure silica, silicalite 1) a maximum CO selectivity of 79.8% with 51.6% CO2 conversion was reached, while in the presence of Rh@ZSM-5 (Si/Al = 30) 98.2% CH4 selectivity with 68.2% CO2 conversion was achieved, under the same reaction conditions (Table 10, entries 21 and 22; Fig. 74). In operando IR measurements allowed attributing these results to limited hydrogen spillover due to the absence of acid sites and fast CO desorption over the Rh@S-1 catalyst. These results offer new opportunities for the rational design of efficient and selective catalysts for a well-defined process.
 Fig. 74  Surroundings of Rh nanoparticles controlled by the Si/Al ratio of the MFI structure determine the CO2 hydrogenation selectivity towards methane or CO production. Reprinted with permission from ref. 789. Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. 
(ii) Bimetallic zeolite-based catalysts for the RWGS. The combination of two metals in an oxide matrix provides a material with new physical–chemicals properties and specially affects the redox abilities due to electronic interactions. Cu-based catalysts exhibit high activity at low temperatures for CO2 hydrogenation and the WGS reaction, but are unstable at higher temperatures due to sintering, while Mo-based catalysts are selective catalysts for the conversion of CO2 into CO. Therefore, the activity of the bimetallic Mo–Cu-supported FAU zeolite catalyst was investigated for the RWGS and Mo/FAU catalysts doped with different amounts of Cu were prepared.791 The H2-TPR study revealed a shift in the reduction temperature for MoO3 indicating that the presence of Cu modifies the electronic properties of Mo, leading to an easier reduction for Mo oxide (Fig. 75). The catalytic study showed that the Mo-FAU catalyst reached 99% CO selectivity and 14.3% CO2 conversion at 500 °C while the doped-Cu catalyst, 7.6 wt% Mo–1.25 wt% Cu/FAU, exhibited the best catalytic performance, and reached 18.5 and 23.5% CO yields with 99% CO selectivity, at 500 and 600 °C, respectively (Table 10, entries 23–26). Then, the enhanced activity of the 7.6 wt% Mo–1.25 wt% Cu/FAU catalyst was attributed to the presence of Cu that improved the reducibility of MoO3 into MoO2.
 Fig. 75  H2-TPR spectra of Mo(x)Cu(1 − x)/FAU samples (x: metal content, mmol g−1). Reprinted with permission from ref. 791. Copyright 2020, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
(iii) Alkali effect over the catalytic activity of zeolite-based catalysts for the RWGS. The addition of alkali metals can greatly improve the activity or modify the selectivity of a catalyst for CO2 conversion due to the enhanced adsorption and activation of CO2 on basic sites.765,792 Following these leads, a recent study of the catalytic activity of Rh supported Y zeolite catalysts (RhY) and Li-doped RhY catalysts (Li/RhY) for CO2 hydrogenation revealed the great impact of the incorporation of alkali on the selectivity of the catalyst. CH4 was the main product over the RhY catalyst, while CO was the main product over the Li-doped RhY catalyst.793 The geometric and electronic structures of Rh species in Y zeolites were similar before the reaction but different after the reaction. Rh particle migration and aggregation out of the pores occurred in RhY, while Rh particles remained stable in Li/RhY owing to the presence of Li (Fig. 76). Different reaction mechanisms were proposed, and the difference in the selectivity process was attributed to the accumulation of CO on the particle surface on Li/RhY inhibiting H2 adsorption, while on RhY, CO adsorbed species react with OH groups and desorbed to enable H2 adsorption and CH4 formation. Thus, in the presence of the Li/RhY catalyst, at 450 °C, 87% CO selectivity at a ∼15% CO2 conversion level was reached whereas in the presence of RhY, 100% CH4 selectivity at a ∼25% CO2 conversion level was achieved (Table 10, entries 27 and 28).
 Fig. 76  Formation of a smaller particle size in Li/RhY than in RhY owing to the deposition of Li atoms on the walls of the cages. Reprinted with permission from ref. 793. Copyright 2000, Academic Press. All rights reserved. 
6.1.4 Conclusion. 
The production of CO from the RWGS is a very attractive process due to its use as a building block for the synthesis of chemicals and hydrocarbons from Fischer–Tropsch synthesis, always considering the use of anthropogenic CO2 and green and renewable hydrogen. The main issue is the co-production of methane. It is crucial to make the RWGS catalysts 100% CO selective for commercialization, as an increase in hydrogen consumption will make the process more expensive, will require a subsequent separation process and will also affect the carbon footprint. In the few last decades, numerous studies on the RWGS using, specially, supported metals, have been carried out. The CO2 adsorption and CO desorption appear key factors for enhancing CO2 conversion and CO selectivity. Nevertheless, new efforts are still required to determine the reaction mechanism and find the working active sites to finally establish the structure–activity/selectivity relationship. Therefore, computational modeling and in situ spectroscopy studies will be fundamental tools for the rational design of efficient and highly selective catalysts for the RWGS. In the present review, a short compilation of works showed that the potential of ordered microporous and mesoporous materials has been scarcely considered and undervalued. The studies described present strategies to maximize CO selectivity and CO2 conversion, improving the metal–support interaction and stability, potentially through the incorporation of a promoter (alkali, second metal), increasing the oxygen mobility generating oxygen vacancies owing to the presence of reducible transition metal oxide or maximizing the dispersion and stability of metal nanoparticles by enveloping within the zeolite pore channel system. Enhancement of CO2 adsorption/activation and fast and easy CO desorption are key steps to consider for the design of new RWGS catalysts and catalyst issues. In addition, future studies will focus on the catalyst deactivation mechanism in order to improve the stability of catalysts for the RWGS. Undoubtedly, the unique properties of zeolites and OMSs as supports to confine, disperse and stabilize metal species due to their pore structure and high surface area make them attractive supports for the development of RWGS catalysts. The benefits of the support morphology and pore topology to enhance the dispersion and attachment of metal species should be fundamental. Therefore, future studies should focus on hierarchical zeolites, two-dimensional zeolite nanosheets and core–shell materials to promote the physical isolation and protection of particles as well as mass transfer.


6.2 Dry reforming of methane (DRM). 
6.2.1 Introduction. 
The catalytic dry reforming of methane into synthesis gas (H2 + CO) is a very attractive CO2 conversion since it makes possible the simultaneous mitigation of two important greenhouse gases, CH4 and CO2 (Fig. 77). DRM is a 100% atom efficiency process and provides a highly regarded H2/CO ratio of 1 for further industrial conversion such as Fischer–Tropsch and other valuable chemicals (methanol). However, in practice, DRM requires high energy consumption due to the very endothermic character of the reaction. Moreover, the high temperatures required by the process induce a rapid deactivation of the catalyst due to sintering of the metal and severe coke deposition. So far, the rapid deactivation of the catalyst together with the high energy use constitutes the biggest obstacle to the development of an environmentally friendly industrial application to date. Therefore, the design of a robust and efficient catalyst at “low temperatures” is topical and very challenging.
 Fig. 77  Dry reforming of methane to syngas production over metal based catalysts. 
6.2.2 Thermodynamics. 
DRM is highly endothermic and a high temperature is required to reach high thermodynamic conversions. Hence, for an industrial implementation, renewable energy sources must be considered in order to achieve an economically and environmentally sustainable process. Simultaneously, the RWGS can take place and decrease the H2/CO ratio to a lower value than an ideal ratio of 1. However, the main drawback remains the strong deactivation of the catalyst because of coke deposition originated mainly from methane decomposition (cracking) and CO disproportionation (Fig. 78). A study based on the standard free energy change determines that DRM occurs above 640 °C and the RWGS above 840 °C, while coke formation by methane cracking takes place from 557 °C and coke formation by CO disproportionation (the Boudouard reaction) is inhibited above 710 °C due to high exothermicity.794–796 Therefore, at a temperature of >800 °C, superficial carbon species are issued from CH4 cracking, and can be easily oxidized by CO2, limiting the formation of coke (route 2, Fig. 79). At lower temperature, low reactive carbon species obtained from the Boudouard reaction are formed and coke deposition is more critical.797 Coke formation not only induces severe catalyst deactivation because of the encapsulation of the active sites or carbon fiber formation on the catalyst surface but also causes reactor plugging. Different studies have determined the limit temperature for carbon deposition for different CO2/CH4 ratios in the feed, and showed for the same feed ratio that the temperature limit for carbon deposition increased as the pressure increased and, that, at a given pressure, the temperature limit for carbon deposition increased as the CO2/CH4 feed ratio decreased, indicating that CO2 excess in the feed has to be used to avoid coke.794–796 However, it was shown that higher CO2/CH4 favours the RWSG and produces the lower H2/CO ratio,796,798 while the incorporation of steam into the system can help preventing the coke formation but decreases CO2 conversion.799,800 Moreover, the use of high pressures even if is of interest from industrial standpoints promotes carbon deposition.801,802 The equilibrium constant for the endothermic DRM reaction increases significantly when increasing the temperature, maximizing CO2 and CH4 conversion, while the Boudouard reaction and CH4 cracking are also favoured. Therefore, the design of active, efficient and highly stable catalyst is decisive for a kinetic control of the DRM process and to avoid coke formation.
 Fig. 78  DRM and competitive reactions. 
 Fig. 79  Mechanism for the adsorption and dissociation of CH4 and CO2 to produce syngas (route (I)) and for the oxidation of active dehydrogenated carbon species by CO2 (route (II)). Reprinted with permission from ref. 805. Copyright 2018, Zunrong Sheng et al. Licensee IntechOpen. 
6.2.3 Mechanism of dry reforming of methane. 
The main accepted mechanism for DRM involves the reversible adsorption and dissociation of CH4 on the catalyst active sites to produce four adsorbed hydrogen active species (that are further converted into H2), and adsorbed carbon species oxidized into CO (reduction of the catalyst/lattice oxygen) and on adsorption and dissociation of CO2 on the support to yield CO and lattice oxygen (oxidation of the catalyst/oxygen vacancy) (Scheme 61 and Fig. 79).803,804 A suitable catalyst has to present high reducibility and oxygen storage capacity enabling high oxygen mobility to successfully support the redox cycle: CO2 being reduced into CO.
 Scheme 61  Main accepted mechanism of the adsorption and dissociation of CH4 and CO2 for DRM. 
6.2.4 Catalysts for DRM. 
In spite of the great challenges from environmental and industrial standpoints, DRM is not currently perceived as an industrially viable process, because of carbon formation, quick sintering/deactivation of conventional reforming catalysts and high energy consumption. Over the last 30 years, DRM studies have shown that Ni catalysts are the most suitable candidates for industrial implementation owing to their low cost and availability in comparison to noble metal (Pt, Ru, and Rh) based catalysts. Noble metals exhibit higher activity because of their ability to dissociate CO2 into CO and O species that favour the CH4 dissociation into CHn and H species. However, unlike noble metal catalysts,806 Ni suffers from strong coke formation and deactivation.807,808 To prevent carbon and nickel carbide formation, an optimum range of operation temperature, between 870 and 1040 °C, was determined.794 Hence, numerous strategies were developed to minimize coke deposition on Ni catalysts, consisting mainly in minimizing and stabilizing the Ni particle size since carbon deposition on the Ni surface requires Ni ensembles, and thus large Ni particles enable severe coke deposition.809,810 Several studies defined the optimum Ni nanoparticle size of few nanometres (<10), for which the carbon deposition is significantly minimized.811,812 Nevertheless, small Ni nanoparticles present low thermal stability under DRM reaction conditions.813,814 Hence, different approaches have been followed to minimize the Ni particle size and maximize the Ni sintering resistance. The choice of a suitable support not only allows a high dispersion of the metal nanoparticles, but can also provide specific physicochemical properties such as basicity (CaO, La2O3, and MgO), high oxygen mobility (oxygen vacancies/storage capacity) (CeO2, ZrO2, and TiO2), and modifying reducibility, and enhances the catalytic activity or improve the catalyst stability, avoiding carbon formation. Additionally, the incorporation of one or more metals as promoters can play different roles such as improving the dispersion and stabilization of the active phase, usually by forming homogeneous solution of oxides and strong metal support interactions, and/or enhancing the catalytic activity owing to the introduction of new physicochemical properties (redox and acid/base).815 Likewise, the incorporation of basic metals such as lanthanides, alkali and rare earth metals showed to enhance the stability of Ni nanoparticles in addition to promote the chemisorption and activation of CO2, and accelerate the reaction of CO2 with carbon species to form CO.816,817 Accordingly, several studies showed that carbon deposition could be limited or suppressed due to the presence of strong Lewis basic sites.818–821
(a) DRM zeolite-based catalysts. 
Among suitable supports, zeolites are of potential interest due to their physicochemical properties to deliver high metal dispersions and stable catalysts under hard reaction conditions. More specifically, MFI and USY zeolites exhibit suitable properties such as microporous structures, high surface areas, and high capacity for CO2 adsorption, and consequently, zeolite based catalysts have been extensively studied for the DRM process.
Different comparative studies showed the impacts of the acidity, reducibility and the structure of a zeolite on the catalytic properties for DRM. The catalytic performance of Ni based-zeolites (A, X, Y and ZSM-5) prepared by impregnation investigated the effects of Ni loading and the zeolite structure.822 The maximum catalytic activity was achieved with the 7 wt% Ni content while coke deposition was observed with the following order of deactivation: Ni/Y < Ni/X < Ni/ZSM-5 < Ni/A. The maximum 91% CH4 and 93% CO2 conversions with H2/CO around 1.8 were maintained at 700 °C for 5 h TOS over 7% Ni/Y that exhibited the highest performance and good potential for DRM (Table 11, entries 1–4). Previously, a comparison of the catalytic performance of Ni/Al2O3, Ni/ZSM-5 and Ni/USY showed the impacts of the acidity and zeolite structure over the catalytic properties of the catalyst and the metal reducibility due to the strong metal–support interaction, which determine the following order of reducibility: Ni/ZSM-5 > Ni/USY > Ni/γ-Al2O3.823 Ni/USY and Ni/γ-Al2O3 catalysts exhibited superior catalytic performance than Ni/ZSM-5 but also had superior Ni contents, 8.7 and 8.1 versus 3.5%, respectively. After 6 h TOS, at 650 °C, 71% and 54% of CH4 conversions and 71.2% and 56.3% CO2 conversions were obtained over Ni/USY and Ni/ZSM-5, respectively (Table 11, entries 5–7). Later on, another study of the catalytic activities of the Ni/γ-Al2O3, Ni/Y, and Ni/H-ZSM-5 samples prepared by impregnation, with the 5 wt% Ni content, confirmed the dependence of the CH4 conversion on the support.824 However, a different stability order of the Ni-based catalysts was established: Ni/H-ZSM-5 > Ni/γ-Al2O3 > Ni/Y. The best catalytic data were obtained over Ni/H-ZSM-5 with a H2/CO ratio close to 1 owing to the lower carbon deposition registered and the higher stability of the textural properties of the catalyst (BET area). High CH4 and CO2 conversions were achieved over all the Ni catalysts, close to 80% with a H2/CO ratio close to 1 along 9 h TOS (Table 11, entries 8–10). The very stable MOR was compared with FAU, both with a 2.5–3 wt% Ni content.825 The MOR based catalysts exhibited the better catalytic performance in terms of conversion and stability (Ni/HMOR > Ni/NaMOR > Ni/HY). At 750 °C, for 24 h TOS, 92% and 87% CO2 conversions and 86% and 75% CH4 conversions were achieved over Ni/H-MOR and Ni/NaMOR, respectively (Table 11, entries 11–14). The data of CO, H2 and H2/CO were not provided. The mixing Ni/H-MOR and Ni/NaMOR and Ni/HY with Al2O3 allowed improving the stability of the catalyst by reducing coke deposition.
