These comparative studies evidenced the potential of zeolites as the support for the design of DRM catalysts. Nevertheless, short-term experiments were performed indicating low catalytic stability. Different strategies were reported to improve the robustness of the zeolite based catalysts and the preparation method, metal doping, and modification of the basicity or morphology have been studied. MFI zeolites were the most support used for the design and development of DRM catalysts while other zeolite structures have been investigated more occasionally being the use of FAU more common.
(i) MFI as the support for the preparation of DRM catalysts.
* ZSM-5 as the support for the preparation of DRM catalysts. ZSM-5 has been widely used as the support for DRM catalysts due to its easy synthesis within a wide range of compositions. Recently, the hierarchical ZSM-5 zeolite with micropores and mesopores was impregnated with Ni and used for DRM. The data of CO2 and CH4 conversions and the H2/CO ratio revealed that a sample with Si/Al = 30 exhibited the highest catalytic performance, at 800 °C. The maximum 98% CO2 and 95% CH4 conversions with a H2/CO ratio close to 0.9 were maintained for 30 h (Table 11, entry 15).826 These results were attributed to the acid strength and crystallinity of the ZSM-5 structure. Samples with lower acidity exhibited lower catalytic activity and showed lower crystallinity and BET area. In addition to high catalytic performance, the Ni/ZSM-5(30) micro-mesoporous sample exhibited high stability and resistance to coke deposition, with no-deactivation registered after 30 h TOS. For all samples, the same tendency was observed, and due to the endothermicity of DRM, CH4 and CO2 conversions and H2/CO ratio increased when the temperature increased.
– Effect of the preparation method of Ni/ZSM-5-based catalysts for DRM. The activity and stability of DRM catalysts strongly depend on Ni nanoparticle dispersion and metal–support interactions, which in turn influence metal sintering and coke resistance. Therefore, the preparation method constitutes an important strategy to control the metal location, deposition and dispersion. The benefits of the chemical and physical effects of high intensity ultrasound for the synthesis of metal supported nanoparticle catalysts and nanomaterials have shown to minimize the metal particle size, improve the distribution and dispersion of the particles, and enhance the catalytic features.827–829 Following this strategy, the sonochemical method was applied to the preparation of Ni/ZSM-5 with different Ni contents (3–20 wt%).830 The characterization data showed that the ZSM-5 crystallinity decreased slightly upon Ni incorporation. A positive effect of the use of ultrasound was observed for lower 3–8 wt% Ni contents with a better dispersion and a decrease in the Ni particle size (lower than 100 nm). Nevertheless, an average large Ni particle size of about 43 nm was determined that can be considered still too large. Indeed, the Ni particle size and stability are critical parameters to control and minimize coke deposition which is formed on the Ni-ensemble and consequently low Ni particles with a size <10 nm are desirable.809,810 The 8 wt% Ni/ZSM-5 sample exhibited the better catalytic performance with a 70% CH4 conversion and a H2/CO ratio of around 0.8–0.9 for 24 h TOS, at 850 °C (Table 11, entry 16), while no data on coke analysis were provided.
– Bimetallic ZSM-5-based catalysts for DRM. The addition of a second metal or a promoter, especially noble metal and rare earth metals, is well known to enhance the activity, selectivity, dispersion and stabilization of metal nanoparticles and coke resistance. The presence of a second suitable metal allows for strong metal/metal and metal/support interactions due to electronic interactions leading to the increase of the metal surface area, modifications of the surface structure and improved lattice oxygen mobility compared to monometallic catalysts.831–836 Some studies reported that, in the case of lanthanide doped catalysts, coke deposition was filamentous and did not affect the catalytic activity but may cause reactor plugging.837–840

Effect of transition metal doping of Ni/ZSM-5-based catalysts for DRM. Recently, the catalytic performance of Ni–Co/ZSM5 monometallic and bimetallic catalysts was studied.841 Cobalt was shown to reduce carbon deposition due to its ability to oxidize adsorbed carbon into CO and CO2.842 Therefore, the preparation of bimetallic catalysts allowed enhancing the catalytic activity and minimizing coke deposition due to the synergetic effect between Ni and Co.842–846 Then, the bimetallic 2.3 wt% Ni–4.6 wt% Co/ZSM5 catalyst exhibited the best catalytic activity and stability with 5 wt% coke formation in comparison with 46 wt% coke formatted on monometallic samples.841 At 800 °C, 80% CH4 conversion and 85% CO2 conversion with 65 and 61% H2 and CO selectivities, respectively, were achieved (Table 11, entries 17–20). The results showed that coke deposition was dependent on the temperature and catalyst composition. Over 2.3 wt% Ni–4.6 wt% Co/ZSM5 at 700 °C and after short 12 h TOS, CH4 and CO2 conversions decreased from 66 to 50% and 71% to 61%, respectively, while the C balance increased from 73 to 80%. Mn has also been studied as the promoter for DRM Ni-based catalysts and enabled suppressing carbon deposition and improving catalyst life. Following these leads, the effect of the incorporation of different Mn loadings on Ni(10 wt%)-zeolites (NH4-ZSM5, NH4-Y, and Na-Y) over their catalytic performance was explored.847 The catalytic results showed that the initial best catalytic activity was achieved in the presence of the 10 wt% Ni-ZSM-5 catalyst, but the CH4 conversion and H2 production decreased within the first 5 h TOS. After the first 5 hours, similar activity was reported for all the 10 wt% Ni-zeolite catalysts. CH4 and CO2 conversions ranged between 22–25% and 34–36%, respectively. The addition of Mn affected the catalytic activity of three types of zeolite supports differently, but their catalytic performance was not improved (Table 11, entries 21–24). The positive effects of low Mn loading were the reduction of carbon deposition and the decrease in the Ni nanoparticle size and dispersion on the zeolite. However, as Mn loading increased, the crystallinity of the zeolite structure and its micropore surface area generally decreased. The incorporation of different ZrO2 loadings to the Ni-ZSM-5 catalyst showed that the presence of ZrO2 enhanced the Ni nanoparticle dispersion and minimized the Ni particle size due to the strong metal–support interaction, and 8 wt% Ni and 5 wt% ZrO2 contents were optimum.848 In the presence of Ni(8%)/ZrO2(5%)-ZSM-5, at 850 °C, 95% CO and 90% H2 yields were reached and remained constant for 10 h TOS (Table 11, entry 25). Very high CO and H2 yields were achieved but the catalytic stability was demonstrated over too short TOS to evaluate its potential as the industrial catalyst.

Effect of rare earth metal doping of Ni/ZSM-5-based catalysts for DRM. Numerous studies of DRM using Ni-based catalysts showed that the incorporation of a second metal or metal oxide such as La, Y, and Ce improved considerably the catalytic activity and limited coke deposition due to the strong electronic interaction between Ni species and the second metal and/or the support favouring high dispersion of Ni nanoparticles and the formation of new active species.849–851 Accordingly, the benefits of La doping on the Ni/ZSM-5 catalytic activity for DRM was explored.852 La2NiO4/ZSM-5 was prepared by the sol–gel technique and the RXD pattern confirmed the formation of La2NiO4 species with a spinel structure in the fresh sample, uniformly and highly dispersed on ZSM-5. The catalytic study showed that 69% CO2 and 67% CH4 conversions and a H2/CO ratio close to 0.9 were higher over the La2NiO4/ZSM-5 catalyst than those over Ni/ZSM-5 or La2NiO4/γ-Al2O3 catalysts. Moreover, La2NiO4/ZSM-5 presented higher stability over 35 h TOS. The improved stability of the La2NiO4/ZSM-5 catalyst was attributed to the formation and decomposition of the La2O2CO3 species that enable the oxidation of dehydrogenated carbon species accumulated on Ni particles. The XRD patterns revealed the disappearance of La2O3 species and the formation of the La2O2CO3 species by CO2 adsorption on La2O3 during the DRM process. Afterwards, the La2O2CO3 species decompose into CO and oxygen species that enable the oxidation of carbon species accumulated on Ni particles into CO (Fig. 80), as it was previously described.851,853 The same group studied the catalytic performance of LaNiOx/ZSM-5 in a pilot plant, which was shaped as the cylinder of LaNiOx/ZSM-5 (∅ = 3 mm × 10–15 mm), and prepared by a sol–gel method.854 97% CH4 and 94% CO2 conversions were achieved and remained constant for 100 h, at 850 °C but with a low GHSV of 1.3 L h−1 gcat−1 (Table 11, entries 26 and 27).
 Fig. 80  Adsorption and dissociation of CH4 and CO2 to produce syngas and for the oxidation of active dehydrogenated carbon species by oxygen issued from La2O2CO3 decomposition. 
The benefits of the CeO2 addition to Ni based-catalysts have been widely reported such as the improvement of Ni nanoparticle dispersion, enhancement of redox properties, high oxygen storage capacity enabling the splitting of the C–O bond and formation of Lewis basic sites promoting the adsorption and activation of CO2.832 Moreover, the enhanced oxygen mobility allowed oxidizing adsorbed carbon species on metallic particles into CO and preventing coke deposition.855,856 Following these leads, the effects of Ce, Ni loading and temperature calcination over the catalytic performance of Ni–Ce/ZSM-5 catalysts were studied under optimized DRM conditions (temperature, CH4/CO2 ratio and space velocity).857 The catalytic data revealed that CH4 and CO2 conversions increased with Ni loading until stabilization (10 wt%) while an optimum in Ce was determined. Previously, it was reported that the Ce content enabled improving oxygen mobility and Ni dispersion and enhancing the DRM rate.627,858,859 The highest activity was achieved for 2 wt% Ce loading with 90% CH4 and 78% CO2 conversions, and under optimized parameters of reaction, up to 99% CH4 and 94% CO2 conversions were achieved. The stability test evidenced good catalytic performance and stability, maintaining 95% CH4 and 85% CO2 conversions with a syngas ratio close to 1, for 40 h TOS without notable deactivation (Table 11, entry 28). Later on, the effect of the addition of the controlled amount of Ce to Ni/ZSM-5 catalysts, the role of Ce in the improvement of the catalytic performance, and the limitations of coke deposition were studied.860 The optimum catalytic results were achieved over 5 wt% Ni–5 wt% Ce/ZSM-5, at 800 °C, excellent 97% CH4 and 91% CO2 conversions with 98% H2 selectivity and H2/CO ∼ 1 for short 24 h TOS while neither the longer stability test nor the regeneration study were performed (Table 11, entry 29). Similar conclusions were drawn. The addition of Ce enhanced Ni particle dispersion, favoured CH4 dissociation and H2 production, and strengthened the gasification of coke owing to enhanced CO2 adsorption and oxidation of adsorbed carbon. A XPS study allowed determining the presence of CeO2 and CeAlO3 in the reduced and used catalysts leading to a proposed redox cycle in which CO2 enabled the oxidation of CeAlO3 and regeneration of CeO2 + Al2O3 (Scheme 62). Moreover, the calculated apparent activation energies of CH4 and CO2 could be reduced by 30% and 40%, respectively.
 Scheme 62  Proposed redox cycle for CeO2 + Al2O3 regeneration during DRM over Ni–Ce/ZSM-5 catalysts. 
The effect of different additives (Cu, Nd, and Mo) over acidity, Ni dispersion and turn over during catalytic performance in the DRM process was studied using the Al2O3-HZSM-5 zeolite as the support.861 The study of the addition of metal oxide revealed that the presence of Cu maximized the H2 yield. Meanwhile, the addition of rare-earth elements influenced the catalytic activity and the Nd incorporation maximized the H2 yield. Finally, the Mo addition maximized the H2 yield up to 70%. The authors concluded that the incorporation of different metal oxides increased the complexity of the catalyst and the acidity, responsible for the improved catalytic activity. Under optimized reaction conditions, at 850 °C, 98% CH4 and 97% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio close to 1 were achieved over NiCuNdMo/Al2O3HZSM-5 (Table 11, entry 30). Unfortunately, no stability test was performed.

Effect of noble metal doping of Ni/ZSM-5-based catalysts for DRM. The benefits of the addition of noble metals to Ni zeolite-based catalysts due to their resistance to coke deposition have also been explored. Then, the preparation of Ni/ZSM-5 catalysts incorporating a small amount of noble metal (Pt) for the DRM process was described.839,862 The catalytic study revealed that the maximum catalytic results corresponded to optimum Ni and Pt metal loadings of 6% and 0.5%, respectively.862 The characterization data allowed attributing the enhancement of catalytic activity and coke resistance to the higher Ni dispersion and decrease of Ni nanoparticle size and improved NiO reducibility owing to the presence of Pt. The TPR study indicated a shift to a lower temperature of NiO reduction for higher Ni contents for monometallic samples. This result was attributed to the formation of nickel species with a larger particle size, with a small interface with the support, and a weak NiO/support interaction that make nickel oxide easy to be reduced. Therefore, the larger the particle size, the lower the reduction temperature. On the other hand, the presence of a small amount of Pt in Ni catalysts evidenced the easier reduction of NiO, probably because Pt is reduced first and activates hydrogen which favours the reduction of NiO by spill over. Moreover, the improved coke resistance of Pt promoted Ni catalysts was attributed to the formation of Pt–Ni particles covered mainly by Ni, leading to high Ni particle dispersion and the formation of a higher amount of active carbon species further oxidized into CO. Thus, at a low reaction temperature (600 °C), modest 28% CH4 and 69% CO2 conversions were achieved (Table 11, entries 31 and 32). Previously, the preparation of Ni–Ru bimetallic catalysts already showed the effect of the Ru incorporation over the catalytic activity and stability (Table 11, entries 33 and 34).839 The Ru addition effect was more important for silica than that for H-ZSM5 supported catalysts. The improvement in catalytic activity and stability was observed for bimetallic samples, which was related to higher dispersion of Ni nanoparticles due to the Ni–Ru cluster formation. Nevertheless, neither any data of conversion nor yield were provided.
– Effect of the basic properties of the Ni/ZSM-5-based catalysts for DRM. The presence of alkali metals may influence the physicochemical properties of Ni catalysts due to electronic interactions of alkali metal/Ni modifying the reducibility of Ni and dispersion, surface acidity and surface CO2 adsoprtion by forming carbonate species. Indeed, the surface basicity influences strongly the CO2 adsorption and activation and plays a key role in C–O bond splitting. Moreover, the CO2 coverage of the catalytic surface allows minimizing CH4 adsorption/dissociation and coke formation. Therefore, to take advantage of basicity, a study of the presence of alkaline promoters such as Ca and K over the catalytic activity of Ni-ZSM-5 catalysts for DRM was carried out. The resistance to coke deposition of KNiCa/ZSM-5 was attributed to the CO2 coverage on the catalytic surface limiting CH4 decomposition. At 800 °C, high 94% CH4 and 95% CO2 conversions and 94% H2 and 95% CO selectivities were recorded (Table 11, entry 35).863 A previous study showed that the high 95% CO2 conversion over KNiCa/ZSM-5 with a high resistance to coke formation along 140 h TOS, at 800 °C, was due to the formation of carbonate species on alkaline metals (Ca) located adjacent to Ni sites, as well as to CO2 dissociation.863–865 Not long ago, the preparation of doped Ni/Fibrous-ZSM-5 catalysts with Ta, Mg, and Ca illustrated the new catalytic properties of fibrous catalysts for the DRM reaction.866 The addition of promoters modified the surface acidity with the following trend Ni–Ga/FZSM-5 > Ni/FZSM-5 > Ni–Mg/FZSM-5 > Ni–Ta/FZSM-5 > Ni–Ca/FZSM-5. The Ta incorporation implies the disappearance of strong acid sites and the formation of medium acid sites enabling the stabilization and redispersion of Ni particles. The incorporation of Mg and Ca involved an increase in the concentration of weak acid sites, while the addition of Ga species increases the concentration of strong acid sites promoting side reactions and coke formation. A H2-TPR study revealed the synergism between Ta and Ni, promoting the reducibility of NiO as it was observed with the addition of Ca and in contrast to the incorporation of Mg where a shift to a higher reduction temperature was formed (Fig. 81). The surface basicity order was Ni–Ca/ZSM-5 > Ni–Mg/FZSM-5 > Ni–Ta/FZSM-5 > Ni–Ga/FZSM-5 > Ni/FZSM-5. The catalytic data showed that samples with higher acidity suffered rapid deactivation, while excessive basicity (Ca) promoted the RWGS reaction. The catalytic results showed that there is a balance between reducibility and acid–base properties to manage optimum adsorption and activation of CO2, preventing CH4 decomposition and enhancing metal activity through strong metal–support interactions, while preventing coke deposition. High catalytic performance was achieved over Ni–Ta/FZSM-5 with 93% CH4 and 98% CO2 conversions and 0.97 H2/CO ratio, with high stability over 80 h TOS, at 800 °C (Table 11, entries 36–41). Very good performance was reported but the reaction temperature was too high. However, since H2 and CO yields were not given, neither information on catalyst regeneration was supplied, it is difficult to ascertain the potential of this catalyst.
 Fig. 81  H2-TPR study of the alkali metal doped Ni-fibrous ZSM-5 catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 866. Copyright 2020, John Wiley & Sons Ltd. 
– Effect of the morphology of the ZSM-5-based catalysts for DRM. In the last few years, the preparation of microcapsule catalysts with a core/shell structure has been widely explored to improve the stability of metal nanoparticles due to the confinement effect preventing sintering, and the resultant catalysts have been applied in different processes.644,867–871 Following these leads, a microcapsule catalyst, Ni/ZSM-5@SiO2, was prepared by the sol–gel method with Ni/ZSM-5 as the core and amorphous SiO2 as the shell.872 The characterization data showed high Ni nanoparticle dispersion and stability due to strengthened interactions between Ni particles and the ZSM-5 core, and the spatial confinement provided by the core–shell structure (Fig. 82).
 Fig. 82  Spatial confinement of Ni-metal particles owing to the core–shell structure of ZSM-5 and SiO2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 872. Copyright 2019, WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Therefore, CH4 and CO2 conversions were higher over coated catalysts than uncoated ones. The superior catalytic performance of the 10 wt% Ni/ZSM-5@SiO2 catalyst in comparison to that of the 10 wt% Ni/ZSM-5 catalyst was ascribed to coke deposition resistance induced by the SiO2 shell owing to the large surface area and the pore volume of the 10 wt% Ni/ZSM-5@SiO2 composite, ensuring higher dispersion and immobilization of Ni particles. A test of catalyst activity and stability over 50 h for 10 wt% Ni/ZSM-5 and 10 wt% Ni/ZSM-5@SiO2 catalysts showed high catalytic performance with activity decay attributed to coke deposition and Ni-sintering. At 800 °C, CH4 and CO2 conversions between 90 and 83% over 50 h of TOS were achieved (Table 11, entries 42 and 43). Recently, the preparation of fibrous materials and particularly of fibrous zeolites offers new opportunities for synthesizing catalysts with a large pore constituted by porous and dendrimeric silica fibres with great accessibility to active sites. Fibrous materials not only provide materials with a large pore system allowing the diffusion of bulky reactants and high surface area, but also the spatial confinement effect of Ni particles. Likewise, Ni/Fibrous-ZSM-5 catalysts exhibited superior catalytic properties for the DRM reaction than microporous Ni/ZSM-5.866 At 750 °C, 55 and 78% CH4 conversions and 61 and 79% CO2 conversions were achieved over Ni/ZSM-5 and Ni/FZSM-5, respectively (Table 11, entries 36 and 37).
* Silicalite-1-based catalysts for DRM. The peculiarity of the silicalite-1 zeolite is the large number of structural defects (silanol groups), up to 32 per unit cell, whose weak acidity affects both the activity and selectivity of the catalyst for different processes.873,874 Therefore, the effect of post-synthesis modifications by thermal treatment after ionic exchange and silylation procedure over Ni-silicalite-1 catalytic performance (CH4 and CO2 conversions, H2/CO and coke deposition) for the DRM process was studied.875,876 Silylation enabled the formation of smaller and more reducible Ni-oxide species, avoiding the formation of nickel silicates (Fig. 83). Therefore, silylated samples reached better CH4 conversion with low coke formation of whisker-type that is favoured over the Ni ensemble of a minimum seven Ni atoms, with a particle size larger than 5 nm, being Ni very small particle size, <2 nm,811 a key factor to inhibit tubular whisker carbon formation.812 At 700 °C, 78–72% CH4 and 88–80% CO2 conversions with H2/CO around 1.05 were maintained over 26 h TOS (Table 11, entries 44 and 45).
 Fig. 83  Effect of the presence of organo-silanes on the silicalite-1 surface over the formation of Ni agglomerates. Reprinted with permission from ref. 876. Copyright 2011, Springer Science Business Media B.V. 
Recently, encapsulated Ni clusters into microporous silicalite-1 catalysts were prepared and used for DRM. The Ni/silicalite catalyst exhibited high stability and no coke deposition under a wide range of conditions.877 The authors claimed the presence of Ni–O–Si species intercalated into the silicalite-1 framework that enables the stabilization of small metallic Ni-clusters preventing sintering and coke formation (Table 11, entry 46).
– Effect of the morphology of the silicalite-1-based catalysts for DRM. The benefits of fibrous mesostructures, with micropore and mesopore systems, such as their high surface area, enhanced diffusion for bulky molecules and accessibility to active sites are of great interest for the synthesis and development of efficient catalysts and their use as the support, to achieve high dispersion of metallic particles and stabilization by the spatial confinement effect.878 In line, mesostructured fibrous MFI was synthesized by the microemulsion zeolite seed crystallization method, and Ni was supported by incorporation via different routes. Ni-mesostructured fibrous MFI catalysts owing to the fibrous morphology, high Ni dispersion, strong metal–support interactions and moderate acidity exhibited the improved stabilization of Ni particles and catalytic activity over time on stream.879 Thus, excellent catalytic performance and high stability of 5 wt% Ni/MFI prepared by wet-impregnation was recorded, with 70% CH4 and 73% CO2 conversions (Table 11, entry 47). Kinetic studies revealed that the activation energy of CH4 and CO2 was dependent on the surface acidity that boosted CH4 cracking and other side reactions. The encapsulation of metal particles into zeolite micropores or cavities allows increasing the activity, selectivity and stability of the catalyst reducing metal sintering (Fig. 84).48,51,707,870,880,881
 Fig. 84  Encapsulation of metal particles into zeolite-based architectures: in framework cavities (a), in intracrystalline mesopores (b), inside or between zeolite nanosheets (c), in polycrystalline shells (d) and in hollow single crystals (e). Reprinted with permission from ref. 882. Copyright 2015, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Since encapsulation can present limitations of diffusion and accessibility to nanoparticles of reactants in sub-nanometric micropores, new developments in zeolite synthesis allowed the preparation of new materials with hierarchical structures containing micropore and mesopore systems or more advanced architectures such the core–shell or hollow structures.882 Hollow zeolites are very attractive due to their crystalline structure, hydrothermal and chemical stabilities and shape-selectivity due to their pore systems and versatility in their properties and compositions.883 Accordingly, the catalytic performance of encapsulated Ni–Pt bimetals in hollow silicalite-1 was reported for the first time in 2015.884 The preparation of hollow 1.5 wt% Ni–0.5 wt% Pt@HolS-1 enabled high dispersion of Ni–Pt bimetallic nanoparticles encapsulated in hollow silicalite-1. The 1.5 wt% Ni–0.5 wt% Pt@Hol S-1 catalyst exhibited higher catalytic performance for DRM over brief 6 h TOS than 1.5 wt% Ni–0.5 wt% Pt/S-1 that suffered rapid deactivation after 1 h, and then 1.5 wt% Ni@Hol S-1 due to the Pt doping effect and improved stability against sintering and coke deposition, only 1.0% carbon deposition was observed. Although the 1.5 wt% Ni–0.5 wt% Pt/S-1 and 1.5 wt% Ni@Hol S-1 catalysts had similar coke deposition, they exhibited different catalytic stabilities (Fig. 85).
 Fig. 85  Coke deposition over spent catalysts. TEM images of (a) 1.5Ni/S-1, (b) 1.5Ni@HolS-1, (c) 1.5Ni–0.5Pt/S-1, and (d) 1.5Ni–0.5Pt@Hol S-1. Reprinted with permission from ref. 884. Copyright 2015, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
The ensemble of Ni atoms with a minimum metal particle diameter was required to form filamentous carbon. In the case of 1.5 wt% Ni/S-1, because of large Ni particles, filamentous carbon deposition occurred inducing rapid deactivation. Since 1.5 wt% Ni@HolS-1 supported well dispersed and encapsulated Ni nanoparticles in the hollow zeolite with a small particle size (∼3.44 nm) and Ni larger particles on the outer surface (∼14.6 nm), filamentous carbon was also formed but the catalytic activity was maintained. Although the Ni–Pt particles exhibit a sintering resistance, 1.5Ni–0.5Pt/S-1 deactivated quickly due to coke deposition without the protective shell, while 1.5Ni–0.5Pt@Hol S-1 exhibited the best catalytic performance and stability owing to the high bimetallic nanoparticle dispersion and the spatial confinement effect of the shell, lowering coke formation. At 800 °C, 0.1 MPa, 72% CH4 and 80% CO2 conversions were maintained for 6 h TOS over 1.5Ni–0.5Pt@Hol S-1 (Table 11, entries 48–51). Good catalytic results were reported over short TOS, and neither any data of H2 and CO yields nor selectivity were reported, which together with low metal contents augured low yields and a short operating life.
– Pt/silicalite-1-based catalysts for DRM. As mentioned above, among the strategies to improve metal supported catalysts and minimize sintering, the encapsulation of metal nanoparticles within the zeolite, hollow or core–shell material constitutes an emerging approach. Accordingly, amorphous-silica-coated metal nanoparticles (<2 nm) were successfully prepared and used in the presence of the organic structure-directing agent, under hydrothermal conditions to convert amorphous-silica-coated Pt nanoparticles into the zeolite and provide a Birdcage-type zeolite catalyst encapsulating small metal nanoparticles (Pt@silicalite-1).885 Pt@silicalite-1 with a low Pt content, 0.1 wt%, exhibited higher and more stable activity than Pt/silicalite-1 for DRM, at 670 °C, over 12 h, moderate 66–56% CO2 and 54–38% CH4 conversions with 0.8–0.6 H2/CO ratio (Table 11, entry 52). The superior catalytic performance of Pt@silicalite-1 was attributed to higher metal dispersion and good sintering resistance. Nevertheless, at 670 °C, rapid deactivation was recorded due to zeolite structure collapse, implying that the thermal stability of the zeolite could play an important role in the catalytic stability of Birdcage-type catalysts for the DRM process.
* CeO2–Ni/MCM-22 catalysts for DRM. The effect of ceria incorporation to metal nanoparticle supported catalysts has been largely reported due to the improvement of metal dispersion and stability, and NiO reducibility due to the presence of the high amount of oxygen vacancies and high oxygen mobilities. Accordingly, the catalytic performance for DRM of a series of Ni–MCM-22-based catalysts with different CeO2 contents, prepared by a sol–gel method, was recently reported.886 The catalytic data showed an optimum cerium content (8 wt%), and the best catalytic performance was obtained over the 8 wt% CeO2–9 wt% Ni/MCM-22 catalyst owing to the high oxygen storage capacity together with improved NiO reducibility (O2 and H2-TPR, Fig. 86). Higher CeO2 loading is unfavourable because Ni particles are partially covered. The characterization data revealed the stability of the spent catalyst structure at 750 °C, and that the addition of CeO2 enabled higher Ni nanoparticle dispersion and improved reducibility. Moreover, the presence of CeO2 in Ni/MCM-22 minimized coke deposition (3–4 wt%) on Ni due to the high oxygen storage capacity at the interface Ni/CeO–Si groups leading to easy oxidation of adsorbed carbon species into CO. Thus, at 750 °C, very good 83–75% CH4 and 99–92% CO2 conversions with H2 and CO selectivities over the 8 wt% CeO2–9 wt% Ni/MCM-22 catalyst with a H2/CO ratio around 0.84 were maintained for 60 h TOS (Table 11, entries 53 and 54). These results illustrated the great potential of MCM-22 to the development of the industrial catalyst although no patent was published.
 Fig. 86  (A) O2-TPD and (B) H2-TPR profiles of (a) 9Ni/MCM-22, (b) 4CeO2–9Ni/MCM-22, (c) 8CeO2–9Ni/MCM-22, and (d) 12CeO2–9Ni/MCM-22. Reprinted with permission from ref. 886. Copyright 2017, American Chemical Society. 
* FAU-based catalysts for DRM. – Co/Y-based catalysts for DRM. The use of sonochemical, plasma and microwave for materials synthesis has much interest due to the impacts of these catalyst preparation methods on physicochemical properties such as the dispersion and stability of metal nanoparticles, surface areas, reduced particle sizes, higher homogeneity, softened synthesis conditions (time and temperature) and reduced cost.887–891 Then, the effect of ultrasounds and Co-loading over the Y zeolite catalytic performance for DRM was shown to enhance the dispersion of Co nanoparticles and surface areas in comparison to impregnation methods,892 and nanoparticles with an average crystallite size of around 30–40 nm were formed. The best catalytic results were obtained for the 10 wt% Co content at a reaction temperature of 850 °C. The maximum 81% CH4 and 90% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio close to 0.76 were achieved, with good selectivity towards H2 and CO production, being those 75 and 88%, respectively, with catalyst moderate stability over 10 h TOS with a continuous decrease in CH4 and CO2 conversions (Table 11, entries 55–57).
– Catalytic performance of bimetallic Faujasite-based catalysts for DRM. The addition of a second metal, especially a noble metal and a rare earth metal, is well known to enhance activity, selectivity and the catalyst coke resistance in comparison to monometallic catalysts. The influence of different preparation methods, wetness impregnation and two solvent routes was compared for the preparation of bimetallic Ni–Rh/Y catalysts for DRM. The catalytic results showed an enhancement of the 7.5 wt% Ni/NaY catalytic properties prepared following two solvents routes.893 Moreover, the incorporation of Rh allowed improving the reducibility of NiO particles due to higher Ni dispersion and enhanced stability. With the 0.1 wt% Rh content, excellent 100% CH4 and CO2 conversions were achieved at 584 °C, respectively, while only 60% CH4 and CO2 conversions were reached at 559 °C, over 0.1 wt% Rh–7.5 wt% Ni/NaY (Table 11, entries 58 and 59). The interesting results were achieved at low temperatures along short 12 h TOS. A study of the Mn incorporation effect over the catalytic activity and stability of three Ni-based zeolite catalysts showed different impacts depending on the support, but the catalytic results were not improved (Table 11, entries 64–67).847 The positive effects of low Mn loading were the reduction of carbon deposition and the decrease of the Ni nanoparticle size and dispersion on the zeolite support. However, as the Mn loading increased, the crystallinity of the zeolite structure and its micropore surface area generally decreased.
– Effect of the basic properties of Ni/Y-based catalysts for DRM. As mentioned above, the presence of basic sites enables minimizing carbon deposition owing to the higher CO2 coverage of the catalytic surface and lower methane decomposition. Accordingly, MgO has been considered a suitable additive to DRM catalysts improving activity and stability.894,895 A study of Mg, Mn, K, and Ca additions onto Ni/HY catalysts revealed the improved resistance of the catalyst to coke deposition.894 Mg, Mn and Ca doping induces an increase in the activity and stability of the Ni/HY catalyst while K doping worsens them with high coke formation and reactor plugging. The BET and XRD characterization data indicated that the presence of Mg allowed reducing the Ni nanoparticle size and enhancing Ni dispersion owing to the partial coverage of NiO particles by MgOx. Moreover, the presence of basic sites favoured the CO2 coverage of the Ni–Mg/HY catalyst surface minimizing the CH4 decomposition and coke deposition. The other characterization data were not provided. At 700 °C, 93% CH4 and 89% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio close to 0.94 were maintained over 720 h (Table 11, entry 68). High catalytic performance was disclosed but no data of H2 and CO selectivities were provided and a high CH4/CO2 ratio of 3 was used.
* BEA zeolite-based catalysts for DRM. The beta zeolite was scarcely used as the support for the development of DRM catalysts. The catalytic activity of bimetallic based zeolite catalysts has been reported, specially incorporating Ni–Rh over MFI or FAU type zeolites due to the great influence of the support.896 Accordingly, the benefit of Rh incorporation into Ni/Beta zeolites was recently described.896 The Rh-beta sample exhibited the higher catalytic results and stability while the addition of Rh to the Ni catalyst allowed enhancing the activity and stability in comparison to the monometallic Ni sample. At 700 °C, 74% CH4 and 78% CO2 conversions and moderate selectivity to CO and H2 with a H2/CO of ∼1.17 were achieved over Rh–Ni/beta catalysts with good resistance to coke formation (2.85 wt%) (Table 11, entries 69–71). Because the presence of basic sites plays an important role over carbon deposition and CH4 decomposition, MgO has been considered a suitable additive to DRM catalysts improving activity and stability.894,895 Accordingly, the addition of MgO to Ni/beta catalysts was shown to enhance the catalytic performance and stability of the DRM catalyst.897 The Ni/10 wt% MgO-beta catalysts exhibited better activity with low carbon deposition (2.0 wt%) after 7.5 h TOS. At 800 °C, 95% CH4 conversion and 97% CO2 conversion with a H2/CO ratio close to 1.05 were achieved (Table 11, entry 72). Earlier, a comparison study of beta and Y zeolites as the support for Ni–Pt based catalysts was reported.898,899 Dealuminated Y and beta zeolites were used as supports for the preparation of bimetallic Ni and Pt catalysts. The dealumination process created mesopores enabling the higher dispersion of the metal nanoparticles and improving catalytic activity. The Y zeolite exhibited a higher total acidity in comparison to the beta zeolite. The catalytic performance of bimetallic Ni–Pt/zeolite catalysts revealed lower activity than that of monometallic Pt samples, 1.70 and 1.90 mol gmetal−1 h−1 for Ni–Pt/Y and Ni–Pt/β, respectively, and 2.0 and 3.20 mol gmetal−1 h−1 for Pt/Y and Pt/β, respectively, while Ni/Y and Ni/β were practically inactive due to severe carbon deposition (∼60 wt%) (Table 11, entries 60–63). The higher activity of bimetallic in comparison to Ni samples was attributed to the adsorption of CO2 on the adjacent Ni site together with Pt. However, the best catalytic data were achieved in the presence of the Pt-beta catalyst, due to the higher accessibility and stability of the beta and lower acidity in comparison to the Y zeolite that suffered strong carbon deposition. On the other hand, the Pt-beta catalyst exhibited higher activity and stability than Pt/Al2O3 and Pt/C due to superior resistance to coke deposition (16 wt%) attributed to improved Pt nanoparticle dispersion. Nevertheless, low catalytic results were reported, and in particular, the CH4 conversion decreased gradually on 24 h from 30 to 20%.
* DRM-combined technology.
– DRM-combined plasma technology. As described above, the non-equilibrium character of the plasma system enables overcoming the barrier energy and thermodynamic limitations at low temperatures in chemical processes such as CO2 conversions.668–670 Various types of non-thermal plasma exist and among them, DBD constitutes a versatile technique with some advantages such as the uniform distribution of micro-discharges, high-energy electrons and operability under ambient conditions.900 Accordingly, DRM was studied under thermal, plasma and plasma-assisted thermal conditions in the presence of Ni (15%) on Al2O3, TiO2 and ZSM-5.901 The best catalytic performance was achieved over Ni/Al2O3 under plasma conditions due to the Ni lower particle size and higher surface area. The best reaction conditions were reached under plasma (DBD) thermal conditions since the reactants can be previously activated in the DBD zone and react in the thermocatalytic reactor (Scheme 63). Moreover, the recombination of different carbon species can lead to the formation of hydrocarbons and alcohols. Good 76% CH4 and 71% CO2 conversions were obtained over 15Ni/ZSM-5, at 500 °C, under plasma-assisted thermal conditions, while 73% CH4 and 68% CO2 conversions were achieved under thermal conditions (Table 11, entries 73 and 74). No data of stability were provided.
 Scheme 63  Equations of the formation of different reactant species and reactions under plasma activation conditions. 
Few years earlier, a study of the DRM process under DBD plasma conditions in the presence of the 4A zeolite was reported.902,903 The effects of the peak voltage, total gas flow rate and CO2/CH4 ratio over CO2 and CH4 conversions and H2/CO were studied. At a high-applied voltage level (from 6 kV to 12 kV), the high energy electrons generated in the plasma discharge zone may dissociate CO2 and CH4 molecules in the gas phase and on the catalyst surface. The results of the DBD process showed that CO2 and CH4 conversions increased by the increase of the peak voltage that greatly affected the H2/CO ratio that varied from 1.8 to 1.3. For the DBD process with the catalyst, the H2/CO ratio was close to 1 for all applied voltages, indicating a simultaneous production of H2 and CO due to catalytic reactions. Therefore, the best catalytic results were reached with 12 kV, 900 Hz and CO2/CH4 with 50% CO2 conversion, 46% CH4 conversion and H2/CO ∼ 1 in the presence of the zeolite (Table 11, entries 75 and 76).
– DRM + POM (partial oxidation of methane) combined processes. To date, the DRM commercialization is not envisioned due to strong limitations such as high energy consumption due to the endothermic process, rapid catalyst deactivation because of coke deposition and water formation that reduce the syngas selectivity and the H2/CO ratio. The possible combination of DRM and POM (partial oxidation of methane) processes constitutes a promising alternative to DRM commercialization since it presents several advantages and allows counteracting the deficiencies of the stand-alone DRM (Scheme 64). The combination of DRM/POM is supposed to improve heat transfer due to the coupling of both exothermic and endothermic reactions leading to a more efficient energy process, while the variation in the feed composition allows tailoring the H2/CO ratio, and the O2 addition limits the coke formation and improves the CH4 conversion and catalyst stability.904–906 Ni-based catalysts are suitable for the development of combined POM + DRM owing to the high nickel activity, availability and price. Moreover, in the two last decades, great efforts have been made to improve the activity, robustness and stability of Ni-based catalysts through the addition of different types and nature of promoters, control of Ni nanoparticle size and dispersion, Ni nanoparticle confinement and Ni improved reducibility, being Ni-based catalysts good candidates for industrial implementation.
 Scheme 64  Equation of POM + DRM reactions. 
Efforts in developing a new class of supported catalysts based on silicon carbide (SiC) showed the SiC great mechanical and thermal properties and inertness. SiC can be prepared in commercially viable shapes into monolith or honeycomb. These desirable properties make SiC a valuable support in comparisons to conventional ones (SiO2, Al2O3, and ZrO2).907 The honeycomb-shaped SiC as the catalyst support can strengthen the heat transfer and mass transfer and due to its hardness and light weight it is an attractive catalyst support for the fluidized bed reactor. Then, the silicalite-1 zeolite was deposited on the honeycomb-like monolithic SiC foam by hydrothermal synthesis. The Ni-silicalite-1-coated SiC (Ni/S-1/SiC) catalyst was so prepared by impregnation and used for combined DRM and POM (Fig. 87).908 POM is a mildly exothermic reaction and enables the production of syngas. Nevertheless, due to the presence of a hot spot and explosion danger, this reaction can be applied only in combination with the endothermic reaction such as DRM. The Ni/S-1/SiC monolith catalyst exhibited high catalytic performance and stability for DRM/POM processes over 30 h TOS, and higher than Ni/S-1 and Ni/SiC catalysts. At 750 °C, 0.1 MPa, 56.1% CH4 and 52.1% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio close to 1.07 were maintained for 30 h TOS with a CH4:CO2:O2 = 8:4:2 ratio (Table 11, entry 77). On the other hand, the H2/CO molar ratio obtained over the Ni/S-1/SiC catalyst could be optimised by varying the CH4:CO2:O2 ratio. The better catalytic results of the Ni/S-1/SiC monolith catalyst were attributed to the strong interactions between the SiC support and metal Ni particles improved by silicalite-1 coated SiC, inhibiting the aggregation and sintering of Ni particles. The TG analysis of the used catalyst revealed that the Ni/S-1/SiC monolith catalyst showed good resistance to coke deposition being 4 wt%.
 Fig. 87  Ni-silicalite-1-coated SiC (Ni/S-1/SiC) catalyst for combined DRM and POM. Reprinted with permission from ref. 908. Copyright 2017, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
Another strategy to enhance the catalyst stability is the addition of a second metal improving the distribution of Ni over the support due to the formation of strong metal–support interactions and smaller metallic particle sizes.909 Accordingly, the mixing of Co and Ni resulted to enhance the catalyst activity and stability for the DRM process.836,910,911 Following these approaches, Ni, Co, and Ni–Co supported on ZSM-5 synthesized by the microwave assisted hydrothermal method (ZSM-5mw) and on the commercial sample (ZSM-5HT) were prepared and used as catalysts for POM and DRM processes to syngas production.912 The Ni and Ni–Co based ZSM-5mw and based ZSM-5HT catalysts exhibited high catalytic activity for POM and DRM processes with a low 2 wt% of the metal content with a syngas yield up to 97% in the temperature range of 850–950 °C (Table 11, entries 78–80). The simultaneous presence of Ni and Co increased the resistance to coke deposition for the DRM reaction and prevented the formation of carbon fibres usually observed on Ni catalysts. The authors did not explain the higher resistance of the bimetallic catalyst to coke deposition and only described the formation of Co3O4 for the Ni–Co based ZSM-5 samples.
(ii) Conclusion. Catalytic dry reforming of methane is an important strategy to mitigate the emissions of CH4 and CO2 greenhouse gases. Ni based-zeolite catalysts owing to the huge properties of zeolites such as the high surface area and thermal stability offer opportunities for the preparation of DRM catalysts. Moreover, different strategies have been reported to improve the dispersion and stabilization of nickel particles by the addition of a suitable second metal (promoter), enabling the establishment of new electronic metal/metal and metal/support interactions that enhance the NiO reducibility and coke resistance owing to the oxidation of carbon species deposited on the Ni particle surface, or by the control of acidity/basicity through the incorporation of alkali metal promoting CO2 adsorption and activation (C–O cleavage). Thus, great opportunities for the development of robust catalysts based on zeolites have been reported and the results summarised in the Table 11 illustrate the high activity of zeolite-based catalysts for the DRM reaction at high temperatures. The high energy consumption remains the main drawback of the technology that will be produced from renewable (wind, water, and sun) or nuclear sources. Considering CO2 conversion and ratio H2/CO as functions of GHSV and TOS some results can be highlighted as shown in Fig. 88 and 89 where Ni/ZSM-5(30), 5 wt% Ni–5 wt% Ce/ZSM-5, La–Ni/ZSM-5 cylinder, Ta–Ni/FZSM-5, 5 wt% Mg–Ni13 wt%/HY and 10 wt% Ni–10 wt% MgO/BEA (Table 11, entries 15, 29, 27, 41, 68 and 72) exhibited the interesting catalytic performance. Nevertheless, only for 5 wt% Ni–5 wt% Ce/ZSM-5, the data of H2 and/or CO2 yield and/or selectivity are given and so the productivity can only be evaluated in this case.
 Fig. 88  CO2 conversion was plotted versus GHSV for different DRM zeolite-based catalysts. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high conversion versus GHSV. 
 Fig. 89  H2/CO ratio was plotted versus TOS (h) for different DRM zeolite-based catalysts. 
(b) OMS-based catalysts for DRM. 
Different strategies have been followed to improve the catalytic properties, robustness and stability of Ni based catalysts towards Ni sintering and coke deposition, such as the incorporation of noble metals and transition metals to enhance the reducibility and dispersion of Ni, or of alkali and alkaline earth metals to create basic sites enhancing the adsorption and activation of CO2 (C–O cleavage), or the confinement of nanoparticles inside hollow, core–shell or mesoporous structures. Accordingly, OMSs have been explored as supports, especially to control the nanoparticle size, dispersion and stability owing to the high surface area and controllable pore size providing the beneficial spatial confinement effect to prevent Ni particle sintering. Recently, a study of the role of the pore size and the wall thickness of a series of mesoporous structures including MCM-41, KIT-6 and SBA-15 over the activity of Ni-catalysts prepared by the impregnation method for DRM was reported.913 The results indicated that small pore diameters and thin silica walls of MCM-41 were responsible for lower catalytic performance due to low ability to prevent Ni sintering. Thus, Ni–MCM-41 exhibited the lower activity in comparison to Ni-SBA-15 and Ni-KIT-6 (Table 12, entries 1–3). Previously, a comparative study of Ni supports over OMS and zeolites showed that CH4 and CO2 conversions were dependent on Ni particle average size that was controlled by the high specific area of the support, strong metal–support interaction and metal reducibility.914 Thus, larger Ni particles (23–29 nm) were preferentially obtained from more readily reduced Ni owing to the weaker interaction with the zeolite support. In contrast, smaller Ni particles (9–11 nm) were formed from slowly reduced Ni due to the strong interaction with the SBA-15 and Al-MCM-41 supports through Ni–O–Si or Ni–Al bonds. Moreover, smaller particles exhibited a higher sintering resistance and the formation of filamentous coke did not induce catalytic deactivation. Filamentous carbon deposition was observed by SEM and from DTG profiles, with amount increasing in the following order Ni/Al-MCM-41 > Ni/SBA-15 > Ni/MFI and negligible for Ni/FAU. At 750 °C, over Ni/Al-MCM-41 and Ni/MFI, 94 and 70–60% CH4 conversions and 93 and 65–55% CO2 conversions were achieved, respectively, along 12 h TOS (Table 12, entries 4–7). These results showed the higher physicochemical properties exhibited by OMSs than those by zeolites to the support and disperse metallic active sites, and design and develop robust and efficient DRM catalysts.
