(i) Ni/SBA-15-based catalysts for DRM. In 2006, the confinement effect of Ni nanoparticles into the stable mesoporous structure of SBA-15 was reported.915 The catalytic performance of Ni/SBA-15 with different Ni contents prepared by impregnation exhibited excellent and stable 90% CO2 and CH4 conversions at 800 °C, over 600 h of TOS, with 92 and 94% H2 and CO selectivities, respectively, over 12.5% Ni/SBA-15 (Table 12, entry 8). After 600 h of TOS, CO2 and CH4 conversions, the catalysts suffer severe deactivation due to coke deposition (14.8 wt%). An additional study of catalyst regeneration would have been the key. The reported catalyst exhibited excellent catalytic performance over the long-term test and its potential to the development of the industrial catalyst should be considered.
* Effect of the preparation method of Ni/SBA-15-based catalysts for DRM. The preparation method of Ni supported catalysts can strongly affect the metal dispersion, particle size and interaction with the support. Therefore, different methods have been reported with the aim to improve the dispersion and stability of metal nanoparticles and favour the strong metal–support interaction. Recently, the catalytic behavior of previously prepared Ni nanoparticles with a homogeneous size of ∼5 nm and further supported on ordered mesoporous silica (SBA-15), alumina and commercial silica was described.916 The characterization and catalytic data revealed that the ordered mesoporous structures were more suitable to stabilize Ni nanoparticles due to the beneficial confinement effects preventing nanoparticle aggregation. The presence of Lewis acid sites in the case of the alumina support was responsible for higher coke formation at a lower temperature. Ni/SBA-15 with 1 wt% Ni exhibited the best catalytic properties. A study of the reaction temperature revealed that 800 °C was the optimum temperature to perform DRM avoiding the strong aggregation phenomenon of Ni nanoparticles that took place at 850 °C. Indeed, at 800 and 850 °C, 91.4 and 45.9% CH4 conversions, 91 and 70% CO2 conversions were reached with 0.79 and 0.97 H2/CO ratios, respectively, over Ni/SBA-15 (Table 12, entries 9–12). The use of organic solvents as dispersants and stabilizing agents for the preparation of supported metallic nanoparticles has been recently developed. The use of ethylene glycol (EG) to support Ni (11 wt%) on SBA-15 as the solvent and the delivery conveyor of the Ni precursor inside the SBA-15 pore systems was first described in 2014.917 The resulting catalyst exhibited improved coke and sintering resistances owing to the confinement effect of SBA-15 and smaller Ni nanoparticles. At 750 °C, 96% CH4 and 87% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio of ∼1 were maintained over Ni/SBA-15-EG along 20 h TOS (Table 12, entries 13 and 14). Later, EG was newly used as the solvent and the delivery conveyor to study the spatial confinement effect of SBA-15 walls over Ni/SBA-15 physicochemical and catalytic features.918 High dispersion of Ni nanoparticles (7 wt%) with a small particle size (∼3.1 nm) embedded into the Al-SBA-15 mesostructure was successfully achieved. The comparison of the catalytic behavior of Ni/SBA-15 catalysts revealed that the sample prepared with EG exhibited better catalytic activity. Moreover, a study of catalytic stability showed that Al conferred higher hydrothermal stability to the Ni/Al-SBA-15-EG catalyst and potential for the prolonged use under harsh conditions. The benefit of the confinement effect to prevent sintering was demonstrated with average Ni particle sizes of 3.2 and 3.6 nm for fresh and spent catalysts, respectively. At 700 °C, 70 and 72% CH4 conversions and 79% CO2 conversion were achieved over Ni/SBA-15-EG and Ni/Al-SBA-15-EG, with 0.91 and 0.95 H2/CO ratios, respectively, along 15 h TOS (Table 12, entries 15–17). The effect of different impregnation techniques (impregnation (IM), rotary evaporator- (RE), shaker (SH) and ultrasounds (US)) for the preparation of 3 wt% Ni/SBA-15 catalysts from waste POFA (palm oil fuel ash) over their catalytic and physicochemical properties was recently investigated.919 The characterization and catalytic data revealed that the impregnation method influenced the Ni particle size and dispersion, and interaction with the support through the formation of Si–O–Ni bonds (FTIR spectra) with the following order of the Ni-support interaction for Ni/SBA-15 samples: RE < SH < IM < US. The basicity distribution of the Ni-containing mesoporous catalysts determined by CO2-TPD evidenced three peaks with different temperature regions, indicating three types of basic active sites for CO2 adsorption on the catalyst surface with different strengths (Fig. 90). The Ni/SBA-15 sample prepared by impregnation under ultrasound exhibited the higher amount of strong basic sites. Finally, the catalytic study showed that the activity and stability varied with the preparation method in the following order: US > IM > SH > RE. Ultrasounds showed to provide the catalysts with a smaller Ni particle size with good dispersion and stronger interactions with the support responsible for the higher catalytic activity and resistance to Ni sintering and coke deposition of the 3 wt% Ni/SBA-15(US) sample. Thus, 80% CO2 conversion and 95–90% CH4 conversions with a H2/CO ratio of 0.89 were maintained over 24 h TOS at 800 °C in the presence of the 3 wt% Ni/SBA-15(US) catalyst (Table 12, entries 18–21).
 Fig. 90  Amount of CO2-TPD of 3 wt% SBA-15 and Ni/SBA-15 prepared by IM, RE, SH and US. Reprinted with permission from ref. 919. Copyright 2019, Hydrogen Energy Publications LLC. Published by Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
On the other hand, the impact of homogeneous precipitation (HP) and impregnation (imp) preparation methods over the catalytic performance of Ni/SBA-15 samples was also compared.920 The characterization results revealed high dispersion and a small size (2–5 nm) of Ni nanoparticles and improved NiO reducibility owing to the strong metal–support interaction with improved sintering resistance. Consequently, the catalytic results showed the better catalytic performance of Ni/SBA-15(HP) than that of Ni/SBA-15(imp). At 800 °C and 100 h TOS, good and stable 74% and 80% CH4 and CO2 conversions with H2/CO up to 0.9 were achieved over Ni/SBA-15(HP) and with certain amount of filamentous coke deposition (Table 12, entries 22 and 23). Previously, the influence of the three Ni/SBA-15 preparation methods, impregnation (IM), precipitation (PM) and precipitation-reduction with ascorbic acid (RM), over their physicochemical and catalytic properties was investigated.921 The characterization data revealed that confined and small Ni particle sizes (∼5 nm) were obtained through PM and RM methods, while through the IM route, Ni was mainly present on the outer support surface with worst dispersion and a higher particle size (∼12 nm). Moreover, different Ni-active phases were dispersed and Ni-species met in the IM sample are NiO species, and those met in the PM sample are only phyllosilicate species, while in the RM sample both phyllosilicate species and NiO-species are met. During the DRM process, the IM catalyst suffered rapid deactivation, while PM and RM catalysts exhibited improved stability, and the PM catalyst showed slightly higher activity and selectivity than the RM sample, resulting in a higher H2/CO ratio (0.87). Thus, 8 wt% Ni/SBA-15(RM) exhibited higher catalytic performance with stable 65% CH4 and 90% CO2 conversions, at 600 °C, over 24 h TOS (Table 12, entries 24–26). Moreover, lower coke deposition in the form of filamentous carbon was formed, closed to 11 wt% for the RM catalyst while 46 wt% was formed over the IM catalyst inducing severe deactivation. The effect of the use of different chelating agents such as ethylene diamine (en), acetic (ace) and citric (cit) acids and without (conventional (cm)) to prepare Ni/SBA-15 by impregnation over Ni dispersion and nanoparticle size was recently examined.922 The benefits of electron-pair donor atoms (O, N) of ligands such as ethylenediamine and citric acid, which possess two and three electron-pair donor atoms respectively, able to form a chelate ring with a single metal with strong steric hindrance, in contrast to citric acid that will always give rise to a multimetal ring structure favourable to metal agglomeration, were shown. Moreover, the viscosity of the chelated metal solution is higher than the solution of inorganic salts, reducing the evaporation of the solvent that prevents the agglomeration of metal particles. Therefore, the assisted chelating agent impregnation method allowed preparing catalysts with highly dispersed and ultrafine Ni nanoparticles (4–6 nm) and with the improved coke deposition resistance. At 800 °C, all the samples initially exhibited high activity while the stability test, over 50 h TOS, showed that samples issued from the modified impregnation method exhibited higher and stable activity with good CO2 and CH4 conversions up to 85% and 80%, respectively (Table 12, entries 27–31). The characterisation data determined a coke formation of less than 10–20 wt% for the catalysts prepared in the presence of the chelating agent, while 40 wt% coke was analysed for the sample prepared by the conventional method.
Ni nanoparticle deposition through the conventional impregnation method is usually controlled by diffusion and by bulk oxide type formation with the weak metal–support interaction. Consequently, the resistance to sintering of Ni nanoparticles is low. As an alternative, a new ammonia evaporation (AE) method was developed where the solvated metal precursor by ammonia is dispersed over the surface of the support which is in suspension in aqueous solution and a subsequent heating evaporates ammonia leading to the hydrolysis of the metal–ammonia complex. Following this synthetic route, the Ni/SBA-15 catalyst was prepared and the catalytic performance checked and compared to the results obtained in the presence of a sample prepared by the conventional impregnation method.923 Therefore, a smaller Ni particle size from 3.05 to 5.17 nm, higher dispersion and homogeneously distribution with an average diameter of 4.09 nm inside the mesopores were obtained following the described AE method, leading to the strong metal–support interaction. Then, a higher metal/support interaction allows achieving the metal with a smaller particle size. In contrast, the broad range of a particle size from 2.47 to 17.1 nm were obtained for the Ni/SBA-15(IM) sample prepared by impregnation. Therefore, even if both catalysts exhibited high initial activity, they showed different stabilities along 100 h TOS. The Ni/SBA-15(AE) catalyst displayed high coke deposition and sintering resistance owing to strong metal–support interactions. Thus, at 750 °C, 75% CH4 and 80% CO2 conversions were maintained over Ni/SBA-15(AE) over 100 h TOS with H2/CO close to 0.85 (Table 12, entries 32–35). All these results showed the impacts of the preparation method on metal dispersion and the particle size controlled by the metal–support interaction, and its impact on the catalytic activity, stability and the resistance of Ni-particles to sintering and coke deposition.
* Preparation of bimetallic Ni/SBA-15-based catalysts for DRM. The impacts of the addition of a suitable second metal or a promoter are well known to modify the electronic properties of Ni and contribute to enhance the dispersion and stabilization of metal nanoparticles by establishing the strong metal–support interaction and thus promoting the catalytic activity, stability and coke resistance.
– Effect of the addition of rare earth metals to Ni/SBA-15-based catalysts for DRM. The benefits of the incorporation of CeO2 have been well described to enhance the catalytic properties and stability of metal-based catalysts by establishing the strong metal–support interaction, providing high oxygen mobility and the formation of active sites at the interface metal/support. Accordingly, the effect of the Ce loading over the catalytic properties of the prepared Ni/SBA-15 by the sol–gel method was explored for DRM.924 The results showed that the optimum 7 wt% Ce content existed corresponding to high catalytic activity and good catalytic stability with 95% CH4 and CO2 conversions, and ∼1 H2/CO ratio, at 700 °C for short 6 h TOS (Table 12, entries 40 and 41). The incorporation of Ce improved Ni dispersion and the interaction of metal–support and minimized coke deposition due to its ability to oxidize carbon species formed on Ni particles. Previously, a study of Ce incorporation into the physicochemical and catalytic features of Ni–Ce/SBA-15 revealed the promoting effect over Ni nanoparticle dispersion and the increase of oxygen mobility and activity owing to Ce incorporation.925 Ce was incorporated by the direct hydrothermal method, favouring high Ce dispersion onto the SBA-15 surface that in turn promoted the formation of small and highly dispersed Ni nanoparticles deposited by the impregnation method. A H2-TPR study showed shifts of the high temperature reduction peak to a lower temperature indicating a change in NiO reducibility attributed to the surface oxygen species on Ni/Ce-SBA-15 samples with higher mobility and activity when increasing the Ce content due to the Ce redox properties and the formation of oxygen vacancies (Fig. 91).926,927 Moreover, the presence of oxygen species on the catalytic surface helps preventing the carbon formation. The best catalytic performance was achieved over 12 wt% Ni/Ce-SBA-15 (Si/Ce = 0.04) with good ∼78% CO2 and 76% CH4 conversions over 40 h TOS at 700 °C (Table 12, entries 42–44).
 Fig. 91  H2-TPR profiles of Ni/Ce-SBA-15 (R = Ce/Si) samples. Reprinted with permission from ref. 925. Copyright 2011, Hydrogen Energy Publications, LLC. Published by Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
Recently, a study of the addition of La and Ce to 10 wt% Ni/SBA-15 catalysts by the co-impregnation method over the physical and chemical properties of the catalysts showed that the incorporation of the promoters enabled reducing the Ni nanoparticle size and improve the dispersion of metallic particles.928 The TPO analysis enabled evaluating the coke deposition amount with the following decreasing order 10 wt% Ni/SBA-15 > 3 wt% Ce–10 wt% Ni/SBA-15 > 3 wt% La–10 wt% Ni/SBA-15 revealing that the promoter addition improved the coke deposition resistance because of their basic properties, favouring CO2 adsorption and dissociation, and carbon oxidation and posterior elimination. Thus, the best catalytic activity and stability were achieved over the 3 wt% La–10%Ni/SBA-15 sample with 93% CH4 and 95% CO2 conversions at 750 °C for 24 h TOS (Table 12, entries 45–47). The La addition to the highly dispersed Ni/SBA-15 catalyst following a new synthetic route using oleic acid as the precursor was previously investigated.929 The benefits of the La incorporation in promoting the strong metal–support interaction was shown by a H2-TPR study. An increase in the La amount from 1 to 2 wt% resulted in an increase in the amount of strongly bound NiO species and a simultaneous decrease in the amount of weakly bound NiO species on the support, while a further increase produced the opposite effect. The deconvoluted TPR at T > 600 °C allowed quantifying the strong NiO/support interaction and evaluated the strong metal–support interaction degree after the normalization of metal–support interactions. Therefore, a correlation between the strong metal–support interactions and CH4 conversion was established (Fig. 92). Moreover, 1% La–Ni/SBA-15 and 2% La–Ni/SBA-15 catalysts had the smallest metal particle sizes, 2.8 and 3.2 nm, respectively, due to the stronger metal–support interaction. Thus, an optimum in La loading of 1 wt% was determined based on the analysis of catalytic performance and characterization data (Ni nanoparticle size, dispersion and stability). At 700 °C, over 1 wt% La–5 wt% Ni/SBA-15, ∼84% CH4 and ∼86% CO2 conversions along brief 12 h TOS were achieved (Table 12, entries 48 and 49). Additionally, CO2 isotopic studies evidenced that CH4 dissociation took place over highly dispersed Ni active centres while CO2 was adsorbed and decomposed on La active centres and enabled the oxidation of deposited carbon on the surface, improving the catalytic stability and minimizing carbon deposition.
 Fig. 92  Relationship between the La content, strong metal–support interaction degree (%) and CH4 conversion. Reprinted with permission from ref. 929. Copyright 2016, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
The benefits of Sm doping over the catalytic and physicochemical properties of Ni and Co/SBA-15 prepared in two steps by the two-solvent impregnation method was also examined for DRM.930 Different tendencies for Sm addition to 10 wt% Co and 10 wt% Ni/SBA-15 were observed. The incorporation of 1 wt% Sm enabled improving the dispersion and reduce the particle size of NiO mainly located inside the mesopores while in the case of Co3O4, higher agglomeration and lower dispersion were observed with particles located mainly on the external surface. A H2-TPR study confirmed that stronger metal/support interactions are established after Sm doping in the case of NiO promoting to a lower particle size and higher sintering resistance. Therefore, the catalytic activity study confirmed the positive effect of the Sm addition to the Ni/SBA-15 sample due to higher Ni dispersion, strong metal–support interaction, and improved oxygen storage capacity. In contrast, a negative effect was observed over the catalytic performance of Co/SBA-15 after the addition of Sm attributed to the favoured Co oxidation and deactivation. Thus, at 700 °C, the medium catalytic results were obtained over 1 wt% Sm–10 wt% Ni/SBA-15 with 58–56% CH4 and 66–64% CO2 conversions over brief 5 h TOS while a small amount of filament type carbon deposition was observed (Table 12, entries 50 and 51).
– Effect of the addition of transition metals to Ni/SBA-15-based catalysts. Studies of the preparation of Ni–Co bimetallic catalysts showed the benefits of the formation of Ni–Co alloy that enhanced the catalytic stability in comparison to monometallic samples mainly due to the accelerated CH4 decomposition and prevention of Co0 oxidation.836,931 Accordingly, the catalytic performance of bimetallic Ni–Co/SBA-15 versus the monometallic catalyst for DRM was recently studied and revealed the positive synergetic effect between Ni and Co.932 The Co incorporation enabled preventing the sintering of Ni nanoparticles due to the formation of Ni–Co alloy that allowed avoiding the reoxidation of Co and reducing coke deposition. At 750 °C, up to 79% CH4 and 89% CO2 conversions were obtained over 4 wt% Ni–1 wt% Co/SBA-15 (Table 12, entry 52). Previously, the catalytic behavior of bimetallic Ni–Co/SBA-15 with 13 wt% Ni and different Co contents (N = wt%) was studied for the DRM reaction.933 The CO2 desorption results showed that Co doping improved the basic properties since three CO2 desorption peaks were recorded for the CN8 sample corresponding to weak (393 °C), moderate (527 °C), and strong (698 °C) basic sites (Fig. 93). The enhanced basicity will boosted CO2 adsorption and dissociation which would supply oxygen species to effectively improve the ability of CO2 in oxidizing carbon species formed from CH4 decomposition, and preventing coke formation. The optimum Co content existed, while Co excess favoured the formation of a large number of O species inducing Co0 oxidation and catalyst deactivation. Moreover, the morphology of carbon deposition varied with the presence of Co, besides on Ni/SBA-15 coke was present as shell-like inducing severe deactivation, on Co–Ni/SBA-15 coke adopted whisker form with a minor poisoning effect. Very good catalytic performance was obtained over the 2 wt% Co–13 wt% Ni/SBA-15 catalyst, at 800 °C, with 76% CH4 and 90% CO2 conversions for the first 300 h of TOS and 80% CH4 and 80% CO2 conversion values for the following 500 h of TOS (Table 12, entries 53 and 54). A similar catalytic behavior was reported for bimetallic Ni–Co/SBA-15 prepared by a β-cyclodextrin modified impregnation method.934 The confinement of SBA-15 and Ni–Co synergetic effects enabled improving the activity and stability of Ni–Co/SBA-15 catalysts. The best catalytic performance was achieved over 4.5 wt% Ni–0.5 wt% Co/SBA-15 and 3 wt% Ni–2 wt% Co/SBA-15 with 90 and 88% CH4 conversions, and 95% CO2 conversion, respectively, at 800 °C for 30 h TOS (Table 12, entries 55 and 56).
 Fig. 93  CO2-TPD profiles of Ni/Co-SBA-15 (N = Co wt%) samples. Reprinted with permission from ref. 933. Copyright 2015, Taylor & Francis. 
The effects of titanium nitride (TiN) incorporation into Ni-SBA-15 by the hydrothermal method and further impregnated with Ni over textural and catalytic features were explored.935 Titanium nitride (TiN) presents interesting electronic properties, basic properties, high density, great surface area and high thermal and oxidation stabilities. The test of activity showed that both CH4 and CO2 conversions increased with Ni loading while the incorporation of TiN enhanced the catalytic activity of Ni-based catalysts owing to basicity improvement favourable to CO2 absorption and dissociation, and the creation of strong interaction between TiN, Ni and the support. The stability test showed that the presence of TiN allowed lowering carbon deposition. The moderate catalytic results were obtained with 70–65% CH4 and ∼70% CO2 conversions at 700 °C, over 10 wt% Ni/5 wt% TiN-SBA-15 for 12 h TOS (Table 12, entries 57–59).
Years ago, a DRM study of the catalytic activity of molybdenum carbide (Mo2C) revealed that these catalysts exhibited a high resistance to coke deposition similar to noble metals.936 The other report showed that the addition of a suitable Ni amount to MoO3 improved the Mo2C catalytic activity and stability for DRM.937 Based on these results, a Ni/Mo2C/SBA-15 catalyst was prepared by the impregnation of SBA-15 in order to take advantage of nickel deactivation by coke deposition to enable carbonaceous species migration onto Mo2C preventing Mo2C oxidation and accelerating carbon elimination.938 The Ni/Mo2C/SBA-15 catalyst exhibited very high catalytic performance along 130 h, with 98% CH4 and 95% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio close to 0.8 at 800 °C (Table 12, entries 60 and 61). Nevertheless, besides these promising results, no further study was pursued.
– Effect of the addition of noble metals to Ni/SBA-15-based catalysts. Noble metals exhibit higher resistance against coke formation and sintering than Ni at high temperatures. Accordingly, the benefits of Rh doping over the catalytic performance of Ni-SBA-15 for DRM were explored. The comparison of the catalytic data of Ni/SBA-15 and Rh–Ni/SBA-15 samples showed the Rh promoting effect over catalytic activity and stability with a small Rh content (0.02 wt%) due to the electronic interaction between Ni and Rh enabling higher metal dispersion and improving NiO species reducibility. Moreover, the formation of Rh–Ni species promotes the activation and dissociation of CO2 with active oxygen release for the gasification of deposited carbon.939 At 650 °C, 66–59% CH4 and 79–78% CO2 conversions were achieved over 0.02 wt% Rh–10 wt% Ni/SBA-15 for 30 h TOS (Table 12, entries 62 and 63).
* Preparation of trimetallic Ni/SBA-15-based catalysts for DRM. The influence of the impregnation method in two or three steps and the Ce–Zr incorporation over the physicochemical and catalytic features of Ni/SBA-15 catalysts for DRM was explored. Owing to their redox properties, Zr and Ce generate high amount of oxygen vacancies near the Ni particle that favours carbon removal from the metallic surface. The catalytic study showed that Ce–Zr addition enhanced the Ni/SBA-15 catalyst selectivity and stability owing to the decrease of the Ni particle size and higher reducibility of NiO species.940 Over 6 wt% Ce–4 wt% Zr–10 wt% Ni/SBA-15 50–40% CH4 and 66–58% CO2 conversions were achieved along 30 h TOS, with a H2/CO ratio close to 0.78, at a low temperature (600 °C). Low 2 wt% coke deposition was determined owing to the high amount of Ce and Zr oxygen vacancies (Table 12, entries 64 and 65). The same group also reported the effect of the preparation method of Ce–Zr–Ni/SBA-15 following, one and two-step impregnation and co-precipitation routes over their physicochemical and catalytic properties.941 The Co-precipitation method allows achieving the sample with confined Ni phyllosilicate with a stronger metal–support interaction while by the impregnation method pore blockage occurred with Ni particles mostly located on the outer surface support. It was concluded that the catalyst prepared by co-precipitation did not favour methane decomposition, thus coke deposition was minimized and the catalytic stability was slightly improved, with 63–61% CH4 and 71–68% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio of ∼0.85 maintained along 24 h TOS at 600 °C (Table 12, entries 66–68). A study of the benefits of the incorporation of CeO2 into the Ni–Mo/SBA-15 catalyst showed the high catalytic performance of both Ni–Mo/SBA-15 and CeO2–Ni–Mo/SBA-15 catalysts. CeO2–Ni–Mo/SBA-15 exhibited slightly higher activity and stability over 100 h, supporting the benefits of the CeO2 incorporation.942 CO2 desorption revealed three peaks and so three kinds of basic sites for the CeO2–Ni–Mo/SBA-15 sample, while N2 adsorption determined a higher specific surface area and pore volume for the CeO2–Ni–Mo/SBA-15 sample. These characterization data indicated that CeO2 lowered the acidity of the support preventing carbon formation by methane decomposition while basic sites enhance the adsorption and dissociation of CO2 and oxygen release to carbon gasification. Moreover, the CeO2 improved dispersion of the metallic active phase in SBA-15 and the reducibility of NiO particles (H2-TPR study). Then, 90% CH4 and 98% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio of 0.9 were maintained over 100 h and no coke deposition was formed, at 800 °C over CeO2–Ni–Mo/SBA-15 (Table 12, entries 69 and 70). Few years ago, a study of the catalytic performance of Ni–Mo/SBA-15 doped with La2O3 revealed that the small amount of La2O3 allowed improving Ni dispersion and stability owing to the spatial confinement effects of SBA-15 mesopores and the creation of the strong interaction with the support, while the formation of La2O2CO3 favoured the elimination of carbon species from the catalytic surface preventing coke deposition (Fig. 81).943 Very good 92% CH4 and 96% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio of 0.9 were maintained over long 200 h TOS in the presence of the Ni–Mo/SBA-15-La2O3 catalyst (Table 12, entries 71 and 72). Unfortunately, no data of H2 and CO selectivity were provided. The catalytic performance of Co–Ni/Sc-SBA-15 catalysts with 5 wt% Co, 5 wt% Ni and different Sc contents prepared by succesive impregnation was also reported for DRM.944 Co and Ni were successfully and highly dispersed in SBA-15 while the addition of Sc improved their resistance to sintering. The Sc incorporation allowed increasing the basicity of the catalyst and thus the adsorption and activation of CO2. Consequently the higher oxygen coverage enabled minimizing carbon formation due to easily oxidation into CO. Finally, Sc incorporation also improved the catalytic performance of Ni–Mo/SBA-15, and high 92–87% CH4 conversion and 93–92% CO2 conversion were maintained over 40 h with a H2/CO ratio close to 1 at 800 °C over the 5Co–5Ni/5Sc-SBA-15 catalyst (Table 12, entries 73–75).
* Effect of the basic properties of Ni/SBA-15-based catalysts for DRM. As aforementioned, the basic properties influence strongly the CO2 adsorption and dissociation, while the CO2 coverage of the catalytic surface allows avoiding CH4 adsorption and decomposition and favours carbon species oxidation. Therefore, the benefits of the basic properties of DRM catalysts have been largely investigated. Accordingly, the catalytic features of a series of MgO/SBA-15 catalysts prepared by one pot synthesis and further impregnated with the 10 wt% Ni and MgO–Ni/SBA-15 samples were compared.945 Through hydrothermal synthesis, MgO was successfully coated on SBA-15 walls while the mesoporous structure was maintained. It was shown that the coating of MgO enabled a decrease in the NiO particle size with higher dispersion, which provide more active sites for DRM. Morevover, MgO incorporation improved the surface basicity enhancing catalytic stability and coke resistance. A CO2-TPD study revealed the appearance of medium and strong basic sites on the Ni/8MgO-SBA-15 sample indicating the formation of superbasicity on mesoporous silica (Fig. 94). Thus, the best catalytic performance was obtained over 8 wt% MgO–10 wt% Ni/SBA-15 prepared by the one-pot method, with medium 71–68% CH4 and 66–62% CO2 conversions, at 800 °C over 40 h TOS (Table 12, entries 76–78). Carbon deposition was responsible for catalyst deactivation in the form of filamentous carbon species. At the same time, the preparation and catalytic performance of Mg-modified Ni/SBA-15 with different Mg loading contents were examined.946 Similar features and results were obtained. The Mg addition improved Ni dispersion and the basic properties of the catalyst owing to the strong metal–support interaction and consequently, coke resistance and catalyst stability. A H2-TPR study revealed a shift of reduction temperature of NiO to higher temperatures attributed to the strong Ni/support interaction due to the addition of Mg. Before the formation of NiO and MgO solid solutions with different compositions, new metallic phase/support interactions and changes in reducibility were reported.947 The enhanced basicity and high CO2 adsorption lowered the coke deposition reducing CO disproportionation and accelerating the oxidation of adsorbed carbon species. Thus, over 8 wt% Mg–10 wt% Ni/SBA-15, 58–66% CH4 and 62–57% CO2 conversions and H2/CO ∼ 1, at 700 °C, were reached over 40 h TOS (Table 12, entries 79 and 80).
 Fig. 94  CO2-TPD profiles of MgO–Ni-SBA-15 samples. Reprinted with permission from ref. 945. Crown Copyright 2013, Published by Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
* Effect of the morphology of Ni/SBA-15-based catalysts for DRM. A new breakthrough was recently performed with the synthesis of fibrous nanosilica such as KCC-1 and FZSM-5 that exhibit high area and micro- and mesopores, increasing the accessibility to active sites and improving considerably their catalytic and diffusion properties. Hence, recently, the successful preparation of spherical mesoporous Ni/dendritic fibrous SBA-15 (Ni/DFSBA-15) catalysts for DRM was reported.948 The catalytic data revealed that CH4 and CO2 conversions increased with Ni loading, with a maximum around 10 wt%, and further decreased. The characterization data showed that the NiO nanoparticle size increased with Ni loading while the BET area decreased. The best catalytic performance was achieved over 10 wt% Ni/DFSBA-15 with 93% CO2 and 91% CH4 conversions, 92% H2 and 96% CO selectivities and 0.95 H2/CO for 30 h TOS, at 800 °C (Table 12, entries 81 and 82). Recently, the same authors reported a study of the influences of operating parameters (reaction temperature, GHSV and CH4/CO2 ratio) over 10Ni/DFSBA-15 using mathematical modelling.949 The results indicated that the temperature had a determinant effect over CO2 conversion while the CH4/CO2 ratio mainly influenced the CH4 conversion and H2/CO. The predicted theoretical DRM allowed achieving high catalytic performance and stability over 30 h TOS. At 794 °C, both CH4 and CO2 conversions of 93–90% with a H2/CO ratio were obtained over the 10 wt% Ni/DFSBA-15 sample that could be regenerated under air (Table 12, entry 83). The in situ IR study determined that the DRM reaction involved the formation of unidentate (a) and bidentate (b) carbonate species as intermediates by CO2 adsorption and dissociation, while H2 and CO were issued of the reaction carbonate species with CH4 (Fig. 95).
 Fig. 95  Formation of (a) bidentate carbonate, (b) unidentate carbonate, and (c) linear carbonyl over Ni/DFSBA-15 during DRM. Reprinted with permission from ref. 949. Copyright 2020, Hydrogen Energy Publications LLC. Published by Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
* Conclusion. SBA-15 due to its high surface area and thermal stability is of interest to prepare Ni-based catalysts for the DRM process making use of the confinement effect of its mesopore system, and numerous works have been published. Different approaches have been followed to improve the dispersion and stabilization of nickel particles by the addition of a suitable promoter and establishing new metal/metal and metal/support interactions and synergies, to enhance the NiO reducibility, coke resistance, and control surface basicity to promote CO2 adsorption and dissociation. The results of Table 12 reveal the high activity of SBA-15-based catalysts although the yield or syngas cannot usually be assessed due to the lack of data on H2 and/or CO yield and/or selectivity. We have attempted to compare all catalysts considering CO2 conversion and H2/CO ratio as functions of GHSV and TOS. Then, some results can be highlighted as shown in Fig. 96 and 97. There, catalysts 12.5 wt% Ni/SBA-15, 1 wt% Ni/SBA-15 (800 °C), 7.5 wt% Ni/SBA-15(en), 7.5 wt% Ni/SBA-15(AE), 2 wt% Co–13 wt% Ni/SBA-15, 0.5 wt% Co–4.5 wt% Ni/SBA-15, Ni–Mo/SBA-15, 10 and 15 wt% Ni/DFSBA-15 (Table 12, entries 8, 9, 31, 32, 53, 55, 69, 81 and 82) exhibited interesting catalytic performance. Nevertheless, only for 12.5 wt% Ni/SBA-15, the 10 and 15 wt% Ni/DFSBA-15 data of H2 and/or CO2 yield and/or selectivity are given and so productivity can only be evaluated, with H2 and CO selectivities around 92–96%.
 Fig. 96  CO2 conversion was plotted versus GHSV for different DRM SBA-15-based catalysts. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high conversion versus GHSV. 
 Fig. 97  H2/CO ratio was plotted versus TOS for different DRM SBA-15-based catalysts. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: H2/CO close to 1 versus TOS. 
(ii) Ni/MCM-41 based catalysts for DRM.
* Effect of the preparation method of Ni/MCM-41-based catalysts for DRM. Years ago, Ni–MCM-41 in which Ni ions were incorporated into the silica framework were prepared by direct hydrothermal synthesis, in that case with a Ni loading of up to 3.9 wt%.673 The comparison of the catalytic data of Ni–MCM-41 catalysts issued from hydrothermal synthesis and by standard impregnation showed the improved catalytic properties for samples prepared by the hydrothermal method. The characterization of Ni/MCM-41 samples indicated a slight increase of the unit cell parameter when increasing the nickel content, supporting the framework incorporation of nickel. H2-TPR profiles did no depict any reduction peak before 360 °C confirming the absence of NiO crystals on the surface. Thus, a higher Ni dispersion and a lower particle size were reached with the sample prepared by the hydrothermal method. At 750 °C, 71–67% CH4 conversion was achieved with good catalytic stability over 30 h, in the presence of the 3.9 wt% Ni/MCM-41 sample prepared by hydrothermal synthesis (Table 13, entries 1 and 2). In a recent work, the effect of the spatial confinement of the MCM-41 mesoporous structure over Ni nanoparticle sintering resistance by varying Ni nanoparticle deposition methods and locations has been investigated.951 Thus, samples with Ni nanoparticles in the inner, outer and inner + outer surface were prepared. The characterization results disclosed the high dispersion of the Ni inner surface, owing to nanoparticle confinement, with a small size of around 2 nm, and the presence of strong metal–support interactions. Moreover, the TEM results and XRD patterns of spent catalysts indicated that the confinement effect of Ni particles inside the pores prevent both the sintering and carbon deposition on Niin particles and ensure the catalytic stability over long TOS. Therefore, the best catalytic performance was achieved over the 10 wt% Niin/MCM-41 catalyst, at 700 °C with 72–67% CH4 and 82–78% CO2 conversions and a H2/CO ratio around 0.78, which was maintained for at least 200 h (Table 13, entries 3–5). High resistance to sintering and carbon deposition were observed since the unconfined Ni/MCM-41 samples (10 wt% Niout/MCM-41, 10 wt% Niin+out/MCM-41) were deactivated within 12 h.

* Preparation of bimetallic Ni/MCM-41-based catalysts for DRM. Suitable metal doping could be a key to improve both the metal particle size and dispersion by establishing metal–metal and metal active phase/support interactions. Consequently, metal sintering and catalyst coke resistances should be enhanced. Moreover, the modification of surface basicity would improve the CO2 adsorption and dissociation unfavouring the CH4 adsorption and decomposition, promoting CO2 dissociation and the release of active oxygen species to boost the gasification of adsorbed carbon species.
Numerous papers disclosing different metal doping impacts of DRM catalysts have been published. For instance, a study of the benefits of Ga-doping over catalytic and textural features of 5 wt% Ni/MCM-41 showed an optimum Ga content, close to 2 wt% to maximise the catalytic performance and stability.952 Due to metal doping, H2-TPR profiles revealed that the reduction of nickel shifted at higher temperatures which was attributed to the strong metal–support interaction that improved Ni particle dispersion and low Ni particle size. A decrease in the amount of medium and strong basic sites was observed when increasing Ga loading, which was attributed to the formation of extra-framework acid sites. For Ga content <3 wt%, the catalytic results of the Ga-doped 5 wt% Ni/MCM-41 sample were superior to those of the un-doped sample and a maximum was observed for 2 wt% Ga–5 wt% Ni/MCM-41. High 90–85% CO2 and 88–83% CH4 conversions, for H2/CO close to 1 and 50% H2 selectivity, were achieved at 800 °C along 30 h TOS (Table 13, entry 6). Moreover, the doped catalyst exhibited higher resistance to coke deposition and Ni sintering. The Sc doping effect over 5 wt% Ni/MCM-41 physicochemical properties demonstrated that Sc loading induced a decrease in the surface area and catalytic activity of 5%Ni/MCM-41 catalysts.953 While Sc loading <1 wt% enabled an increase in CO2 conversion up to 38% for the 0.1 wt% Sc content. The presence of Sc small amount favoured the strong interaction between the metal and the support leading to higher dispersion of Ni nanoparticles and also increased the surface basicity. Therefore, enhanced CO2 adsorption and activation led to the presence of a high amount of active oxygen species promoting the oxidation of carbon species and lowering coke deposition. At 800 °C, the best catalytic performance was achieved over the 0.1 wt% Sc–5 wt% Ni/MCM-41 catalyst, achieving stable and high 87% CH4 and 85% CO2 conversions with a H2/CO ratio of 0.94 and a H2 yield close to 86%, along short 7 h TOS (Table 13, entries 7–9). Years ago, the impact of the incorporation of different transition metals (Zr, Ti, and Mn) into the silica framework over the catalytic and textural properties of Ni/MCM-41 samples prepared by the hydrothermal method was explored.954 The characterization data disclosed that higher Ni dispersion was achieved for Zr-doping in contrast to Ti- and Mn-doping. XRD patterns evidenced the presence of the amorphous silica matrix for Ni–Ti/and Ni–Mn/MCM-41 spent catalysts, suggesting that the Ti and Mn incorporations worsen the thermal stability of the MCM-41 silica support. In contrast, the presence of Zr cations in the framework stabilizes the MCM-41 structure. The catalytic performance of Zr-doped Ni/MCM-41 catalysts was similar or superior to the catalytic activity of the Ni/MCM-41 catalyst. In contrast, the decoration of Ni particles by TiOx and MnOx for Ti- and Mn-based Ni/MCM-41 catalysts hindered the accessibility to Ni-active centers and lowered their catalytic performance. Accordingly, Zr doped catalysts exhibited higher activity and stability and were maximum over 1 wt% Zr–2 wt% Ni/MCM-41, with maintained 92% CH4 conversion, H2/CO ratio close to 0.95, at 750 °C over 72 h TOS (Table 13, entries 10–13). However, neither any data of CO2 conversion nor H2 or CO selectivity were reported. A Gd doping study of 5 wt% Ni/MCM-41 prepared by the co-impregnation method revealed the improvement of CO2 adsorption and activation through the Gd2O2CO3 species formation similar to La2O2CO3 (Fig. 81) preventing coke deposition and also enhancing Ni reducibility.955 Over the 0.1 wt% Gd-promoted catalyst, high 88% CH4 and 92% CO2 conversions with H2/CO close to 1 were maintained over short 6.5 h TOS while carbon deposition was negligible (Table 13, entries 14–17). The catalytic performance of Ni/MCM-41 catalysts doped with Ru and Mg by impregnation showed that Ru incorporation improved the activity and stability of Ni/MCM-41 catalysts, being maximum H2 selectivity achieved over 1 wt% Ru@Ni/MCM-41, while Mg doping enhanced the 1 wt% Ru@Ni–MCM-41 catalytic performance increasing the CH4 conversion and limiting the RWGS reaction.956 However, low 31% CH4 and 37% CO2 conversions with H2/CO < 1 were achieved at 600 °C after 4 h of TOS (Table 13, entries 18–23). The TGA revealed coke formation with a high weight loss for all samples (45–65%) that did not particularly affected the catalytic activity of the catalyst over 4 h TOS, but induced the blockage of a reactor at longer 7 h TOS. The same group previously described a similar study of the preparation and catalytic performance of Ni/MCM-41 samples doped with Rh.957 Mono- and bimetallic MCM-41 catalysts were prepared either by Rh impregnation (Rh@Ni–MCM-41) or by one-pot hydrothermal synthesis (Rh–Ni/MCM-41). The optimum 0.19 Ni/Si ratio was determined achievieng low 31% CH4 and 41% CO2 conversions for 14 h at 600 °C (Table 13, entries 24–29). The Rh incorporation was shown to improve the catalytic activity and stability. However, the catalyst prepared by the impregnation method suffered higher coke deposition at longer reaction times. Earlier, a comparative study of the catalytic behavior of monometallic 20 wt% Ni/MCM-41 and bimetallic Pd–20 wt% Ni/MCM-41 samples demonstrated the promoting effect of Pd-doping on the activity and stability of 20 wt% Ni/MCM-41 catalysts owing to the synergetic effect between Ni and Pd improving Ni nanoparticle dispersion and reducibility.958 At 650 °C, over 0.3 wt% Pd–20 wt% Ni/MCM-41, the best catalytic results were achieved and maintained for 10 h TOS. Low 36% CH4 and 51% CO2 conversions with 19% H2 and 24% CO selectivities and a H2/CO ratio close to 1 were achieved (Table 13, entries 30–32). Despite their high thermal stability against sintering and the formation of coke, the doping of Ni/MCM-41 catalysts with noble metals did not provide much improvement neither in the catalyst resistance to coke formation and deactivation nor in the catalytic activity.
* Effect of the basic properties over the Ni/MCM-41-based catalyst activity for DRM. Suitable basic properties are well-known to allow CO2 adsorption and optimum catalytic coverage preventing CH4 decomposition and severe carbon deposition. Therefore, the addition of ceria, zirconia or magnesia positively modified the physicochemical properties of the support and improved not only CO2 adsorption but also the Ni interaction with the support enhancing Ni dispersion and stability. Following these leads, Y2O3–Ni/MgO–MCM-41 catalysts were successfully prepared by the one-pot synthesis method that revealed a suitable route to manage the uniform distribution of MgO, NiO and Y2O3 on the MCM-41 surface.959 The incorporation of MgO allowed improving the basicity while the incorporation of Y2O3 enabled promoting the reducibility and preventing the formation of an intermediate phase between Ni(Mg) and the support limiting Ni sintering. Moreover, the incorporation of Y2O3 enhanced the amount of oxygen vacancies favourable to CO2 adsorption, activation and dissociation lowering carbon deposition. A H2-TPR study showed a shift to a lower temperature of a broader NiO reduction peak, after Y2O3 incorporation, indicating that Y2O3 promotes nickel dispersion and strongly modifies the metal reducibility. Then, the best catalytic performance was achieved over 2 wt% Y2O3–Ni/MgO–MCM-41 with 71% CH4 and 84% CO2 conversions, 0.85 H2/CO ratio and with good stability over 20 h TOS at 750 °C (Table 13, entries 33 and 34). Coke deposition in the form of tip type of nanotubes caused almost no deactivation of the catalyst. Good catalytic results were reported but no data of Ni and MgO contents were provided.
* Conclusion. Similar approaches to SBA-15 were followed to develop improved DRM catalysts using MCM-41 as the support. Therefore, different attempts to improve the dispersion and stabilization of nickel particles by adding a suitable metal to control the surface basicity to promote CO2 adsorption and dissociation favouring carbon gasification were reported. The results in Table 13 summarise the catalytic data obtained in the presence of MCM-41-based catalysts for the DRM reaction at high temperatures. Considering GHSV, the reaction temperature and TOS, some results can be highlighted as shown in Fig. 98 and 99. High CO2 conversion and suitable H2/CO may be achieved; however the data of H2 and/or CO2 yield and/or selectivity are required to evaluate H2/CO productivity and the potential of the catalysts.
 Fig. 98  H2/CO ratio was plotted versus TOS for different DRM MCM-41-based catalysts. 
 Fig. 99  CO2 conversion was plotted versus GHSV for different DRM MCM-41-based catalysts. 
(iii) Conclusion. The main drawback of DRM is the high temperature required due to the endothermicity of the process, which not only results in high energy consumption that is not economically and environmentally sustainable, but is also incompatible with the reduction of CO2 emissions. Therefore, any industrial application must foresee the exclusive consumption of heat produced from renewable (sun, wind, and water) or nuclear sources. In addition to the high energy cost, the rapid deactivation of the catalyst must be overcome. The present review illustrates the high activity of the Ni-based zeolite and OMS catalysts, despite deactivation due to Ni sintering and coke deposition which, in turn, can plug the reactor in long-term tests. Thus, numerous attempts have been made to improve Ni-catalyst stability minimizing metal particle sizes, and improving metal dispersion and reducibility. The use of the high surface area support and second or multi metal-doping constitutes the main strategies, improving the metal–support interaction and modifying acid–base and redox properties. Thus, zeolites and OMSs offer opportunities for the development of the robust and efficient DRM catalyst. Unfortunately, most of the here referenced works remained far from an industrial implementation since the studies lack of the long-term stability test, catalytic regeneration study, and the process were performed at a high temperature (>750 °C) with catalytic performance close to thermodynamic equilibrium. The small pore size system of zeolites compared to mesoporous materials may be a weakness to reach a high dispersion of small metal nanoparticles and probably more prone to pore blockage due to metal sintering and/or coke deposition. Hence, the collection of references shows a greater number of studies exploring the potential of SBA-15 as the support fully supported by promising results that should make it a good candidate for the development of DRM catalysts. The high surface area, confinement effect of the mesoporous structure and thermal stability of SBA-15 should be a key to achieve high Ni nanoparticle dispersion and stability using the optimum preparation method and suitable metal promoters. Fig. 100 and 101 compare the best catalytic results reached over Ni-OMS and Ni-zeolite based catalysts compiled in the literature. Actually, the data of CO and/or H2 yield and/or selectivity are missing and would be necessary to validate this ranking where Ta–Ni/FZSM-5, 0.5 wt% Co–4.5 wt% Ni/SBA-15, 2 wt% Ga–5 wt% Ni/MCM-41, 1.5 wt% Zr–2 wt% Ni/MCM-41, 10 wt% Ni–10 wt% MgO/BEA, 10 wt% Ni/DFSBA-15, 5 wt% Ni–5 wt% Ce/ZSM-5, 1 wt% Ni/SBA-15 and Ni–Mo/SBA-15 exhibited the more relevant catalytic features considering the H2/CO ratio and CO2 conversion as functions of GHSV and TOS. Furthermore, the design of the DRM reactor alternating cycles of the reaction and catalyst regeneration should be a key.
 Fig. 100  H2/CO ratio was plotted versus TOS for different DRM zeolites and OMS-based catalysts. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: H2/CO ratio close to 1 versus TOS. 
 Fig. 101  CO2 conversion was plotted versus GHSV for different DRM zeolites and OMS-based catalysts. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high conversion versus GHSV. 
On the other hand, the combination of POM and DRM processes constitutes a great strategy and challenge to overcome the main limitation of the energetic cost by taking advantage of the POM exothermicity. To date, the POM + DRM route has been insufficiently explored and even less in the presence of zeolite and OMS based materials.
To conclude, future approaches should focus on the structure and morphology of the DRM catalyst to improve the stability and dispersion of metal active species due to the confinement effect and on the elimination or prevention of coke formation. Therefore, to improve catalyst stability, a crucial approach must avoid coke formation by modifying the catalyst composition and incorporating active sites for rapid carbon oxidation. Thus, the properties of the DRM catalyst should be improved to tune the redox properties, enhance the dispersion and stabilization of metal active species, and maximize the oxygen mobility by incorporating suitable promoters. In addition, greater consideration should be given to the morphology of the support in order to disperse, stabilize and also isolate the metal species.
Following this, a special focus should be given to OMS materials, hierarchical zeolites due to their meso- and macroscale secondary porosity coupled with a microporous primary structure, two-dimensional zeolite nanosheets and core–shell materials to promote the physical isolation and shielding of the particles could be of interest.
Finally, the validation of catalytic activity and stability will be performed under realistic conditions for long TOS.


6.3 Dry reforming of alcohols (DR-alcohols). 
Synthesis gas (syngas) is the feedstock for a broad range of processes such as Fischer–Tropsch, MeOH and DME synthesis that in turn outline the worldwide market of fuels and chemicals. An additional source based on the renewable feedstock to produce syngas is the dry reforming (DR) or reforming with CO2 of alcohols providing from bio-resources (Fig. 102). In this context, bio-ethanol and glycerol issued from the biodiesel constitute ideal feedstock to effectively close carbon cycle providing valuable chemicals and using the main greenhouse gases, help mitigating the effect of anthropogenic CO2. In this sense, a recent mini review exposed the potential of DR of ethanol (DRE) and glycerol (DRG).960 Considering DRM to manufacture syngas, the main drawback is that CH4 is not a renewable feedstock in contrast to ethanol that constitutes an important substrate readily available from biomass (forest and agro-industrial wastes, energy crops). Moreover, from thermodynamics standpoints, DRE is favoured at a temperature higher than 318 °C, whereas DRM at a temperature above 600 °C. Thus, DRE may allow diversifying and making more flexible and sustainable the production of syngas. The production of glycerol is more limited although the DRG constitutes a great opportunity to value the main byproduct of biodiesel production. Despite the advantages of DRE and DRG, these represent emerging technologies poorly explored and several papers covered the use of zeolites and OMS based catalysts.
 Fig. 102  Production of syngas from the DR of renewable alcohols issued from biomass. 
6.3.1 Dry reforming of ethanol (DRE). 
(a) Thermodynamics. 
A recent paper explored the thermodynamics of ethanol reforming with CO2.961 DRE is an endothermic process that involves numerous competitive and parallel reactions that determine the syngas yield and are influenced by different parameters such as the temperature, pressure and CO2/EtOH ratio. Ethanol can undergo dehydrogenation, dehydration and decomposition as well as the side products formed are involved in different secondary reactions. Some reactions of the complex reaction system are displayed in Scheme 65. In order to evaluate the impacts of some parameters of the reaction such as the temperature, CO2/EtOH, pressure and addition of inert gas over H2 and CO yields, a thermodynamic analysis via the Gibbs free energy minimization method was performed indicating that high H2 and CO yields up to 95 and 97% may be reached at a high temperature (900–1050 °C), with a CO2/EtOH molar ratio around 1 and atmospheric pressure, being the main reaction products CH4 and C with H2, H2O, CO and CO2, and the carbon formation favoured at low temperatures.961
 Scheme 65  Some parallel reactions involved in the complex DRE reaction system. 
(b) Catalysts for DRE. 
To make possible the development of viable DRE technology, the design and development of efficient, selective and robust catalyst is the key. Therefore, the addition of suitable promoters, multiple active phases maximizing the metal nanoparticle dispersion and stability through the strong metal/support interaction are crucial together with the design of optimized catalyst regeneration. As it occurs for dry reforming of CH4, Cu, Co, Rh and Ni supported solid catalysts have been largely used for DRE, being Ni the most explored metal because of its high activity, selectivity, availability and price, besides its rapid sintering and deactivation at high temperatures.962–965 Therefore, the choice of a suitable support with a high surface area and/or appropriate redox properties has shown to be crucial to achieve high NiO dispersion with a small particle size which give a Ni higher resistance to coke deposition and sintering. As discussed in this review, the addition of a suitable promoter (Zr, Ce, La, Mn, Co, and Mg) contributes to improve the activity and stability of Ni-based catalysts. In the bibliography, the catalytic activity of zeolite based materials for DRE has not been explored, while only two works described the catalytic performance of OMS based catalysts. The benefits of the incorporation of Ce in the SBA-15 structure have been widely described to promote the oxygen surface mobility owing to the special redox properties of Ce (Ce3+/C4+) and oxygen vacancy formation. Accordingly, the suitable catalytic performance of Rh/CeO2 has been demonstrated due to the interaction between Rh and CeO2 and the advanced properties of CeO2 for DRE.966,967 Following these leads, in 2009, the catalytic performance of Rh/Ce-SBA-15 catalysts prepared by impregnation of Ce-SBA-15 with different Ce/Si molar ratios issued from direct synthesis was explored.968 Firstly, the catalytic performances of 5 wt%-Rh, Ni, Co, and Pd supported on SBA-15 were checked, revealing the lower catalytic activity of 5 wt% Ni/SBA-15 exhibiting the higher catalytic activity of 5 wt% Rh–SBA-15. Moreover, a decrease of the metal content allowed improving the catalytic performance up to 470% exhibited by 1 wt% Rh/SBA-15 in comparison to 5 wt% Rh/SBA-15. Therefore, 1 wt% Rh/Ce-SBA-15 catalysts with a Ce/Si molar ratio ranging from 0 to 1 were prepared. The stability of the ordered mesoporous structure was affected by the incorporation of Ce and could be conserved for Ce/Si up to 1/5. The characterization data showed the optimum Rh nanoparticle dispersion and a lower particle size, 5–8 nm, for Ce/Si = 20, probably due to the strong metal–metal (Rh–Ce) interaction.969 The XPS analysis showed a shift to slightly lower binding energies of Rh (3d5/2) and Rh (3d3/2) of Rh0 with further incorporation of Ce (>1/20), indicating the formation of higher Rh particle sizes (Fig. 103), in agreement with the H2-TPR results that showed a shift of the reduction peak to a higher temperature, attributed to the decrease of the Rh dispersion. The activity over 1 wt% Rh/Ce-SBA-15 catalysts increased with a Ce/Si ratio from 0 to 1/20 attributed to the improved mobility of surface oxygen species because of the incorporation of Ce being the more suitable Ce/Si molar ratio equal to 1/20. Thus, at 750 °C, good catalytic performance was achieved over 1 wt% Rh/Ce-SBA-15 (Ce/Si = 1/20) with a H2/CO ratio around 1, CO2 conversion up to 76% and mole percentage of the gas product of 42% for H2 and CO, 14% for CO2, over 24 h TOS, with EtOH/CO2 = 1 and WHSV = 15594 h−1.
 Fig. 103  Rh 3d XP spectra of 1%Rh/Ce-SBA-15 catalysts with different Ce/Si molar ratios. Reprinted with permission from ref. 968. Copyright 2009, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
More recently, the catalytic performance of Ni/KIT-6 prepared by the methanol-assisted impregnation method was reported.970 The characterization results showed that the use of KIT-6 as the support enabled improving the dispersion and decreased Ni particle size owing to the confinement effect in comparison with Ni/SiO2. Therefore, smaller Ni particle sizes allowed preventing the coke formation, and the strong metal/support interaction lowered Ni sintering improving the catalytic stability and activity. Thus, at 550 °C, good catalytic performance was achieved over 14 wt% Ni/KIT-6 with a H2/CO ratio around 1.5, an EtOH conversion close to 100% and mole compositions of the gas product of 49% for H2, 32% for CO and 19% for CH4 along 40 h TOS, with EtOH/CO2 = 1 and GHSV = 40 L gcat−1 h−1.
(c) Conclusion. 
The catalytic results showed that almost complete conversion of ethanol can be achieved with almost H2 theoretical yield, making DRE a promising technology to produce syngas closing carbon cycle, and requiring solar or nuclear based heat sources in the same way that processes working at high temperatures, to ensure carbon neutrality. In the presence of OMS based catalysts good catalytic performance was achieved and similar to the best catalytic results achieved in the presence of 2 wt% Ir/CeO2 or 15 wt% Cu/Ce0.8Zr0.2O2 that exhibited high catalytic stability over 70–90 h TOS.971,972 Since DRE is an emerging process, valuable mechanism insights are required. Molecular modeling and operando studies will be necessary for the design and ab initio synthesis of efficient and stable advanced catalysts to reach sustainable syngas manufacturing.
6.3.2 Dry reforming of glycerol (DRG). 
Glycerol is a byproduct of biodiesel production and constitutes a promising source for syngas production. Glycerol steam reforming and aqueous phase reforming are routes to produce syngas.973,974 Nevertheless, the high H2/CO ratio unfavourable for Fischer–Tropsch synthesis along with high CO2 production was reached, forcing to disregard this route.975,976 Thus, dry reforming of glycerol constitutes a promising alternative to produce syngas and solve glycerol crisis converting two wastes into valuable products.
(a) Thermodynamics. 
Following the equations of Scheme 66, 3 moles of H2 and 4 moles of CO per mole of glycerol can be produced by DRG, while direct glycerol decomposition can produce 4 moles of H2 and 3 moles of CO per mole of glycerol. Other reactions such as methanation of the WGS reaction can take place. From thermodynamic standpoints, the complete decomposition of glycerol is very likely. Since CO2 can be involved in DRG and converted into syngas, a study of CO2 conversion has been considered. Therefore, a thermodynamic analysis of DRG for H2 and syngas synthesis was performed with a total Gibbs free energy minimization method and the impact of different reaction parameters were explored.977 The maximum H2 and syngas yields are obtained at atmospheric pressure and are influenced by the CO2/glycerol ratio. Accordingly, up to 7 moles of syngas can be produced per mole of glycerol with a CO2/glycerol ratio of >3, while the H2 yield is the maximum for a CO2/glycerol ratio of <1. It was observed that the syngas yield increased with temperature and CO2/glycerol ratio, and the optimum reaction conditions are 727 °C and CO2/glycerol = 1. Under these conditions, 6.4 mol of syngas are produced by a mole of glycerol with H2/CO = 1 whereas coke deposition is thermodynamically prevented.
 Scheme 66  DRG and glycerol decomposition. 
(b) Catalysts for DRG. 
The data on dry reforming of glycerol are limited in the literature, and we found only one reference exploring the activity of Ni/SBA-15 catalysts.978 The most used catalysts for GDR, as it occurs for numerous CO2 conversion processes at high temperatures, are Ni-based catalysts due to the Ni lower price, high catalytic activity and availability. Besides Ni-based catalysts suffer high deactivation due to metal sintering and coke formation, different studies explored the catalytic performance of noble metals since they are more stable against sintering and coke formation at high temperatures.979 Moreover, the scientific community investigated and demonstrated that the incorporation of a second metal strengthens the metal (Ni)/support interactions that lower the physical migration of metal nanoparticles and also establish new metal–metal interactions providing new redox properties such as the doping of Ni-based catalysts with CaO, CeO2 or La2O3.980 Accordingly, the good and outstanding catalytic performance of the 3 wt% La-promoted alumina supported 20 wt% Ni catalyst, with a H2/CO ratio lower than 2, was reported.981 The syngas production was in a first stage attributed to the glycerol decomposition pathway and further to the RWGS and carbon gasification as described in the literature.964,982 Good catalyst stability and performance were recorded over 72 h TOS, at 750 °C, with a high 90% glycerol conversion and stable H2/CO = 1.2–1.7, with CO2/glycerol = 5. Recently, Re was added to Ni/CaO as the promoter for DRG, enhancing the H2 yield and glycerol conversion owing to the formation of acid sites favouring the adsorption of OH groups inducing an increase in the catalytic activity. Also, the reaction kinetic and characterization studies of Pt-Re/C catalysts for GDR indicated that Re improved the rate of glycerol reforming by reducing the binding energy of CO to Pt, leading to the lower coverage of the surface and higher accessibility to active sites by reaction intermediates and/or products, and additionally favours the WGS reaction and C–O bond cleavage (Fig. 104).
 Fig. 104  Benefits of the incorporation of Re to Pt/C catalysts owing to the formation of Re–OH species for catalytic reforming of oxygenated hydrocarbons. Reprinted with permission from ref. 979. Copyright 2008, Elsevier Inc. All rights reserved. 
Following these leads, the 15 wt% Ni/SBA-15 and 3 wt% Re–15 wt% Ni/SBA-15 samples were prepared by impregnation. The addition of Re improved NiO dispersion and allowed decreasing the Ni particle size. The study of the catalytic performance of 15 wt% Ni/SBA-15 and 3 wt% Re–15 wt% Ni/SBA-15 revealed a higher glycerol conversion of 57% and a higher hydrogen yield of 55% in the presence of 3 wt% Re–15 wt% Ni/SBA-15, at 700 °C, with CO2/glycerol = 1. The improvement of catalytic activity was attributed to the oxygen storage capacity and mobility of Re that enabled minimizing carbon deposition by gasification of carbon species. A study of reaction parameters showed that CO2/glycerol = 1 was the most suitable ratio while an increase in temperature enhanced competitive methanation and CO2 hydrogenation. No data about glycerol flow, GHSV or H2/CO were reported that makes difficult the performance evaluation.
(c) Conclusion. 
Dry reforming of ethanol and glycerol, while constitute promising routes to produce syngas, remains an emerging technology that requires knowledge and mechanistic insights into the design of efficient and robust catalysts to achieve sustainable syngas manufacturing. In particular, to solve the crisis of glycerol as the biodiesel by-product and valorize glycerol, the DRG may constitute an alternative route. However, considering the worldwide syngas production and consumption and the urgency in mitigating the CO2 emissions, the DRG would be an almost marginal alternative. Therefore, considering global syngas production, the DRE results are more attractive.

7 Oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of alkanes using CO2
Although there have been great advances in the utilization of CO2 as a building block for chemical and fuel synthesis, there still exist several inherent drawbacks due to CO2 inertness and high thermodynamic and kinetic barriers that need to be overcome, requiring high-energy consumption and green hydrogen production which hinder its industrial implementation. An alternative technological method emerged in the 1990s based on the benefits of the use of CO2 as a soft oxidant and promoter in catalytic processes.983–985 Back then, the use of CO2 for dehydrogenation of ethyl benzene allowed reaching higher conversion while H2O and CO were the new reaction products in addition to H2 and styrene. These data indicated that the reaction underwent an oxidative dehydrogenation pathway in which CO2 was dissociated into CO and surface oxygen that can abstract hydrogen to form H2O (Scheme 67). Thus, CO2 constantly ensured the lattice oxygen supply and H2 elimination equilibrium switching (Le Chatelier's principle) via RWGS. Consequently, the use of CO2 as a soft oxidant to perform oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes presents three clear positive benefits with respect to catalytic dehydrogenation: enhances equilibrium conversion due to H2 conversion through RWGS, avoids olefin's side reaction due to partial blockage of the active sites by CO2 adsorption, and improves catalytic life due to coke removal by the Boudouard reaction. In addition, CO2, the main greenhouse gas, is reduced into CO, a more valuable chemical. Years ago, the oxidizing capability of different gases for coke gasification was explored and ordered as follows: O2 (105) > H2O (3) > CO2 (1) > H2 (0.003), illustrating the CO2 oxidative capacity in addition to heat capability.986 Thus, the use of CO2 as a soft oxidant may be a new breakthrough in CO2 utilization, considering that the global industrial CO2 use per year is about 230 Mt, while total anthropogenic CO2 emissions are about 38Gt; this highlights the high demand to convert CO2.987–990
 Scheme 67  Oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes assisted by CO2. 

7.1 Introduction. 
Catalytic dehydrogenation of alkanes constitutes a powerful and widely applied one-step reaction in the industry, and provides hydrogen and olefins that are precursors of a huge variety of industrial products. Nevertheless, catalytic dehydrogenation is thermodynamically limited and highly endothermic and requires a high temperature (550–750 °C) and a low paraffin partial pressure (0.1 MPa).991 In addition to coke formation because of high temperature, inducing catalyst deactivation and reactor clogging, the higher reactivity of olefins in comparison to alkane precursors leads to undesired side reactions such as hydrogenolysis, cracking and isomerization. Therefore, different approaches have been explored and the oxidative dehydrogenation using CO2 as a soft oxidant instead of oxygen as alternative emerged and strengthened, because of several advantages from energetic, thermodynamic and kinetic (rate of reaction, higher alkene selectivity) standpoints, such as the suppression of the over-oxidation of substrates and products, the reduction of carbon deposits and the use of CO2, the main greenhouse gas. Then, oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes has been extensively investigated as an energy-saving and more environmentally friendly strategy to produce alkenes in the presence of MOx supported catalysts mainly with non-precious metals such as Ni, Fe, Co, Cr, La, Cu or V. The catalytic and textural properties of transition and rare earth metal oxides have been widely explored owing to their ability to perform the oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes.983,988,990,992–994 Redox and acid/base properties play a fundamental role in the adsorption and activation of the reactants, in the dehydrogenation step, and in the regeneration of the oxidation state of the metal oxide catalyst (Fig. 105).
 Fig. 105  Scheme of different alkane ODH reaction pathways. 
7.1.1 Mechanistic insights. 
Efforts have been made to identify the intermediates of the reaction and establish reaction pathways by combining experimental and computational studies. Likewise, different reaction paths influenced by physicochemical properties were proposed for alkane oxidative dehydrogenation either involving lattice oxygen of metal oxide species further restored by CO2 reduction, or by combining simple dehydrogenation and RWGS, although different reaction pathways may be involved in the presence of the same catalyst (Fig. 105).
7.1.2 Role of redox properties. 
The redox sites of metal oxide catalysts are involved in the transfer of an oxygen atom from metal oxide to the hydrocarbon to complete oxidative dehydrogenation and the production of water. Therefore, the redox cycle is completed when reduced metal oxide is reoxidized by CO2, thus releasing CO. The mechanism is referred to as the Mars-van-Krevelen mechanism and also redox or regenerative mechanism (Scheme 68).995–997
 Scheme 68  Role of redox properties of a metal oxide catalyst in the ODH of the alkane mechanism. 
7.1.3 Role of lattice oxygen. 
It was revealed that the presence of lattice oxygen in the oxides plays a fundamental role and is closely related to the redox properties. Indeed, the oxygen in the network allows the transfer of an oxygen atom from the metal oxide for the abstraction of hydrogen from the alkane, with the consequent production of water and reduction of the oxidation state of the metal. Therefore, CO2 contributes to reoxidize the metal and restore the original metal oxidation state and oxygen lattice (Scheme 69).983,998
 Scheme 69  Role of lattice oxygen and CO2 in the ODH of alkane. 
7.1.4 Role of the acid/base sites. 
The presence of acid/base sites in metal oxide catalysts plays an important role in alkane and CO2 adsorption. Indeed acid sites collaborate in the activation of the alkane according to its nature (aromatic cycle) while the basic sites involve CO2 activation and hydrogen atom activation/abstraction. Furthermore, the acid/base properties influence the reaction pathway that can partially proceed by simple dehydrogenation and oxidative dehydrogenation (Scheme 70).999–1001 On the other hand, the excess of acid sites can influence process selectivity and catalyze side reactions such as cracking, isomerization and condensation.
 Scheme 70  Role of acid/base sites in the ODH of the alkane pathway. 
7.1.5 Metal oxide based catalysts for the ODH of alkanes. 
The more widely studied catalytic systems for ODH are chromium, vanadium and iron based catalysts with suitable oxidation states that play a determining role in catalytic efficiency.
VOx-materials have been largely used as catalysts for alkane ODH owing to their activity and selectivity. The metal/support interaction and vanadium loading influenced the dispersion and stabilization as well as the nature of vanadium species.990,993,1002–1004 CrOx materials have also exhibited high catalytic activity and selectivity for alkane ODH owing to the multi-oxidation states of Cr.993,1005 Actually, the redox cycle and Cr active species are still controversial and different pairs have been proposed such as Cr6+/Cr3+ or Cr3+/Cr2+. Catalytic performance of Cr-based samples is determined by the nature and dispersion of chromia active species, in turn influenced by the content and support. The addition of second metals or promoters with specific redox or acid/base properties improves the catalytic performance of the catalyst due to the enhancement in metal oxide dispersion and stability, acid/base and redox properties that strongly influence the ODH pathway.983,989,1006,1007 The catalytic behavior of the synthesized catalysts also depends on the reaction conditions, and because a high reaction temperature is required, metal sintering and coke deposition induce rapid and severe deactivation, which has so far hindered industrial application. Thus, the research and development of new catalysts for alkane ODH with CO2 is a major challenge. In this context, zeolites and OMS due to their versatility in chemical composition, high surface area with a moldable pore size and variability in pore system structures offer opportunities to disperse and stabilize active metal oxides.


7.2 Styrene production by ethylbenzene (EB) ODH. 
The global styrene (ST) market varies with its widespread application as monomer in the synthesis of synthetic rubber, resins, plastics and mainly polystyrene and production of several other copolymers. The worldwide styrene market reached 43.3 B$ in 2019 corresponding to a production around 20 Mt year−1. ST is mainly produced by catalytic dehydrogenation of ethylbenzene (EB) using K-promoted iron oxide catalysts, in the presence of excess stream, at 600–700 °C and LHSV around 0.43 h−1.1008,1009 Due to the endothermic nature of the process (ΔH298K = 117.6 kJ mol−1), high energy consumption and temperature are required, resulting in coke formation and catalyst deactivation (Scheme 71). Therefore, different approaches have been studied and reaction coupling constitutes an effective approach to improve the equilibrium conversion.1010,1011 Accordingly, numerous investigations explored the reaction coupling of EB in the presence of CO2 instead of steam or O2 combined with RWGS. Therefore, the calculated consumption energy for ST production for the coupling process was 7.9 × 108 J t−1versus 6.2 × 109–7.9 × 109 J t−1 for current catalytic dehydrogenation. Thus, the oxidative dehydrogenation using CO2 as a soft oxidant as alternative emerged and strengthened because of several advantages from energetic, thermodynamic and kinetic (rate of reaction, higher ST selectivity) standpoints, avoiding the over-oxidation of alkane and consuming CO2, the main greenhouse gas.1012 Hence, the design and development of efficient and robust catalysts is very challenging, and thus EB oxidative dehydrogenation has been widely explored as an energy-saving and more environmentally friendly strategy to produce ST.
 Scheme 71  Thermodynamics of the ODH of ethane with CO2 as a soft oxidant. 
Transition and rare earth metal oxide based catalysts exhibit suitable catalytic activity and have been widely investigated. Fe,1006 V,990,1013,1014 Zr,1015 Ce,1016,1017 Mn,1018,1019 and Cr1020,1021 oxides exhibited appropriate catalytic properties to perform the oxidative dehydrogenation with CO2 of ethylbenzene.983,990 However, the rapid deactivation of the catalysts is an impediment to the industrial implementation and there is great interest in the design and development of efficient and robust ODH catalysts. To maximize the dispersion and stabilization of the metallic oxide phase, the great potential of microporous and mesoporous ordered materials as supports has been explored due to their high surface area, variability in the pore size system, versatility in their framework composition and textural properties, and high thermal and chemical stability.
7.2.1 Zeolite-based catalysts for EB ODH. 
The use of TiO2–ZrO2 binary oxides as catalysts and supports has found many applications in catalysis due to their excellent redox properties and oxygen storage capacity as it was illustrated along the present review.1022 Accordingly, an attempt of preparation and use as a catalyst for EB ODH of TiO2–ZrO2 oxides deposited onto mesoporous silicalite-1 (CS-1) was performed. First, metal hydroxide precipitated carbon nanoparticles were prepared by a pH controlled precipitation method of metal precursors onto carbon nanoparticles and further used for the hydrothermal synthesis of CS-1.1000 The characterization data showed the XRD pattern as a typical reflectance of MFI type zeolite while no reflectance of the crystalline phase of the TiO2–ZrO2 oxides was detected, indicating that no mixed oxide was occluded on the surface. The registered catalytic performance of TiO2–ZrO2/CS-1 revealed the better resistance of the supported catalysts; however, low catalytic results were achieved, at 650 °C, 30% EB conversion and 70–75% ST selectivity were maintained over 9 h TOS (Table 14, entries 18 and 19). The low selectivity was attributed to the higher amount of strong acid and base sites determined by NH3-TPD and CO2-TPD may be due to the formation of solid solutions (ZrTiO4).
