The suitable catalytic properties of iron based catalysts for dehydrogenation reaction have been demonstrated.1008,1023,1024 Accordingly the preparation and use as catalysts of Fe3O4/ZSM-5 samples with different metal loadings for EB ODH were explored.1025–1027 The catalytic results showed the efficient dispersion of iron oxide over the high ZSM-5 surface area and activity towards CO2 dissociation into CO and oxygen active species to enable RWGS. This enhanced activity was attributed to oxygen deficiency of the supported Fe3O4 and the presence of Fe2+ species. Nevertheless, at 600 °C, low 42% EB conversion and 89% ST selectivity were achieved (Table 14, entries 20–22). The addition of a promoter constitutes an efficient strategy to tune the redox properties and enhance the catalytic activity of metal oxide supported samples.1028,1029 In this sense, the addition of K has been reported to enhance the rate of carbon gasification from an iron oxide catalyst, and extensive studies have determined the formation of potassium monoferrite (KFeO2) on magnetite bulk (Fe3O4) as the active site for dehydrogenation.1030–1032 Accordingly, Fe2O3 supported ZSM-5 type chromosilicate (F/CS) samples were prepared following the solid-state dispersion method of different γ-Fe2O3 loadings in the structure of K or Na chromosilicate samples, KCS and NCS, respectively.1033,1034 The catalytic results confirmed the critical role of K as a promoter besides Cr and the synergetic effect between both metals to improve the catalytic activity, with a higher influence than with Na. Moreover, the impacts of Fe2O3 addition over catalytic activity depended on loading and it can be noted that, firstly, ST selectivity increased and further decreased. Therefore, at 600 °C, highest 92% and 96% ST selectivity for 5% and 10% Fe2O3 loadings over KCS and NCS support, respectively, was reached. Up to 51% ST yield and 96% ST selectivity were achieved over 10 wt% Fe2O3/NCS catalyst. However, the potassium chromosilicate support before washing (KCSBW) exhibited the best catalytic performance with 56% ST yield and 96% ST selectivity (Table 14, entries 23–25). Accordingly, the synergetic effect between Cr and K was considered more effective than the synergetic effect between Fe and K. In contrast, Fe2O3 addition to NCS revealed the synergetic effect between Fe and Na, promoting the catalytic activity with greater magnitude than that of the NCS support. Stability study demonstrated a slight EB conversion decrease from 58 to 56% after the first three runs because of coke deposition that could be recovered after calcination on the active sites of the catalyst.
7.2.2 OMS-based catalysts for EB ODH. 
(a) Monometallic OMS-based catalysts for EB ODH. 
V-supported catalysts exhibit high activity and selectivity towards styrene formation, the high dispersion and oxidation state (V5+) of vanadium species being the key parameters.1013,1035 In particular, V-supported on activated carbon,1036,1037 Al2O3,1038,1039 and MgO,1035,1040,1041 at 530–600 °C and 0.1 MPa allowed reaching high catalytic performance, while the addition of suitable promoters such as Cr, La or Mg resulted in increasing the activity.1042 A recent DFT study revealed that ethylbenzene dehydrogenation takes place on the vanadyl oxygen O(1) site of the V2O5(001) surface with H2O formation to H2 elimination with abstraction of O vacancy on the V2O5(001) surface. Nevertheless, because of the high activation energy (3.16 eV), CO2 is not able to properly restore the oxygen vacancy leading to progressive catalyst deactivation.1043 As the support is the key to improve catalyst stability, highly dispersed V supported Al-MCM-41 (V/Al-MCM-41) catalysts were prepared.1044 The catalytic study for EB ODH showed an increase in the catalytic activity with V loading increase up to 6 wt% owing to high coverage and dispersion of low polymeric VOx species, and further decrease due to V2O5 crystallite formation. UV-Raman spectroscopy of the different samples showed bands at 341 and 914 cm−1 assigned to the bending mode of the V–O–V bond and the symmetric O–V–O stretching of polymeric vanadia species; the band at 1018 cm−1 was assigned to the symmetric stretching mode of the VO bond of the isolated tetrahedral VO4 species anchored on AlMCM-41, while the band at 996 cm−1 indicated the formation of V2O5 and increased with the V content (Fig. 106). Moreover, the Al-MCM-41 support showed to improve VOx species dispersion in comparison to the Si-MCM-41 support and stabilization of V5+ active species owing to stronger metal–support interaction. The catalyst deactivation was mainly attributed to the reduction of V5+ into V3+, while the coke deposition did not alter catalyst stability. High ST selectivity was reached at 550 °C over 20 h TOS, with a good 58–48% EB conversion (Table 14, entries 1 and 2).
 Fig. 106  UV-Raman spectra of VOx/Al-MCM-41 samples with different vanadium loadings (wt%) (a) 2, (b) 4, (c) 6, (d) 6 and (e) 8. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1044. Copyright 2019, John Wiley & Sons, Ltd. 
Years ago a study of the catalytic behavior of VOx/Al-MCM-41 revealed similar tendency.1045 1.0 mmol g−1 V−1 loading was determined to be optimal for the formation of isolated and monolayer polymeric VOx species over AlMCM-41. Therefore, 50% ST yield and 97% ST selectivity were reached at 550 °C (Table 14, entry 3). Previously, a high catalytic activity of VOx/Si-MCM-41 with 1.5 mmol g−1 V−1 optimum loading was described,1046 and similar conclusions were reported. The dispersion and the nature of VOx species depended on the V loading. Moreover, two reaction pathways for ethylbenzene dehydrogenation were displayed: direct oxidative dehydrogenation of EB with CO2 (40%) and combination of simple dehydrogenation of EB with RWGS. 70% ST yield and 98% ST were obtained at 550 °C (Table 14, entry 4). Interesting catalytic activity of VOx/MCM-41 was reported; however, low catalytic stability was reached, and no catalyst regeneration and recycling study was performed.
Bimetallic La2O3–V2O5/MCM-41 catalysts with different V and 10 wt% La content were prepared by impregnation and tested for EB ODH.1047 The benefits of the addition of a second metal over the V dispersion was shown while the nature of VOx species varied with V loading, and the polyvanadate formation occurred for a high V content (20 wt%). Moreover, V loading affects surface acidity. The NH3-TPD spectra revealed the presence of VO43− acid sites in the 15V/MCM-41 sample that affects the activity of the catalyst and induces EB and ST cracking. For V loading below 15%, uniform La2O3 and V2O5 dispersion was reached. The key role of La2O3 to effectively lower coke deposition owing to CO2 adsorption and stabilization into La2O2CO3 has been fully described. Moreover, the addition of La2O3 allowed improving the rate of VO32− oxidation due to the presence of an oxygen lattice that can be ensured by CO2 adsorption and dissociation via La2O2CO3. Then, an enhancement of EB conversion over 10La15V/MCM-41 was observed in comparison to 15V/MCM-41. At 600 °C, high catalytic performance was obtained over 10 wt% La–15 wt% V/MCM-41 and attributed to the high specific surface area, the large pore volume and the optimized La/V ratio. Thus, EB 86.5% conversion and 91% ST selectivity after 4 h TOS were achieved and 75% EB conversion was maintained after 12 h TOS (Table 14, entries 5 and 6). Nevertheless, carbon deposition induced catalyst deactivation. The same group published a similar work reporting the catalytic performance of bimetallic LaVOx/SBA-15 samples with different V contents showing the importance of the nature of VOx species and dispersion controlled by V loading.1048 The addition of La allowed preventing the carbon deposition and improving catalytic stability. The synergism between La and V enhanced the catalytic performance of V-catalysts which was maximized over 10 wt% La2O3–15 wt% V2O5/SBA-15 sample owing to the presence of VO43− active species, achieving 74% ST yield, at 600 °C (Table 14, entries 7 and 8). The catalyst was regenerated under air calcination at 550 °C. Promising catalytic behavior for EB ODH was reported; nevertheless, studies of long-term stability and regeneration of the catalysts are required to evaluate their potential.
Even if the catalytic behavior of Cr-based catalysts for alkane ODH has been widely explored, few examples were reported in the literature using micro- and mesoporous ordered silicates as the support for EB ODH. One study investigated the catalytic performance of Cr/MCM-41 catalysts prepared by the direct hydrothermal synthesis for EB ODH. The catalytic data showed an increase of the catalytic activity with Cr loading until 1.7 wt%.1021 Specifically, Cr(VI)O4 in tetrahedral coordination was determined as active monochromate species and reduced to Cr(III)O6 in octahedral coordination as inactive polychromate species inducing the catalyst deactivation. A regeneration study under oxygen allowed recovering the catalytic activity. Mechanistic insights revealed that CO co-formation with ST and H2 disappearance confirmed that RWGS took place over Cr-MCM-41. It was concluded that ST was produced following two pathways, direct ODH of EB with CO2 and simple dehydrogenation of EB combined with RWGS. High 70% ST yield and 98% ST were obtained at 550 °C (Table 14, entry 9).
(b) Bimetallic OMS-based catalysts for EB ODH. 
The catalytic performance of bimetallic CoFe2O4/MCM-41 catalysts prepared by impregnation and compared with that of Fe2O3/MCM-41 was recently reported.1049 The characterization data (XRD, Raman, Mössbauer and XPS) revealed the structural properties and environment of Fe and Co, and the formation of partially inverted Co ferrite where Co2+ and Fe3+ occupy both octahedral and tetrahedral positions. The catalytic data demonstrated the superior selectivity and stability of the CoFe2O4 phase compared to Fe2O3 owing to high thermal stability of the structure. Mechanistic insights indicated that EB adsorption occurred preferentially over Fe3+ sites than Co2+ and preferentially dehydrogenation took place on the octahedral sites in comparison to the tetrahedral sites. 30% ST yield and 99% ST selectivity were maintained at 550 °C along 5 h TOS (Table 14, entries 10 and 11). Low catalytic performance was reached and insufficient data were provided to estimate the production.
The use of CeO2–ZrO2 mixed oxides as catalysts and catalyst supports has found many applications owing to their excellent redox properties and oxygen storage capacity as discussed in this review.1050–1052 Therefore, for alkane ODH, the catalytic activity of CeO2–ZrO2 mixed oxides has been widely explored and it was shown that their catalytic performance depended on textural and physicochemical properties, the main drawback being the structure stability at high temperatures. Therefore, a strategy for the dispersion and stabilization of CeO2–ZrO2 mixed oxides over a high area support is required. Following these leads, the catalytic performance of CeO2/ZrO2 pure oxides, mixed oxides and supported mixed oxides on SBA-15 prepared by precipitation, co-precipitation and precipitation-deposition methods was explored.1053 The characterization data showed that the BET surface area of the supported catalyst was lower than that of original SBA-15 and decreasing with increasing loading due to pore blockage. XRD patterns displayed that the SBA-15 structure was mostly maintained with a slight structural ordering decline, whereas no XRD reflections were determined for the supported CeO2–ZrO2 phase suggesting that no crystalline phase of the mixed oxide was formed. The catalytic results revealed an enhancement of the catalytic activity due to the synergetic effect between Zr and Ce compared to individual bulk oxide but no specific characterization data (H2-TPR, CO2-TPD, Raman) were studied. CeO2–ZrO2/SBA-15 exhibited good and stable catalytic activity, showing the potential of SBA-15 as a support, with 65% EB conversion and 93% ST selectivity maintained over 10 h TOS, at 650 °C (Table 14, entries 12–14).
(c) Effect of the preparation method of OMS-based catalysts on EB ODH. 
It is well known that the preparation method can strongly affect the metal structure and dispersion that in turn influence the catalytic activity and selectivity. Accordingly, M/MCM-41 (M = Co, Cu, Cr, Fe or Ni) following impregnation (IM) and isomorphic substitution (DM) methods were prepared.1054 Depending on the preparation procedure, cationic (Cu2+, Co2+, and Cr4+/Cr3+) or metal oxide species were detected in the MCM-41 structure or dispersed on the MCM-41 surface, respectively. H2-TPR analysis showed that isomorphically substituted samples were difficult to reduce, except Ni- and Fe-containing catalysts, whereas impregnated (MeOx) catalysts possessed more easily reducible species (Fig. 107). Consequently, the isomorphically substituted catalysts exhibited superior catalytic performance than the impregnated series, according to the following activity order: Ni(DM)/MCM-41 > Fe(DM)/MCM-41 > Cr(DM)/MCM-41 > Co(DM)/MCM-41 > Cu(DM)/MCM-41, with modest 52% ST selectivity achieved over Ni(DM)/MCM-41 and maximum 93 and 95% ST selectivity reached over Fe(DM)/MCM-41 and Cr(DM)/MCM-41, respectively, at 550 °C (Table 14, entries 15–17). Moreover, Ni2+ and Fe3+ MCM-41 framework, as well as NiOx and Fe2O3 extra-framework species were continuously oxidized by CO2 ensuring the regeneration and availability of active sites for EB dehydrogenation. CO2 plays a vital role in preserving the lattice oxygen on the catalyst surface. Study of deactivation revealed that the coke nature influenced the catalytic stability, and polyaromatics formation over Ni0 active sites was responsible for catalyst deactivation.
 Fig. 107  H2-TPR profiles of isomorphically substituted samples (DM (a)) and impregnated samples (MeOx (b)). Reprinted with permission from ref. 1054. Copyright 2009, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
7.2.3 Conclusions. 
The use of CO2 as a soft oxidant offers several advantages to ensure and enhance the catalytic activity due to its ability to activate ODH directly promoting EB ODH or by combination of alkane dehydrogenation and RWGS, thus favouring thermodynamics. The catalytic results showed that high ST selectivity could be reached at a good conversion level with OMS based catalysts (V/ and Cr/MCM-41, La-V/MCMC-41 and SBA-15) (Fig. 108 and 109). Nevertheless, catalyst lifetime was insufficiently studied to improve the stability and regeneration opportunities. More efforts should be spent to study catalyst robustness and reactor configuration to achieve and maintain high EB yield and selectivity offering SBA-15 support great potential. Despite their better thermal stability and robustness, zeolites because of smaller pore size may present some limitation to efficiently and homogeneously disperse metallic oxides, with high amounts of active species and metal loading. Hence, future study should focus on the benefits of hierarchical zeolite application due to its meso- and macroscale secondary porosity, together with the microporous primary structure, two-dimensional zeolite nanosheets and core–shell materials to improve the accessibility to the active center and mass transfer. In addition, OMS owing to their large pore structure offer great opportunities to prepare catalysts with high metallic phase dispersion and sintering stability owing to the confinement effect of the mesopores and strong interaction metal/support, combined with thermal stability.
 Fig. 108  ST yield versus EB conversion in the presence of the different catalysts discussed in this review. 
 Fig. 109  ST yield versus EB LHSV in the presence of the different catalysts discussed in this review. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: ST high yield versus EB LHSV. 


7.3 Ethylene production by ethane ODH. 
7.3.1 Introduction. 
Ethylene is a major chemical building block with worldwide production. Moreover, it is also the largest olefin on the market, derived from petroleum, and used as feedstock for the commercial production of intermediates and polymers such as ethylene oxide, ethylene dichloride, ethyl benzene, polyethylene, etc. The global production of ethylene amounted to 191.5 Mt in 2019 and is expected to increase up to 283 Mt in 2024. Ethylene is mainly produced by steam cracking of hydrocarbons like natural gas and naphtha at high temperatures, with strong drawbacks such as high energy consumption, rapid coke formation and CO2 emissions.1055–1057 Thus different alternative processes for ethylene production have been explored such as ethanol dehydration based on biomass and sustainable feedstock or the oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of ethane.1058,1059 In the last decade, the ODH of alkanes strengthened since it offers several advantages from kinetics and environmental standpoints.1060 Dehydrogenation is an endothermic process which in the presence of oxygen becomes exothermic, since the produced H2 is removed by oxidation into water and the equilibrium shifted (Scheme 72). However, the flammability of ethane, exothermicity of the reaction, over-oxidation of ethylene, the hazardous use of pure oxygen and probable hotspot formation hampered the industrial implementation. Therefore, the use of CO2 as a mild oxidant constitutes an alternative to produce ethylene and consumes anthropogenic CO2 and lengthens catalyst life minimizing coke deposition. Nevertheless, the lack of efficient and robust catalysts delayed the industrial application, with the industry requirement being an ethylene productivity of > 1 gC2H4 gcat−1 h−1, at a temperature as low as possible.1059,1061
 Scheme 72  Thermodynamics of ODH of ethane with CO2 as a soft oxidant. 
7.3.2 Zeolite based catalysts for ethane ODH. 
(a) Cr/zeolite-based catalysts for ethane ODH. 
Years ago, the catalytic activity of different transition metal oxides (Cr, V, and Ga) supported on protonic zeolites prepared by impregnation was explored.1062,1063 The surface acidity of zeolite plays a crucial role in chemical transformation, and for the ethane ODH the following activity order was obtained: Cr/H-ZSM-5 (Si/Al = 850) > Cr/H-ZSM-5 (Si/Al = 95) > Cr/H-ZSM-5 (Si/Al = 45) > Cr/H-ZSM-5 (Si/Al = 15). These results showed the impact of the Si/Al ratio and acid properties over the nature of Cr active species. The initial rate of ethylene formation was 1.6 times higher over Cr/H-ZSM-5 (Si/Al = 850) than over Cr/SiO2 while Cr/H-ZSM-5 catalysts, with a lower Si/Al ratio, exhibited low activity. On the other hand, Ga and V/ZSM-5 (Si/Al = 850) showed low activity for ethane ODH. TPR-spectra showed the presence of a reduction peak at high temperature (∼450 °C) assigned to the high oxidation state Cr species (Cr(VI)) with a key role in dehydrogenation activity. The authors also claimed the CO2 importance in the re-oxidation of Cr3+ species into Cr6+ active species and in removing coke from the catalytic surface, thus prolonging the catalyst life. At 650 °C, the maximum rate of the formation of ethylene was 0.516 mmol gcat−1 min−1 with 28% and 90% ethylene yield and selectivity (Table 15, entry 45). More recently, a series of submicron Cr/ZSM-5-supported samples were prepared by impregnation of ZSM-5 with submicron size and their catalytic and textural properties were explored.1064 XPS study allowed analyzing the two oxidation states of Cr species, and the deconvolution of the Cr2p2/3 spectra enabled obtaining two bands at 576.5 and 579.5 eV, assigned to Cr3+ and Cr6+, respectively, and determining a decreasing Cr6+/Cr3+ ratio when Si/Al increased and Na content decreased. These results showed the importance of zeolite framework composition and sodium presence on the nature of chromium species. The catalyst stability increased with the Si/Al ratio while Na samples exhibited superior catalytic performance than the protonic one. It has been widely described that the nature of chromium oxide is crucial and that crystalline Cr2O3 exhibits low activity while oligomeric chromium species are more active than isolated ones. Therefore the difference in activity for Cr/NaZSM-5 and Cr/HZSM-5 was attributed to the presence of dispersed Cr(VI) species in the form of polymeric chromates and monochromates in Cr/NaZSM-5 and to the presence of crystalline Cr2O3 in Cr/HZSM-5, determined by laser Raman spectroscopy. An optimum value of Cr close to 3 wt% was found. The best catalytic performance was achieved over 3 wt% Cr/NaZSM-5 (Si/Al = 160), at 650 °C, with initial 65% ethane conversion and 49% ethylene yield and good catalytic stability with practically constant 46% ethylene yield over 50 h TOS (Table 15, entries 46–48). A recent work reported an attempt of CO2 capture and utilization for ethane ODH in two stages, by combining in a fixed-bed reactor a physically mixed adsorbent and catalyst, composed of a double K–Ca salt and a Cr-impregnated H-ZSM-5 zeolite.988 A CO2 adsorption study revealed that K–Ca exhibited the highest CO2 uptake (5.2 mmol g−1) while characterization-activity results of Cr/H-ZSM-5 samples revealed an optimum 10 wt% Cr loading, with the highest TOF number and the lowest Cr surface density. The combined capture utilization process consisted first of CO2 adsorption at 600 °C, with 10% CO2/Ar, followed by the ethane ODH reaction at 700 °C with 5% C2H6/Ar. With GHSV = 5000 mL g−1 h−1, a (K–Ca)/(Cr10/HZSM-5) ratio of 1, 25% ethane conversion, 88% and 22% ethylene selectivity and yield and 14% CO2 conversion were achieved. Nevertheless, catalytic stability and adsorption studies revealed a constant deactivation of the catalyst and decrease of CO2 adsorption capacity due to adsorbent particle sintering and reduction of Cr active species (Cr6+) (Table 15, entry 50).

Recently, a series of 5 wt% Cr/clinoptilolite samples were prepared by impregnation. Therefore, the benefits of post-synthesis treatment with NH4NO3 and/or HNO3 over the textural and catalytic properties of Cr-Clinoptilolite samples were explored. Different treatments were applied to natural clinoptilolite zeolite (CLT-R) with NH4NO3 (CLT-A), with (CLT-A) and with NH4NO3 + HNO3 (CLT-IA).1065 The catalytic and characterization data revealed that clinoptilolite treatments constituted an efficient and attractive strategy to provide Cr-supported clinoptilolite catalysts with a higher surface area and a lower particles size owing to cation exchange and dealumination, leading to higher dispersion and smaller particle size of Cr. Moreover, a decrease in surface acidity was observed due to dealumination and resulted favourable to ethylene desorption, and promoting ethylene selectivity. Therefore, the benefits of acid treatment coupled with NH4NO3 treatment were shown and, at 700 °C, the better catalytic results were obtained over 5 wt% Cr/CLT-IA with 39% ethylene yield and 99% ethylene selectivity, over 5 h TOS (Table 15, entries 50–53).
(b) Zn/zeolite-based catalysts for ethane ODH. 
The catalytic behavior of highly dispersed Zn-based SSZ-13 (NaS50) zeolite with different Zn contents was explored for ethane ODH.1066 The catalytic data revealed that conversion was correlated with Zn loading, indicating that Zn constituted the single active site of the catalyst. The catalytic activity of zeolites based on Zn with different structures was compared and the superior 2.9 wt% Zn/NaS50 catalytic performance stood out. The following activity order was established, NaS50 > NaZSM-5 > MCM-22 > NaY, interestingly opposite to the size of the pore diameter order SSZ-13 (0.38 Å) < ZSM-5 and MCM-22 (0.55 Å) < Y (0.78 Å), indicating that zeolites with a small pore opening favoured CO2 diffusion and adsorption. Then, DFT and operando studies were performed to determine the role of CO2 in the dehydrogenation of C2H6 over Zn catalysts, considering two types of active sites based on the EXAFS data, [Zn–O–Zn]2+ and Zn2+, corresponding to zinc oxide and zinc ion located on SSZ-13 zeolite. The proposed C2H6 dehydrogenation mechanism involves two parts that are C2H6 activation and C2H4 desorption, and hydrogen desorption. Thus, C2H6 dehydrogenation involves the first C–H cleavage leading to the formation of Brønsted acid zinc hydroxyl group (ZnOH) while the second C–H bond cleavage leads to the formation of Zn–H. Therefore the elimination of hydrogen was envisioned by H2 desorption, the most energy demanding step over both the considered active sites, or by CO2 addition on the Zn–H moiety to form Zn–COOH with further formic acid desorption that constitutes a lower energy demanding step (Fig. 110). However, operando dual-beam Fourier transform infrared spectrometry results revealed that ethane ODH mechanism is similar regardless of the presence or absence of CO2, although stretching vibration in the region of 2385–2296 cm−1 typical of HCOO− was recorded in the presence of CO2, supporting the DFT calculations and the formation of formic acid to remove hydrogen.
 Fig. 110  Energy profiles for ethane ODH on Zn2+, without and with CO2 as a soft oxidant. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1066. Copyright 2021, American Chemical Society. 
A catalytic stability test, over 200 min, revealed a good and stable 0.9 wt% Zn/NaS50 catalytic performance, with 30% ethane conversion and 95–98% ethylene selectivity, while 2.9 wt% Zn/NaS50 suffered deactivation due to carbon deposition (Table 15, entries 54 and 55).
7.3.3 OMS-based catalysts for ethane ODH. 
(a) Cr/OMS-based catalysts for ethane ODH. 
Even though several catalytic system based on transition metal oxide were explored, silica-supported chromia revealed to be a more suitable ethane ODH catalyst with CO2, with up to 56% ethane conversion and 93% ethylene selectivity at 650 °C.1067 Nevertheless, the catalytic performance remained insufficient for commercial application,1068 and the chromium dispersion and stability represent the bottleneck to maximize the catalytic activity and life.1069–1071 Chromium oxide based materials constitute promising catalysts for ethane ODH and the chromium species nature and dispersion are of paramount importance. Cr6+ was determined as the most active species, and optimum and uniform dispersion as monolayer coverage allows avoiding the formation of polymeric species, which are less active and selective. Therefore, the use of OMS and zeolites as supports, owing to their surface area, thermal stability and versatility in framework composition, offers great opportunities to reach maximum chromia dispersion and stabilization to the design and development of efficient and robust ODH catalysts. The impact of the preparation method on metal dispersion and particle size has been widely reported since they in turn affect the catalytic properties of the prepared catalyst. Accordingly, the importance of the addition sequence of chromium and silica precursors on the textural and catalytic properties of Cr/MCM-41 materials with different Cr loadings was explored, with Cr-TEOS or TEOS-Cr indicating the order of addition of the precursors, i.e., chromium and silica and, silica and chromium, respectively.1072 TEM results revealed that the TEOS-Cr addition order provided mesoporous silica with a very small nanoparticle size while the SEM study indicated higher CrOx species dispersion with a maximum Cr6+/Cr3+ ratio obtained for 8 wt% Cr/MCM-41-Cr-TEOS sample. In concordance with the characterization data, the best catalytic performance was achieved over 8 wt% Cr/MCM-41-Cr-TEOS sample with 58% ethane conversion and 53% ethylene yield at 700 °C (Table 15, entries 1–7). Previously, a study of the catalytic performance of Cr/MCM-41 catalysts with different Cr contents, prepared by the impregnation method, revealed that an optimum chromium content of 8 wt% allowed reaching higher chromium species dispersion with monolayer coverage on MCM-41, smallest particle size and high surface area and optimum redox properties.1073 Similar results were reached and 56% ethane conversion and 94% ethylene selectivity were obtained over 8 wt% Cr/MCM-41, at 700 °C (Table 15, entries 8–11). Earlier the catalytic activity of Cr/MCM-41 for ethane ODH was reported.1074,1075 Characterization study determined the partial framework insertion of Cr into MCM-41 and Cr dispersion on the support MCM-41 surface, while Cr6+ and Cr3+ were determined as the catalytic active site. Ethane ODH involved the reduction of the Cr6+ sites by ethane dehydrogenation and the reoxidation of Cr3+ by surface oxygen species issued from CO2 dissociation or by CO2. The best catalytic performance was achieved over 5 wt% Cr/MCM-41 catalyst with 43% ethane conversion and 87% ethylene selectivity at 700 °C (Table 15, entries 12–14).
The benefits of sulfate modification of the silica support have been reported for the preparation of active ODH Cr-based catalysts.1067 The surface acidity has been reported as a key parameter to activate alkane and strongly influenced the catalytic performance of ODH catalysts, and the sulfatation showed to enhance the catalytic activity of the Cr/SiO2 catalyst but induced a slight decrease in ethylene selectivity. Accordingly, a chromium sulfate modified SBA-15 material with 6% sulfate and 5% Cr (5 wt% Cr/S-SBA-15) was prepared by impregnation.1076 TEM images indicated that sulfation induced a higher and homogeneous dispersion of Cr species in the monolayer and with smaller particle size, while XPS and Raman spectra revealed an increase of the Cr6+/Cr3+ ratio after sulfate modification which drastically influenced the catalytic activity. The TPR profile showed a shift of the reduction peak towards higher temperature, attributed to stronger interaction between Cr species and SBA-15, leading to higher dispersion. Consequently, the catalytic performance was higher over 5 wt% Cr/S-SBA-15 in terms of conversion and selectivity but remained lower than 5 wt% Cr/SiO2 where 56% ethane conversion and 93% ethylene selectivity were achieved at 650 °C.1067 At 650 °C, 61% ethane conversion and 82% ethylene selectivity were reached over 5 wt% Cr/S-SBA-15, while 45% ethane conversion and 77% ethylene selectivity were reached over 5 wt% Cr/SBA-15 (Table 15, entries 15–17). A study of catalytic stability revealed good stability over 16 h TOS with loss of 12 points of ethylene yield.
(i) Bimetallic OMS-based catalysts for ethane ODH. In the recent decades, and as it was mentioned many times in this review, in the preparation of bimetallic and multi-metallic catalysts much attention was paid to achieve a uniformly and highly disperse metallic active phase by establishing strong metal/metal and metal/support interactions due to electronic effects, improving metal stability against physical migration, and to enhance the catalytic activity. Following these leads, recently the synergetic effect of the addition of a second metal oxide (Co, Ce, Zn, Nb, V, and Mo) to the Cr-MCM-41 sample, over the textural, physicochemical and catalytic properties, was investigated.1077 Accordingly MCM-41-supported bimetallic oxides containing Ce, Co, and Zn were prepared following one-pot synthesis, while samples containing Mo, V and Nb were prepared following a two steps synthesis (hydrothermal + impregnation). The mesoporous structure of MCM-41 and high BET surface area were maintained after the incorporation of both metal oxides. H2-TPR profiles showed different reduction peaks from those of the Cr-monometallic sample attributed to metal/metal interaction, and revealed an increase in the Cr6+ concentration for 4 wt% Cr–4 wt% Ce/MCM41 while higher temperature reduction peaks were registered for 4 wt% Cr–4 wt% Mo/MCM41 and 4 wt% Cr–4 wt% V/MCM-41 which could imply the worse reducibility of chromium. The catalytic activity of Cr-based catalysts is related to the amount of Cr6+ species and the characterization study indicated that incorporation of Ce or Co improved the reducibility of Cr6+ owing to the redox properties of CeO2 and Co3O4. Therefore, the catalytic activity could be correlated with amount of redox sites and dispersion of the metallic phase, and Cr–Ce/MCM-41 and Cr–Co/MCM-41 samples exhibited the higher catalytic activity while Cr–V/MCM-41 and Cr–Mo/MCM-41 displayed lower activity and selectivity to ethylene, and the highest ethylene selectivity and lower activity were reached over Cr–Zn/MCM-41 and Cr–Nb/MCM-41 (Table 15, entries 18–23). The highest catalytic performance was reached over 4 wt% Cr–4 wt% Ce/MCM-41 with 37.9% ethane conversion and 35.1% ethylene yield. In addition to medium catalytic results, the catalyst showed constant deactivation over 8 h TOS. The same group studied the impact of the addition of different TiO2 loadings over the catalytic and textural properties of 8 wt% Cr–Ti/MCM-41 samples.1078 MCM-41 samples were first modified with TiO2 addition prior to Cr loading. The synergetic effect between both metal allowed improving Cr dispersion, and Cr6+ concentration and enhancing metal–support interaction. TPR profiles registered two reduction peaks corresponding to the reduction of Cr6+ species dispersed on the α-Cr2O3 surfaces at low temperatures, and Cr6+ species interacting with the framework of siliceous support at higher temperatures. An optimum in the Cr6+ amount interacting with the support was observed for 1.9 wt% Ti, which exhibits high Cr dispersion, predicting optimized catalytic activity and stability. Therefore, the best catalytic results were reached over 8 wt% Cr–1.9 wt% Ti/MCM41 with 52% ethane conversion and 48% ethylene yield at 700 °C (Table 15, entries 24–27). Nevertheless, a constant decrease of the catalytic performance was observed over 8 h TOS (Table 15, entry 28). In the same way, a series of Cr/Ce-MCM-41 samples were prepared by first adding CeO2 and then Cr loading.1079 Moreover, the benefits of utilization of plasma energy for catalyst preparation (Cr/Ce-MCM-41(Si/Ce = 25)-p) over their morphological and catalytic features were observed. TEM analysis evidenced the alteration of the MCM-41 structure when excess of ceria was used leading to structure deterioration (Si/Ce > 25). The incorporation of ceria allowed enhancing the dispersion of Cr while the plasma treatment further improved the distribution and uniformity of nanoparticles in the narrow range of 7.4–22 nm. The authors claimed that the synergistic effect of ceria incorporation and plasma treatment strengthened the interaction between Cr/MCM-41 preventing the migration of Cr particles and controlling the size of Cr ensemble. Therefore, the best catalytic performance was achieved over Cr/Ce-MCM-41(Si/Ce = 25)-p with 62% ethylene yield and 64% ethane conversion, at 700 °C (Table 15, entries 29–32). A comparison of catalytic reported data is difficult because of the difference and lack of details of reaction conditions. Therefore, it is not possible to determine ethylene productivity. However, Cr/Ce-MCM-41(Si/Ce = 25)-p exhibited high catalytic performance and stability and offers great potential for the development of an industrial catalyst. A similar study was reported using SBA-15 as the support in which Cr–Ce/SBA-15 samples with different Ce loadings were prepared by co-impregnation.1069 The characterization study showed the improvement of Cr dispersion due to the incorporation of Ce with the preservation of the SBA-15 structure. The TPR profiles registered three reduction peaks, at 457 °C, corresponding to the reduction of Cr6+ species, and at 378 and 799 °C to the reduction of surface ceria oxide (Fig. 111). The comparison of H2-TPR profiles of 5Cr–Ce/SBA-15 and 10Ce/SBA-15 samples indicated that Ce reduction temperature decreased due to Ce/Cr interaction that modifies the redox ability of Ce species. H2 consumption increased with Ce loading to reach a maximum for 5Cr–10Ce/SBA-15 sample corresponding to the maximum amount of Cr6+ species. Therefore, the catalytic activity varied with Ce content, was optimum for 10 wt%, and good catalytic performance was displayed over 5 wt% Cr–10 wt% Ce/SBA-15, with 55% ethane conversion and 96% ethylene selectivity at 700 °C (Table 15, entries 33–36). The catalyst showed constant catalytic activity deactivation with TOS, registering a conversion decrease from 55% until 45% and could not be fully recovered after thermal treatment under CO2.
 Fig. 111  H2-TPR profiles of SBA-15 samples: (a) 10Ce/SBA-15; (b) bulk Cr2O3; (c) 2.5Cr/SBA-15; (d) 5Cr/SBA-15; (e) 7.5Cr/SBA-15; (f) 10Cr/SBA-15; (g) 5Cr–5Ce/SBA-15; (h) 5Cr–7.5Ce/SBA-15; (i) 5Cr–10Ce/SBA-15; and (j) 5Cr–15Ce/SBA-15. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1069. Copyright 2008, Springer Science Business Media, LLC. 
Years ago, the study and development of monolithic catalysts revealed several advantages of these composites, such as low-pressure drop, high heat and mass transfer rates and minimum axial dispersion owing to their unified structure in a single block. To take advantages of monolithic catalyst features, monolithic Cr/SBA-15/Al2O3/FeCrAl catalysts were prepared using FeCrAl foils with honeycomb structure as the support and SBA-15/Al2O3 as the washcoat.1080 Then, different Cr content catalysts were prepared by impregnation. H2-TPR profiles depict two Cr6+ reduction peaks at 374–397 °C and 510 °C, corresponding to soluble Cr6+ species (isolated chromates on the surface) and to the grafted Cr6+ species on the silica surface, respectively. XPS spectra of Cr2p over fresh and used 5% Cr/SBA-15/Al2O3/FeCrAl monolithic catalysts showed the presence of two Cr species, Cr3+ and Cr6+, with a higher Cr6+/Cr3+ ratio in the fresh catalyst than in the spent one. Catalytic data registered at 750 °C showed that ethylene yield increased with Cr loading up to 5% and further decreased accordingly with the Cr6+/Cr3+ ratio and the nature of chromium species (Table 15, entries 37–40). A stability test showed the high catalytic stability of 5 wt% Cr/SBA-15/Al2O3/FeCrAl over a period of 1100 h TOS. Ethane conversion and ethylene selectivity remained practically constant along 1100 h and around 66% and 95%, respectively (Fig. 112). This work constitutes the best catalytic test reported for ethane ODH.
 Fig. 112  Catalytic performance of 5 wt% Cr/SBA-15/Al2O3/FeCrAl catalyst for ethane ODH maintained over 1100 h. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1080. Copyright 2008, American Chemical Society. 
(b) V/OMS-based catalysts for ethane ODH. 
V-supported catalysts exhibited high activity and selectivity for ethane ODH,1081,1082 and the high vanadium dispersion, loading and support play a key role in the nature of vanadium species and activity. The most active species are monomeric V5+ while vanadate dimeric or oligomeric structures can coexist at higher V-loadings.1083 The nature of the support through the establishment of strong interactions could influence the metal dispersion and stability. As well as, basic supports favour the formation of stable vanadium mixed oxides, while acid supports contribute to high dispersion of vanadium species avoiding the formation of polymeric species. Therefore, a recent study reported the influence of the introduction of different metals into MCM-41 framework on the nature of vanadium species, activity and stability of ODH catalysts.1084 Thus a series of VOx/MCM-41, VOx/Mg-MCM-41, VOx/Mg–Al-MCM-41 and VOx/Mg–Zr-MCM-41 were prepared. The incorporation of different metals (Mg, Al, Zr) affected the catalytic activity, the structural and textural properties (crystallinity, particle size, dispersion) and acid/base properties. XRD patterns showed that the high surface area of MCM-41 enables high dispersion of vanadium and no peak of crystalline V2O5 was registered. The incorporation of Mg and Zr into the VOx/Mg–Zr-MCM-41 sample led to the formation of MgO and the tetragonal phase of zirconia. Moreover, the framework substitution of a larger diameter cation such as Zr4+ and Mg2+ by smaller Si4+ induced modifications of bond length of M–O–Si and then led to the MCM-41 structure deformation. FESEM and EDX analyses revealed improved V dispersion on the VOx/Mg–Zr-MCM-41 sample attributed to the surface acidity due to framework zirconia. The surface acidity determines alkane activation; greater the acidity, higher the ethane conversion. Thus the framework incorporation of Mg, Al and Zr produced different samples with different acid strengths and hence different catalytic behaviors. Accordingly, from NH3-TPD, the following order of acidity was obtained: VOx/MCM-41 < VOx/Mg-MCM-41 < VOx/Mg–Al-MCM-41 < VOx/Mg–Zr-MCM-41. UV-Vis diffuse reflectance spectra allowed determining two types of vanadium oxo-species, one dimensional oligomeric and two-dimensional polymeric units in octahedral coordination. The best catalytic performance for ethane ODH was observed for the VOx/Mg–Zr-MCM-41, which contained only oligomeric tetrahedral vanadium species with higher dispersion and the higher amount of acid sites. Thus, at 700 °C, 55% ethane conversion and 43% ethylene yield with a productivity of 0.48 gC2H4 gcat−1 h−1 and good catalytic stability over 10 h TOS were reached over VOx/Mg–Zr-MCM-41 (Table 15, entries 41–44). Additionally, a mechanism based on catalytic, characterization studies and literature data was proposed following the Mars–Van Krevelen mechanism (Fig. 113).
 Fig. 113  Proposed ethane ODH mechanism over vanadium MCM-41-based catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1084. Copyright 2017, Elsevier Inc. All rights reserved. 
7.3.4 Conclusions. 
Most of the reported catalysts for ethane ODH are CrOx-based, implying severe limitations due to the carcinogenicity of chromium species (Cr6+) and severe control and measures in the catalyst preparation, use and elimination. Therefore, alternative should be deeply considered. Besides, the catalytic performance of 5 wt% Cr/SBA-15/Al2O3/FeCrAl over 1100 h reached goals of production and catalyst stability with good 63% ethylene yield, highlighting the potential of SBA-15 as a support. Actually, catalyst success will depend on catalyst and operation costs, catalyst stability and coke deposition besides the catalytic performances and features. The use of zeolites as a support due to the pore size characteristic is limited, since a high active metal content is required to achieve high catalytic activity provided by a large number of active sites, and to achieve high productivity, leading to a decrease in surface area and blockage of the zeolite pores. Therefore, the preparation of hierarchical zeolite-based ODH catalysts, due to their meso- and macroscale secondary porosity, together with the microporous primary structure and two-dimensional zeolite nanosheets based ODH catalysts, should allow the preparation of zeolitic catalysts with a high content of metal species with improved catalytic activity and mass transfer.
In Fig. 114 and 115 C2H6 conversion and C2H4 yield are plotted versus C2H6 WHSV and conversion, respectively, highlighting the catalytic performance of 5 wt% Cr/SBA-15/Al2O3/FeCrAl monolithic and Ce–Cr/MCM-41 catalysts.
 Fig. 114  Ethylene yield versus ethane conversion in the presence of the different catalysts discussed in this review. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high C2H6 conversion versus C2H6 WHSV. 
 Fig. 115  Ethane conversion versus ethane WHSV in the presence of the different catalysts discussed in this review. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high C2H6 yield versus high C2H6 conversion. 


7.4 Propylene production by propane ODH. 
7.4.1 Introduction. 
Propylene is a valuable platform molecule for the production of a large number of chemical commodities (propylene oxide, acrylic acid, acrylonitrile, isopropanol, cumene, and butyraldehyde) and polymers. The propylene global production in 2020 was 108 Mt and is expected to reach 128 Mt by 2027, and the current industrial production is based on catalytic propane dehydrogenation.991,1085 From thermodynamic standpoints (Scheme 73), propane ODH with CO2 involves different competitive endothermic reactions and mainly propane dry reforming which is thermodynamically less constrained than ethane and methane dry reforming.1086 Other reactions such as cracking and propylene and propane decomposition take place readily. On the other hand, since propane ODH is a reaction with volume increase due to process stoichiometry, the co-feeding of inert gas is favourable to propane conversion enhancement, decreasing propylene production. Therefore, the challenge lies in developing a selective catalyst to promote propane ODH and kinetically suppress propane dry reforming. RWGS favours the equilibrium shifting whereas reverse Boudouard reaction improves catalyst life. In addition, efficient separation methods are required for CO2 and CO separation.1087
 Scheme 73  Thermodynamics of oxidative dehydrogenation of propane with CO2 as a soft oxidant and simultaneous processes. 
Recent reviews deal with the oxidative dehydrogenation of propane with soft oxidants such as CO2, NOx and S-containing compounds using solid catalysts.993,1002,1087 The extensive report carefully analyzed and described the impact of metal oxide nature (redox (CrOx, VOx) and non-redox (Ga2O3)), morphology and dispersion, support, addition of promoters and method of preparation. For propane ODH with CO2, CrOx-based catalysts revealed higher propylene selectivity at a higher propane conversion level than other metal oxide based catalysts playing key role redox cycles, CrOx species nature and dispersion, and the textural and chemical properties of the support.1002 The use of CO2 as a soft oxidant presents some benefits as it allows completing redox cycles, shifting equilibrium due to H2 removal by RWGS, improving catalyst life by coke elimination through reverse Boudouard reaction and enhancing propylene selectivity due to competitive adsorption with CO2 favouring propylene desorption. Still efforts are required to elucidate the reaction mechanism, to improve the stability and regeneration of the catalysts, and to design efficient, robust and selective catalysts towards C–H bond rather than C–C bond activation, using metallic oxide-based catalysts free of Cr due to its carcinogenicity. The acid/base properties constitute a crucial parameter not only because they affect the active metallic phase dispersion but also because different reactants with different adsorption properties are involved. Indeed, CO2 adsorption and activation are favoured onto basic sites while propane and H2 preferably adsorb on acidic sites, while undesired reaction such as cracking, isomerization and oligomerization can take place. Thus a balance in acidity and basicity surface should exist.
7.4.2 Zeolite-based catalysts for propane ODH. 
(a) Effect of surface acidity over catalytic properties for propane ODH. 
Cr/ZSM-5 catalyst with a high Si/Al ratio of 240 was first reported for propane dehydrogenation in 2011.1088 The catalytic study displayed the effect of the ZSM-5 crystallite size on the catalytic activity. Hence, laser Raman spectroscopy allowed determining the nature of Cr species, and CrVI species (monochromates) were shown to be responsible for the catalytic activity of the Cr-supported catalysts in alkane oxidative dehydrogenation with CO2. The best catalytic performance was reached over the 3 wt% Cr/ZSM-5-S with 0.4 μm crystal size than over 3 wt% Cr/ZSM-5-L with 2 μm crystal size due to the presence of a higher amount of CrVI species. Good catalytic data were registered over 3 wt% Cr/ZSM-5-S, with 48% propane conversion and 86% propylene selectivity, after 0.16 h TOS, at 550 °C (Table 16, entry 1). Surface acidity is a key parameter to selectively adsorb and activate the reactants, and different strategies can be followed to modify the acidity of zeolite by dealumination using acid treatment or by steaming at high temperature. Accordingly, a series of Cr/Si and Al-Beta samples were prepared with different Cr loadings by impregnation using AlBeta sample with Si/Al = 17 and dealuminated SiBeta with Si/Al = 1000.1089,1090 The catalytic behavior for propane ODH revealed the negative effect of the presence of Al over the catalytic activity of 2 wt% Cr/AlBeta achieving low 4.5% propane conversion and 2% propylene yield, at 550 °C, attributed to higher surface acidity. In contrast, good catalytic performance was registered for the dealuminated samples showing the benefit of dealumination over the catalytic properties. For the Cr/SiBeta catalyst series, an increase in the catalytic performance was noted with a Cr content of up to 2 wt% according to the number of Cr redox species and a further decrease. Indeed, quantitative results of H2-TPR determined that in the H2 volume consumption/Crtotalcontent ratio was maximum, with a drastic decrease for higher Cr content in the range of 2–7 wt%, suggesting that Cr is mainly present as non-redox Cr species in the form of Cr2O3 amorphous and crystalline as it was supported by UV-V diffuse reflectance spectroscopy spectra illustrating two peaks at 270 nm and 360 nm assigned to the charge transfer of Cr6+–O2− transition of monochromate species onto the support (Fig. 116). Maximum 87% propylene selectivity and 25% propane conversion were maintained over 6 h TOS at 450 °C (Table 16, entries 2–5).

 Fig. 116  UV-Vis DRS spectra recorded at room temperature for Cr/BA samples: (a) 0.5CrSiBEA, (b) 1CrSiBEA, (c) 2CrSiBEA, (d) 5CrSiBEA and (e) 7CrSiBEA. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1090. Copyright 2019, Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. 
Another strategy to modify and weaken the acidic properties of zeolite is the isomorphous substitution of Al by another metal such as boron. Therefore, Cr/silicalite-1 and Cr/H[B]MFI (incorporating boron) were prepared and further treated by steaming (Cr/H[B]MFI-st).1091 H2-TPR spectra revealed that no reduction peak of Cr6+ into Cr3+ was observed for Cr/silicalite-1 and Cr/H[B]MFI-st, while it was detected for the boron containing sample (Cr/H[B]MFI). The presence of boron allowed the formation of some Cr6+ species readily reduced into Cr3+ by H2 and also by steaming treatment. Then, steaming treatment enabled the auto-reduction of Cr6+ into Cr3+ located near to boron to counterbalance the negative charge of the framework. The comparison of the catalytic performance revealed that Cr/H[B]MFI exhibited higher catalytic activity and stability than Cr/silicalite-1. The propane conversion over Cr/H[B]MFI decreased rapidly from 50% to 20% in 2 h while this was around 10% over Cr/silicalite-1. When the catalytic performance of Cr/H[B]MFI-st and Cr/H[B]MFI samples was compared for 70 h TOS, a constant decrease of propane conversion from 30 to 7% was recorded over Cr/H[B]MFI over 60 h TOS, while ∼13% propane conversion remained stable over Cr/H[B]MFI-st. These results were attributed to the weakened Brønsted acidity because of steaming treatment and the differences in the nature of Cr species, the Cr3+/Cr6+ ratio and the formation of polychromates (Table 16, entries 6–8). Dealumination strategy by steaming of HZSM-5 zeolite to optimize acid properties of Ga2O3/HZSM-5 was also used to improve the catalytic performance of the Ga2O3/HZSM-5 catalyst.1092 A NH3-TPD study showed only one desorption peak with a small area at 264 °C attributed to a low Al framework content for HZSM-5 zeolite, while after gallium oxide incorporation, a second peak at 465 °C was recorded, indicating that some strong acid sites are formed. After steaming and dealumination, acidity was drastically weakened. The excess of loss acidity was attributed to the loss of interaction of Ga2O3 with the support due to the absence of Al. Hence, the catalytic performance of Ga2O3/HZSM-5 samples varied with the Si/Al ratio, and propane conversion order was Ga2O3/HZSM-5(700) ≫ Ga2O3/HZSM-5(650) > Ga2O3/HZSM-5(750) ∼ Ga2O3/HZSM-5(600) > Ga2O3/HZSM-5 ∼ Ga2O3/HZSM-5(800) ∼ Ga2O3/silicalite. Therefore, the catalytic performance was maximum over Ga2O3/HZSM-5(700), 22% propene yield, and remained stable over 100 h (Table 16, entries 12 and 13). Catalyst stability increased with increasing Si/Al ratio because coke deposition lowered. As previously published, the proposed mechanism for propane ODH over Ga2O3 supported catalysts should follow the heterolytic dissociation pathway, where the presence of Brønsted acid sites in zeolite should promote the dehydrogenation reaction (Fig. 117).1093 On the other hand the role of CO2 was explored, and a control of CO2 and CO concentration for propane ODH allowed determining that CO production was twice CO2 conversion, leading to assume that CO2 mainly participates in the Boudouard reaction and coke gasification.
 Fig. 117  Ethane ODH mechanism on gallium oxide supported catalysts adapted from ref. 1092 and 1093. 
The introduction of phosphoros into the zeolite by impregnation constitutes another strategy to modify the acid properties of zeolite because of the formation of weaker Brønsted acid sites in addition to thermal stability improvement.1094–1097 Thus, the same group prepared a series of Ga2O3/P-HZSM-5 samples with different P percentages (Ga2O3/P-HZSM-5(n)) and studied the catalytic performance for propane ODH.1098 The catalytic results revealed that phosphorus incorporation allowed improving the catalytic performance of pristine Ga2O3/HZSM-5, and the best catalytic performance was achieved over Ga2O3/P-HZSM-5(1.5) with 22% propane conversion and 19% propylene yield after stabilization (Table 16, entries 14–16). A constant catalytic performance decrease was observed over TOS to reach a steady state after 50 h. The propene yield first increased with P loading, until 1.5% and then decreased. This trend was attributed to the decrease of the amount of strong acid sites that favour side reactions and to the increase of the amount of weak acid sites which should play a minor role in the propane ODH process.
The influence of the Si/Al molar ratio for ZnO/HZSM-5 samples prepared from different Si/Al ratios in the synthesis gel using NaAlO2 as the Al source, confirmed that high amount of acid sites decreased the catalytic activity and stability due to side reactions.1099 Therefore an increase in catalyst stability with increasing the Si/Al ratio was observed and ascribed to the decrease in surface acidity supported by NH3-TPD and FT-IR of pyridine adsorption spectra. The role of CO2 was also studied by varying CO2 partial pressure, and confirming the benefits of the use of CO2 to convert H2 into water through RWGS reaction and thus promoting propane ODH, also fomenting coke gasification, while a higher partial pressure induced catalyst deactivation due to competitive adsorption of CO2 and propane over the catalytic surface. Therefore, the best catalytic performance was achieved over ZnO/HZSM-5 with a Si/Al ratio = 160, with 41.5% propane conversion, 26% propylene yield and higher catalytic stability over 3 h TOS (Table 16, entries 9–11).
(b) Effect of the preparation method on the catalytic properties for propane ODH. 
3D-printing technology constitutes a new tool to prepare materials with tuned monolithic properties (surface area, channel size, chemical composition and loading) which is not possible by extrusion. 3D-printing has been successfully used to prepare adsorbent catalyst monoliths such as zeolites, aminosilicates, and MOFs.1100–1105 Following these leads, a series of Ga, V, and Zr H-ZSM-5 monolithic catalysts with high metal oxide contents were prepared by 3D printing technology (Fig. 118).1106 Characterization of the textural properties revealed that homogeneous distribution of metal oxides was reached for all samples and based on the H2-TPR results, V3+, V4+, V5+, Ga2+ and Zr4+ species were formed, demonstrating the variety of peaks assigned to the different oxidation states of vanadium and thus, the redox properties of the prepared samples. The NH3-TPD profiles of the 3D-printed catalyst monoliths revealed the presence of acid sites with different strengths accordingly to the presence of gallium oxide, vanadium oxide and also zirconium oxide while high-strength acid site formation due to ZrV2O7 superstructures was also detected. In summary, 15V–15Zr@ZSM-5 and 15Ga–15V@ZSM-5 had higher amounts of strong acid sites, while 15V–15Zr@ZSM-5 and 15Ga–15Zr@ZSM-5 had weaker acid sites. 15Ga–15V–15Zr@ZSM-5 contained low-, medium-, and high-strength acid in agreement with the mixture of three metal oxides. The best catalytic performance was obtained over 15 wt% V–15 wt% Zr@ZSM-5 with ∼38% propane conversion and 90% propylene selectivity remaining stable over 6 h TOS, at 550 °C, although all of the monoliths tested exhibited good catalytic performance and stability (Table 16, entries 17–20). These results demonstrated the applicability of 3D printing technology for preparing active propane ODH catalysts.
 Fig. 118  Photographs of (a) 15Ga–15Zr@ZSM-5, (b) 15Ga–15V@ZSM-5, (c) 15V–15Zr@ZSM5, and (d) 10Ga–10V-10Zr@ZSM-5 monoliths. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1106. Copyright 2020, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
7.4.3 OMS-based catalysts for propane ODH. 
The dispersion and stability of metal species is key and determine the catalytic activity and can be influenced by the metal/support interaction, acid/base and textural properties. Thus, for the design and development of efficient and robust propane ODH catalysts, ordered mesoporous materials such as SBA-15 and MCM-41 constitute great candidates to deposit metal oxides despite their lower thermal stability in comparison to zeolites.
(a) V/OMS-based catalysts for propane ODH. 
As aforementioned, the preparation method and vanadia content allow controlling the nature of vanadia species while the carrier can play an important role. The surface VOx active species are present in sub-monolayer coverage with surface areas close to 300 m2 g−1 and containing 8 V atoms per nm2 for various supports, except for SiO2 because of lower metal/support interaction with 2.5 V atoms per nm2.1107 Recently, a series of V-MCM-41 catalysts were prepared by hydrothermal synthesis,1108 and low amounts of V species were introduced into the MCM-41 framework while conserving the high specific surface area and ordered mesoporous structure. High dispersion of V active species (monomeric and dimeric) was achieved; however, due to high vanadium content an increase in the number of acid sites, weak and strong was detected, as well as the formation of V2O5 crystallites. Laser Raman spectroscopy allowed stating the formation of bulk V2O5 at high V loading to which the decrease in catalytic activity for propane ODH was attributed. Owing to higher dispersion of VO43−, 6.8 wt% V-MCM-41 exhibited the best catalytic performance. An increase of the CO2/C3H8 ratio, from 1:1 up to 5:1, allowed improving propane conversion from 35 to 45%, whereas propene selectivity did not suffer a significant change. Thus, maximum 45% propane conversion and 92% propylene selectivity were reached at 600 °C with suitable CO2/C3H8 = 4:1 (Table 16, entries 21–23). A reaction mechanism was proposed involving as the first step, the H abstraction by O of the OV group from propane secondary C atom, and in the second step, H abstraction occurs by the O of the V–O–Si bond, yielding two hydroxyl groups whose dehydration produces water and the formation of oxygen vacancy, with propene being released. Along these two steps, vanadyl group is reduced from VV into VIII and finally re-oxidized by CO2, restoring VO group and oxygen lattice (Fig. 119). Good catalytic activity was reported but over a short 2 h TOS. A study of catalyst recycling revealed that the regeneration of the catalyst over 4 cycles was possible owing to oxidative treatment, the deactivation being due to the reduction V active sites and coke deposition.
 Fig. 119  Reaction and regeneration cycle mechanism for the propane ODH over V-MCM-41 catalysts. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1108. Copyright 2018, Dalian Institute of Chemical Physics, the Chinese Academy of Sciences. Published by Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
(b) Cr/OMS-based catalysts for propane ODH. 
The catalytic activity and selectivity of supported chromia materials for alkane dehydrogenation have been studied for many years, with the dispersion and formation of monochromate active species being the key parameters. Years ago, it was demonstrated that the MCM-41 ordered mesoporous structure and its high surface area allow high dispersion and amount of Cr6+ active species achieving similar catalytic performance than Cr/SiO2 for propane ODH (Table 16, entries 24 and 25).1109 Previously, Cr/MCM-41 materials were successfully prepared by direct hydrothermal (DHT) and template-ion exchange (TIE) methods without destroying the mesoporous structure of MCM-41 for Si/Cr ratio > 25. Difference in chromium species was observed, and for samples prepared by DHT (Cr/MCM-41(DHT)), monochromate species were formed while for samples prepared by the TIE method (Cr/MCM-41(TIE)), large amount of polychromate species were formed besides monochromate species.1110 Nevertheless, both samples exhibited similar catalytic performance with 92% propylene oxide selectivity and 28% and 29% propane conversion over 3 wt% Cr/MCM-41(DHT) and 3 wt% Cr/MCM-41(TIE) catalysts, respectively, at 550 °C (Table 16, entries 26 and 27). However, no stability data on catalytic activity were provided. Later, four series of Cr supported catalysts with different Cr contents (0.7–13.7 wt%) over MCM-41, amorphous silica and mesoporous silica with cubic (SBA-1) and hexagonal phase (SBA-15) were prepared by impregnation and tested for propane ODH.1111–1113 Higher dispersion of chromium species was achieved over mesoporous supports owing to their high surface area, implying a higher content of Cr6+ for mesoporous materials. The nature of chromium species varied with the Cr content, and Cr6+ species directly anchored are mainly formed at a low Cr content, whereas at a higher content Cr2O3 particles are produced. However, the amount of Cr6+ species depends on the support and specifically the surface area. For MCM-41 series, an optimum 6.8 wt% Cr content was determined reaching 40% propane conversion and 88.5% propylene selectivity at 550 °C (Table 16, entries 28–31).1113 Optimum Cr species dispersion was demonstrated as a crucial parameter to promote propane ODH and depended on the presence of Cr6+ species as precursors of Cr active species formed by a different redox cycles of Cr2+/Cr3+ in contrast to mainly described redox cycle of Cr3+/Cr6+.
In the same way, the catalytic behavior of Cr supported on silica, SBA-1 and SBA-15 with different Cr loadings was recently compared.1111,1112 Similar conclusions were reported, with the catalytic activity being dependent on the Cr6+ amount and dispersion in monolayer coverage controlled by Cr content and support surface area (Fig. 120). Moreover, Cr6+ species were determined as precursors of Cr3+ and Cr2+ active species for propane dehydrogenation and formed Cr2+/Cr3+ redox cycle earlier proposed.1113,1114 Moderate catalytic performance after 10 min TOS at 550 °C was obtained over 3.4 wt%/SBA-1 and 3.4 wt%/SBA-15, reaching 33 and 27% propane conversion, and 88 and 89% propylene selectivity, respectively (Table 16, entries 31 and 32).1112  Previously, the catalytic properties of the Cr/SBA-15 catalyst for propane ODH had already been described.1115 2 wt% Cr/SBA-15 exhibited better catalytic performance than other Cr-supported catalysts due to high Cr species dispersion, reaching low 24% propane conversion and 84% propylene selectivity, at 650 °C, over 6 h TOS (Table 16, entry 33). The preparation and use for propane ODH of monolithic V–Cr/SBA-15/Al2O3/FeCrAl catalysts, using FeCrAl foils as the support and SBA-15/Al2O3 as the washcoat and V–Cr/SBA-15 as the active phase, was described with 10 wt% V and 0–12.5 wt% Cr contents.1116 The SBA-15 mesoporous structure was conserved after vanadium and chromium impregnation, and V5+ tetrahedral species were determined as the main active species, with high dispersion and enhanced redox properties, owing to strong V–Cr interaction. The incorporation of Cr allowed increasing the redox capacities of V and a lower temperature shift of the V reduction peak was observed from the TPR profiles. Optimum catalytic performance was reached over 10 wt% V–10 wt% Cr/SBA–15/Al2O3/FeCrAl sample, with good 43% propane conversion and 86% propylene selectivity at 650 °C (Table 16, entries 34–39).
 Fig. 120  H2-TPR profiles of four sample series with different Cr contents: SiO2-p (A), SiO2-a (B), SBA-15 (C) and SBA-1 (D) catalysts series. 0.7, 2.1, 3.4 and 6.8 mean wt% of Cr. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1111. Copyright 2011, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
7.4.4 Conclusions. 
Propane ODH with CO2 is an attractive approach to produce propylene showing different advantages such as shifting of equilibrium owing to combination with RWGS, completion of redox cycle with CO2 participation, and improvement of catalytic life due to coke elimination through reverse Boudouard reaction. However, many unknown data still remain to be clarified such as redox cycle and involved species, mechanisms, competitive adsorption between CO2, alkane and alkene and, above all, to investigate the stability, reuse and regeneration of catalysts in order to give industrial application a chance. Therefore, computational modeling and in situ spectroscopy studies will be fundamental tools for the rational design of efficient and stable ODH catalysts. Fig. 121 and 122 depict a graphical comparison of the different catalytic performance of propane ODH catalysts to evaluate the productivity or selectivity of the process. High catalytic data were reached, with high propane conversion up to 42%, and propylene selectivity up to 92% with rapid deactivation of the catalyst (V or Cr/MCM-41), while monolithic V–Cr/SBA-15 based catalysts exhibited the best catalytic performance. For industrial application, propylene productivity is a benchmark and propylene selectivity is of paramount importance. To compare the catalysts’ life additional studies are required since each process has a specific state-of-the-art technology, an optimized reactor system including catalyst regeneration cycles, and the reaction time can be short with catalyst regeneration intervals allowing continuous use for years.991 For example the CATOFIN® process, a widely applied technology, alternates incessantly dehydrogenation, regeneration, and purge stages with a few minutes of duration for each stage, in a multiple reactor bed to ensure constant propylene production (Fig. 123).1117
 Fig. 121  Propylene yield versus propane conversion in the presence of the different catalysts discussed in this review. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high C3H8 yield versus high C3H8 conversion. 
 Fig. 122  Propane conversion versus propane WHSV in the presence of the different catalysts discussed in this review. The ovoid highlights the best catalytic performance: high C3H8 conversion versus C3H8 WHSV. 
 Fig. 123  Process flow diagram of the Catofin propane process. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1118. Copyright. 


7.5 Isobutene production by isobutane ODH. 
7.5.1 Introduction. 
Isobutene constitutes an important chemical building block in the production of polyisobutene, methyl tert-butyl ether, butyl rubber, and methyl acrylates. Currently, the two main methods to produce isobutene based on fossil source are naphtha steam cracking and fluidized catalytic cracking, which cannot fully supply the increasing demand. Thus, catalytic dehydrogenation of isobutane represents a potential and alternative route to produce isobutene.991,1119,1120 However, isobutane dehydrogenation raises limitations such as thermodynamic equilibrium, high-energy consumption, and severe catalyst deactivation. Over CrOx/Al2O3 commercial catalyst, at 560 °C, 55% isobutene conversion and up to 93.5% isobutene selectivity can be achieved.1121 As aforementioned, oxidative dehydrogenation using O2 is attractive because of its exothermic nature but limited selectivity is reached due to side reactions and deep oxidation of products. Therefore, oxidative dehydrogenation with CO2 becomes an attractive approach scarcely explored in the presence of zeolite and OMS based catalysts (Scheme 74).
 Scheme 74  Oxidative dehydrogenation of isobutene with CO2. 
7.5.2 OMS- and zeolite-based catalysts for isobutane ODH. 
As reviewed before, vanadium and chromium metal oxide supported materials are the most widely used and studied catalysts for alkanes oxidative dehydrogenation with CO2 despite their toxicity. In line, a series of Cr-supported silicalite-1 samples were prepared by impregnation, tested for isobutene ODH with CO2, and compared to the catalytic performance of Cr/SBA-15 sample.1115 A combination mechanism of simple isobutane dehydrogenation with the RWGS was proposed, following redox mechanism assisted by CO2 involving the Cr6+/Cr3+ redox cycle (Fig. 124). H2-TPR data revealed a higher Cr6+ amount over 3 wt% Cr/silicalite-1 than 3 wt% Cr/SBA-15 owing to better dispersion attributed to abundant nest silanol groups present in silicalite-1 zeolite. A comparison of catalytic performance 3 wt% Cr/Silicate-1 and 3 wt% Cr/SBA-15 revealed that higher isobutene conversion was achieved on 3 wt% Cr/Silicate-1 owing to higher amount of Cr active species, while higher selectivity was reached over 3 wt% Cr/SBA-15 attributed to the lower acidity of the SBA-15 sample. The characterization of spent catalysts showed that coke was more easily burnt off over 3 wt% Cr/Silicate-1 while a higher Cr6+/Cr3+ ratio was also determined indicating that Cr species interacting with nest silanols were more easily re-oxidized by CO2 during the ODH process. Moreover, the XRD pattern suggested the degradation of the SBA-15 ordered hexagonal structure. Thus, at 570 °C, maximum 36.5% isobutane conversion with 71% isobutene selectivity over 3 wt% Cr/silicalite-1 was reached (Table 17, entries 1–3).
