Previously, a series of 3 wt% Cr/SBA-15 samples doped with different amounts of Ce (1–5 wt%) were prepared by impregnation and their catalytic behavior studied for isobutene ODH with CO2.1122 The benefits of the incorporation of a second metal have been demonstrated throughout this review owing to the establishment of new metal/metal and metal/support interactions improving active metal phase dispersion and stabilization. In line, the incorporation of ceria into the 3 wt% Cr/SBA sample allowed enhancing the Cr6+ species amount attributed to the improved Cr species dispersion owing to ceria incorporation. Thus, the maximum catalytic performance was registered over 3 wt% Cr–3 wt% Ce/SBA-15 sample, with 35.5% isobutane conversion and 90% isobutene selectivity at 570 °C, 10 min TOS (Table 17, entries 4–8). The highest activity of 3 wt% Cr–3 wt% Ce/SBA-15 catalyst was attributed to the highest reducibility of Cr species determined by H2-TPR. A study of regeneration and recycling by calcination in air, showed that over 3 wt% Cr–3 wt% Ce/SBA-15 sample, for the first run, isobutene conversion decreased from 35.5% to 22.5% after 4 h TOS and that the original activity could be almost fully restored by calcination and for four runs, owing to the removal of coke and reoxidation of Cr3+ to Cr6+ active species (Fig. 125).
 Fig. 125  Conversion and selectivity versus TOS over 3 wt% Cr–3 wt% Ce/SBA-15 catalyst for isobutene ODH for 4 cycles. (■) conversion, (□) selectivity. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1122. Copyright 2017, SIOC, CAS, Shanghai, & WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
The good catalytic performance of a series of VOx/SBA-15 with different V loadings (1–11 wt%) prepared by impregnation for isobutane ODH was also demonstrated.1123 The catalytic and characterization data revealed that catalytic activity was dependent on dispersion and the amount of vanadium species. NH3-TPD profiles displayed that acid site formation was due to vanadia incorporation, and that 7 wt% V/SBA-15 exhibited lower acidity than 7 wt% V/MCM-41. Maximum catalytic performance was achieved over 7 wt% V/SBA-15, reaching 41% isobutane conversion and 85% isobutene selectivity due to highest reducibility and amount of dispersed vanadium species determined by H2-TPR and Raman spectroscopy (Table 17, entries 9–11). Comparison of catalytic performance of 7 wt% V/SBA-15 and 7 wt% V/MCM-41 showed that lower selectivity was achieved over 7 wt% V/MCM-41 due to higher acidity, while lower conversion was attributed to lower amount of VOx active species. A study of catalyst recycle and regeneration, for four consecutive uses and for 4 h TOS, showed that catalytic performance was recovered after calcination owing to coke removal and Cr species oxidation.
7.5.3 Conclusions. 
Isobutane ODH with CO2 constitutes an attractive approach to produce isobutene showing different advantages compared to catalytic dehydrogenation. In comparison to propane ODH, fewer papers have been reported in the literature but the isobutene yield and selectivity are high. Moreover, as currently it occurs for catalytic dehydrogenation, the study and development of new catalysts and technologies suitable for propane ODH will be valid and applicable to isobutene ODH.


7.6 Conclusions. 
Alkane ODH with CO2 as soft oxidation is an interesting approach with many advantages compared to catalytic dehydrogenation or ODH with oxygen, promoting ODH directly or combining alkane dehydrogenation and RWGS with globally favoured thermodynamics and improved catalyst life owing to the reverse Boudouard reaction. However, many unknown data remain to be clarified such as the redox cycle and involved species, mechanism, competitive adsorption between CO2, alkane and alkene, and desorption of the product and specially the stability, reuse and regeneration of catalysts for commercialization. To achieve the required knowledge and understanding, computational modeling and in situ spectroscopy studies will be essential tools for the rational design of efficient and stable ODH catalysts. Good catalytic results were achieved in the presence of OMS-based catalysts that should stimulate and drive the scientific community and industry to explore their potential up to the last step of commercial application. Moreover, to overcome the limitation of microporosity of zeolites, the benefits of the development of catalysts based on hierarchical zeolites and two-dimensional zeolite nanosheets to promote physical isolation and protection of particles as well as mass transfer should be explored.

C Electro and photocatalytic conversion of CO2
The use of solar energy to convert CO2 into chemicals and fuels has received great attention in the last 30 years. Indeed, electro- and photo-reduction reactions have great potential for producing fuel molecules such as CO, CH4, HCOOH, HCHO, MeOH and EtOH, using solar energy directly or indirectly. In photocatalysis, also called artificial photosynthesis, electrons are produced from the excitons of semiconductor photocatalysts by means of photonic excitation, whereas in electrocatalysis, the electrons are produced from an external electric field that, in turn, can be produced by photovoltaic solar cells, i.e. indirectly using solar energy.
1 Electroreduction of CO2
Electrocatalytic CO2 reduction is a very attractive CO2 conversion method due to its ambient reaction conditions and simplicity of device fabrication. However, there are several challenges, such as low CO2 solubility, high CO2 activation energy, low energy efficiency and low selectivity of the process. But the main drawbacks such as the low stability of the electrodes and their deactivation in less than 100 h restrict their commercialization (metal poisoning and agglomeration of the active centers),1124 while the low selectivity of the process requires costly separation steps and energy consumption.1125 The low efficiency of solar to chemical energy conversion is due to the difficulties in fulfilling all the requirements: high catalytic activity at the electrode surface, sufficient passivation of the electrode surface (limiting the recombination of carriers), formation of a high energy barrier at the semiconductor/solution interface, and proximity of energy levels between the semiconductor and the reactants to minimize energy loss in interfacial reactions.
In the last few decades, the large number of investigations on electrocatalysts, reaction conditions and devices for CO2 electroreduction have allowed the development of the knowledge and the improvement of the catalytic performance of electrocatalysts. The current understanding of the reaction mechanism requires further exploration involving theoretical calculations and in situ characterization techniques to monitor the variations in the electronic structure of the active sites and to detect metastable intermediates. Therefore, research should not be limited to the development of excellent electrocatalysts, but to an overall understanding of the electrolysis system. So far, the design and development of electrocatalysts, due to multiple requirements and complexity to improve their efficiency and stability, have not considered the use of zeolite and OMS probably due to their intrinsic properties and capabilities to generate and transfer electrons (potential, faradaic efficiency, and energy efficiency), and it is reasonable to consider that future research will focus on other approaches.
2 Photoreduction of CO2

2.1 Fundamentals of photocatalytic CO2 reduction. 
Photoreduction of CO2 is a very attractive strategy for the conversion of carbon dioxide into fuels, making possible a circular economy (Fig. 126). Photoreduction of CO2 is not a selective process and several products are obtained simultaneously. The physicochemical properties of a photocatalyst are determinants for the development of an efficient process and for controlling the different stages of light absorption, charge separation and migration, adsorption of reactants (CO2 and H2O), activation and conversion (redox surface reactions) and desorption of the products (Fig. 127).
 Fig. 126  Photoreduction of CO2 to solar fuels. 
 Fig. 127  Different steps involved in the photocatalytic reduction of CO2 over a semiconductor photocatalyst. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1126. Copyright 2017, Jinghua Wu et al. Published by WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
The photocatalytic reduction of CO2 requires high energy to break the CO bond and form the C–H bond, along with the participation of a high number of electrons (2, 4, 6, 8, 10, 12 or more electrons) to produce CO, HCOOH, CH4, HCHO, MeOH and others (Table 18). Then, the high redox potential for an electron transfer to CO2 is −1.9 eV due to the linear geometry of the CO2 molecule and the energy requirement to bend and inject an electron into its orbitals. Although the redox potential is lower than the conduction band (CB) of most semiconductors, some photocatalysts can adsorb CO2 and bend the configuration of the molecule. Thus, CO2 can have a lower LUMO level allowing electron transfer. To compare, the thermocatalytic reduction of CO2 requires elevated temperatures and pressures, while photocatalysis occurs at room temperature and ambient pressure under solar irradiation, and requires an efficient semiconductor. The photoreduction of CO2 constitutes a very attractive technology to produce fuel under atmospheric conditions and at least energy costs. Thereby, the photocatalytic properties of semiconductors have received much attention in recent decades. Among the main semiconductor photocatalysts used (MgO, Ga2O3, ZnO, CdS, GaP, SiC, WO3, Fe2O3, ZrO2),1126,1127 TiO2-based photocatalysts are preferred due to TiO2 availability, cheapness and their appropriate band-gap.1128 Moreover, these materials can be doped with metals such as Pt, Cu, Ag, Fe, Co, and Ni to limit charge recombination and favor the photocatalytic process.1129 Different strategies have been explored such as co-catalyst deposition, defect creation, increasing the specific surface area in order to increase the number of active sites to obtain an efficient photocatalyst with good charge separation, fast charge transfer, high absorption of the solar spectrum, low cost and high stability.1130
Table 18 Standard electrochemical potentials for CO2 reduction in water at pH ∼ 7

Upon irradiation, electrons from the valence band (VB) are excited to the conduction band (CB) leaving vacancies (“holes”) in the valence band. Therefore, the electrons and holes produced can be involved in a photocatalytic reaction (Fig. 127 and 128). The photoreduction requires a reductant and the normally preferred one is water owing to its richness, non-toxicity, and effectiveness. The main problem with the use of water as a hydrogen donor and hole quencher is that water competes to absorb electrons in the conduction band to generate hydrogen. In addition, this hydrogen is generally formed in higher yields than CO2 photoreduction products. Other reductants such as trimethylamine (amines) or Na2S capable of consuming the photogenerated holes during the photocatalytic reduction of CO2 are also used, especially in the gas phase, conferring structural stability to the photocatalysts.1131
 Fig. 128  Different reaction pathways and selectivity in vapor or liquid phase (conduction band (CB) and valence band (VB)). Reprinted with permission from ref. 1132. Copyright 2017, MDPI. 
The reaction medium is another parameter that drastically affects the reaction pathway and selectivity of the photoreduction process. A gas phase reaction through rapid vapor phase deoxygenation leads to selectivity towards methane, while a liquid phase reaction through rapid dehydrogenation of water leads to HCOOH, HCOH and CH3OH (Fig. 128).1132
To date, the efficiency of photoreduction of CO2 has been less than 1%, and it has been estimated that an efficiency of 10% or higher is necessary for economic feasibility.1133 Accumulation and rigorous interpretation of results based on knowledge and trial/error is a way to the development of efficient photocatalysts but the development of fundamental knowledge is crucial. However, the lack of understanding of the factors that control product distribution requires systematic studies and understanding of the parameters driving this distribution, such as kinetics, thermodynamics, surface adsorption and activation of CO2, determination of active species that allow high reduction potential of photogenerated electrons and simultaneous electron and proton transfer. Undoubtedly, computational chemistry and the design of materials will be fundamental to progress in understanding material properties and reaction mechanisms.
A large variety of photocatalytic systems with or without a co-catalyst or Z-scheme component compositions for CO2 photoreduction have been explored. Nevertheless, only a few studies that focused on the development of zeolite- and OMS-based photocatalysts have been reported. A recent review summarized the recent developments and future prospects of the application of zeolite-based photocatalysts for CO2 photoreduction.1134 Herein, the main advances and contributions of zeolite- and OMS-based photocatalysts for the photoreduction of CO2 to produce fuels are discussed. However, although it is a very attractive process due to its technological and experimental characteristics, the efficiency is too low even with the best photocatalyst to be considered for commercialization.


2.2 Photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2. 
2.2.1 Zeolite-based catalysts for the photoreduction of CO2. 
(a) TiO2/zeolite-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2. 
Zeolites are used as catalysts in numerous chemical processes due to their high specific surface area, pore structures, stability, Lewis and Brønsted acid and base properties, ability to host organic and metal complexes, nanoparticles and metal oxides, high CO2 uptake capacity1135 and ability to stabilize radical cations and trap electrons.1136,1137 All these properties can be tuned to obtain zeolites with superior CO2 adsorption and activation ability. Unfortunately, the application of zeolites as photocatalysts for CO2 photoreduction has been rather disregarded probably because it was assumed that the “ideal zeolite” was a good insulator1138 so research has focused on the development of semiconductors for CO2 photoreduction. The photoactivity of zeolite is related to the possible photoexcitation of the (Mn+–O2−) to (M(n−1)+–O−)* pair. Thus, different approaches have been explored to introduce substituted or anchored heteroatoms into zeolite structures to enhance their photocatalytic properties. Moreover, zeolites provide oxygen defects that promote better charge separation while OH groups favor the proton and charge transport. These physicochemical properties of zeolites make them attractive supports for the design and synthesis of photocatalysts with different active centers specific for competitive adsorption/desorption of CO2, CO, H2O and CH4 and redox reactions.
Several semiconductor photocatalysts have been extensively studied for the photoreduction of CO2 such as ZnO, TiO2, Fe2O3, graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4) and Cu2O. Among these, the adsorption, photoinduced activation and reduction of CO2 to CH4 have been extensively studied and demonstrated on TiO2 surfaces.1139 Therefore, several studies of zeolite-based photocatalysts have focused on the incorporation of TiO2 in either bulk form, highly dispersed nanoparticles, wall-embedded complexes or isomorphic substitutions (tetrahedrally coordinated).
A study of dispersion of TiO2 within Y zeolite cavities and frameworks, prepared following an ion-exchange method and hydrothermal synthesis (ex-Ti-oxide/Y-zeolite and Ti-oxide/Y-zeolites), showed the photocatalytic activity for the reduction of CO2 with H2O to produce CH4 and CH3OH.1140 Characterization data indicated that TiO2 species were highly dispersed within the zeolite cavities and were present in tetrahedral coordination. The photocatalytic performance for the formation of CH4 and CH3OH indicated that selectivities strongly depend on the type of catalyst. Thus, over ex-Ti-oxide/Y-zeolite, high activity and selectivity to CH3OH was registered, while CH4 was the main product over bulk TiO2 and Ti-oxide/Y-zeolite (Table 19, entries 1 and 2). The changes in the photocatalytic performance were attributed to the higher TiO2 dispersion and charge transfer excited state of the highly dispersed titanium oxide in the ex-Ti-oxide/Y-zeolite sample.

In 2001, the photocatalytic activity of Ti-Beta zeolites in a hydroxide and fluoride medium for the reduction of CO2 was described.1141 The higher hydrophilicity of the sample synthesized in a hydroxide medium (Ti-Beta(OH)) allowed higher efficiency in the quenching of the photoluminescence in the presence of H2O. Ti-Beta(OH) exhibited higher photocatalytic activity than Ti-Beta(F) attributed to the higher concentration of photoexcited active sites, (Ti3+–O−)*, while higher CH3OH selectivity was reached over Ti-Beta(F) photocatalysts. These results revealed the key role of hydrophilic–hydrophobic properties that promote proton and charge transfer as well as the adsorption of water on the reactivity and selectivity for the photoreduction of CO2 (Table 19, entries 4–6).
(b) Organic semiconductor/zeolite-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2. 
Another approach to improve the photocatalytic properties of zeolite-based materials is the incorporation of organic semiconductors, such as polythiophene (PT). Then, the preparation of the photocatalyst Na-ZSM-5-PT by chemical oxidative polymerization of thiophene for reduction of CO2 to formic and acetic acid was described.1142 The advantage of a PT/zeolite photocatalyst is the absorption of the radiation at wavelengths ≤650 nm, invading the infrared region and enabling the use of a wider range of wavelengths for the photoreduction of CO2 (Table 19, entry 16). In line, an advanced photocatalyst was prepared combining the photocatalytic properties of TS-1 nanosheets were combined with those of a semiconductor (graphitic carbon nitride (CN)) to enhance light absorption, especially for visible light. Thus, TS-1 nanosheets were hybridized with hydroxylated CN nanosheets to form a 2D/2D hCN/TS-1 Z-scheme nanocomposite that exhibited higher photocatalytic activity than the pristine TS-1 nanosheets (Table 19, entries 6 and 7). The photocatalytic performance increased with the increasing loaded amounts of CN up to 10 wt%, to reach the maximum owing to optimum charge separation due to strengthened interfacial interactions favorable to charge transfer.
(c) Effect of the morphology over the activity of zeolite-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2. 
The microporous nature of zeolite can hinder mass transfer and hamper the catalytic reaction. To this end, the use of zeolite nanosheets with thicknesses of a few nanometers, the introduction of a hierarchical porous structure and the introduction of macropores for the design of novel zeolite-based photocatalysts would be valuable strategies for future developments. Accordingly, the preparation of two-dimensional (2D) TS-1 nanosheets was reported not only to take advantage of the larger surface area but also to reduce the charge transfer distance for CO2 photoreduction exhibiting higher photocatalytic performance for the reduction of CO2 than the 3D sample (Table 19, entry 8).1143 Following the same approach, the synthesis and photocatalytic performance of zeolite SAPO-5 nanosheets for the photoreduction of CO2 was reported.1144 The photocatalytic study revealed that SAPO-5 ultrathin nanosheets exhibited six times higher photocatalytic activity than SAPO-5 microrods for CH4 formation and higher than other standard zeolites. The results were attributed to the higher amount of exposed Al atoms owing to the morphology of nanosheets that enhances not only the CO2 adsorption capacity but also the excited state electron capture and transfer. In addition, the nanosheet morphology allowed the improvement of the mass transfer process. All these improved physicochemical properties enabled longer excited state lifetime on SAPO-5 nanosheets and led to higher photoactivity for the reduction of CO2 (Table 19, entries 14 and 15).
(d) Effect of metallic doping over the activity of zeolite-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2. 
As stated above, the possible photoexcitation of the (Mn+–O2−) to (M(n−1)+–O−)* pair is responsible for the photocatalytic activity of zeolites. Therefore, different strategies have been followed to introduce heteroatoms into zeolite structures and to enhance their photocatalytic activity. Hence, the introduction of efficient electron scavengers such as noble metals such as Au, Pd or Ag that are relatively inexpensive, allows improving the photocatalytic performance. Titanosilicate-1 (TS-1) is an important zeolite with isomorphic substitution of TiIV species in the MFI zeolite framework, which has long been applied to various photocatalytic and oxidation reactions.1145,1146 The photocatalytic activity of zeolitic titanosilicate has been explored in various studies, and the activity of Ti–O species was reported to be higher than that of Ti species octahedrally coordinated of bulk TiO2 nanoparticles for CO2 photoreduction.1134,1141,1146 Therefore, in addition to the benefits of the pore system, large surface area and the possible modifications of redox and acid/base properties with functional components, different strategies have been followed to improve the photocatalytic activity of these materials, such as the deposition of metal clusters and metal oxide clusters, metal nanoparticles and metal oxide nanoparticles and polymeric semiconductor nanoparticles. Moreover, the presence of a high number of silanol groups confers high hydrophobicity to the surface of TS-1 that favors proton and charge transfer as well as adsorption of water.1147 The synthesis of Ag and TiO2 nanoparticles co-loaded zeolite TS-1 (Ag–TiO2/TS-1) by the ion-exchange and in situ photodeposition method in successive steps was recently reported for its use as photocatalyst for the reduction of CO2.1148 XPS and EPR analyses revealed that Ag–TiO2/TS-1 nanocomposite exhibits the higher amount of Ti3+ defects and oxygen vacancies, allowing better visible light adsorption and separation efficiency of the photogenerated charges. The high photocatalytic activity was attributed to the properties of TS-1, to the highly exposed reduction planes {101} of TiO2 and to the uniformly dispersed Ag nanoparticles. The TS-1 high surface area and rich defects enabled the high dispersion of active species and molecular adsorption of CO2, as well as accelerated charge separation by hole and electron trapping. Over 1Ag–16.9TiO2/TS-1, 3.16 for CO and 0.47 μmol g−1 h−1 high production rate for CO and CH4 under visible light irradiation, respectively, were reached and maintained along three cycles (Table 19, entry 7).
The photocatalytic activity of the ex-Ti-oxide/Y-zeolite catalyst was enhanced by the addition of Pt to promote the charge separation favoring CH4 over CH3OH formation (Table 19, entry 3).
Also, the photocatalytic performance of TS-1 nanosheet hybridized material with a semiconductor (graphitic carbon nitride (CN)), hCN/TS-1, was improved by the deposition of Ag nanoparticles. The hCN/TS-1 composite selectively decorated with Ag nanoparticles (Ag/hCN/TS-1) exhibited a high surface area and a 2D/2D heterojunction, improving the visible light absorption, charge separation and photocatalytic efficiency. The content (wt%) of CN and Ag was optimized and the 1.5Ag/hCN10/TS-1 composite displayed the best catalytic performance for CO2 photoreduction, and the yield in the presence of 1.5Ag/hCN10/TS-1 was approximately 7 times higher than that of pristine TS-1 (Table 19, entry 10). In this zeolite-based composite photocatalyst, the charge transfer was achieved under visible light irradiation (λ < 390 nm). The electrons photogenerated by TS-1 combined with the holes of CN allowed a prolonged excitation of the photoelectron lifetime, which was transferred to the Ag nanoparticles enabling a higher efficiency for the photoreduction of CO2.
The incorporation of other hetero-elements has been also reported.1149 The iron-containing zeolite HZSM-5 photocatalyst was used to prepare a Ti-HZSM-5 hybrid material by the sol–gel method. The characterization study revealed that Ti species were highly dispersed in a lower coordination state on the surface of HZSM-5 zeolite. The pristine HZSM-5 sample contains 0.03 wt% Fe which is tetrahedrally coordinated within the silica framework,1150 so the subsequent deposition of Ti species leads to the coexistence of Ti and Fe species on the surface of HZSM-5. Simultaneous photoexcitation under ultraviolet light allows the combination of two nearby excited states ((Fe2+–O−)* and (Ti3+–O−)*) that induce an increase of the excited electron concentration and an acceleration of the electron transfer rate over Ti-HZSM-5, as evidenced by the higher photocurrent response of the 0.99Ti-HZSM-5 sample, attributed to a higher efficiency of the separation of the photogenerated electron–hole pairs (Table 19, entry 11).
The preparation of a series of FeOX/ZSM-5 composites with different Fe contents for the photoreduction of CO2 to CO and aldehyde was described.1151 The unique interaction between FeOX and the ZSM-5 support leads to a large dispersion of FeOX which generates an alternative way to separate and migrate charge carriers and avoid the recombination of photogenerated electron–hole pairs. Consequently, the lifetime of charge carriers was prolonged. The photocatalytic results revealed that an increasing Fe content allowed reaching a maximum CO2 conversion rate corresponding to 3 wt% FeOX, with a further decrease. Therefore, over 3FeOX/ZSM-5 photocatalyst, a maximum 17.6 μmol g−1 h−1 high conversion rate of CO2 with a high aldehyde selectivity (43.1%) was reached (Table 19, entry 12).
In fact, the photocatalytic properties of zeolite have been associated with the presence of impurities in the sample such as Fe due to the presence of the (Fe3+–O2−)/(Fe2+–O−)* pair.1134,1152 Thereby, the photocatalytic activity of the commercial HZSM-5 zeolite for the photoreduction of CO2 with H2O at room temperature has been explored.1138 Under UV-light irradiation, Fe-O species act as excited (Fe2+–O−)* photoactive sites for the CO2 reduction reaction. The photocatalytic results revealed that the activity for CO production varied with the source of various zeolites. The highest 3.32 μmol g−1 h−1 CO production rate was achieved over the HZSM-5 sample CO2 (Table 19, entry 13).
2.2.2 Conclusion. 

Table 19 summarizes the main results obtained for CO2 photoreduction in the presence of zeolite-based photocatalysts. The productivity and fuel production cannot be compared exactly due to the variability in reaction conditions and reaction system configurations, but a general view can be given. The main drawbacks are the narrow absorption range of visible light together with low photoconversion efficiency, which limits the application of these photocatalytic systems. Compared to the study of semiconductor photocatalysts, the study of zeolite-based photocatalysts has been quite disregarded. The unique properties of microporous zeolites make them a viable photocatalytic alternative to sophisticated chemical and semiconductor processes. Their microstructures have a high surface area that favors a higher light uptake, a high CO2 adsorption capacity and physicochemical and adsorption/desorption properties that allow the activation of the molecules, providing them with a high reaction rate and photocatalytic activity. The synthesis and application of hierarchical zeolites to the development of photocatalysts should enable the enhancement of the unique properties of zeolites and counteract mass transport limitations. Hierarchical zeolites possess meso- and macroscale secondary porosity along with microporous primary structures. These materials then exhibit the conventional properties of zeolites with enhanced diffusion of molecules to solve the mass transport limitation. It is hoped that a combination of molecular simulation and machine learning techniques can be applied to the design of zeolite photocatalysts leading to an in-depth understanding of zeolite-based photocatalysts necessary for future research and development of the photoreduction of CO2.
2.2.3 OMS-based catalysts for the photoreduction of CO2. 
The photoconversion process involves three crucial steps: the light adsorption on the surface of the photocatalyst to produce electron–hole, the photogeneration carrier transport, and the oxidation–reduction reactions. The use of OMS based materials owing to their high surface area and their ability to support and host metallic nanoparticles and metal oxides, organometallic complexes and organic molecules to form specific active species allow improving the photocatalytic conversion of CO2 of the pristine materials.
(a) TiO2/OMS-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2. 
In 1998, the photocatalytic activity of titanium oxide species embedded within the framework of OMS Ti-MCM-41 and Ti-MCM-48 prepared by a hydrothermal synthesis was explored for the reduction of CO2 with H2O at 55 °C to produce CH4 and CH3OH in the gas phase.1153 The characterization study indicated that Ti species were highly dispersed within the zeolite framework and in tetrahedral coordination. The photocatalytic reaction rate and selectivity showed to be dependent on the type of catalyst. Over Ti-containing OMS and zeolite type catalysts, higher amount of CH3OH was produced while CH4 was the main product over bulk TiO2. These results were attributed to the charge transfer photoexcited state of the highly dispersed titanium oxide species (Ti3+–O−)* (Table 20, entry 1). The same trend was observed for the photocatalytic reduction of CO2 over Ti-FSM-16. The CH3OH production was related to the local structure of the Ti-oxide species and the photocatalytic activity attributed to the charge transfer excited state dispersed tetrahedrally coordinated Ti-oxide species (Ti3+–O−)* (Table 20, entry 2).1154 More recently, the photoreduction of CO2 was studied in the presence of optimized Ti-mesoporous silica, Ti-SBA-15 and Ti-KIT-6, with different Si/Ti ratios.1155 Photocatalytic study revealed that Ti-KIT-6 calcined with Si/Ti = 100 exhibited the best photocatalytic performance for the reduction of CO2 with water in the gas phase which was attributed to the higher accessibility to the active sites together with the lower number of TiO2 agglomerates and higher number of isolated Ti species determined by UV-Vis spectroscopy, TEM and XPS analyses. The concentrations of OH groups were found to play a key role due to the competitive adsorption of CO2 and H2O on the catalyst surface that influences the process selectivity towards CH4 or CH3OH. The higher concentrations of the OH groups, detected from the FT-IR spectra for Ti-KIT-6-calcined with Si/Ti = 100, control the adsorption properties and the reaction pathway (Table 20, entry 3). Therefore, CH4 formation increased with H2O adsorption increasing due to the greater hydrophilicity of the material. An appropriate way to boost the activation of inert CO2 molecules is its adsorption on the surface of any semiconductor to lower its energy barrier.1156 Therefore, a mechanism starting with the CO2 and H2O adsorption on isolated tetrahedrally coordinated Ti4+ centers was proposed ((Fig. 129). The UV light absorption enables charge-transfer excited states (Ti3+–O−)* with the photogenerated electron and hole localized on neighboring atoms responsible for a higher lifetime of the excited Ti3+–O− (54 μs)1157 in comparison to bulk TiO2 powder (nanosecond order). The interaction of CO2 and H2O on the (Ti3+–O−)* photoexcited active sites leads to the formation of intermediates species and finally to C, H and OH radicals further combined to generate CH4, H2 and CH3OH. The source and intensity of the UV light and the H2O/CO2 ratios are the key factors influencing the photoactivity of the catalyst, the main drawback of this system being that the sunlight is not short enough.

 Fig. 129  Reaction mechanism for the photocatalytic reduction of CO2 with H2O vapor over the Ti-KIT-6 photocatalyst. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1155. Copyright 2014 Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
(b) Addition of a co-catalyst to TiO2/OMS-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2. 
Different strategies have been developed to modify the acid/base properties of the photocatalyst surface in order to enhance the CO2 adsorption capacity, promote the separation of photogenerated electron–hole pairs (e−/h+) and to inhibit recombination such as the deposition or anchoring of organic and inorganic species.
(i) Effect of metallic doping over the photocatalytic activity for reduction of CO2. A series of TiO2/SBA-15 and Cu/TiO2/SBA-15 photocatalysts were synthesized by the sol–gel route and used for the photoreduction of CO2 to methanol.1158 The amount of TiO2 loading was shown to affect the crystallize size and the mesoporous structure of the catalyst, and an optimum loading of 45 wt% TiO2 with a crystalline size of ∼5.5 nm was determined. The addition of Cu as a co-catalyst to trap photogenerated electrons and inhibit recombination electron–hole pairs (e−/h+) allowed the enhancement of the catalytic activity. Therefore, over 2 wt% Cu/TiO2/SBA-15 catalysts, high photocatalytic activity was achieved with 627 μmol g−1 h−1 methanol productivity (Table 20, entry 4).
The effects of Cu-porphyrin (CuTPP) impregnation over the photocatalytic properties of Ti-MCM-48 materials for photoreduction of CO2 to CH3OH were recently explored.1159 The results showed that 85.88 μmol g−1 L−1 CH3OH productivity was reached over Ti-MCM 48 (Ti/Si = 25) owing to the presence of an excess of Ti3+ sites and mid-gap energy states enabling the transfer of photoexcited electrons from the Ti3+ sites selectively to the adsorbed CO2 molecules. Moreover, the impregnation of CuTPP (1.1 wt%) enhanced methanol yield up to 297 μmol g−1 L−1 (Table 20, entries 5 and 6). The photoluminescence study confirmed the charge quenching due to the electron transition from the CuTPP to Ti-MCM-48 material. The interaction between CuTPP and Ti sites of Ti-MCM-48 was responsible for the increase in the amount of Ti3+ sites due to the effective interfacial charge transfer. A mechanism for the charge separation/transportation in heterojunctions and photoreduction of CO2 to CH3OH over CuTPP/Ti-MCM-48 was proposed under visible light irradiation (Fig. 130).
 Fig. 130  Proposed charge transfer mechanism for CO2 reduction to CH3OH over CuTPP/Ti-MCM-48. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1159. Copyright 2018, Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved. 
The photoactivity and selectivity of the MgO/Ti-MCM-41 sample were also improved when the surface was decorated by Pt nanoparticles, reaching 8835 ppm g−1 h−1 CH4 productivity and 93% CH4 selectivity.1160 These results were attributed to the rapid transfer of photogenerated electrons from the molecular sieve to the cocatalyst (Pt) which favored the separation rate of the photogenerated electron and hole pairs and avoid their combination. When Pd was loaded, 94% CO selectivity was achieved (Table 20, entries 8 and 9).
(ii) Effect of alkaline and earth metallic doping over the photocatalytic activity for the reduction of CO2. A study of the effects of the modifications of the surface acidity and active sites by impregnation with basic-earth metal oxides and noble metal nanoparticles of Ti-MCM-41 samples prepared with a different molar ratio of Si/Ti was developed for the reduction of CO2.1160 The modified Ti-MCM-41 samples were used as photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2 to CH4 and CO. Over Ti-MCM-41 (Si/Ti = 10), 93 ppm g−1 h−1 CH4 production and 29% CH4 selectivity were achieved, while the impregnation of MgO to Ti-MCM-41 increased the photoreduction activity by 1.5 times with 42% CH4 selectivity (Table 20, entry 7). A study of Ca doping of Ti-MCM-41 effects was previously described for the photoreduction of CO2 to CH4.1161 The Ca doping allowed the improvement of CO2 absorption and the reported catalytic performance of Ca/TixSiMCM-41 was superior to that of the Ca/TixSiO2 nano-sized material. CaO is able to trap and release electrons in the conduction band of TiO2 and exhibits a high CO2 adsorption capacity, which improves the photoreduction of CO2. On the other hand, the photoexcited electrons in the CB can be efficiently accepted and transferred to the absorbed CO2 in CaO, so Ca/TixSiMCM-41 acts as an efficient reducing agent. Positive holes in CV can be trapped by OH or H2O species. These processes promote the separation of photogenerated electron–hole pairs (e−/h+), inhibit recombination and favor CO2 reduction. Nevertheless, an excess of Ti reduces the photoactivity probably due to the establishment of charge recombination centers within the semiconductor structure. Then, CH4 was produced over the Ca(10 wt%)/Ti35Si65MCM-41 catalyst at 82.0 μmol gcat−1 L−1 after 8 h TOS (Table 20, entry 10). These results showed that modifications of surface acidity and active sites allowed controlling the selectivity and efficiency of the CO2 reduction process.
(iii) Effect of amines doping over the photocatalytic activity for the reduction of CO2. The benefits of tetraethylenepentamine (TEPA) impregnated on the Ti-MCM-41 sample over the photocatalytic activity were investigated for CO2 reduction to CH4. The photocatalytic results showed that the presence of TEPA allowed for the improvement in the adsorption and activation of CO2. For a 1% TEPA content, optimum dispersion of the amino group was obtained and 232 ppm g−1 h−1 CH4 productivity was reached (Fig. 131) (Table 20, entry 11). However, the high impregnation amount of TEPA caused the blockage of the channel hampered CO2 conversion.1162
 Fig. 131  Proposed photocatalytic mechanism for CO2 reduction TEPA/Ti-MCM-41. Reprinted with permission from ref. 1162. Copyright 2020, Springer Nature B.V. 
The photoreduction of CO2 was also studied in the liquid phase in the presence of monoethanolamine (MEA) over Ti-MCM-41 to produce methane.1163 Monoethanolamine was used for both reductant and adsorption properties towards CO2. The results indicated that this novel method opens the possibility of integrating the most mature CO2 capture process of MEA absorption and the CO2 photocatalytic reduction process into one process. Ti–MCM-41 exhibited higher photocatalytic performance than commercial TiO2, and over Ti–MCM-41, 62.42 μmol gcat−1 CH4 productivity was reached under UV light that limits the application of this process (Table 20, entry 12).
(c) Perovskite/OMS-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2. 
An MCM-41@CBB (Cs3Bi2Br9) composite has been prepared following a facile and effective impregnation method to confine Cs3Bi2Br9 nanoparticles (lead-free halide perovskite) using an MCM-41 molecular sieve.1164 This MCM-41 @CBB has exhibited good photocatalytic performance for the reduction of CO2 to CO under visible light irradiation with 17.24 μmol g−1 h−1. This result was attributed to the space-confined growth of CBB in MCM-41, which promotes the carrier lifetime meaningfully, which is favorable for electron–hole pair separation and subsequent transfer. Other composites were prepared using SBA-3, SBA-15 and MCM-48 as support. The photocatalytic study revealed that these photocatalysts exhibited activities 2.97–13.4 fold higher than that of pure CBB, with 25.4, 23.7 and 5.62 μmol g−1 h−1 CO productivities, over SBA-3@CBB, SBA-15@CBB and MCM-48@CBB, respectively. CO selectivity was close to 78% for CBB and almost 100% for molecular sieve photocatalysts@CBB (Table 20, entries 13–16). Moreover, these results are comparable with those reported for many lead halide perovskites, and among the best of the lead-free perovskites. This work demonstrated that the space-confined growth of CBB in molecular sieves constitutes an excellent strategy to improve the photocatalytic CO2 reduction performance and stability of lead-free perovskite.
(d) CoO/OMS-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2. 
The benefits of CoO nanoparticles deposition on SBA-15 over the photocatalytic performance were explored.1165 High photocatalytic activity was observed for the reduction of CO2 to CO with 83% CO selectivity and a CO production rate of 25 626 μmol h−1 g−1. The results were attributed to enhanced dispersion and stability of the CoO NPs, the presence of hydroxyl groups on the surface, and absorption capacity of SBA-15 support (Table 20, entry 17).
2.2.4 Conclusion. 

Table 20 summarizes the main results obtained for CO2 photoreduction in the presence of OMS-based photocatalysts. The productivity and fuel performance cannot be compared exactly due to the change in reaction conditions and reactor configuration and catalyst preparation procedure, but a general view can be given. In general, the main drawbacks are the narrow absorption range of visible light together with low photoconversion efficiency (yield), which limits the application of these photocatalytic systems.
2.2.5 Conclusion. 
The main drawbacks of the photoreduction of CO2 to simple fuel molecules are the narrow absorption range of visible light together with low photoconversion efficiency, which limits the application of these photocatalytic systems. In comparison to the large number of studies reported for semiconductor photocatalysts, the study of zeolite-based photocatalysts has been quite disregarded. This fact has been attributed to the insulator property of zeolites. Nevertheless, the photocatalytic activity of zeolites associated with the possible photoexcitation of the (Mn+–O2−) to (M(n−1)+–O−)* pair and the key role of hydrophilic–hydrophobic properties governed by the presence of silanol groups to promote proton and charge transfer and water adsorption have been fully recognized and demonstrated. Moreover, the unique properties of zeolites and OMS make them viable photocatalytic alternatives to sophisticated chemical and semiconductor processes. Their porous structures have a high surface area that favors a higher light uptake, a high CO2 adsorption capacity and tunable physicochemical and adsorption/desorption properties that allow the activation of the molecules, providing them with a high reaction rate and photocatalytic activity. Moreover, the synthesis and application of hierarchical zeolites to the development of photocatalysts should enable the enhancement of the unique properties of zeolites and counteract mass transport limitations.
A combination of molecular simulation, machine learning techniques and in situ spectroscopy should help to determine the reaction mechanism and find the active sites that work to finally establish the structure–activity/selectivity relationship. This understanding is necessary for the rational design of zeolite and OMS-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2.

D Summary and perspectives
The use of CO2 as a C1 building block to mitigate CO2 emissions and at the same time produce sustainable chemicals or fuels is a challenging and promising alternative to meet the global demand for chemicals and energy. In this review, we have collected the different reactions involving zeolite and OMS-based catalysts to convert CO2 into valuable chemicals and fuels (Fig. 132). Their flexibility in the composition and structure such as support and/or a host of metals, metalorgano-complexes, and organo-catalysts and robustness were highlighted exhibiting the great potential of zeolite and OMS catalysts to boost CO2 chemical conversion. Today, the use of CO2 as a building block still requires a lot of effort, fundamental knowledge and insights to understand the mechanisms, determining the intermediate species and the active centres for each stage. The stability of CO2 and the high activation energy barrier to overcome thermodynamic equilibrium, requiring high energy consumption, are the main impediments and bottlenecks to commercialisation that keep these processes still in the laboratory research stage.
 Fig. 132  Scheme of CO2 conversion to valuable chemicals and fuels using zeolite- and OMS-based catalysts discussed in this review. 
From environmental standpoints, reactor design and energy efficiency are key and heat production must be based on renewable (wind, hydro and solar) or nuclear energy sources to minimize the carbon footprint and meet carbon neutrality. Moreover, the use of high temperature enhances the formation of undesirable products, making it crucial to design and develop efficient and selective catalysts. For CO2 conversion, heterogeneous catalysts are attractive and preferred for industrial implementation due to their catalytic features such as activity, robustness, efficiency, recovery and recycling, easy handling making them economically advantageous, environmentally friendly minimizing wastes. To enable CO2 conversion, efficient and high-throughput CO2 capture technologies with adequate purity, probably located at point sources, will first have to be available. In the same way, for the hydrogenation process, H2 will be available and green. Despite these barriers, CO2 constitutes an indisputable and necessary source of carbon to produce the chemicals and fuels that our society requires and on which it depends.
In the present review, we revise and examine the zeolite- and OMS-based materials activity and opportunities to convert CO2 into valuable chemicals and fuels. We summarise the main advances, limitations and major challenges to be overcome in order to successfully convert anthropogenic CO2. The synthesis of cyclic carbonates by cycloaddition of CO2 and epoxides in the presence of advanced multifunctional catalysts based on zeolites and OMS has been extensively studied, and the main issues are low catalyst stability and few recycling opportunities, as well as the use of co-catalysts and harsh operating conditions that hinder the feasibility of this transformation. However, we can highlight the catalytic performance of zeolites and OMS containing the structure directing agent as active sites for catalyzing the cycloaddition with interesting opportunities for stability and recycling and limited catalyst cost. The cycloaddition constitutes an attractive and important approach because it involves a high-energy three-membered ring substrate, being a 100% atom economy reaction. Moreover, the use of CO2 constitutes a great opportunity to substitute toxic carbonylating agents such as phosgene and derivatives. Nevertheless, the inertness and stability of CO2 proved to be the major impediments. On one hand, marginal routes have been explored to produce cyclic carbonates such as olefin oxidative carboxylation and CO2 carbonylation of diols that received low consideration. On the other hand, the synthesis of dialkyl-carbonates is thermodynamically limited and received poor attention as it is the case of the preparation of acyclic carbamates or 2-oxazolidinones.
The synthesis of carboxylic acids such as acetic acid by reaction between CH4 and CO2, and propanoic acid by reaction between ethane and CO2 are practically impossible from an industrial perspective because of high energetic barriers. With respect to the synthesis of formic acid, the few references found in the bibliography evidence that CO2 hydrogenation using zeolite- and OMS-based catalysts has been insufficiently explored besides the requirement of novel and efficient catalytic system for the very challenging production of formic acid as a potential way to chemically store hydrogen.
The direct hydrogenation of CO2 in methanol to industrial production is still an immature process and still requires improvement of the catalytic performance in terms of activity, stability and MeOH selectivity. For future study, the adsorption properties (hydrophobicity) of the surface should be considered while exhaustive and comprehensive studies of active species and intermediates of reaction will be required for a successful ab initio design of an efficient and robust catalyst, taking into accounts thermodynamics since the reaction is feasible from low temperature. Considering the morphology, topology, thermal stability, hydrophobic/hydrophilic, acid/base and redox properties of zeolites and OMS materials, greater consideration should focus on these materials when designing new catalysts.
The direct transformation of CO2 into DME is an attractive route considering the environmental and economic issues involved. Nevertheless, the developed catalysts exhibited limited DME selectivity because of high CO production, in addition to the drastic deactivation catalyst due to water production. Nonetheless, the catalytic performance of La–Cu–ZnO–Al2O3/HZSM-5 and Cu–ZnO–Al2O3/HZSM-5 due to the good DME selectivity and productivity was highlighted. However, the direct transformation of CO2 into DME remained still an immature technology from an industrial perspective and studies taking into account catalyst stability and regeneration step should be performed.
Among CO2 hydrogenation reactions, CO2 methanation is the most favoured reaction from thermodynamics. The activation of CO2 is of paramount importance to reach high methanation activity at lower reaction temperatures constituting crucial parameters, the oxygen mobility and basic properties of the support, as well as the hydrophobicity of the catalytic surface to limit the water adsorption and inhibitory effect. Among the various zeolite-based and OMS-based catalysts herein reported, USY-based catalysts exhibited great potential owing to improved stability provided by post synthesis treatments. From commercial and industrial perspectives, several catalytic processes and reactors technology are available that should be transferred to CO2 methanation as high conversion and CH4 selectivity close to 100% are reached at the laboratory scale. However, the viability for large-scale CO2 fixation also depends on readily availability and price of CO2 and H2 issued from waste and/or renewable sources in order to develop sustainable processes, based on the heat produced from renewable or nuclear energy to move towards zero carbon footprint.
CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH is an attractive process to obtain new building block molecules and energy carriers. Although this process has been widely studied in the last decade, few studies focused on the development of catalysts based on zeolites or OMS besides the benefits of confinement effect, nanoparticle stabilization by electronic features and shape-selectivity (reactants, products, and transition states). The best yield has been achieved over the RhFeLi/TiO2 catalyst with 4.91% C2+OH, and maximum 100% ethanol selectivity on the organic product basis has been reached over the Cu@Na-Beta catalyst. The main challenge is to improve the yield and selectivity of C2+OH. To this end, the ab initio design of the catalysts, introducing the necessary active sites, rationalised and simulated by computational chemistry can be a decisive tool. Zeolites and OMS-based catalysts constitute good candidates for the development of selective and stable catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation to C2+OH, but they have been so far under-considered.
CO2 hydrogenation to gasoline, olefins and aromatics offers new opportunities to convert CO2 into value-added products and pave a path to CO2 circularity. In the last decade, significant advances in the development of tandem catalysts based on metal oxides and zeolites have been made for the selective hydrogenation of CO2 to lower olefins (C2–C4), gasoline (C5–C11) and aromatics (C6–C8) of wide interest to the chemical industry. The high activity of the heterogeneous catalyst is usually attributed to the synergy between the active sites and the support and/or the promoter. The improvement of process selectivity is still required and is challenging, and should avoid expensive separation steps. Thus, new studies on the specific modification of zeolite morphology, such as tuning the pore size and poisoning of external acid sites, should allow controlling the hydrocarbon product selectivity. Undoubtedly, the future tandem catalyst for direct hydrogenation of CO2 to C2+ will be based on zeolite-type materials.
The CO2 transformation into syngas constitutes a winning approach towards the sustainable production of chemicals and fuels towards a carbon neutral society. Further syngas conversion into MeOH or through the Fischer Tropsch process constitutes direct routes to the production of fuels and chemicals through technologies already explored, developed and implemented. Accordingly, the production of CO from RWGS is challenging because of its huge interest as a building block. Numerous studies investigated the RWGS process but new efforts are still required to determine the reaction mechanism and achieve comprehensive knowledge about the nature of active sites to finally establish structure–activity/selectivity relationship. In the present review, we recompiled a few studies revealing that the potential of zeolites and OMS materials has been scarcely considered and undervalued. These studies described important strategies to maximize CO selectivity and CO2 conversion, to enhance metal–support interaction and stability, to improve CO2 adsorption/activation and fast and easy CO desorption, which are determinants for the design of new RWGS catalysts. Future studies of catalytic stability and deactivation are also essential for RWGS development and implementation. Much effort has to be devoted to gain a greater knowledge and for understanding of RWGS, and a final push is needed to move from laboratory studies to the industrial scale, where zeolites and OMS based materials should play a more relevant role.
The catalytic conversion of DRM into syngas (H2 + CO) enables the simultaneous mitigation of two main greenhouse gases, CH4 and CO2, which is a 100% atom efficiency process, and provides a highly regarded H2/CO ratio of 1 for further industrial conversion. However, in practice, DRM requires high energy extra consumption due to endothermicity, inducing fast catalyst deactivation, which compromises its industrial implementation as an environmental-friendly method to date. Therefore, the design of a robust and efficient catalyst at “low temperature” is topical and very challenging. On the other hand, the viability of the technology depends on the capture and availability of both CO2 and CH4, which cannot be of a fossil source. The present review illustrates the high activity of Ni-based zeolites and OMS catalysts despite the deactivation due to Ni sintering and coke deposition that in turn may block access to the active sites and plug the reactor in long-term tests. The use of high surface area support and bi- or multi metal-doping constitutes the main strategies to enhance the metal–support interaction and match suitable acid–base and redox properties. Zeolites and OMS offered great opportunities for the design and development of robust and efficient DRM catalysts but the catalytic performance remained far from an industrial application. The studies require long-term stability test and catalytic regeneration step while the operating temperature is very high (>750 °C), with catalytic performance close to thermodynamic equilibrium. From a materials standpoint, the small pore size system of zeolites compared to mesoporous materials may be a weakness to reach a high dispersion of small metal nanoparticles and probably more prone to pore blockage. A large collection of references shows the potential of SBA-15 as support, which makes SBA-15 a great candidate for the development of DRM catalysts owing to the high surface area, confinement effect of mesoporous structure and thermal stability that should allow achieving high Ni nanoparticles dispersion and stability using optimum preparation method and suitable metallic promoters. Furthermore, the design of DRM reactors with alternating cycles of reaction and catalyst regeneration should be key. On the other hand, the combination of POM and DRM processes offers opportunities to overcome the main limitation of energetic cost by taking advantage of the POM exothermicity, although the POM + DRM route has been insufficiently explored and even less so in the presence of zeolite and OMS catalysts.
Another route based on renewable feedstock to produce syngas is the dry reforming (DR) of alcohols derived from bio-resources such as bio-ethanol and glycerol derived from biodiesel, effectively closing the carbon cycle by producing valuable chemicals and using the main greenhouse gas, mitigating the effects of anthropogenic CO2. Besides, dry reforming of ethanol and glycerol are emerging technologies that require knowledge and mechanistic insights to design efficient catalysts, reach sustainable syngas manufacturing, and solve the crisis of glycerol as a biodiesel byproduct. However, from the point of view of global syngas production and consumption and the urgency to mitigate CO2 emissions, the DRG would constitute an almost marginal alternative and DRE results a more promising and attractive approach.
As an alternative to catalytic dehydrogenation, oxidative dehydrogenation using CO2 as a soft oxidant emerged and strengthened due to several advantages from energetic, thermodynamic and kinetic (rate of reaction, higher alkene selectivity) standpoints, suppressing substrate and product over-oxidation, reducing carbon deposits and consuming CO2, the main greenhouse gas. Then, oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes has been extensively investigated as an energy-saving and more environmentally friendly strategy to produce alkenes. The use of CO2 as a soft oxidant for alkanes ODH involves several advantages to ensure and enhance the catalytic activity due to its ability to activate ODH directly promoting alkane ODH or by a combination of alkane dehydrogenation and RWGS, and thus favouring thermodynamics. In this review, the collected results for EB ODH revealed that high ST selectivity was reached at a good conversion level. Nevertheless, the lifetime of the catalysts has been insufficiently studied and future studies devote more efforts to studying the robustness of catalysts and reactor configurations to achieve and maintain high EB yield and selectivity over a long operating time, combining reaction and regeneration stages. SBA-15 exhibits great potential in contrast to zeolite that despite its better thermal stability and robustness and presents a limitation to efficiently and homogeneously disperse the high amount of metallic oxide due to the diffusion restrictions and the blockage of pores. Regarding ethane ODH, most of the catalysts are CrOx-based, implying severe limitations due to the carcinogenicity of chromium species and hazardous catalyst preparation, use and elimination. Besides, 5 wt% Cr/SBA-15/Al2O3/FeCrAl catalytic performance allowed reaching interesting goals of production and catalyst stability with a good 63% ethylene yield, over 1100 h highlighting the potential of SBA-15 as support. Actually, the catalyst success will depend on catalyst and operation costs, and catalyst stability besides the catalytic performances and features. Propane ODH with CO2 was also reviewed and good catalytic data were reached, although propane selectivity and productivity must be further improved.
The main drawbacks to the photoreduction of CO2 to simple fuel molecules are the narrow absorption range of visible light together with low photoconversion efficiency, which limits the application of these photocatalytic systems. The photocatalytic activity of zeolite associated with the possible photoexcitation of the (Mn+–O2−) to (M(n−1)+–O−)* pair as well as the key role of hydrophilic–hydrophobic properties governed by the presence of silanol groups to promote proton and charge transfer and water adsorption have been fully recognized and demonstrated. Moreover, the unique properties of zeolites and OMS make them viable photocatalytic alternatives to sophisticated chemical and semiconductor processes. Their adjustable porous structures have a high surface area that favors a higher light harvesting, a high CO2 adsorption capacity and tunable physicochemical and adsorption/desorption properties that allow the activation of the molecules, providing them with high reaction rate and photocatalytic activity. Moreover, the future synthesis and application of hierarchical zeolites to the development of photocatalysts should be a new breakthrough to improve mass transfer and ensure their development. Future research based on a combination of molecular simulation, machine learning techniques and in situ spectroscopy should help to determine the reaction mechanism and find the active sites that work to finally establish the structure–activity/selectivity relationship. This understanding is necessary for the rational design of zeolite- and OMS-based photocatalysts for the reduction of CO2.
In the present review, we have analyzed the current state of the art in the zeolite- and OMS-based materials activity and opportunities to convert CO2 into valuable chemicals essential for our daily lives and to pave the way towards reducing carbon footprint. The development of efficient and competitive CO2 capture and green H2 technologies are essential to moving towards CO2 conversion, while the potential of zeolite- and OMS-based materials should be part of the success. Hence, we hope that this review will be a useful contribution to the scientific community and will constitute a new stimulus for the use of zeolite and OMS based materials, and the development of new processes and technologies to boost the conversion of CO2 into chemicals and fuels.

Alexandra Velty
	Dr Alexandra Velty graduated in Chemical Engineering at the University of Rennes and received her BSc degree in Chemistry at the University of Nancy (France). She completed her PhD under the supervision of Prof. Avelino Corma at the ITQ (UPV-CSIC) in Valencia (Spain) in 2003 in the field of heterogeneous catalysis and green chemistry for the preparation of fine chemicals. Until now, she has been working in Prof. Avelino Corma's group as a permanent member of the Polytechnic University of Valencia. Her research interests focus on heterogeneous catalysis and sustainable chemistry for biomass transformation and fine chemical preparation.

Avelino Corma
	Prof. Avelino Corma, professor and founder of the Instituto de Tecnología Química (CSIC-UPV) in Valencia (Spain), has been carrying out research in heterogeneous catalysis in academia and in collaboration with companies. He has worked on fundamental aspects of acid–base and redox catalysis with the aim of understanding the nature of the active sites and reaction mechanisms. With these bases have been developed catalysts that are being used commercially in several industrial processes. He is an internationally recognized expert in solid acid and bifunctional catalysts for oil refining, petrochemistry and chemical process. He has published more than 1000 research papers and is an inventor of more than 130 patents.
